                              (ii)
                          Editor’s Note

      Cloistered in a solitary house at Athani, a tiny town in a remote commer
of Karnatak, Shri K.D. Sangoram (29.12.1897 — 29.11.1983), drowned
himself in the exploration of the Vedas. His Vedic studies date back to his
early college days. An erudite Sanskrit scholar, he was a deeply religious
soul. He ardently advocated that though the Vedic treasure was an Indian
heritage, it should not remain an exclusive property of Indians, but should
hold a torch for all humanity. He entertained a keen desire to place before
the scholarly world the mystical message of our ancient seers by tracing
a Pathway to God as reflected in the Vedas. The Vedic Mantra combines
style with substance, and music with meaning. Shri Sangoram selected
hymns from the holy texts and classified them under suitable heads. In this,
he closely followed the method adopted by Professor R.D. Ranade, the great
philosopher-saint for whom he cherished high reverence. Shri Sangoram
had a passion for excellence and perfection. He ‘sought no relaxation from
the never-hasting, never-resting work’. His rumination went on continu-
ously; but it did not perhaps reach his idea of perfection. Years rolled by.
Yet the actual composition of the English volume did not commence. His
brother-disciple, Shri M.S. Deshpande, was constantly goading him to start
the transcription. But the work was not destined to be handled by him. Shri
Sangoram’s various pre-occupations in social, educational and cultural
fields and his indifferent health just did not allow him to begin the work.
Fearing that the valuable material would remain on the shelves for indefinite
time, Shri Deshpande urged him to hand over the Vedic selections to me
for writing the English exposition. Thus the source material came to me.
The blue-print along with the bricks was at hand. My work was to lay brick
to brick and shape the material into an edifice. The design to be adopted
was psychology and philosophy of religion. Parallels          from Western
philosophy were to be drawn for the purpose. That is why Socrates and
Plato, Descartes and Kant, James and Taylor appear in the present
narration.

      Shri Sangoram gladly and promptly supplied whatever clarification
was sought. In a short time a major portion of the final draft was ready.
He carefully went through it. Bhavan’s Journal, Bombay, came forward to
serialise the work. Letters of appreciation flowed from the readers and Shri
Sangoram was overwhelmed. Efforts to complete the work re-doubled.
Only a few pages of the English exposition remained to be read by him.
Thereafter all the ten chapters were to be polished and the Press Copy
Pathway to God in the Vedas



prepared. In September 1983, when I had been to Athani to discuss a few
points regarding the proposed publication, Shri Sangoram promised that
he would stay with me in Bombay for some time to finalise all the chapters.
But at this crucial time, all of a sudden, death snatched him away. He was
hale and hearty on the fateful morning of 29th November, 1983. At about
11.30 A.M. he went out to post a letter and returned — slightly exhausted.
He felt uneasy. A doctor administered some medicine and assured to be
back after a while. Minutes ticked away. A slight gurgling in the throat,
a spasm of cough and then — the eternal sleep from which there is no
waking.


      The swift march of the writing work came to a grinding halt.
 Undreampt of difficulties from unexpected quarters cropped up in the way
 of completion and publication of the volume. More than a decade passed
 and the work began to gather dust. But Nature repairs the ravages wrought
 by time and circumstances. It was in January       1995 that hurdles were
 removed and the work put on the rails again. It gathered momentum with
 the unstinted cooperation of Shri Sangoram’s admirers. The first instalment
_ Of the MS was sent to the Press on Yugadi — the Hindu New Year Day.

       Shri Sangoram’s was a unique personality. He was closely associated
 with a number of public charitable Bodies. He would burn midnight oil
 carefully scrutinising financial matters just like Saint Ekanath who kept
  awake a whole night to locate a mistake of a farthing in the public accounts.
  Trust money was a sacred cow to Shri Sangoram, and he firmly held that
  the custodians had a special responsibility to see that not a pie was mis-
  spent. An alert honesty is the price of becoming a member of a Public Trust,
  he maintained. Shri Sangoram was generous to the core. When ruined
  spendthrifts hanged on him with their tales of woe, he hastened to their
  succour. Like Oliver Goldsmith’s Village Parson, Sangoram’s pity gave ere
  charity began. Moreover, though he relieved their pain, he never chided
  their vagrant wanderings. He was often reminded by his friends that he was
   cheated to the tune of a few thousands or that some one had inflicted an
   unkindest cut on him; but he would only smile and despatch peoples’
   ingratitude with just a gentle wave of his hand. It appeared as though he
   ‘forgot their vices in their woe’. His selfless service was a shining example
   of Niskama-karma taught in the Bhagavad-gita. In the early sixties, a village
   party of amateurish musicians came to the Nimbal Ashram during a
   teligious festival. Shri Sangoram was one of the main pillars of the Ashram.
   The unskilled artists insisted that their performance be included in the
    celebration-schedule. It was an ill-organised and rustic-mannered group.



                                       iv
                                                                      Editorial



Then began the drab singing accompanied by a novice on the Tabla and
the village maestro with ill-tuned cymbal. Members of the audience left the
scene one by one. But Shri Sangoram sat the whole night and listened with
rapt attention   to   their big,   booming,    buzzing confusion   couched   in
stentorious tones. Occasionally his appreciation would come out in the form
of ‘Well done’, ‘Wonderful’. Later he observed: “What is important in the
performance of the simple village folk is not music but the fervent devotion
or Bhava with which they are imbued”.

      Shri Sangoram combined in himself rare gifts of energy,      intelligence
and wisdom. His nature, however, was always to remain in the background.
He worked, but silently; he gave in charity, but his left hand knew not what
his right hand had given. He meditated for long hours, but never made an
exhibition of it. He had no complaint against any one or any thing. He was
very polite and always had a smiling demeanour. It was a pleasure to work
in his quiet and dignified presence. Unruffled in the midst of calamities
(which were not a few in his eventful life), he exuded robust optimism and
cheerfulness. His outer calmness was a replica of his deep, inner stillness.
He was a versatile scholar well-versed in Law, Literature or Astrology. A
connoisseur of music, he was also endowed with a melodious voice. It was
indeed a treat to listen to his musical renderings of the songs of JnaneSvara
or Tukarama,     Kabir    or (1514858,        Purandaradisa   or Sarpabhisana
Sivayogi. It is reported that in his college-days he acted in dramas only
to win laurels. He had also made a name as a good cricketeer.
       He was singularly cast to be a researcher and went from village to
village and from house to house to get a shred of paper relating to persons
or places of the Nimbargi spiritual school to which he belonged. He had
preserved valuable documents that are an authoritative source-material for
the history of his Sampradaya. With his colossal memory, he used to hum
the Vedic Stktas with proper accents. He pointed out verses which had a
definite spiritual import. Richness and maturity of thought combined with
profound religious experience, make his writings highly weighty.
      He had left his legal practice — where he shone with unparalleled
grace — early in the day and took to God-devotion and philanthropic work.
Preservation of holy relics and articles, construction or repair of temples,
publication of philosophical and religious books, running and financing of
charitable institutions or starting of schools and colleges, are some of the
areas where he has left his indelible imprint. No one cared more, no one
helped more, no one toiled more, no one sacrificed more and no one
suffered more than Shri K.D. Sangoram for the furtherance of the sacred
cause for which his mentor, Gurudey R. D. Ranade stood.
Pathway to God in the Vedas



     Shri Sangoram took Rgveda as the basis for his selections and
specified with minute details how a particular Mantra appeared in other
Vedas with a little variation. A single word was sufficient for him to revive
these parallels. For example, he jotted down notes like this: “The Rgveda
Mantra X.141.4 is the same as Yajurveda XXXIII.86 and Atharvaveda
ग्रा.20.6 with some variations. Yajurveda makes two changes: For the word
बृहस्पति म्‌ in the 1२९५९१३, it substitutes सुसंदृशा and for the word संगत्याम्‌ it
puts in two words अनमीवः and संगमे. Atharvaveda also effects two changes:
instead of बृहस्पति म्‌ it uses the expression उभावि ह and at the end of the
Mantras, it adds the phrase दानकामश्च         नो   भुवत्‌.” The Brahmanas, the
Aranyakas, the Upanisads, the Bhagavad-gita and the Vedanta-sitras were
 combed by Shri Sangoram. Saunaka’s Revidhana or his Brhaddevata,
 Yaska’s Nirukta, commentaries on Vedic compositions by Uvatacarya,
 Mahidharacarya or Bhattabhaskara were consulted. Shri Sangoram had
 Studied Sayana’s commentary on the Vedas and has frequently quoted from
 it. There are many references to Madhva’s Rgbhasya. The Mahabharata
 and the Bhagavata found a place of honour in his reading material. He had
 a mastery over Svatmarama Swamin’s Hathayoga-pradipika. He supple-
 mented his knowledge of Yogic physiology by a study of Caraka’s Astanga-
 hrdaya. Acaryas of the Vedanta school make their appearance in his notes.
 In a word, he scanned the whole of traditional literature thoroughly. So far
 about collection of Sanskrit sources.

        Then followed the equally mighty task of translation and interpreta-
  tion. Shri Sangoram was a voracious reader and knew Kannada, Marathi,
  English, Sanskrit and Hindi. He enriched his Vedic scholarship by roaming
  through the vast territory of treatises on the Vedas in these languages. Some
  of the Vedic scholars he studied are mentioned below language-wise.

        English : V.S. Agarwal, Shiva Dayal Arya, Aurobindo, A.C. Bose,
  Devi Chand, Ralph T.H. Griffith, T.V. Kapali Shastri, C. Kunhan Raja,
  Ludwig, Gopalacharya, A.B. Purani, M.P. Pandit, Sampurnananda, H.P.
  Venkat Rao and H.H. Wilson.

         Marathi : Bapat Shastri, Sadashiv Shastri Bhide, Siddheshwar
   Shastri Chitray, R.G. Kolangade, Bhau Shastri Lele, Ramachandra Vinayak
   Patwardhan, S.D. Satavalekar and Tulzapurkar.

         Kannada : Anantarangachar, Chakravarti and Laxman Babani Pai.
         Hindi : Abhaya Dev Vidyalankar, Bhagavadacharya, Mangal Dev
    Shastri, Ram Govind Trivedi and Virendra Shastri.


                                         vi
                                                                     Editorial



      Swami Dayananda’s works were equally important to him. Even
stray articles on the Vedas appearing in periodicals like Brahma Vidya,
Prabuddha Bharata, Vedic Light, Bhavan’s Journal, Purushartha, Vaidika
Dharma, Jana Jnana, Hindu Vishva or Prasad did not escape his alert eye.
Annual numbers like Yoganka, Dharmanka, Sadhananka, Bhagavannamanka
or the Cultural Heritage of India Series, provided grist for his reading mill.
He had a catholic out-look and cherised great love for the Bible. We find
in his notes quotations from St. Matthew, St. John, The Acts or the Romans
of the New Testament.

     Shri Sangoram perused a number of translations of the Vedic
Mantras but in most cases, gave his own rendering. Every interpreter of
a Vedic Mantra was dear to him; but Truth was dearer still; and so he
always looked for corroborative evidence for any interpretation.

      I consider it as one of my good fortunes to bring to light Shn
Sangoram’s valuable work. Both the author and his silent research spread
over half a century remained away from lime-light. The work of English
exposition was. undertaken as a labour of love. The responsibility of
publication is cherished as an humble tribute to the memory of a noble
soul.

      Thanks are due to Shri Surendra Sangoram, son of Shri K.D.
Sangoram, for giving permission to publish the work. Dr. P.G. Lalye, Pune,
Dr. G.B. Palsule Pune, Dr. R.L. Joshi, Solapur, Dr. Mrs. Shailaja
Karandikar, Pune, have laid me under great obligation by providing
references and explaining knotty problems, whether grammatical or
philological. Authorities of Delhi University, Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan,
Bombay, Indira Gandhi National Centre for the Arts, New Delhi,
Bhandarkar Oriental Research Institute, Pune, D.A.V. College, Solapur,
gave me free access to their reference sections. Dr. S.G. Kulkarni,
Hyderabad University, provided documentation of passages quoted in the
text. I am grateful to Shri V.G. Nitsure, Bijapur, who made a clean and
neat Press Copy and to Shri S.D. Shintre, Solapur, for typing and re-typing
the chapters at various stages. Shri N.S. Kulkarni, Solapur and Smt.
Pushpa Nakhate, Solapur, volunteered to write out the Sanskrit sources.
Shri S.K. Joshi, Solapur, verified the typescript. Sou. Sunanda Shintre,
Solapur, checked the Sanskrit sources. Dr. A.D. Kulkarni, Bijapur and Shri
A.V. Jog, Solapur, helped me out from many a difficult situation. Shri V.R.
Kotbagi, Belgaum, always encouraged me with the words : ‘Not merely
fare-well, but also fare forward’. Credit for the attractive cover-page, goes
to Sou. Sushama Agnihotri, Pune. Shri M.R. Gokhale, New Delhi, was kind

enough to arrange for printing paper at a concessional rate. The elegence
of the book is due to the untiring efforts of Shri Randhir Singh Rohilla

Enterprises, Delhi.

     The loss of Shri K.D. Sangoram, Shri M.S. Deshpande, Dr. Mrs.
Shailaja Karandikar and Shri V.R. Kotbagi who would have loved to see
the completion of the work, fills me with a poignant feeling.

                                    211
                             Introduction

       The Vedas constitute the original source of philosophy and religion
of India, the fons et origo of its culture. The very ethos of India is based
upon the Vedic sanctions; its customs and traditions, its very way of life,
its histories and mythologies are nothing but an exfoliation of Vedic roots.
Its   systems   of philosophy,   in   order to   be   authoritative,   must   be   in
consonance with the Vedic teachings. We should never forget that the
Vedas containing the oldest culture of the world have provided to us a
vantage ground from which we can look at the later civilizations and
understand them.

       We find in the Vedas the thrill of poetry, theology and emotion as
well as coolness of reasoning. The message which was already laden with
meaning developed musical wings in the Simaveda. Lokmanya Tilak while
commenting on the Gita-expression वेदानां सामवेदोऽस्मि    says that the greatness
of the Samaveda lies in its musical quality. In the path of devotion, the
singing of the praises of the Lord occupies a very high place and Samaveda
had in a way fostered Bhakti by putting Rgveda Mantras to tune. The
Bhagavad-gita being the foremost exponent of Bhakti, it is no wonder that
it selected Samaveda as a manifestation (Vibhiti) of God.

       The Vedic seers had an unparalleled aesthetic sense; with a keen eye
on beauty they have given us some of the finest descriptions of nature. Their
whole life was spent on the lap of nature which for them was a living force.
The communion between man and nature is an evidence of their exalted
sensibility. Rudolf Otto would say that this heightened sensibility of the
experience of beauty or sublimity is the schema of the experience of the
Numen, meaning thereby that the former would arouse the latter and that
in the deeply felt aesthetic experience, we have the nearest analogue to the
spiritual experience. We notice in these sacred texts a distinct heliotropism,
the craving for light, both physical and spiritual. References to Jyoti,
Swama-Jyoti, Bha, Teja or Sirya are strewn liberally.

       For the Vedic Rsis nature was full of benevolent deities to whom they
prayed again and again and prayed superbly. Hamlet-wise, we may say that
the prayer is the thing in which they wanted to catch the grace of the deities.
The Vedas express loftiest sentiments that man can feel for his deity. We
may take one example, that of Agni, the Fire-god. Agni is the intermediary
between man and God. In this he discharges a schematic function by
interpreting the devotee to God and God to the devotee. Agni is Janus-faced
Pathway to God in the Vedas



with one face tumed towards God and the other towards man. It 1s
interesting to note that Agni is actually described as having two heads (&
शीर्षे) in Rgveda (IV.58.3). He invites gods to the sacrifices performed by
men, carries oblations offered by men to gods and brings back their
blessings to men in turn. He is the giver of heat and light. He bestows a
of knowledge which destroys the darkness of ignorance. Agni thus become
the purifier, the sanctifier and the ultimate benefactor. He is the precept
           ifi
                            णं
                                            ८
                                                                त
                                                                                 or,




protector, warm friend and supreme shelter.

       There is an unbroken continuity in the movement of Indian thought
especially in the realm of philosophical and spiritual pursuit.
                                                                      ne oe
expression, शि ष्यस्तेऽहं शाधि मां त्वां प्रपन्नम्‌ (1.7), where the disciple is
imploring his teacher to impart knowledge to him can well be traces back
 to Rgveda expression शि क्ष सु अपत्यस्य शि क्षोः (1.19.3) (enlighten this sincere
 child of yours). There is also ideological similarity between Vedic
 teachings and ideas developed in later eras. The Vedas talk of the river
 of existence coming to an end at the bund (Setu) of God.

 त्वं नो अग्न आयुषु त्वं देवेषु पूर्व्य वस्व एक इरज्यसि ।
 त्वामापः परि स्रुतः परि   यंति   स्वसेतवो नभंतामन्यके समे
                                                        ।। (२९६५९५० 11.39.10)
 The concept of Setu or bund is used by the Vedanta-sitra to indicate oe.

 परमतः सेतून्मानसंबंधभेदव्यपदेशेभ्यः (111.2.31). We may also note that the idea 0
  Jivanmukti or liberation here and now (इह) is first enunciated in the
  Taittiriya Aranyaka (11.12.16), where we have the celebrated expression
  वि दान्‌ अमृतः इह भवति (the enlightened one becomes immortal in this very
  life).

            The experience of divine lustre which we find in the 1
  “If the light of a thousand suns were to burst forth all at once in the sky,
   it would be like a splendour of that mighty one” (XI.12)
                                                                    , is a re-echo of
   what Puruhanman Angirasa said :

   यद्द्याव इन्द्र ते शतं शतं भूमीरुत स्युः।
   न त्वा वज्रि न्‌ wed सूर्या अनु      न जातमष्ट रोदसी
                                                      ।। (२१४५९१५ ४पा.70.5)
   Or again, when 1181008919 describes the supersensuous experience,
                                                                          न      ६६.

                                                                        the
   blind man is able to see, the deaf man is able to hear and the Jame man
    is able to go in pursuit”, he is confirming the same Vedic Truth: You
    enabled the blind to see and the lame one to walk:

    अनु at जहि ता नयोऽन्धं श्रोणं च वृत्रहन्।‌
    न तत्‌ ते सुम्नमष्टवे।। (1२६५९0२ IV.30.19)

                                                2
                                                                                    Introduction



Though     mighty     in    volume     and     mightier    in   value,    the       Vedas    have
unfortunately met with mightiest negligence. We vainly say that ours is a
Vedic civilization, but we shudder to study the Vedas. It is as though we
have developed a kind of Vedo-phobia resulting in an unconscious fear
of approaching them. And if anyone utters the name of Sayanabhasya, we
flee from the scene as if chased by terror. This is a perfect example of

Gaudapada’s remark, अभये भयदर्शि नः (they apprehend fear where there is
fearlessness). In fact, Saéyanabhasya elucidates the meaning of a Vedic
Mantra    in   so   simple    a   fashion     that any    one   who has         a   preliminary
acquaintance with Sanskrit can follow him. Another misunderstanding is
regarding the nature of Atharvaveda which is more misunderstood than
understood.      Besides     sharing    the    censure    on    other    Vedas,       that   they
prescribe meaningless rituals, and sing superfluous eulogies addressed
to heavenly office-bearers, the Atharva has become a victim of another
charge. It is taken to be synonymous with sorcery, witch-craft or black-
magic. The Atharvaveda may contain these elements, no doubt, but it also
embodies       sublime     spiritual   experiences       in no way       inferior to those
described in Rgveda. An example or two from Atharvaveda may be cited.

अकामो धीरो अमृतः स्वयंभू रसेन तृप्तो न कुतश्चनोनः।
तमेव वि द्वान्न बि भाय मृत्योरात्मानं धीरमजरं युवानम्
                                                   । ।‌ (९.8.44)
(Desireless, serene, immortal, self-existent, lacking nothing ... is He the
Atman. On realising the Atman who is serene, undecaying and ever
youthful, one has no fear of death). Look again. Rsi Atharvan exclaims:

मुग्धा देवा उत शुनायजंतोत AA: पुरुधायजंत|
य इमं यज्ञं मनसा चि केत प्र णो वोचस्तमि हेह ब्रवः।। (शा.5.5)
(The ignorant sacrifice by offering a dog, others do it by offering cows.
He who sacrifices his mind to God is alone praiseworthy).

      It is wrong to see only crass materialism in the Vedas. We would
be doing injustice to the lofty message if we understand the words literally
instead of grasping the inner meaning. We have to break the shell of words
to partake of the kemel of real significance. Hence the necessity of
symbolic interpretation. However, a word of caution is needed. We cannot
say that we have understood the whole of Vedic symbolism. Nonetheless,
the petitional prayers in the Vedas apparently seeking material comforts
may have a spiritual import. A discerning eye can trace in the Vedas the
prototype of spiritual life. Take the Vedic institution of Yajfia. The root
“Yaj’ means to worship, and as meditation is the sole essence of worship,
Yajna may well stand for meditation. Like-wise the word Dyu (heaven) may



                                              3
Pathway to God in the Vedas



symbolise cranium or head or Brahmarandhra of Yogic physiology;
Antariksa or Antarala (intermediate space) may be taken as heart; Prthvi
or earth may mean lower portion of the body.

       Coming to Soma, another victim of misunderstanding, it may be
pointed out that Soma does not necessarily mean the intoxicating liquor.
It is ambrosial juice which a mystic, rapt in meditation, partakes of. It is
Amrtarasa or Ramarasa in which terms the saints express their flavour
experience. We may consider a Mantra from Rgveda. Savitri Surya Rsika
declares :

आच्छदि धानेर्गुपि तो वार्हतेः सोम रक्षि तः|
ग्राव्गामि न्‌ शृण्वन्‌ ति ष्ठसि न ते अश्नाति   पार्थि वः।। (२४९५९१9 X.85.4)
(Sheltered by protective rules and guarded by singers of Brhat Sama, you
stand as if listening to the music of press-stones. Never can an earthly being
partake of thee). The expression न ते अश्नाति               पार्थि वः (no earthly being
partakes of the juice) clearly indicates that Soma is not the herbal intoxicant
but the divine juice which oozes at the height of intense meditation. The
same idea that no one who dwells on earth tastes Soma is repeated in
Yajurveda: “You, Oh Soma, are the accomplisher of sacrifice. Through you
we have attained the light eternal and have become immortal. From earthly
existence we have risen to the state of illumination; we have realised the
gods and also the self-effulgent light” (VIII.52).

        It is worth noting (a) how the Vedic Rsis displayed an insight into
 the working of human mind or (b) how they could foreshadow very
 important concepts accepted by later-day ethical thought.

       (a) We see clearly from a Mantra that the sages were aware of the
 process of re-channelising mind’s energy, which process is known in
 moder psychology as sublimation. They had understood that the
 assistance of mind is needed in all that we do. It being the nature of the
 mind to go the way of the senses, the Rsis wanted to redirect its powers
 Godward. In other words the mind is normally ego-centric; it entertains
 ideas which please the empirical self or the Jivatman. But the Vedic sages
 wanted the mind to become God-centred. It should meditate on the idea of
  the Almighty.
            The Vedas have used a very significant term, Sivasamkalpa,
  to convey this shift in the attitude of the mind. May the mind be
  Sivasamkalpa or God-oriented. We may use a term Jivasamkalpa on the
  analogy of the Vedic term Sivasamkalpa and say that the mind is expected
  to abandon its usual nature of self-centredness, Jivasamkalpa and attach
  itself to God - Sivasamkalpa. This is the sum and substance of the

                                                4
                                                                         Introduction



 Sivasamkalpa Mantras in Yajurveda (XXXIV.1-6). The words तन्मे मनः
शि वसंकल्पमस्तु reverberate like music in the ears reminding us that the powers
of the mind need to be sublimated. The ancient thinkers might not have
conducted any research in a systematic or scientific way; but it is certain
that by their keen observation they had concluded that all improvement of
the society and the world started with the purification of one’s own mind.

       (b) Another modern concept found in the Vedas is that of moral
autonomy. It is contended, and rightly so, by ethical writers that in order
to be held responsible for his actions the agent must be free. If the moral
command has an external source, the doer of an action will not remain free,
and hence the importance of moral autonomy. Here is a Mantra from
Rgveda:

परि चि न्मर्तो द्रवि णं ममन्यात्‌ ऋतस्य पथा नमसा वि वासेत्‌।
उत स्वेन रतुना सं वदेत श्रेयासं दक्षं मनसा जगृभ्यात्
                                                  | ।‌ (X.31.2)
It prescribes that a man may think about wealth and strive to acquire it by
adhering to the path of eternal moral law and by humble devotion to God.
In this respect he should consult his own conscience and stick firmly to
his best judgement. We have here the sanest advice to be followed in
mundane life. One is not dissuaded from acquiring wealth; but one is
exhorted to acquire it by right means. And who is to determine the right
means? The Mantra says: one has to (1) consult one’s conscience स्वेन ऋतुना
सं वदेत and (ii) stick to its best judgement श्रेयांसं दक्षं मनसा जगृभ्यात्|‌ Here
the Vedic Rsi has anticipated Kant, one of the greatest moral philosophers
of all times. In Kant, the moral law is self-imposed; the self is the author
of the moral law. This is the principle of autonomy as opposed to
heteronomy where the law is imposed by an out-sider. Kant had thought
that no earlier moral system had taken note of this principle of autonomy.
Had Kant read the words, स्वेन क्रतुना सं वदेत in the above Mantra he would
have at once realised that in the Vedas is found the doctrine of autonomy
in clearest terms. The second point in the Mantra is that moral guidance
should be taken from our own best judgement, 4ara दक्षं मनसा. This idea
too is found in Kant in a fully developed form. Sreyas is the idea of the
good    as   contrasted   with   Preyas    which    is   the   idea   of the   pleasant.
Incidentally, a contrast between Preyas and Sreyas was later made famous
by Kathopanisad. The Vedic Mantra under discussion speaks neither of the
word Preyas nor of the contrast. In Sreyas there is obedience to the moral
law and no indulgence in passions. Now, the Vedic concept of Sreyas is
on a par with Kantian concept of reason. In Kant reason unwaveringly obeys
Pathway to God in the Vedas


the moral law because it is bereft of passions and impulses. He further says
that those actions alone are moral where reason is the motive; thus reason
becomes the real moral guide. According to the Vedas man pote consult
his own mind and the mind must be alert to the moral end #                                दक्ष, so
that it gives the best judgement. And in Kant man’s reason itself gives the
moral law and the same reason constituting the moral end gives the best
judgement. To the moral universe, or Kingdom of Ends, “every rational
being belongs as a member, who, though universally legislative, is yet
submitted to the laws he enacts.”’ Thus the Vedic Mantra, formulated
centuries before Kant, enunciates one of the central doctrines of moral
philosophy.

            With all this theoretical insight the Vedic                 people     were     never
indifferent to the worldly life. The whole attitude to life was characterised
by a robust optimism, persistent vitalism and practical realism. They
 would neyer stand morbid melancholy and groundless fear, and were
 completely free from gloomy forebodings. What we see in the present day
 world strikes a contrast. Modern industrial civilization                        (with all due
 Tespect to its utilitarian side) has, one must admit, squeezed out of man
 all enthusiasm; and what remains is bare skin. Nowhere do we find today
 that bubbling enthusiasm, that irrepressible zest for life which we find
 depicted in many a Mantra. “Shed your idleness, shed lethargy; be up and
 doing, because the gods do not like him who sleeps in idleness”:

 इच्छन्ति      देवाः सुन्वन्तं न स्वप्नाय स्पृहयन्ति ।
 यन्ति      प्रमादमतन्द्राः 11 (९२९५०५० VII.2.18)

 That society, said Vivekananda, is the highest, where the highest truth
  becomes practical. In Vedic times attainment of God was not merely
  theoretically prescribed; Spirituality was the way of life and what was to
  be attained had become incumbent on every one to attain. To those who
  have no motivation and hence no energy to work, Rsi KaSyapa Asita gives
   a significant directive :
   अनु प्रलास आयवः पदं नवीयो अक्रमुः |

   रुचे जनन्त सूर्यम्‌।। (Reveda 1%.23.2)
    (Go in search of the sun, and in case you do not find him, create one). What
     a bold advice! The implication is that one has to go in search
                                                                                 of new ground
    and should not offer idle excuses. Take another verse : “awake, arise; this
     life-giving Prana has arrived, Darkness has vanished...” (Reveda 1.113.16).
         1 The Critique of Pure Reason, Tr.N.K. Smith, Macmillan, St. Martin’s Press, 1970, २. VII.62
                                                                               Introduction



The Message is definite and forceful. Yet one lyric exhorts men to cross

over the river which is full of stones, अश्मन्वती रीयते सं रभध्वम्‌ (२९५९५३१ X.53.8).
Difficulties in crossing are admitted; still a beneficent and blissful ideal of
spiritual wealth is held before men.

        Making offerings to the deities, asking them for good things in life
and enjoying them fully without any trace of dissatisfaction with the present
life and without a tinge of pessimism are important aspects of the mental
make-up of the Vedic people. They had a faith in the positivity of life and
therefore had no inhibitions in sharing in the common joys of life. They
addressed prayers to deities for cows, for wealth and for progeny. This is
a fascinating example of healthy-mindedness both in their simple pastoral
life as well as in their high spiritual pursuit. In fact, they made no hard and
fast distinction       between      secular     and   religious   matters.   The    technical
distinction between Abhyudaya and NihSreyas was yet to be effected. They
looked on all duties as religious. Really speaking religious spirit in duty
implies faith, sincerity and dedication. But as we have forgotten these
implications we tend to make the word religious an anathema. The ancient
people were aware that sundering the religious from the secular is bound
to degrade both;         for then,     the secular duties would only come to be
performed in a most neglected fashion and religious duties would be done,
only with an impoverished spirit.' Their practice was to perform even social
duties as religious thus preserving earnestness and seriousness. Whatever
was inconsistent with spiritual life could not be acceptable even in secular
life.   This    created     a   healthy        atmosphere   for   virtues    like   humility,
selflessness, sympathy or service to take root. In short, they pursued an
ideal of universal harmony without a discordant note, ‘a cosmical republic
governed by a law of harmony’. More important is the sense of gratitude
they feel-for the higher powers for their awards. They profusely express
their gratefulness through a number of weighty prayers.

तच्चक्षुः देवहि तं पुरस्तात्‌ शुक्रमुच्चरतू।
पश्येम शरदः शतं जीवेम शरदः शतम्
                             ।।‌ (Yajurveda XXXVI.24)
(That eye, the gracious gift of God, has appeared before us. May we be
blessed to enjoy this vision for a hundred years).

        The Vedic thinkers are the pioneers in giving to posterity the lofty
concepts of one God, one World and one Humanity. Rgveda I.164.46 gives
the concept of one God, as it says: Reality is one, though the wise speak

1 A.E. Taylor, The Faith ofa Moralist, Series I, Macmillan and Co. Ltd., St. Martin’s Street,
London, 1932, Pp 341 ff
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of it variously: Uh सत्‌ वि प्रा बहुधा वदन्ति । Then we have a celebrated
expression:

वेनस्तत्‌ पश्यन्नि हि तं गुहा daa वि श्वं भवत्येकनीडम्‌ (Yajurveda XXXII.8)
(Vena beheld that deeply hidden Being where the world makes its home
in a single nest). Here is the idea of one common home or one world. One
God, one world and hence one humanity. The Vedic idea of one humanity,
as expressed in the valedictory Mantra of Rgveda, is nothing but singleness
of purpose, and hence the exhortation to work in unison.

समानी व आकूति ः समाना हदयानि          वः।
समानमस्तु वो मनो यथा वः सुसहासति ।। (X.191.4)

(Common be your aim; united be your hearts; of one accord be your minds
so that all of you may live together happily and peacefully). All these three
concepts were intertwined in their social philosophy             of coordination,
cooperation and coexistence.

      We find that the Rsis were ever eager to share their riches with
fellowmen. They were pleased with life and were at peace with themselves;
that is why they could radiate peace. They insisted on doing everything with
one mind, knowing fully well the psychological significance of the spirit of
 cooperation in lending security and stability to life. No wonder that the
 society based on such a firm foundation of peace-and-stability has retained
 even today some of its freshness and its virtues, in spite of having passed
 through centuries of vicissitudes. What little urge for honest work we have
 today, we owe to them. They had insight enough to realise that, while it is
 vainglorious to aim at assimilation with Brahman without displaying any
 benevolence to fellowmen, it is suicidal to keep human society as the only
 end and the supreme object of love without a spirit of loyalty to a kingdom
 which is not of this world. They served God through man, and man, for the
 sake of God. Duties to man — even secular duties at that — become sanctified
 when they are done as service to God. It is in this spirit that we have to
 understand the prayers of Vedic Rsis for the universal welfare and harmony.

 सं गच्छध्वं सं वदध्वं सं वो मनांसि
                                  जानताम्।‌
 देवा भागं यथा पूर्वे संजानाना उपासते
                                   ।। (२९५९०५० X.191.2)
  (Come ye together, be harmonious in conversation, let your minds be of
  one accord, just in the manner in which the most venerable gods assemble
  together and enjoy harmoniously in their respective positions).

                     *          *           *     *          *
                                                                    Introduction



      What is presented in subsequent pages is philosophy of mysticism in
the Vedas. Prof. २२१. Ranade has systematically dealt with mysticism by
going deep into both the psychology of mysticism and philosophy of
mysticism. Thus mysticism ‘comes to be treated as an independent subject
dealing with an important aspect of human experience. Prof. Ranade’s
objective was to understand the nature of God and delineate the means of
appropriating Him; thus he combined both theoretical and practical aspects
of mysticism and set a new pattern in the formulation of a Pathway to God.
In his treatment, the prolegomena, chiefly psycho-ethical, deals with the
incentives to and moral preparation for spiritual life. First of all, it is
necessary to see as to why man turns, if he turns at all, to God. Thereafter
he has to imbibe in himself certain virtues which are a sine gua non for
treading the pathway to God. Then follows philosophical topics like, the
nature of God and the spiritual teacher; the relation between the spiritual
teacher and disciple; or nature and value of the Name of God. This
theoretical portion   leads   to practical aspects,   viz. the   methodology of
meditation and the difficulties which the aspirant encounters in his spiritual
pilgrimage. Past this stage of chiaroscuro where God plays the game ofhide
and seek, comes the stage where a devotee gets varied and rich spiritual
experiences which confer blessedness on him.

      We have followed this line of treatment in the present work. For this
purpose, sources from the Vedas were culled out, arranged systematically
topic-wise and interpreted so as to find a Pathway to God in the Vedas.

      We have quoted experiences of thinkers and saints of modern times
while dealing with Vedic thought. This is bound to appear as anachronism.
The intention in alluding to their views is to show that whatever has been
developed in later times was already present in the Vedas in seed-form.
The very fact that these religious or spiritual experiences are lived and re-
lived age after age is proof enough that the Vedic utterances are neither
the spinnings of a massive intellect nor fancies of a fertile imagination, but
vivid accounts of ‘a first hand intuitive experience of Reality’. The mystical
experiences of later saints do not hang in the air. They stand anchored to
the beatific experiences of a seer at the dawn of civilization. The ancient
truths get confirmed again and again while modern thought in turn becomes
validated. Well might one say that there is a stream of spiritual thought
eternally flowing. In any spiritual experience, ‘the eye of omniscience might
read all the foregone spiritual history of a culture’.
                              CHAPTER I




             Incentives to Spiritual Life


      1. Search for Perfection. Wertheimer, one of the pioneers of
the Gestalt School of psychology, concluded after a number of
experiments, that in perception, an individual has a tendency to see
 an imperfect form as a perfect one; a near circle, as a circle; a less
 organised figure, as a more organised figure. Gaps are filled, lines
 are shortened or lengthened, so that what is perceived has some
 meaning. And what is true of perception is much more true of
 memory. Irregularities in figures that are seen, are ironed out and
 they become better figures when remembered. Organised wholes or
 total patterns are psychologically prior to relations or elements into
 which an object is analysed. This is a very important characteristic
 of the human mind. Though we need not agree with the Gestaltists
 in all that they say, we must admit the truth in their theory of
 perception and memory, that as a matter of psychological fact, man
 wants to correct incompleteness or restore balance in what he sees
 or does. Put before a child of five years a picture of a human face
 with only one eye drawn, and you will notice that the child will
 instantly   draw   the   other   eye.   Imperfection,   fragmentariness,
 inadequacy, in a word, a loose screw anywhere, makes the mind
 uneasy. Unfortunately, bad training and worse examples blunt this
, faculty of the mind and a grown-up person learns to live with
 indiscipline and hotch-potch performance of works. But the original
 nature always holds a brief for.perfection. It is no wonder therefore,
 that this urge for wholeness or search for perfection is clearly
  present in Vedic literature. It is a natural way of a healthy mind. The
  Vedic Rsi, the watchward of whose philosophy was harmony, seeks
  a God who fulfils the abiding longings of a human mind. If man’s
  genuine and universal aspirations go unanswered, it would sound
  like a discordant note in the playing of the cosmic symphony. “If
  it is the fundamental principle of a sound philosophy that all
                                                  Incentives to Spiritual Life



existence forms a harmonious unity, then if we can discover what
are the essential and permanent features in the demands made by
art, morality and religion upon the world, we may be sure that these
demands are somehow met and made good in the scheme of things”!
What then are such demands and how far are they met in this world?
And if they are not so met, where and by whom does man expect
them to be made good?

       “A perpetual war is kindled”, laments Hume, “amongst all
living creatures. Necessity, hunger, want, stimulate the strong and
courageous. Fear, anxiety, terror, agitate the weak and infirm. The
first entrance into life gives anguish to the new-born infant and to
its wretched parent. Weakness, impotence, distress, attend each
stage of that life; and it is at last finished in agony and horror’”.? All
this seems to be unmixed evil and does not justify its own existence
because evil is not a precondition of good tidings to come. Should
evil necessarily exist? Reason cannot give an unqualified “Yes’ to
the question. And if evil is not indispensable, can there not be a state
which is free from these malignities, a condition which is washed
clean of its black spots, a place which is swept off pain and
suffering? And more still, is there not a power which bestows bliss
and peace? And man turns to God, who, he thinks, is unalloyed joy.
Man, therefore, seeks God as a remover of physical evil and as
source of serene beatitude.

       Howsoever mighty a man may be in his strength, in possession,
in wealth or in intellect, a time comes when they all prove futile.
He comes to realise that man is the greatest enemy of man. His
efforts become inefficacious and he stands supportless. At such
moments, he turns to God for help. Psychologically, in this way, man
looks up to God as a manna in the wilderness.

       The law of continuity convinces man that there must be a
perfect Being as a finale to the imperfections in the world. The world,


1   A.E. Taylor, Elements of Metaphysics, Methuen and Co. Ltd., 36 Essex Street,
    W.C. London, 1952, P. 382                                                      ।

2   Quoted in Pringle Pattison’s Idea of God, Oxford University Press, 35 West,
    32nd Street, New York, 1920, P. 17


                                       11
Pathway to God in the Vedas



however, belies one’s hopes for finding a Being of superlative
excellence here below. And hence the mind weaves all good things
in an idea. This idea of a supreme Being or God is that of the sum-
total of all reality, the most original Being, the highest Being, or the
Being of all beings, says Kant in the Transcendental Dialectic. In this
part of the Critique of Pure Reason, we find a construction of the idea
of God with which Kant so often lures the reader, but which he as
often declares to be merely an idea and not a being or existence. Such
a Being, he maintains, can not be proved by speculative reason. But
who has ever proved God with the help of reason? Syllogistic
reasoning, which proceeds from ‘all’ to ‘some’ can not prove God,
because there is no term wider in import from which God can be
deductively inferred. But are all syllogisms only analytic, making
explicit in the conclusion what is implicitly contained in the major
premise? The syllogism round which psychology of religion veers,
is not analytic but synthetic, says Edward Caird.' It leads us from
the finite to the Infinite. The world is finite, incomplete, imperfect.
The synthetic syllogism adds infinity, completeness or perfection to
these qualities and reaches the idea of the Infinite, the Complete, the
Perfect. This it calls God. Logically, thus, man starts from the part
 and argues to the whole and seeks to establish God on the basis of
 an inductive or synthetic syllogism, which puts more in the
 conclusion than what is contained in the premises. The passage from
 the finite to the Infinite is sanctioned because psychology of religion
 demands it. The fleeting world must have some basis which is itself
 stable; the ephemeral must be based on permanence, because without
 an abiding substance no change can be explained. The contingent has
  its footing in the necessary. “All religion springs out of the sense of
  nothingness, unreality, transitoriness - in other words, of the
  essentially negative character of the finite world”.* Because the
 world sinks beneath us, we search a foundation that is firm.
 Metaphysically, therefore, man refuses to grant reality to the limited
 world and confers it on God who is an infinite source of every thing.


  1   E. Caird, The Critical Philosophy of Kant, Vol II, James Maclehose and Sons,
      Glasgow, 1909, Pp 114 ff
  2 Ibid, P. 115
                                             Incentives to Spiritual Life



     Inspite of Kant’s rejection of speculative arguments for the
existence of God, there is still left room for moral theology. If no
justice is seen in the world, if merit goes unrewarded and wrong-
doing goes unpunished here below, there must be a place where this
is corrected. And so morally, man relies on a God who equates merit
with happiness.

     But the faith and conviction remain unconsummated unless
man has direct contact with Divinity. Mystically therefore, man
longs to experience the Infinite while yet remaining finite.

     These constitute different incentives to spiritual life, or reasons
why a man turns to God. The Bhagavad-gita has made famous a
classification of seekers after God. We are told that, those who seek
God, belong to four different types. There are the Artas or the
afflicted souls, who approach God for the redress from pain. Then
there are those who are moved by curiosity, by a desire to know.
They are the Jijnasus. Still others belong to the category of the profit-
minded who care for the cash-value results. They are the Artharthins.
The final type represents those who try to appropriate God for the
sake of spiritual illumination. They are called the Jnanins. The first
three have strings tied to their devotion, while the fourth alone
worships God for the sake of God.

      If we look to the countless supplications addressed to various
deities by the Vedic Rsis, we find that petitions of the first type i.e.
those arising out of deep sorrow, are few and far between. Heart-
rending solicitations of a soul encircled by evil or condemned to hell,
are almost absent. We do come across the second group i.e. requests
which are a result of curiosity or wonder. They exhibit the spirit of
enquiry of the Vedic people. By far the majority of panegyrics belong
to the third category. Most of the incentives can be classified as
originating from desire to gain something. Not infrequently the
verses represent charters of demands coming from end-seekers.
Lastly, Mantras of the last type, i.e. prayers for the bliss of God are
strewn in the Vedas almost everywhere.

      We shall study the incentives to spiritual life as found in the
Vedas under three heads — intellectual, moral and eudaemonistic.

                                   13
Pathway to God in the Vedas



These are not water-tight compartments, but convenient groups for
dealing with the incentives. They only indicate the predominant line
of approach. The first bunch of incentives displays the inquisitive
nature of the Rsis; the second, their desire for moral excellence and
the third, their longing for spiritual wealth, in which material welfare
has its own appointed place. There is no outright rejection of worldly
prosperity so long as it does not conflict with moral and religious
life. The sage makes other demands too. He wishes to be a recipient
of religious experiences which make life worthy. He also wants to
be immortal. The body is perishable, no doubt, but the seer hopes
 to attain to a stage where one has overcome the fear of death. He
 wants tranquillity and divine bliss and he beseeches the higher
 powers to grant his request. Divine bliss, according to the Vedic
 sage, comes to fulfil and not to destroy.

      2. Intellectual Incentives. Man is filled with wonder when he
 observes the working of the marvellous machine — the human body.
 He then asks: “who is the first One that created man’s brain, his head,
 his forehead, or his mouth and then rose to the heavens?” (S.1). Or,
 “who created the seven openings on the face — two ears, two nostrils,
 two eyes, and the mouth? By His power, men and animals traverse
 the course of their lives. Who is It that set in motion the different
 vital breaths like Prana, Apana, Vyana or Samana, in the body?”
 (S.2).
      Perhaps naturalistic explanation was no explanation for the Rsi.
 He may not accept that things came into existence or faded out of
 existence of their own accord. There is, according to him, a creator
 of the world. Who is He? It is also taken for granted that an ordinary
 man may not be able to fathom the depths of the cosmos and that
 it requires a rich fund of knowledge to understand creation. ‘““Who
 is that wise man that knows the proper application of metre
 (Chandas)? Who has attained to the original source of speech? Who
 is regarded as the eighth hero of the sacrificing priests and who has
 tracked the two bay steeds of Indra?” (S.3).

      Man’s desire for knowledge is not confined only to his body
 or to things that surround him. It turns to the cosmos.            Rsi
 ViSvakarman Bhauvana asks, “what was the original cause and what

                                   14
                                             Incentives to Spiritual Life



was the material cause by means of which the creator of this
universe, who had the vision of this creation, created this earth
below, and the heaven above? Which was the tree, and what forest.
out of which the earth and heavens were fashioned? The learned
thinkers enquire as to what is that, upon which He is stationed
holding all the worlds” (S.4). The answer to the question is provided
by the Taittirlya Brahmana. It says: “Brahman is the forest and
Brahman the tree out of which the earth and the heaven were
fashioned and I am declaring after thoughtful deliberation that it is
Brahman, which existed and supported all the worlds” (S.5). Side
by side of the curiosity into the nature of creation, there is seen a
reverential feeling towards the Creator. “Let us worship Him who
created these worlds and all the creatures that came into existence
after Him. May we secure the friendship of Indra by offering hymns
of praise to him and thus wait upon our generous lord with humble
adorations” (S.6). There is an awareness of one’s own ignorance and
a need of receiving instruction from some one, who is more mature
and more wise.    “Immature in understanding and undiscerning in
mind, I am inquiring about the secret residences of gods and the
seven threads by means of which this garment of universe is
prepared and spread out” (S.7). “Who is that seer that knows the
creator (father) who is beneath the upper realm and above the lower
realm? Who is that seer that can tell as to whence this divine mind
originated?” (S.8). It is very interesting to note how the Vedic
Mantras reveal a wide variety of enquiries and how, through such
enquiries, a definite idea of God gradually emerges. God is deemed
as one and the only Reality, as a mysterious and awe-inspiring Being
or as a principle which, with all intellectual abilities of men, remains
beyond their comprehension. “You know all the births and origins,
Oh omniscient God, you alone know what principle there is beyond
this creation, and also what subsequently comes along. There is only
one principle beyond this creation, there is another substance just
following that one, which is very difficult to obtain. I know that
greatest of creations and hence 1 declare that men with rank
materialistic outlook are lower in standard” (S.9). While the unitary
character of the ultimate reality is quite clear from this Mantra, there
is also a suggestion that pursuit of only material wealth is surely

                                   15
Pathway to God in the Vedas



a hindrance in the way of obtaining divine bliss. There are Mantras
with definite agnostic overtones. Phenomena are not fully known by
an ordinary man, they are known only by God, the great knower.
“The bright day and the dark night are traversing the earth and the
heavens. Vaisvanara Agni destroys the darkness with his lustre, in
the manner of a king who ascends in power. I do not know the warp
and the woof of the sacrifice, its real nature is unknown to me. Also
unknown is the real nature of the universe with its subtle and gross
elements. Who will explain it all to me? He who reigns over the earth
and surveys the heavens by taking on the form of the sun can alone
know every thing. Time and on he instructs the mortals in this
wisdom” (S.10).

      The mention of instruction brings into bold relief the necessity
of a spiritual teacher. Ultimate reality may be unknown to the rank
 and file, but it reveals itself to a chosen few, whose mission it is
 to impart instruction to the ignorant and the needy. “Let him, who
 really knows, open his heart and impart the knowledge regarding the
 beautiful bird (Atman), whose real habitation is unknown as it is
 hidden in the heart. Let us realise the Atman within us” (S.11). God
 is hidden in the hearts of men. Men have to collect Him by all
 possible means. Sayana in his commentary on this Mantra interprets
 the word Pada in the Mantra to mean the highest end for which every
 one must Strive.

      Thus the Vedic seer tries to fathom into his heart in order to
visualise the divine Being. A mysterious aura surrounds the ultimate
reality, and hence, the aspirant’s desire to know it redoubles. The
curious Dirghatamas Aucathya says: “Being ignorant, I, for the sake
of knowledge, ask the learned sages, as to who is the unborn
mysterious one who has pillared up these six regions” (S.12). The
agnostic trend is also clear in another Mantra. “Neither today, nor
tomorrow can It be said to exist. Who can know the inapprehensible
Adbhuta?” (S.13). The word Adbhuta covers a wide spectrum of
feelings because therein are included awe, wonder, incomprehensi-
bility or even indefinability. It is a mystery which man wants to
 unravel, but before which his abilities look dwarfish. Rudolf Otto’s
 comment on the term    ‘Adbhuta’ is very illuminating.    “It is the


                                  16
                                                Incentives to Spiritual Life



 experience of our ‘shuddering’. But, on the other hand, Adbhuta is
_ also the name for the supreme transcendent marvel and its attractive
 spell, the element of ‘fascination’, even for the eternal Brahman
 himself and his salvation, the Adbhutam that passes beyond the
 reach of speech”.' God has a tremendous, attractive force, though
 He remains beyond knowledge. He will remain so for those, who are
 blinded by ignorance and egoism. “You do not know Him who
 created all this universe; nor that One which is within you. Enrapt
 on all sides by the mist of ignorance are the hymn-chanters, who
 move about chanting hymns with egoism and aiming merely at
 material gains” (S.14). The suggestion in the Mantra that moral
 purity is necessary for knowing God is very significant. For moral
 excellence, or destruction of his sins, man approaches God. Though
 fully aware of his own limited capacity for knowledge which makes
 him somewhat of an agnostic, the sage continues to strive for
 knowledge. The vastness of cosmos, the infinite variety of natural
 phenomena, the polar cycles of long-drawn nights, and the waxing
 and waning of days with an almost unending twilight in between,
 would make any thinker, at the dawn of civilization, feel palpably
 tiny to have even an inkling into the nature and majesty of God. But
 the Rsi, who is ever optimistic, hopes for light. Because of geo-
 graphical circumstances, it had become habitual with him to wait for
 light. His yearning was not merely for the physical light, but for the
 light of knowledge. Moreover, knowledge for him was not merely
 intellectual or logical knowledge; real knowledge consisted in the
 direct experience or mystical realisation of God. “Dispel the dark
 ignorance so deeply ingrained in me; destroy all the enemies that
 greatly agitate my mind; and bless me with the experience of the
 supernal light which I so eagerly long for” (S.15). Sayana in his
 commentary explains गुह्यं तमः or concealed darkness as गुहारूपे हदये
 भवं तमोभावरूपाज्ञानम्‌ (the ignorance and passions that infest the cave
 of the heart). He further explains ज्योति   07 light as परतत्त्वसाक्षात्काररूपं
 ज्ञानम्‌ (illumination which results from the realisation of the highest


 1   The Idea of the Holy, Humphrey Milford, Oxford University Press, 1926,
     P. 133


                                     17
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Reality). Thus it is the spiritual light for which the sage is craving.
The same buoyant attitude is seen in another Rk : “Like birds with
beautiful wings, the seekers Priyamedhas have humbly approached
Indra. Dispel darkness, fill our eyes with the divine light and release
us from the bonds that have firmly wound round us” (S.16). With
a dauntless spirit, the sage urges men to toil for the blissful light.
“Oh seeker, climb up, never suffer a defeat. Free yourself from the
bonds of death. Don’t keep yourself away from the realm of light
and from Agni and Surya” (S.17).
      The Mahabharata narrates that to a question by the Yaksa as
to what the greatest wonder in this world is, Yudhisthira replied that
though every day man sees that people are making their final journey
towards the place of Yama, he does not realise that he too has to
 go by the same route one day. Man thinks that he is immortal. How
 can he do so, in the face of the great exodus he witnesses every
 day? The answer is simple. The tendency to exclude himself from
 the mortals is an externalisation of his innermost wish to be
 immortal. He does not want the thread of life to be cut off abruptly
 and feels sorry when he sees that life, after all, is evanescent. “This
 body is like a bubble in water, being constituted by nails, trunk,
 hands, arms, thighs, stomach, head, hair, flesh, blood, bones and
 muscles” (S.18). Creation, alas, does not have the same character-
 istics everywhere. There is unequal distribution of natural gifts, and
 this becomes obvious at every step. “Oh Lord, at the beginning you
 endowed   the holy gods      with the choicest gift - the        gift   of
immortality. Thereafter, as a gift to men, you          started   mortal
existence, life succeeding life” (S.19). Though there is realisation
that he has been given a   lesser of the two gifts, man hopes to live
on, if not in one continuous life, at least in life after life. “As day
comes after day and season comes after season without fail, Oh
great sustainer, create a chain of lives” (S.20). Thus we find that
man goes in appeal to God for a lease of life. Death constitutes the
greatest single enemy of man. “Remove this foot of death from our
existence. Let the long life be lengthened still more. Let us lead a
life that is pure and clean and which is full of assets as well as
progeny” (S.21). And the request is granted. Here is an assurance
as expressed by the sage Samkusuka Yamayana: “I have put this

                                  18
                                             Incentives to Spiritual Life



limit to a man’s life. Let him not spoil this treasure of a hundred-
year life. Let him live that long and keep death at a distance” (5.22).
It has been recognised that the Vedic people had a great zest for
life. They wanted to live long and live well. Readiness for effort
and hopes for new things are seen in a number of Mantras. “The
river studded with stones is flowing on; cautiously move together.
Get up my friends and cross over. Let us leave behind those that
are evil-minded and let us cross over to those powers that are
beneficial” (S.23). “Along with Sirya, the son of Aditi, I share in
the glory of the glorious and the wisdom of the learned” (S.24). Or
again, “we eagerly long for that perpetual divine light which
illumines the path of Truth and keeps all seekers on the path of
righteousness” (S.25). But no one can hope to achieve riches without
effort. Fortified with courage and confidence, one has to overcome
the stumbling blocks on the way. “Dear seekers, you have now to
wade through waters of mortal life. Having been fortunate enough
to get this human life, try to attain to what is immortal nectar. The
divine mother is cooking this food for you” (S.26).

     Calamities, no less than ephemerality of life, lead man
Godward. No help can be as reassuring as that coming from that
inexhaustible, celestial source of mercy. “Oh Agni, who are ever so
young, you free your, devotees from all dangers and shower grace
on him. This is a region of thieves and mean-minded people. We
have approached you and apprised you of our difficulties. When
kindled, Agni will never hand us over to the sinful and sharp-tongued
ones” (S.27). “Oh you chanters, compose Mantras that are full of
literary grace for the sake of Indra. He who offers sacrifices to Indra,
is saved from dangers of very long standing” (S.28).

      The mode of propitiating the deities at Vedic times, was two-
fold. One was to sing eulogies to them; and this explains the
presence of a good number of praise-songs in the Vedas. The other
was to perform sacrifices and offer libations to the deities through
Agni, who wasa trusted friend and who used to reach these offerings
to gods. Sacrifices are thus a mode of worship, a symbol of prayer
or contemplation on God. Meditation on the name of God which was
the main stay of a Rsi’s life, is verily a sacrifice. By ardent

                                   19
Pathway to God in the Vedas



meditation thé seers made gods their allies. “If a man is under the
protection of Mitra, Varuna and Aryama, who are profoundly wise,
is it ever possible that such a man can be injured with deceit?”
(S.29). “Have favour of his grace; offer honours to the supremely
powerful Indra. In our struggle for achieving plenitude, whenever we
come across difficulties of any sort or any magnitude, prayer to Indra
should be our sole resort” (5.30). Again, “Agni, kindled and brilliant
and fed with oblation, slays the encircling unkind forces with a
desire to offer treasure of wealth to his devotees” (S.31). We also
 come across a verse which testifies to the heart-felt devotion of the
 ancient sage. It is only too well-known that man remembers God
 only when he is in difficulty; when surrounded by fortune and
 pleasure, God’s name sinks into oblivion. But not so with the Rsi;
 he invokes the Lord to lead him not only when buffetted by
 misfortunes but also at the time when fortune is smiling on him.
 Kabir in later times remarked that those who remember God even
 in times of plenty, will never suffer any sorrow. “You are our guide,
 Oh Indra, in favourable circumstances as well as in unfavourable
 ones; lead us. Your steeds are powerful and know no fatigue. With
 them lead us towards vast treasures” (5.32).
      Many are the afflictions that attack a man in his life on earth;
 there are physical ailments and mental worries. Moreover, the fear
 of enemies or unfriendly people always looms large. For redemption
 from all these, man solicits divine help. Soma or the spiritual nectar
 which a mystic enjoys during concentrated meditation, removes all
 his ills. Here is a mention of the experience of partaking of the
 ambrosial juice which cleanses the body as well as the mind and
 restores man’s wellbeing. “Today at Sun-rise, Oh Mitra and Varuna,
 I have tasted Soma; for Soma is the only effective medicine for the
 affllicted” (S.33). “Bring for us vigour, Oh ASwins, and favour us
 with a sprinkling of your honey. Prolong our life, ward off unholy
 forces from us, destroy hostile power and be our companions for
 ever” (S.34). There are times when inspite of man’s devotions, his
 adversities persist; and man invokes God’s mercy. Such ardent
 prayers, though rare, are found in the Vedas. “I am the man who sang
 your glory at the time of Soma sacrifice. Yet, torturing worries
 overpower me like a wolf assailing a thirsty deer. Give attention to

                                  20
                                                  Incentives to Spiritual Life



this, Oh, heaven and earth, like rival wives on both sides, my ribs
are oppressing me. Oh Satakratu, biting cares are devouring me,
your devotee, just like rats devour the weaver’s threads” (S.35). And
there is fear from enemies that pounce upon a man from unknown
quarters, which makes life miserable and full of tensions. As he does
not know the retiring place of the enemy, he cannot forearm himself
for the fight; and hence assistance is sought from above. “Pray, let
not the unknown, the malevolent, the deeply mischievous, the sinful
and the malignant overpower us. Being protected by your grace we
may cross safely through manifold calamities” (S.36). The seer is
fully aware of the enemy sitting inside a man’s mind and creating
havoc—the enemy in the form of our vices. He wants his mind to
be purified and his speech to be turned sweet. It must be understood
that mere agreeableness of speech is of no use unless it is an
expression of a virtuous mind. Wholesomeness of speech must be
an expression of the wholesomeness of intentions. The Vedic sage
is here imploring the lord to sanctify his mind and also his speech.
“Inspire, oh Savitr, the sacrificer to obtain pure wealth. May Savitr,
resident of the heavens and guardian of speech, accord to us a
magnanimous mind and a sweet tongue” (S. 37).

      3. Moral Incentives. Along with physical and psychological
evils, moral evil or sin also makes man miserable. He looks to God
with confidential intimacy and begs to be absolved from effects of
wrong actions. “Whether with an eager longing for gain or without it,
or whether at the time of its fulfilment, if I ever intended any sin or
injury to anyone, while in wakeful state or in sleep, pray, God Agni
may kindly bear away from me all inequities and wrong doings”
(S.38). The seer Brahma urges, “Knowingly or unknowingly if there
has been any lapse, release us with one mind, from all of them. If
there is a back-sliding in waking state or in sleep, ifIhave committed
sins in the past or may commit any in future, release me from them
as   fastened   cattle   from   the   peg”   (S.39).   The   Rsi   indulgently
requests the lord that he be not punished for moral transgressions.
Not merely this, he wants immunity even from any guilt that may be
committed in future. People these days ask for anticipatory bail, i.e.
bail even before arrest. This request of the Rsi is similar in intent; it
is asking for anticipatory pardon. The devotee is sure that any


                                      21
Pathway to God in the Vedas



dereliction on his part, will be forgiven by the benevolent God.
“Raise him, Oh Lord, who has fallen. Cleanse him of his obliquities
and bestow long life on him” (S.40). Invitations are sent out to gods
to come and grace the sacrificial halls. When authorities are present,
there is no possibility of any kind of trouble. “Oh Indra and Varuna,
 the man whose sacrifice you attend, is not contaminated by any sin
 or difficulty. Come with your divine gifts. Listen to our songs of
 applause. Be our friends, for your friendship is ever benign” (S.41).
 Presence of deities is really the presence of godly feelings and
  thoughts in ourselves. The more widespread and more burning they
  are, the less the chance of man’s straying into misconduct. We have
  to kindle virtues in us so that the vices leave the mind; for they can
  ill afford to live in the same house as virtues. “Agni’s face is visible
   in all directions. Let him burn our sins. Agni’s face is visible in all
 directions. May he carry us beyond blemishes as men are carried in
 a boat” (5.42).
      The Vedic sage, with an insight ot a psychologist, prays for
 freedom from bondage of three types. “Oh Varuna release us from
 the uppermost bondage, from the nethermost one as well as from
 that which lies in between. We shall then be completely free from
  any taint and will always be within the bounds of your laws” (S.43).
  Now, what are these three bondages? They are, according to one
  interpretation, the inducements of the three qualities, Sattva, Rajas
  and Tamas. According to another, they are three types of Karmas,
  the uppermost ones are the sacrifices, the middle ones are charities
  and the lower ones are misdeeds of man. A man who wants to enjoy
  bliss of God, does not want even fruits of good actions, because,
  after all, they are also binding. Yet one more and better interpretation
  is to understand the three bondages as those of intellect, mind ahd
  senses. All the three in their own way misbehave. It is not correct
  to condemn the sense-organs alone. The shameless senses commit
  the crime, no doubt; but the demoralised mind is an abetter, and the
  corrupt intellect connives at their unprincipled behaviour, nay, at
  times it even provokes the senses and causes their fall. An aspirant
  therefore must guard himself from all sides. And so he clamours for
  divine assistance. “Send away all evil, Oh Lord and send us all good
  things” (S.44).

                                     22
                                                         Incentives to Spiritual Life



     4. Eudaemonistic Incentives. There is a very large group of
prayers which covertly or overtly presents a demand sheet. The
ancient people did not think that there is anything wrong in enjoying
worldly pleasures and therefore they had no inhibition in asking for
them. “To Indra alone we appeal for his friendship, for wealth and
heroic might. The powerful Indra who showers bounties is able to
help us” (S.45). “Indra is the sovereign of heaven and earth, waters
and mountains. Indra is the lord of prosperity and wisdom. Hence
Indra ought to be invoked for acquisition as well as protection of
riches”    (S.46).    It    is   interesting    to    note    that   the    words   Yoga
(acquisition)   and        ksema     (protection)      occur     here      together. The
concept of Yoga-ksema was later made famous by the Bhagavad-gita
where the Lord assumes the responsibility of looking after the
welfare of the devotee. Yoga and Ksema refer not only to material
wealth but to all types of gains, intellectual, moral and spiritual.
“Pray, give strength to our bodies, Oh Indra, and give strength to our
bulls; give strength to our sons and progeny, so that they may attain
to fullness in life. For, you are verily the sole dispenser of strength”
(S.47). The word Balada (giver of strength) occuring elsewhere also,
clearly shows that it is the very nature of Indra to distribute things.
Man’s asking only provides an occasion for his generosity. However,
there are other verses which indicate as if Indra’s boons are given
because he is propitiated with something. “Confer upon us, who are
affluent   in offerings, oh Agni, that well                   renowned power and
manifold    riches.        Associated    with        Rudras    and   Vasus,     give   us
unfailing protection” (S.48). “It is                 Indra and Indra alone, who
bestows wealth on mortal man who offers oblations to him. Indra
verily is a powerful lord whom none can resist” (S.49). “I solicit the
help of Indra, the lord of the Rbhus and pray to him for partaking of
Soma along with Rbhus, Vajas and Maruts. May Varuna and Mitra
and ASwins speed us to wealth, wisdom and victory” (5.50). Indra
seems to be a popular sovereign, for he is available to everyone
irrespective of one’s status. “Men of all classes, the high, the low
and the middle, invoke Indra. Those that struggle hard, as well as
those that rest with contentment, invoke Indra. Those that remain at
home and those that are engaged in fighting, also invoke Indra. Men
desirous of wealth invoke Indra” (S.51). We may refer to one stanza

                                          23
Pathway to God in the Vedas



which demands wealth as a matter of right. “Oh Indra, bring for us
a variety of riches even from afar. Those riches are to be inherited
by a son from his father, by a youngster from the elders or by a
student from his teacher” (S.52).
      With all such requests for material wealth it would be wrong
to think that the Vedic society was completely earth-bound. It had
its feet on the terra firma no doubt, but its eyes were always glued
to the light from above. The Vedic literature is evidence enough for
 the high spiritual development of the sages. They were great
 devotees, they had practised severe penance, had experienced Truth
 at first hand and it is only as a result of their deep absorption in the
 divine, that they have uttered the Mantras. In T.S. Eliot’s words,
 apprehension of the point of intersection between the Timeless and
 time was their occupation. Worshipping by means of sacrifice which
 can be taken as symbolic of meditation on the sacred divine name
 or one-pointed remembrance of God’s name seems to be a discipline
 to be rigorously followed. In its train comes the singing of devotional
 songs, which, as is evident from the practice prevalent in most of
 the historieal religions, is a popular mode of veneration. They had
 known the bliss of mystical experience and so they again turned to
 God for the more and more of it.
       There has been made a distinction between the way of the flesh
 and the way of the spirit; the former is easy but fruitless, the latter,
 hard but fruitful. “I have heard of two paths, by which the mortals
 travel, one leads to gods and the other to the fathers. All those
 situated between heaven and earth take either of the two paths”
 (S.53). Rsi Uparibabhrava exclaims: “Oh you devotee, go beyond
 good (Bhadra) and achieve what is the ultimate end (Sreyas). May
 the learned Brhaspati be your guide. May the heroic rule over the
 world and may all enmity come to an end” (S.54). “The path of
 righteousness is clearly visible. It will lead us beyond all miseries.
 The way to divine glory is also within the reach of the eye”, sings
 an enthusiastic Rsi (S.55). Those whom the gods help, are
 automatically led to the right path and to success. “When the spoils
 of war are conferred, Indra is prayed for final instructions. They who
 have Indra on their side, will verily win” (S.56). “I had the good
 fortune of having a vision of Him, whom the whole world should

                                    24
                                                     Incentives to Spiritual Life



behold. On this very earth, I saw His chariot. He accepted my verbal
offerings” (S.57). Sayana’s commentary on this Mantra runs: He,
on whom the whole world should feast its eyes, had manifested
Himself to shower grace on us (अस्मदनुग्रहार्थ अत्र आवि र्भूतम्‌), implying
that God is not chary in his bounties, and He comes on His own to
endow gifts. It is up to man to make the best of it. “The man who
is a fortunate recipient of thy love, becomes a person of good and
wide reputation and leads a happy and comfortable life” (S.58). That
the sage is desirous of spiritual illumination, becomes clear from
other Rks also. “With profound respects, Oh Agni, I vouchsafe to
this infallible truth that you are the owner of all the terrestrial and
celestial supplies. Tell us, however, Oh Jatavedas, what portion of
this wealth you are going to settle on us. Pray, let me know the best
in their unknown possessions which can be obtained by following
this path” (S.59). “Verily, Oh Indra, we devotees hasten to you with
some offering for manifold spiritual gains, which are resplendent
and   blissful.   Pray,   favour   us   with   the    same”   (S.60).   Having
approached Indra, the sovereign Lord, it is no use asking for goods
which are subject to destruction. And hence a request for ever fresh
things. “Oh Agni, we pray to you for becoming recipients of your
most coveted wealth, which does not decay and perish” (S.61). There
is a verse whic) urges one to plod on and on and go ahead of the
compeers. “You are wise, you are strong. You are the protector of
the body. Achieve the supreme goal and out-shine those who are
your equals. You are power, you are illuminator. You are supreme
happiness and light. Achieve the supreme goal and excel those who
are your equals” (S.62).
      5. Yearnings for Mystical Experience. One thing the saint-
poet longs for, is the mystical experience of God. Other gains are
only epiphenomena. Mystical experience, it 15 well-known, does not
involve the sense-organs. However, these supersensuous experi-
ences resemble those that are gathered with the help of the sense-
organs i.e., there are experiences of form and light and colours
without the physical eye being used. Supersensuousness of spiritual
experience is an accepted fact and it is sought to be corroborated
by mystical experience of form and light which a blind man can
enjoy. Needless to say there are also experiences of sound, taste,

                                        25
Pathway to God in the Vedas


smell or touch. They are wondrous manifestations of God and they
confer an inexpressible beatific joy on the mystic. Such are the
experiences for which the sage is so keen. “Oh Sirya, you radiate
light, are beautiful and move about in the world as you wish. You
stand face to face with the devotees, so that they may feast their eyes
upon your form” (S.63). The form is not merely delightful to the eye,
 it has the power to help an aspirant to cross over the worldly
existence. The word Taranih (तरणि ः) in the Mantra is explained by
Sayana as संसाराव्धेः तारकोऽसि (you carry us over the sea of this
