ephemeral existence). “May we, Oh Agni, have our desires fulfilled
 through your grace. Through your grace, lord of wealth, may we
 obtain spiritual well-being and good company. May we share food
 with all others and may we partake of your purity and your
 imperishable light” (S.64). “Pray, bless us with tranquillity and
 guide us into the world of spiritual light for enjoyment of all the
  felicities in life. Kindly lead us on now, to a richer and fuller life”
  (S.65). The seer sings to the almighty hymns of overflowing
  applause. “Let us offer our homage to Indra who is the embodiment
  of truth itself, and not to one tainted with untruth. A deadly blow is
  dealt to those who do not have devotion in offering Soma, whereas
   manifold lights are granted to the devout seekers who dedicate their
   Soma offerings to Indra. Of good omen are the blessings of Indra”
   (S.66). “We pray to Indra for the sake of his greatness which that
   most powerful hero possesses, which is also boundless, undecaying
 and serene. Kindly allow us, Oh Lord, to share in your grandeur, so
 that we may enjoy it fully’ (S.67). The high aspirations of the
 devotee are note-worthy. He is not satisfied merely by the revelation
 of the lord but wants to share in his radiance. This is a sure indication
 of the insatiable thirst of the saint for spiritual joy. It whets his
 appetite more and more as he keeps ascending in his experiences.
 “By the power of your mercy and with the aid of your protecting
 means, may we be granted the everlasting mystical vision of the sun”
 (S.68). “We pray for a pretty long life with your soothing vision
  before our eyes —a long life with your soothing vision” (S.69).
       The Vedic aspirant is hymning the lord for yet another goal —
  sainthood. Real sainthood consists in attaining to permanent and
  growing religious experiences, or in achieving equanimity. It is a
                                    26
                                             Incentives to Spiritual Life



state characterised by deep devotion to god, by joy and peace and
by almost an irresistible urge to broadcast the same to the world,
without so much as an inkling of any expectation. “Have fondness
for me, Indra. Lengthen our lives, heighten our devotion. Be satisfied
with whatever words I speak and make me your real devotee” (S.70).
“Kindly favour us with the spiritual wealth which sanctifies worldly
wealth.   Pray, raise   me   to the status of seerhood     like   that of
Jamadagni” (S.71). The ardour to be always a recipient of religious
experiences is seen in a number of eclogues. “Will you make me ,
the guardian of the people? Will you make me a prominent ruler,
Oh benignant and all-powerful God, will you make me a seer (Rsi)
who relishes the divine Soma juice? Will you make me the master
of ever-lasting spiritual riches?” (S.72). Sainthood, though a very
coveted position in the pilgrim’s progress towards spiritual perfec-
tion, does not mark an end either of austerities or of mystical
experiences. The saint continues to meditate on the name of God and
continues to enjoy new and yet new visions. After all, as Prof. R.D.
Ranade said, who can put a limit to the ways in which god manifests
Himself? “Saints, who are endowed with illumination, continue to
engage themselves in worshipping the supreme Reality for further
heights in ecstatic enjoyments. A seeker who is able to visualise
them face to face, verily himself becomes a sage having realisation
of Brahman, the ultimate Reality” (S.73). The same unlimited
character of experiences is seen in another Rk uttered by the holy
man Madhucchandas Vaisvamitra. “Accept my humble Soma
offering, Indra, and be pleased; favour me with a new light in my
life and make me a seer having innumerable, supersenuous spiritual
experiences” (S.74). Madhvacarya in his Rg-bhasya stresses this
very point that the saint wants to reap thousand-fold benefits from
God. He comments on the words सहस्रसाम्‌ ऋषि म्‌ in this Mantra as
ऋषि ं सहस्रलाभं मां कुरु (make me a saint and recipient of thousands
of gains). Sadyana too comments: मां सहस्रसां सहस्रसंख्याकलाभोपेतं ऋषि ं
कुरू   (let me be blessed with Rsihood and bounties that can be
measured in thousands).

       6. Desire for Immortality. There are still other hopes for the
realisation of which the lyric-singer turns to God.          He wants

                                   27
Pathway to God in the Vedas



immortality
         in this very life; he wishes to win over death itself. He
knows that his essence is not body but soul and hopes that his soul
should live in bliss though the body may fall off. “A mortal as I am,
with heart filled with reverence and devotion, 1 am invoking you who
are immortal. Oh, Jatavedas, pray, bestow glory upon us and upon
our family; let me attain to immortality” (S.75). “You are the
emperors of the world, Mitra and Varuna; you look dazzling in an
assembly. We beg of you with folded hands the gift of rains, grace
 and immortality. Your word fills heaven and earth like thunder”
 (S.76). Immortality is certainly not physical immortality, for, it is
 neither asked for, nor granted. What the Rsi is visualising is a state,
 where, after realising one’s identity with the Infinite, one lives on,
 not merely as a finite being, but as a finite in the Infinite. “May
 Vivasvan make death run away from us. Let Surya keep us fit tll
 old age” (S.77). Or, “Adorable is your greatness and life-giving your
 vision, like the sun. Pray, lead us hence to immortality” (S.78). All
 deficiency is associated with mortal existence and all excellence
 with immortality. Miseries, sorrows, sufferings, sins must all be
 removed before one attains to immortality. “Oh omniscient God, if
 inadvertently any lapse has appeared in our conduct, kindly save us
 from its evil effects. May we attain to immortality along with our
 companions” (S.79). Similar is the import of the famous Mantra in
 the Yajurveda (also repeated in the ISopanisad) which reconciles the
 claims
      of action and knowledge and which declares immortality as
 a fruit of the tree of real knowledge or intuitive realisation of God.
 “He who knows the real place of action and knowledge, crosses the
 ocean of death by means of action, and gains immortality by means
 of knowledge” (S.80). Prof. R.D. Ranade’s interpretation of the two
 concepts, Vidya and Avidya enables us to understand the above
 Mantra very clearly and gives us a new perspective. By carefully
 and skillfully dissecting the Mantra layer by layer, and finding out
 parallels for these concepts from Greek philosophy, Professor
 Ranade shows that here is present in the form of a suggestion the
 Bhagavad-gita doctrine of disinterested action. We may summarise
 his account. The Mantra preceding this one states that those who
 follow the path of ignorance, go to blind darkness and those who
 follow the path of knowledge, go to greater darkness still. One fails

                                    28
                                                                    Incentives to Spiritual Life



to understand how the path of knowledge can ever lead to greater
darkness. It must be remembered that not knowledge but the pride
of possessing that knowledge, is a man’s undoing. One who merely
pursues the path of false knowledge (Avidya), gains nothing; but
even    those      who       pride   themselves           for       having    right   knowledge
(Vidya),    meet        with    worse      fate.     It   is    a    clever combination         of
recognising the real            place of lower knowledge and                          possessing
without pride the higher knowledge, that one stands to gain. The
lower and higher categories may be sense-knowledge and intellec-
tual knowledge (termed ‘opinion’ and ‘knowledge’ respectively in
Greek philosophy) which are synthesised in intuition; they may also
refer to    the    path       of action     and      path       of knowledge          which are
synthesised        in    distinterested      action.           Such     a    synthesis   can    be
effected    only        by   him     who    goes      beyond          sense-knowledge          and
intellect and attains to intuitive apprehension of God.!

       Neither sense nor intellect is discarded; they are the negative
and the positive             ‘moments’      in the whole process; they are re-
arranged and transcended in the higher synthesis. This type of
apprehension, “while it lifts us beyond the sphere of an opposition,
which seemed absolute, brings into view a principle of unity which
was hidden beneath the difference of its terms...”? “The reality of
things, since it cannot be apprehended by our divided intelligence
either through perception or through conception, either through
sense or through understanding, must be apprehended by an intuitive
intelligence, for which the division of the two does not exist, or by
which it is transcended; i.e. it can be apprehended only by an
intelligence       which       is    entirely      different          from    ours’.*    Such    a
knowledge-process possesses the immediacy of sense-perception


1   Those who are interested should refer to the excellent exposition of the Mantra
    in the two books: (a) R.D. Ranade, A Constructive Survey of Upanishadic
    Philosophy, (2nd Edition), Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay-400 007, 1968, Pp.
    218-219 (b) S.K. Belvalker and R.D. Ranade, The Creative Period, Bilvakunja
    Publishing House, Poona, 1927, Pp. 171-174

2   ६. Caird, The Critical Philosophy of Kant, Vol. I, James Maclehose and Sons,
    Glasgow, 1909, P. 176

3   Ibid. P. 174



                                                29
Pathway to God in the Vedas



and objectivity of intellect and thus corrects the defects of both. Kant
calls it perceptive intelligence or intuitive understanding; but he
does not grant this capacity to human beings. The Vedic Rsi on the
other hand, was positive about the fact that man does possess this
faculty and that it alone enables him to gain immortality. This is
the power of mystical realisation of God. Avidya and Vidya are not
merely epistemological categories; they represent two apparently
opposite stages in any dialectic; they are the thesis and the antithesis
which are later combined in the synthesis. That is why, Avidya and
 Vidya were taken by Prof. Ranade to mean the path of action and
 the path of knowledge. The Mantra in question virtually breaks down
 under the weight of the message it conveys.
      7. Hope for Divine Bliss. There is a breathtakingly long list
 of demands. Yet another incentive for the sage to turn to God is his
 craving for blessedness. “Mortal man should necessarily pray to the
 Adityas for the gift of unparallelled raptures. As human beings, we
 are by nature prone to death. Pray, prolong our life so that we may
 relish the taste of real life” (S.81). “When a man, enlightened with
 the fire of devotion, sacrifices every thing for the sake of God, then
 only will he be able to experience the taste of the supreme beatitude”
 (S.82). Yet another seer hums: “May we attain to that covetable
 realm where God-devoted sages are privileged to enjoy the ecstatic
 delight. In that supreme realm of Visnu, there is the ever bubbling
 fountain of joy. He is the nearest of kin to all of us” (S.83). There
 is a Mantra in which we find a mention of different categories of
 existence — the physical like sea, the biological like animals and
 the psychological like man’s heart. “The whole universe depends on
 the support of your light and glory, whether in the sea or in the life
 of animals or in the heart of man. Favour us with the enjoyment of
 that honey-flavoured wave of contentment” (S.84). The sage as is
 his wont, will offer glorificatory poems to God for this ecstatic state.
 “Offer your prayers to him who is ever youthful, is non-violent in
 words, is the source of all felicity and is ready to guide you to Truth
 through the ocean of worldly existence” (S.85). There is a clear
 awareness in the Rsi that he alone can be transported to the region
 of bliss, who, by the force of his meditation, is entitled to a direct
 vision of God, because that vision itself is the bliss supreme. “Men,

                                   30
                                            Incentives to Spiritual Life



like fair-winged birds sucking honey, encamp on the tree of this
universe and procreate. On the top of that tree there is, they say, the
most enchantingly sweet fruit. Nobody can claim it except the one
who sees face to face God, the father of the universe” (S.86).
     The ideal the Vedic Rsi really cherished, was a judicious
combination of worldly happiness and spiritual bliss. As seen above,
itis a right synthesis ofthis-worldliness and other-worldliness, each
having its rightful place. Both active life and contemplative life are
valued and a healthy blend ofthe two is sought after. “I find recourse
to none other except you for prosperity, strength, stateliness and
spiritual eminence” (S.87). “Shower on us, oh Indra, the streams
ofcelestial and terrestrial distinction and exaltation, infuse splendid
vigour in your devotee” (S.88). In this Mantra as in the next two,
two different words Parthiva (earthly) and Divya (heavenly) are used
for these two distinct types of majesty. The sages knew their
respective standings in life. “All dignity, worldly and spiritual, is
yours, Oh Indra and Agni. Pray, extend to us that nobility which is
capable of nurturing all living beings” (S.89). The sage Visvamanas
Vaiyasva prays : “Oh Mitra and Varuna, who have control over gifts
as well as over heavenly and earthly food, send down peerless
showers towards us” (S.90). An Atharvaveda Mantra exudes a
freshness as it longs after consolidation of knowledge and bliss. The
Rsi calls on God again and again, to elevate him. “Oh Vacaspati,
come here, come again. Confer light on me. Let me be a permanent
abode of knowledge” (S.91). The same confident and lively attitude
is seen in another Mantra also. “With that divine food, which was
virtuously prepared for Brahman by the first promulgator of moral
laws, Prajapati, which sustains the world and which does not harm
anyone; with that food I shall conquer death. With that food, by
which the creators won over death, which food was obtained with
virtue and austerities, which was prepared for Brahman, 1 shall
overcome death. With that divine food from which nectar was born,
which became the overlord of Gayatri, in which are established the
Vedas; with that food I shall cross over death” (S.92). For the Vedic
Rsi hopes raised are hopes fulfilled. By the moral-spiritual path of
meditation on the name of God, he feels sure to reach the highest
end. “May my life be prolonged by meditation, may my vital airs

                                  31
Pathway to God in the Vedas



become more zestful by meditation, may my eyes, and ears become
sharp and my back be solid by meditation. We have become the
children of Prajapati; Oh gods, we have reached the heavens, we
have become immortal” (S.93).
         A survey of incentives shows that various reasons put a man
on the pathway to God. Fully aware of his imperfection, and at the
same time being convinced that only the most perfect can remould
 imperfection into perfection, man runs for divine support. For a
 radiant intellect, the aspirant looks up to the mass of radiance; for
 moral insight, he approaches the morally perfect; for bliss, he prays
 to the bliss incarnate. A fair sample of handpicked Mantras gives
 us an insight into the mind of the Vedic seeker after truth.
 Uninhibited in his demands, he is profusely thankful when the
 prayers are granted; this spotlights his open-mindedness. He admits
 his faults and asks for forgiveness and this shows his practical
 wisdom. He wishes for earthly riches but subordinates them to
 divine peace; this reflects his healthy eudaemonistic mentality. In
 the hymns there is mention of need of instruction, of moral
 discipline, or of grace. All this strikes a respondent chord in the heart
 of any modern devotee. The outpourings do not represent the mental
 states peculiar only to the Vedic sage. His is a paradigm case
 wherein is found the reflection of abiding hopes in the human heart.
 Through these praise-songs, offered with a mood of tranquil dignity,
 any seeker will feel the pulse of his great great ancestor. The Vedas
  give us indisputably the first saga of spiritual life.

       प्रजापतेः प्रजा अभूम, LAT: अगन्म, अमृता अभूम ।। यजुर्वेद 1X.21




                                            40
                             CHAPTER II




                    Moral Preparation


     A house is cleansed of cobwebs and is re-decorated for the
visit of an important guest. Per chance the guest may not turn up;
but the host knows that it is risky to allow things to be topsy-turvy
and in a state of unpreparedness. Will not the visitor be unhappy?
Will he not even go back? Tulasidasa has already warned us that
God Narayana may visit us at any time and in any guise.                  A
conscientious house-holder will always keep every thing ready with
expectation and hope of the guest making his appearance any
moment. So does an earnest aspirant. He makes his mind, body,
intellect and speech pure by shedding vices and beautifies his soul
by inculcating virtues. Eternal vigilance, thus, is the price we have
to pay for not missing divine visitation. This constitutes moral
preparation and it makes a devotee worthy of the lofty nearness
of the Divine. On our way to God, this tilling of the ground needs
be given the status of a loyal companion. Though we have to
traverse the route all by ourselves, we can not refuse the helping
hand of the companion, nor can leave him            in the middle of the
course. Similarly moral equipment makes us more and more fit to
enter into the kingdom of God. Ethical ripening is never complete
and goes on ever developing as we continue our journey. The
process   of   bettering   ourselves   is   so   inseparable   from   other
observances for spiritual progress, that it is more apt to say that
we improve ourselves by trodding the moral path. We shall see
how the training of mind occupies the initial stage. The fickle mind
has to be subjected to a two-way treatment — washing out vices
like idleness, sleep or passions and decorating by virtues like truth,
purity, continence and so on.     We shall next take up the topic of
moral standard. Rule of law, rule of God and self-rule are the
classical candidates, whose worth is to be tested. In the end we
shall take note of significance of moral readiness in spiritual life.
Pathway to God in the Vedas



     1. Mind and its Control. First and foremost, it is man’s mind
which needs purification. It is endowed with tremendous speed and
an unobstructed entrance to the remotest corner of the universe.
Assisted by the sense-organs, it goes on desiring a myriad of
objects;   it thinks its own thoughts and spins its own doubts. In a
word, it is engaged in all activities except the one that will lead one
God-ward. Hence the necessity of curbing its activities, imposing
controls on it, and putting it on the proper track. Thus the prayer of
Pracetas Angirasa runs: “My mind is engaged in thousand and one
 activities. Oh, instigator of the mind! Vanish from my sight, go far
 far away. See that the goddess of evil does not cast her shadow here.
 My mind touches innumerable objects. Men ought to desire only that
 which is auspicious and obtain the highest blessing. I crave for a
 blissful sight of Vaivasvata” (S.1). “Some of your speculations are
 auspicious while some are      inauspicious.   You can    contain   all
 thoughts within you” (S.2). This Mantra refers to a very peculiar
 characteristic of our mind. It wishes for all pleasurable things and
 even works under the delusion that it has all that was longed for.
 This is just day-dreaming or wishful-thinking. At the same time it
 entertains ghastliest ideas which even its worst enemy          would
 shudder to think of. Sometimes it is at the peak of a mountain while
 the next moment it is at the bottom of a vale — all by imagination.
 Thus some of its words are auspicious while others speak of ill
 omens. It is well-known that the senses are immersed in external
 objects that provide pleasure. Naturally they turn away from God.
 The mind usually keeps their company. But such is the uncontrol-
 lable force and unstableness of the mind that perhaps purifying the
 senses may become easier than keeping the mind steady. “My ears.
 long to hear the voice of God and my eyes long to see that flame
 of light lodged in the heart. But the mind wanders in yonder regions.
 What words should I speak and what thoughts should J entertain?”
 (S.3). The first task an aspirant is faced with, is to make the mind
 steady by arresting its unhindered wanderings. The roamings will
 come to an end if its thirst for objects disappears; and so catharsis
 of the mind from greed, comes to be the initial concern of every one.
 “If a devotee oft and on sweeps clean his mind, Soma will get for
 him an invaluable gift of jewels” (S.4). The jewel stands for spiritual

                                   42
                                                     Moral Preparation



experience and that can be had only by a spotless    mi
diseases and agonies from us; destroy the wicked |
our mind of all evil ideas. Oh, Adityas, keep us away from sin.
the vengeful aggressors far from us, correct our thoug
make hatred evaporate from our minds. Bless us wi

which will (ree a committed sinner from his black
There is also a prayer by the sage Tapumirdhan Barhaspatya      !
removal of three types of evil — moral, psychological and pi
(5.6).

     Spiritual literature in every land recommends company of the
good as an antidote to mind’s evil thoughts. Saints and sages exert
their influence on the minds of ordinary men so that the latter are
ashamed to sin in their company. “Both Indra and Vayu are welcome
guests; and so | invite them amidst us. All our friends will be filled
with wholesome ideas in their company” (5.7). “Let all gods with
noble minds assemble here for the celebration of the sacrifice
originated by ViSvakarman, the architect of the universe. To the
presiding deity of the sacrifice, who is as if the eye or mouth or
supporter of the sacrifice, I offer oblations with the power of my
speech, power of audition and power of mind” (S.8). With such a
welcome bent to the mind, spiritual life becomes possible.

     The rinsing of the mind, however, is effected not merely by
removing evil deliberations but by filling it with sacred ones. Mind’s
very nature is to entertain ideas and it is next to impossible to empty
it of dirty cerebrations. It must have some ideas which it goes on
chewing, for it abhors a vacuum. So trying to flush the debris of
unhealthy ideas from the mind is an exercise in futility so long as
no good ideas are provided to it to sport with. The most profitable
way is to feed it with virtuous reflections. Thus the common prayer
of saints for going beyond the mind must be understood in this
positive way. The expressions Amana or Amanaska (metamorphos-
ing mind into no-mind), Manolaya, or Manamagna-yoga (absorption
of mind into itself) or Unmana (transcending the mind) do not imply
a negative concept of a vacant mind. They imply concentration of
mind on God. Deep meditation may lead to less and less mental
modifications when the mind becomes steady. Therefore, the first

                                  43
Pathway to God in the Vedas



step on the way is to divert mind’s energies from its usual pleasure-
seeking machinations and direct them towards pious cognitions.
Though the mind is ever restless, it is amenable to discipline; though
it is happiness-loving, it is at the same time, appreciative of high
ideals. Many anthems are a pointer to the deep insight of a Vedic
Rsi into the nature of mind, its enormous fund of energy and the
possibility of harnessing that energy for carrying out                any job
whatsoever. Modern psychology terms this process as sublimation,
the Vedic seer called it turning Godwards. “Oh Soma, make our mind
holy, wise and dutiful. You are great; let us delight in the warmth
of your heart as cattle in green pastures” (S.9). By far the most
 beautiful passage for the re-channelisation of mind’s energies is a
 cluster of Sivasankalpa Mantras. These psalms do not refer to the
 monkeyish pranks of the mind at all; on the other hand they mention
 only its vast capability of executing a large number of good deeds.
 The   mind   referred   to   here   has    undergone   austerities    and   is
 completely sublimated and ennobled. We get here a picture of the
 wonders it can work when properly educated. The lines, laced with
 music, enthrone the mind and we feel as if we are reading about the
 exploits of a well-groomed, mature intellect and not about the
 proverbially unsteady mind. It is only the perorative refrain, namely,

 तन्मे मनः शि वसंकल्पमस्तु which reminds us that the stanzas refer to the
 mind. Here, if anywhere, we see how mind can go beyond itself.
 “That which traverses long distances while awake as also during
 sleep, that which is the light of lights, may that mind of mine be
 absorbed in noble thoughts. That with which wise people accomplish
 their tasks and excel in assemblies and sacrificial duties, that
 mysterious spirit residing inside all beings, may that mind of mine
 be absorbed in noble thoughts. That which is knowledge and intellect,
 as well as strength, that which is the immortal light in the hearts, that
 without which no work whatsoever can be undertaken, may that mind
 of mine be absorbed in noble thoughts. That immortal principle which
 comprehends both the past and the future, that which helps the
 accomplishment ofthe sacrifice performed by seven sacrificers, may
 that mind of mine be absorbed in noble thoughts. That in which
 १२९५९५३, Samaveda and Yajurveda are established as spokes in a
 wheel, that which enters into every single activity of people, may that

                                       44
                                                         Moral Preparation



mind of mine be absorbed in noble thoughts. That which guides
men’s deeds as the charioteer guides the movements of his horses,
that which is speedy and deathless and which inhabits the hearts, may
that mind of mine be absorbed in noble thoughts” (S.10).

      2. Avoidance of Vices. We saw that a two-way treatment is
involved as regards purification of mind. In the first place we have
to remove rotten and impure ideas from it and secondly we have to
fill it up with immaculate ones. The two processes are simultaneous
and not successive; they are also inter-dependent in that both of them
are complementary and work together for the furtherance of the
ideal. The first   process is avoidance of vices and the second,
cultivation of virtues. Avoidance of vices is easier said than done
because it is like swimming against the stream. The mind’s energies
are   flowing   along   with   the   current   of propensities   or   mental
inclinations, says William James. To go the way of impulses, you
do not need any effort. It is action in the line of least resistance. But
to reach the ideal, propensities are to be overcome. Now psycho-
logically the ideal does not possess a sufficient moving force. That
force is to be augmented by our effort and maintained in the face
of resistance from natural tendencies. Thus moral action is action
in the line of greatest resistance. The smallest temptation is enough
to induce a man to lay down his oars and the boat goes down tossing
aimlessly along the swirling waters of passions and in a moment
nullifies man’s perseverence. A constant vigil           is called for to
maintain the tenacity of effort and redouble it when necessary.

      Our infirmities are generally taken to be a result of excess of
Tamas which gives rise to ignorance and blunder. We shall consider
the vices in some convenient groups; but it does not mean that they
are exclusive of each other, nor is the list exhaustive. Vices are good
companions of each other and if one vice is operating, others
willingly extend their help. Thus any bad act can be said to be due
to different obliquities acting severally or collectively. Again their
constitution and even garbs are so similar that a wrong action can
as justifiably be said to be a result of lust (Kama) as of avarice
(Lobha). We may first consider the defects that are predominantly
physical, i.e., idleness and sleep; then take up the group which is


                                      45
Pathway to God in the Vedas



traditionally known as Sadripus or six enemies of man. We shall
then deal with bad habits that a man develops and bad company that
he is lured to. All of them are great impediments and need purging.
     (a) Idleness and Sleep. “The heavens build the house of the
body with various constituents. The wise man treats different
colours in different ways. He heightens the glory of the radiant,
 offers the red to the sacrifice and removes the black” (S.11). The
 body is constituted of three elements — Sattva, Rajas and Tamas.
 The words used in the Mantra are रुशती लोहि नी and कृष्णा; they are
 colours indicating the elements. The wise person glorifies Sattva,
 sacrifices Rajas and destroys Tamas. The prayer of Rsi Grtsamada
 is: “Let me not be troubled by idleness in my devotions nor should
 I be a victim of lethargy. I should never try to avoid my duty of
 straining Soma, nor should I even speak such a thing by word of
 mouth. For, God fulfils my desires, blesses me with riches, alerts
 my intellect and brings me on to a proper path” (S.12). Tiredness,
 warn psychologists, is more imaginary than real. We feel tired
 because   we   are   habituated    to    feel    tired;   but   with   a   little
  determination, we can defer the point of fatigue and carry on work
  for some more time. Idleness is the greatest enemy of man which
  destroys his physical well-being and wealth. It also spells his
  spiritual ruin. Earning riches and using them righteously are very
  important. If one is given to inactivity even gods do not care for
  him. “I hate him who is indolent as well as him who does not know
  how to utilise his wealth        properly. Both of them           meet    with
  destruction” (S.13). “Gods love him who strains Soma. They do not
  like men who sleep away their time. They are themselves alert and
  correct man’s faults” (S.14). “He who disobeys you and is absorbed
  in sleep will perish of his own short-comings. Oh Indra, preserve
  your bounties for him who toils to offer Soma” (S.15). Ignorance
  and addiction to vices are reciprocally related; they increase each
  other’s strength. Ordinarily a cause remains a cause, and effect,
  effect. A cause does not become an effect or vice versa. Kant,
  however, popularised the         concept       of reciprocal    causality.    A
  backlash from the effect influences the cause in turn. Therefore, in
  a sense, the effect becomes cause and the cause becomes effect.
  Reciprocity thus implies that two events are inter-influencing and

                                         46
                                                     Moral Preparation



become causes and effects of each other. Thus out of ignorance man
takes to bad habits, and bad habits in turn cloud his intellect. “Oh
Varuna it is not solely my will that is responsible for my sins.
Liquors, anger, gambling and thoughtlessness all contribute to them.
Even an elder leads one astray.       A dream is sometimes sufficient
to seduct a man”, observes the sage Maitravaruni Vasistha (S.16).
This Mantra calls for two comments. (i) If not his own will, we may
ask, whose will is it, any way, that is responsible for the vicious
act? Bad habits like liquor are not an external agency; it is the
individual who allows himself to lapse into them. Rather than blame
them, one should blame oneself, and own the responsibility of
misbehaviour. The Vedic seer, however, fully knows human nature
of passing on to others the accountability of evil-doing. (ii) The
Vedic Rsi here shows a wonderful insight into the power which a
dream possesses. Sometimes dream-experiences cause fear and
even drive a person mad. At times, in a dream many an insoluble
problem is solved. At the same time, instances are not wanting when
a person receives spiritual blessings in a dream-state. On the whole,
a   dream-experience   should   not    be brushed   aside   as   merely
hallucinatory. We may call a dream unreal, but it causes effects that
are real and one has to enjoy or suffer them. It is remarkable that
our forefathers knew long ago what a dream can do, when the
science of psychology was yet to be born.
      (b) The Six Passions. Kama (lust), Krodha (anger), Lobha
(avarice), Moha (infatuation), Mada (pride) and Matsara (jealousy)
look to be a paltry number of six. But they are a man’s undoing. Of
them, Kama is the most powerful one. Lust for varied sense-objects
makes a man blind to his real good. Hence the necessity of curbing
desires in general. And wants know no end. “This offering is meant
for Agni who is called Kama or desire. He is consumer of all things
in the universe (Visvat), He is both a giver and taker. He is powerful
and intelligent. He is an irrespressible wanderer. May Savitr who
wears gold ornaments on his hands, Indra, Brhaspati, Varuna, Mitra,
Agni, ViSwedevas and Angirasa hear my supplication to pacify this
meat-eater’, observes Rsi Vasistha (S.17). A number of prayers
speak of the sway of temptations over man. Aware of the fact that
they are impediments on the way to moral and spiritual progress,

                                  47
Pathway to God in the Vedas



the Rsi requests the deities to weed them out. “Let not the goddess
of evil (Nirrti) who is ever-increasing in strength, overpower us. Let
her be destroyed along with our thirst for objects” (S.18). “The two
black vultures soar in the sky. They increase our sorrow and famish
our body. They inflict pain on us. I cross over them like a tired ox
or cow, like a barking dog or like an invading wolf” (S.19). The two
vultures are longing and avarice which the Rsi is confident of
 controlling. “Oh Agni, disarm him with your weapons. The enemy
 wants to make slaves of us by means of eyes, mind or thought, 1.6.
 by offering seductive objects to different senses. I offer the whole
 of myself for the sake of liberation” (S.20). Apparently for this sage
 as for Spinoza, freedom from appetites is itself freedom from human
 bondage. Cupidities are a bondage; control over them, is freedom.
 Man as belonging to the realm of nature, is bound to be under the
 thumb of wants; but as a member of the realm of ends, he must rise
 above them. “After all, we are petty mortals and shall hanker after
 pleasures. But Oh wielder of the thunderbolt, raise us above them
 and make us perfect” (5.21). “Many are our fancies but in the
 presence of Soma, 1 pray to gods to forgive us for our short-
 comings” (S.22). The things which we covet have a power of
  allurement and man has to fortify himself if he has to combat them.
  “Agasti performed austerities which were hard as digging with a
  spade. He also pined after progeny and strength. But he by his great
  power conquered both material wealth and spiritual good” (S.23).
  There has been a specific mention of the sex-instinct, indulgence in
  which leads to a man’s down-fall. “Oh Indra, let the male and the
  female demons be done to death. Let them, whose sport it is to
  sabotage a man’s onward march, be beheaded. Let them not even
  live till sunrise” (S.24). “May not the ghouls demolish our efforts.
  May not the enemies inhabiting inside, attack us. The noble Indra
  wishes to rule over men of different natures. Let not those given to
  sex come near our sacrifice” (S.25).
       (c) Other Temptations. Different kinds of craving which lay
  waste man’s moral effort, are mentioned as deadly foes. All of them
  stem from greed which is the root cause. They are only new styles
  in which appetite covers up its scheming. We shall note in the sequel
  a few of thi. The eclogues are directed against these frailties; they

                                    48
                                                      Moral Preparation



pray to deities to wash off the same and express the conviction that
he who possesses purity, is helped by God. “Disregard him who
pours Soma in anger or drinks it in an unholy place. Partake of the
offerings given by us” (S.26). “He who offers food to a hungry man
lingering at the door for a morsel of food, is really generous. He
obtains the fruit of sacrifice. Even adversaries become his friends.
The rich should always give in charity which takes them on to the
right path. Verily money always changes hands just as the wheels
of a chariot always move. Wealth in the hands of an avarice-ridden
man is unfruitful. He who eats by himself without giving to the gods
and to the deserving, verily eats sin. It is like poison”, are the words
of Rsi Bhiksu Angirasa (S.27). “Bound by the infatuating strings of
worldly life, this man is struggling hard and is looking eagerly for
deliverance. Pray, Oh Agni, release him from shackles. It is only
when he becomes free from blinding temptations that he will be a
blessed recipient of divine illumination” (S.28). When a man is
under the sway of anger, avarice or craving, his intellect is clouded,
discrimination dimmed and generosity blunted. There is back-sliding
in his moral life and he becomes a victim of other weaknesses like
pride and jealousy. “He who strains Soma for Indra day and night,
receives his grace. But he who is proud of his progeny, who vainly
decks his body or enjoys the company of the base, is forsaken by
Indra” (S.29). “Come one, come all. Let us praise Indra who
possesses treasures and is luminous. His love will save us from the
dreary effects of pride” (S.30). “We wipe out from your heart the
first wave ofjealousy and the wave that comes next. We sweep your
heart clean of that root of sorrow. As the earth possesses a mind
that is but dead or more dead than dead or as the mind of a dying
person is already dead, so is the mind of a jealous person. We rout
out jealousy from your mind which is narrow and which is liable to
fall” (S.31). The dead mind here indicates the hardened mind ofa
jealous person or a mind that is completely devoid of any softness
of love. “Soma, you are our armour, wide and strong to protect us
from the hateful mischiefs wrought by the enemies residing within
our body as well as brought by those that are outside” (S.32). All
the six fancies are mentioned in one Mantra which finds these vices
in the animal world. The verse desires their removal from man’s

                                   49
Pathway to God in the Vedas



mind. “Oh Indra, kill him who like a sparrow is given to lust; kill
him who like a wolf is given to anger; kill him who like a vulture
is given to avarice; kill him who like an owl is given to infatuation;
kill him who like an eagle is given to pride; kill him who like a dog
is given to hatred. Kill the demons with a stone” (S.33). God knows
our motives for He is all-seer. He punishes the wicked and rewards
the good. “Men can not earn a fortune by fraudulent means. Wealth
frowns on him who is wicked. In fullness of time, illumination
should be bestowed on a devotee like me” (S.34). Feeling sure that
 he knows God’s ways, Rsi Irimbithi Kanva exclaims: “You know
 who is truthful and who is sinful. You stay where holiness resides”
 (S.35).

      (d) Bad Habits. It is the reign of covetousness over the mind
 which results in a man’s addiction to foul habits like gambling with
 dice. “The dice fall to the earth and then spring upwards. Themselves
 handless, they enslave a man who possesses hands. They are like
 magical coal, themselves cool, they burn the heart of the gamester”
 (S.36). “My resolution not to play dice is firm because dice-playing
 would attract censure from friends. But the sound of dice makes my
 resolution crumble and I run to the sinful gambling house after the
 manner of an unchaste woman” (S.37). It is such lapses, psychology
 tells us, that make slaves of us all. We wrongly think that one solitary
 lapse will not eat into our determination, but alas! it does. It throws
 us into the habit of making and breaking resolves and we become
 slaves of hungers and slaves of dirty habits. There is another psalm,
 which, instead of giving just negative advice of not doing evil acts,
 prescribes healthy substitutes. “The holy Savitr asked me to take to
 farming instead of running after gambling and take delight in what
 we get as a sufficient bounty. “You will be blessed with a good wife,
 blessed with wealth and cattle’, he said” (S.38). There are seven
 deadly sins from which a man should save himself. “The wise sages
 have prescribed seven rules. Breaking even one of them makes man
 a sinner. Agni who supervises the observance of. these rules is
 present in all the worlds,” warns the sage Trita Aptya (S.39). The
 seven sins are: drinking, gambling, sex, hunting, chastisement,
 cruelty and finding fault with others (पानं, अक्षाः, स्त्रि यः, मृगया, दण्डः,


                                      50
                                                           Moral Preparation



पारुष्य, अन्यदूषणम्‌). A verse from Nirukta (VI.27) enumerates
somewhat different sins: स्तेयं, तल्पारोहणं, ब्रह्महत्या, भ्रूणहत्या, सुरापानं,
दुष्कृत-कर्मणां पुनः पुनः सेवा, पातके अनृतोद्यम्‌ ive. theft, sex, killing a
Brahmin, killing a baby in the womb, drinking, repeatedly practising
evil things and hiding one’s sins. The warning is that a single one
of these, is a stain on man’s purity. What is more important is that
aman should not remain off his guard under the impression that there
is no witness to his sins, for Agni who is omnipresent, is the eternal
witness. It is interesting to note that Mahatma Gandhi also warned
the nation against seven sins :
—
      Politics without principles,
      Wealth without work,
Wn    Pleasure without conscience,
      Knowledge without character,
+ 5
      Commerce without morality,
~     Science without humanity and
=     Worship without sacrifice.
      An aspirant is always advised to avoid the company of the
wicked and irreligious persons because it is very likely that he will
stray away from his path of virtue because of them. “Pray, deliver
us not unto the gangs of the robbers who cherish in their hearts ill-
will towards gods. They will deprive us of our valuable possessions
though we are harmless and straight-forward. We, your devotees, do
not cherish any thing higher than an ardent love for you” (S.40).
“Whisk away from our path the evil-minded, arrogant and deceitful
men threatening to obstruct us without any provocation. Make our
progress on the pathway to God smooth and comfortable. You are
the protector of our person and a saviour who inspires us with
encouraging words. Pray, destroy these revilers of God. Let not
people be under the impression that even unrighteous men can taste
the divine bliss” (S.41). Who are those whose company must
particularly be avoided? “Agni drives them far away who have no
will to work, who talk in riddles, whose tongue hurts others, the
bargainers, the faithless, who go away from the path of rectitude or
who disregard sacrifices. When Agni becomes the chief, he lowers

                                       51
Pathway to God in the Vedas



the status of those who do not perform any sacrifice” (S.42). Sins
are committed by mind as by speech and he who talks ill of the gods
should be kept away by devotees. “Those who shun the right path,
 like a brotherless woman, and who seek evil like a woman who
 betrays her husband, are sinners. It is they who entertain ugly
 thoughts or speak untruth, that have created hell” (S.43). Sayana has
interpreted अनृताः as those whose minds are defiled and असत्या: as
those whose speech is defiled. The company of those who revere
 truth is to be sought. “Any one will realise that they are quite justified
 who talk of justness and truth of Mitra and Varuna. If a weapon of
 the enemy has three sharp blades, Mitra and Varuna use weapons
 having four blades and vanquish the godless” (S.44). To hell with
 the haters of gods; victory to Truth.

      3. Cultivation of Virtues. Morality is internal and not external;
 a person must be good and not merely do good; for, a man may well
 entertain immoral ideas in mind and still do apparently good actions
 by hand. Purity of motives, therefore, occupies a higher place in the
 scale, At the same time of what value a man’s holy intentions be if
 they are not translated into action? It is true that a man’s vision must
 be clear, but his action must also be imminent, so that the link
 between good thought and good action is not snapped. Generally,
  vices keep a right of way and retain occupation of the track of action;
  they fly past with gusto and leave virtues inert till the end of time.
  The fall-out from vices smothers virtues and leaves them pale and
  impotent. It is here that an extra force is to be applied to stimulate
  the holy thoughts to assume an imperative mood. A mere talk of
  banishing errors or even their actual removal will not be sufficient.
  Inculcation of pious wishes is only like a legislative body; it remains
  inert without an executive body. This latter is the practice of virtues,
  and constitutes the social aspect of moral preparation. As in the
  removal of faults, here also there is the psychological preliminary
  and that consists in filling the mind and reason with worthy thoughts
  and giving a healthy bent to them. “Oh Visnu, bless us with an
  intention that is pure, and has the good of others before it. May we
  enjoy the treasures gained by such a mind” (S.45). “Oh Lord, may
  we have guileless brains as well as the company of great men” (3.46).

                                     52
                                                         Moral Preparation



The Vedic sage is not satisfied with the friendship of the earthly, he
‘aspires for the company of the heavenly figures. “The primary duty
of man as a mortal being, is to develop the friendship of God, the
supreme guide. In order to enjoy and fulfil the purpose of life one
is required to win spiritual illumination” (S.47). It is God who is
responsible for the constitution of man’s abilities; even if man wants
to fill the mind with God, the initiative should really come from the
Eternal. “God became absorbed in the praises sung by devotees and
the devotees became absorbed in praising Pisan who is a guardian
of law. May he send healing forces to us, for, it is he who governs
the wits ofall” (S.48). The sage Aindra Vimada says: “Oh Agni, fill
our   breasts   with   noble   thoughts.   Your   mind   has   gained   the
companionship with gods; pass on to us the noble nature of your
heart. Give to the sage Vimada, right judgement, food, strength and
wealth” (S.49).


      No work on morality or religion would be complete without
enumeration of virtues, be they individual or social. The Bhagavad-
gita has discussed the topic time and on. In the seventh canto of the
Bhagavata we find a similar listing of virtues. In the Vedas there
is no separate section for the subject as in a systematised thesis;
but references to different angelic qualities are seen throughout.
“The earth is supported by truth, the heavens by the sun. The
Adityas are upheld by Rta and Soma rules supreme in heaven”
(S.50)   The earth continues in its course because there are noble
people here. Kabir says: Had there been no saints in the world, the
whole universe would have been burnt to ashes.


      (a) Truth. “The great truth, the stern cosmic law, dedication,
austerities or sacrifice are necessary for the maintenance of order
on the earth. May that mother earth, who controls the past and the
present, make available a wide area of work to us” (S.51). This refers
to the necessity of uprightness in society, which is the field of
activity of every person. “The wise know that there goes on a
competition between truth and falsehood. Veracity is simple and that
is protected by Soma. Deception is slain by him” (S.52). “May the
creator of the earth not harm us, nor should the creator of heavens


                                    53
Pathway to God in the Vedas



harm us. The latter with truth as his order, brought into being the
great sweet waters” (S.53). The sages had great faith in the powers
of honesty and believed that it alone would win ultimately. But
truthfulness was practised by gods alone, laments the Satapatha
Brahmana and man made dishonesty his law (S.54). “Straight-
forwardness and crookedness had the same appearance. It was
Prajapati who discriminated between them. He inspired people to
have faith in the former and distrust in the latter’ (S.55). Here is
presented a universal maxim that before actually taking to the just
way it is utterly necessary to distinguish between right and wrong.
Error comes in the guise of correctness; ignorance in the garb of
knowledge; falsehood poses itself as truth; unreality as reality; and
sin apes the style of holiness. These are the illusions in the fields
of logic, psychology, epistemology, metaphysics and ethics respec-
 tively. The initial task before an aspirant is critical enquiry; then
 alone can the correct line of a healthy course be taken and stuck to.
The diagnostic tool for such analysis is discrimination or Vyakarana
 as mentioned in the Mantra. It came to be recognised as Viveka later
 on, and became one of the basic qualifications of an aspirant.


      (b) Purity. This is another merit which is held in great respect.
 “Oh Soma, you have a purifying nature. You enter into all parts of
 the body of him who partakes of you. He who does not observe rites
 and austerities, can never obtain your sacred juice. They who
 perform sacrifices, get plenty of it. Purifier Soma, who is like a
 blazing fire to his enemies, has spread out in the heavens with
 glowing rays of light emanating from him; and the gushing currents
 of his juice which protect the pure, who ascend with inherent force,
 occupy the topmost region of heaven. The wonderful Aditya, the
 creator of gods, protects the heavens. Soma, with a noose in hands
 binds his enemies. Only they who are unsullied can drink the sweet
 nectar of Soma,” asserts sage Pavitra Angirasa (S.56). “Sovereigns
 though they are, they honour with love the gifts from the devotees.
 They are the most generous among generous and are always with
 those who are fair and sincere” (S.57). Gods do not forsake those
 who are guileless; they also wipe out whatever trace of impurity a
 person may have in him. This makes it possible for an aspirant to

                                   54
                                                    Moral Preparation



get spiritual experience. “May waters, the mother of the universe
sanctify us. May they carry away all unworthiness with their current.
Thus cleansed I shall rise upwards” (S.58). A similar prayer for
excellence is addressed to Soma. “It is but proper that we praise you
by Uktha psalms, Oh killer of Vrtra.   Yourself holy, Oh majestic one,
you make others holy” (S.59). Again, a host of gods are called upon
to clear the mind of the aspirant. “Oh Soma, remove all that which
causes fear, whether from near or from afar. May that all-creator
Soma, make us untainted as he has powers ofhunting sins from their
hide-outs. Oh Agni, you possess sanctifying lustre; make our bodies
sinless by it. Your rays reach all corners of space; rinse our minds
clean by them so that we may strain the Soma juice. Oh Soma, rub
out our lapses both by your correcting powers and by sacredness of
the juice. Oh all-inspirer Soma and Agni, by your three radiant
manifestations, Agni, Vayu and 50199, make us holy. May Indra
purify us and so may the Vasus. May all the gods purify me and Oh
omniscient Agni, you also purify me” (S.60). The Yajurveda adds
one more verse here: “Make me honourable Oh radiant Agni, by your
crystal-clear holy light, especially in view of my proper observance
of sacrificial rites” (S.61). One verse from the cluster of Rgveda
Mantras just referred to, occurs also in Atharvaveda. Rsi Santati puts
the word चक्षसे in place of वि द्वतः in the Mantra. The change makes
a better reading. It means, ‘make me spotless so that I may have a
vision of you’ (S.62). The Atharvaveda also adds another Mantra:
“May the just lord give me rectitude so that with sufficient strength
and proper understanding, I may be able to accomplish fully the
purpose of life” (S.63).
     (c) Austerities and Continence. These were given their due
place in spiritual life. “We are undergoing austerities (Tapas), Oh
Agni, they are austerities of a special kind. We follow what is
prescribed in the holy texts. By these we shall enjoy long life and
clear intellects,” says Rsi Atharvan (S.64). “Desirous of blessings
I offer libations. I have gained sacred knowledge and am observing
continence (Brahmacarya). May the gods make me happy” (S.65).
“Through self-control and penance did the gods overcome death;
through continence Indra gives valour to gods” (S.66). It is when
a man comes at the cross-roads that he is baffled. Before him there

                                  55
Pathway to God in the Vedas



are two ways, one of goodness and the other of evil. The paths seem
to be intertwined. Hence a supplication to God for right judgment.
“Show me the two tracks separately, that of rectitude and that of
crookedness by untying the knot and disentangling them. Flow unto
me, Oh Soma, with a loud song on your tongue” (5.67). “May the
Lord protect us and prevent us from going astray. May we be
endowed with good health. Drinking of Soma should make me shine
like a blazing fire. Let my analysis be sharp and enthusiasm
growing” (S.68). Great importance was thus laid on moral behaviour
(Sadacarana) which includes many other saintly qualities in it. In
fact, inner nobility expresses itself in exemplary conduct. The sage
prays both for right intellect and right conduct — both for peerless
thought and behaviour of sterling quality, Sumati and Suniti. “You
 lead men by the proper route and do not allow sin and pain to touch
them. You nullify the anger of haters of Brahman; this is a great
 achievement of yours, Oh Brhaspati” (S.69). If a man would just
 stick to the ethical path, he would get whatever is gain-worthy. “Oh
 Agni, give us that glory which is praiseworthy and which increases
 life. Victory and wealth, which many a man covets, can be obtained
 only by adhering to the clean course” (S.70). Those who value
 rectitude become the friends of gods. “Rbhus were really skilful and
 industrious. While yet human, they acquired Indra’s   friendship. They
 did not swerve from the laudable path. The sons of Sudhanvan were
 meritorious. By their conduct and their intelligence, they gained vast
 powers and became immortal” (5.71).
      (d) Other Virtues. There are references to other virtues like
 charity, love, compassion etc. One nearly comes to entertain an
 anachronistic idea that the sage of the Vedas must have studied the
 Yoga-sitras which mention all these virtues, before one remembers
 that the composition of Patanjali’s aphorisms was still centuries
 away. “Those who are themselves enlightened, and look on the
 bound or the unlucky ones with compassion and help them, really
 deserve to be freed instantly by ViSvakarman, the friend of people”
 (S.72). The sound psychological truth couched in these words,
 should not be missed. We should not look on the unlucky with
 compassion merely with our minds (Manasa) but also with eyes
 (Caksusa). Now the term Caksusa by implication (Laksana), exhorts

                                  56
                                                       Moral Preparation



us to actively help the needy. Only mental sympathy, a mere inert
sentimentality which does not prompt us to any action is hollow.
Compassion lies in actually helping and not in filling one’s own heart
with empathetic feelings. Then there are psalms soaked in sweetness
of honey both in words and meaning. “Let sweetness linger on the
tip of my tongue (Jihvaya agre madhu) as also on the root of it (Jihva-
mule madhtlakam), let it fill my deeds and my mind. My behaviour
may be sweet (Madhumat); my walking away be like-wise sweet and
sweet be my speech. Let me be sweet honey inside and outside
(Madhusandrsa)”, sings Rsi Atharvan (S.73). “May my going away
and coming back be sweet. Through your aids, oh ASwins, enrich
our sweetness” (S.74). Tenderness in behaviour may be termed one
of the most important of virtues. Like mercy, affection is twice
blessed, it blesses him who exudes it and him who receives it. It
calms down the mind and facilitates the reign of peace in the minds
of both the giver and the receiver. On the contrary, anger is twice
cursed; for, it not only harms the victim but also destroys the mental
peace of the angry person. It is only with love that one can attract
others to oneself. “A skilful charioteer drives his horses in whatever
direction he pleases. Praise be to the reins which, remaining behind
the horses attract their minds to themselves” (S.75). It is not the
lifeless reins but the controller of these reins, i.e. the charioteer, who
deserves credit for his loving action. Next comes charity. “He who
pleases gods by his generosity lives in the highest heavens. He
enters into the kingdom of gods. Rivers on the earth and in the
heavens carry    clarified butter to him. The earth is full of niceties
for his sake. The riches in the world are meant for him who is liberal;
the sun and the moon shine for him; the immortal position is reserved
for him. They who give, increase their own life and the life of others.
May those who donate things never suffer pain or blemish; not for
them the disabled state of old age. All people will help them while
the miserly suffer all misfortunes” (S.76). “The bestowers will never
die or be destroyed. They are not pestered by sorrows. The earth and
the heavens pour bounties on them” (S.77). “Those who spend their
wealth for others’ liberation (Rayisinah) obtain bliss, position and
wealth. He, who never performs a sacrifice (Avratah) cannot obtain
what he desires” (S.78). Rayisinah is understood as Moksa-dhana-


                                   57
Pathway to God in the Vedas



kamiah, i.e. those who sacrifice for the good of others and Avratah
as Paramartha-prapti-samkalpa-Sinyah i.e. who has no thought for
any charity.

     (e) Social Virtues. Human nature was subjected to a search,
both extensively and intensively, by the Vedic Rsis. Mention of a
wide variety of social virtues and the deep study of man’s mental
make-up, are proof of this. Atharvaveda enumerates qualities like
performance of Vedic sacrifices, philanthropic works, truthfulness,
modesty, benevolence, strength or tolerance (S.79). Incidentally,
Vedic sacrifices (Ista) and philanthropic works (0178) were not held
in high esteem by the Upanisads as is clear from Mundaka (1.2.10).
and Prasna (1.9). Rgveda refers to respect for the moral law, devotion
 or austerities (S.80). There was unflinching faith in the efficacy and
 fruitfulness of the moral life. “We have reached the avenue of
 straight-forwardness which is easy to traverse. A traveller on this
 course gets wealth and his enemies are vanquished” declares the
 mystic RjiSvan Bharadvaja (S.81). “Those who have left off the
 ancient beat, and have refused to hear God’s praises and have
 engaged themselves in unworthy acts, are really blind and deaf. They
 will never reach their goal” (5.82). Sdyana has interpreted the term
  ‘blind’ as spiritually blind, i.e. those who do not see the good in
  others and the term ‘deaf’ as spiritually deaf, i.e. those who do not
 hear the praises of gods.

       (f) Devotion, the Cardinal Virtue. Faith or devotion to God
 seems to be the cardinal virtue with the ancient moralist. “It is by
 faith that Agni is kindled, by faith offerings are made. We praise
 faith which occupies the top-most place. Gods, sacrificers and those
 who are protected by Vayu, long for faith from the heart of their
 hearts. Riches are acquired by faith. We solicit faith in the morning,
 at midday and again in the evening, when the sun sets. Oh faith, be
 you our guide in this world.” This is the invocation of the lady seer
 Sraddha Kamayani (S.83). The Munis were of great faith, their
 simple pastoral life moved round the pivot of faith or devotion. God
 was everything to them. “Only a devotee with upright behaviour is
 able to overcome his foemen. It is the worshipper of God who can
 subdue the ungodly adversaries. Only a competent warrior can make

                                   58
                                                      Moral Preparation



short work of his opponents in battle. A devout soul alone can enjoy
blissful fruit of worship, and not the one who worships not” (S.84).
We are reminded time and again, that devotion will do everything
for an aspirant. “Self-discipline helps in consecration; by consecra-
tion one rises up to great worthiness; by worthiness is conferred
faith; and faith leads up to truth”, assures the Rsi (5.85). “All saintly
men proclaim your loftiness. Their prosperity and their spiritual
progress speak of your grace; for, they owe that to you. Oh great
one, you are ever our friend, our father and mother, our very
protector. All of,
                 us should know this well” (5.86). The devotee has
to realise that God is the be-all and end-all, says the Mantra. The
only course open to man is submission to God and this is the highest
expression of devotion or Bhakti. Nimbarka, the great Vedantin, has
taken this Mantra as a Vedic authority for Saranagati or self-
surrender. Thus is devotion the facile princeps among virtues; it
crowns the moral preparation of a seeker.


     4. The Moral Standard. We now come to a very important
theoretical question in the science of morality — the question of
moral standard. Who gives us the moral Law? To whom should we
listen, so that we are within the approved orbit? Such queries receive
different answers. Sometimes we are told that Rta or the cosmic
principle is itself supreme. Thus instead of being only a metaphysi-
cal law, Rta comes to be recognised and respected as a metaphysico-
moral authority. The second answer which suggest itself from a
study of the Mantras, is that gods are supposed to be prescribing
and setting forth norms and man’s good lies in obeying them.
Finally, we also find utterances where neither the eternal law of Rta
 is taken as a moral standard nor are the commands of gods; and
 the function of prescribing moral laws is reserved for oneself. The
science of ethics terms these three alternatives as rule of Law, rule
 of God and rule of Self, respectively. The first two are heteronomous
 as the rule is external while the last is autonomous as the rule is
 internal. There is no elaborate discussion in the Vedas regarding the
 moral standard, but the suggestions in the Mantras are clear and
 imply all these theories. They are like raw diamonds waiting to be
 cut and polished before they send out the rays of theoretical wisdom.

                                    59
Pathway to God in the Vedas



     (a) Rule of Law. We shall briefly consider all the three theories
in the sequel. Coming to the rule of law, we find that there is an
eloquent   glorification   of Rta,   the   supreme   law,   and   copious
descriptions of what it brings about in different spheres. Cosmologi-
cally it has absolute sway in the world of nature so that by its rules
the trees remain green (S.87). Psychologically Rta offers great
support to an aspirant because, “he who does not leave the territory
of Rta, gets protection from Mitra, Aryama or Varuna”, assures the
sage Manu Vaivasvata (5.88). It also helps a devotee go beyond his
miseries. The exploits of Rta are no less significant in the field of
morals. “Your costumes are, as if, made of light; your behaviour is
spotless; no falsehood remains hidden from you, because, Oh Mitra
and Varuna, you walk by the highway of Rta” (5.89). “It is they who
take resort to Rta that can see through the crooked designs of the
enemy” (S.90). We are told again, “It is in accordance with the law
of Rta that the sun withdraws his fiery rays, spreads them out
regularly and proclaims his royal prestige to the world. The vast army
of the heartless enemies is done to death. So let us not be kept away
from companionship with Rta and gods. Let us attain liberation”
 (S.91). It is in the spiritual efforts that the good offices of Rta prove
 to be a great helping hand. “The path of the gods (Devayana) which
 is another name for Rta becomes a guide to him who wishes to rise
 above the world of pleasures and craves for the world of light. When
 he performs holy actions, the path of gods reveals itself to him in
 a flood of light” (5.92).
      (b) Rule of God. The majesty of laws prescribed by God is
 maintained a number of times. “No man, even if he had the strength
 of a hundred human beings, can out-live the statutes of God” (S.93).
 “The Brahmanas obtained the Brahma psalm for the sake of Indra. No
 wise person transgresses the rules of Indra, nor do any ofthe deities”
 (S.94). Though the rule of God is absolutely powerful, it is not stern.
 This subordination of sterner to gentler virtues is a noteworthy point
 of Vedic wisdom. Later-day ethical philosophy took this as a criterion
of moral progress. God is a monarch no doubt, but he is a merciful
patriarch. “Oh powerful God, your sovereignty is unlimited. Like light
you rule over all quarters. But your discipline does not hurt, just as
a soft cord round the neck of a calf does not hurt it” (S.95).

                                     60
                                                   Moral Preparation



     (c) Rule of Self. Hard or soft, an external rule is after all
external. There must be discipline, no doubt, but it should be self-
imposed and not other-imposed. The best expression of a moral law,
therefore, is the rule of the self. “Oh Varuna, loosen all the nooses
for us, may they be hard, soft or of a medium quality. We shall not
transgress your discipline and shall enjoy freedom” (S.96). However
lenient the rules   may be, they are taken as     restrictions when
prescribed by others. The sage does not want to throw away the rule
of law, but wants to make God’s law his own, so that in the very
act of observing the law, he is free. This is a very exalted status
which a devotee can reach; for, here God’s law has become his own
law; heteronomy has turned into autonomy. Autonomy is meaningful
only when the self has become God-like. There is another Mantra
which expresses this fact even more emphatically. “A man may
think about wealth and strive to acquire it by adhering to the course
of eternal moral law and by humble devotion to God. In this respect
he should consult his own conscience and in mind stick firmly to
his best judgment”, says Rsi Kavasa Ailtisa (S.97). Four points may
be considered here. (i) Eudaemonism is writ large on the face of
this verse. There is no unnecessary shunning from worldly benefits.
This testifies to the healthy-mindedness of the Vedic seers. They
wanted the good of both the worlds and they saw no contradiction
in it. (ii) However, they put forth a condition to be fulfilled. The
wealth is to be acquired in a just manner, (Rtasya patha). The
doctrine of purity of means is seen here. There is no insistence on
asceticism, we can acquire and enjoy wealth; but only the moral
method of acquiring it, entitles us to its enjoyment. (11) Now arises
the question as to who can determine the morality of means. Whom
are we to consult for ensuring the moral quality of the means? The
answer of the Vedic seer is crisp. He says: Consult your own
conscience (Svena kratuna samvadeta). You are yourself the guide.
Morality of an action is to be determined by no out-side agent. This
is autonomy pure and simple. No outside law, not even God’s law
imposes any restriction on us. We should conscientiously think and
outline our plan of action. (iv) The sage of this Mantra possesses
so sound a judgment that one can not be too smart for him.
Apparently the freedom given to ourselves to determine the morality

                                 61
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of an action seems dangerous. I can acquire wealth by deceitful
means and declare that my conscience had sanctioned those ways
as just. And when I am given freedom to choose my own instrument
by consulting my own conscience, no one can question the propriety
thereof. Again, man for whom the line of pleasure is more easy and
more attractive, is likely to choose a wrong course and still go scot-
free because he is his own law. The ancient moralist knew this
psychology and also knew the ethical side of the question of
determining the morality of an action. He now says: It is alright that
you yourself decide the means; but take care that this ‘you’ is “Philip
the sober’ and not ‘Philip the drunk’. Passions lead you away from
your spiritual good. Hence that mind and that mind alone should be
 your guide which always points to your ultimate end (Sreyamsam
 daksam manasa jagrbhyat). This aspect of mind is not earthly; it is
 the divinity in man. Thus our moral guide is neither man, nor God,
 but God in man. You have to see Him within as Kant saw the
 supreme moral law within himself. The divine aspect of man was
 termed by Kant as the Kingdom of Ends. He stated that man is a
 law maker so far as he is a member of the Kingdom of Ends, and
 is a Subject as   a member of ordinary human kingdom. Thus the
 Kantian concepts of the Kingdom of Ends and autonomy of moral
 law, are found pre-fabricated in the Mantra under consideration. The
 concepts   were   fully   developed,    neatly   worded   and   carefully
 preserved for posterity in the time capsule of Vedic tradition.
      Now a question of some theoretical interest crops up. Does
 moral preparation have a pulling force so that god has no alternative
 but to bless the devotee? And if so, does it not seriously limit God’s
 freedom? Well, Leibnitz was faced with the same problem. For him
 the present world is the best possible world. At the same time God
 is free. Now if we say that the quality of the world compelled God
 to choose it, God’s freedom is nullified. And if we say that God
 chose this world without any regard to its good quality, it harms the
 Law of Sufficient Reason, for nothing can take place unless there
 is a sufficient reason for it. To preserve both the sanctity of the Law
 of Sufficient Reason and God’s freedom, Leibnitz said that God
 could have chosen any world from the innumerable possible worlds;
 but the present world, being the best one, only provided a Sufficient

                                    62
                                                                               Moral Preparation



Reason for being chosen and did not compel him to choose it. It just
provided the ground. We do not know whether such a                                     solution is
really a solution. But it must be stated that we have to employ some
such ‘solution’ to solve the problem of moral worth and God’s grace.
God may shower grace when and where He chooses; we can not
compel Him to do so. But our moral development completes the
preliminaries, so that in case He chooses to come, we are prepared
for His visit. If not a compelling force, our fitness should’ serve as
an attractive force. It should give us strength to watch and pray for
grace. And this grace-worthiness is what ‘the wisdom of God
discerns, His goodness chooses and His power effects.’

41.   उत वा यो नो मर्चयादनागसो अरातीवा मर्तः सानुको वृकः।
      वृहस्पते अप तं वर्तया पथः सुगं नो अस्यै देववीतये कृधि ।।

      त्रातारं त्वा तनूनां हवामहे अवस्पर्तरधि वक्तारमस्मयुम।्‌
