                                                   71
                              CHAPTER III




                   The Problem of God


       The inarticulate cry of the new born baby sounds like the
articulate words कोऽहम्‌ (who am 1) and Ramadiasa, the great
Maharashtra saint, comments that the baby, as soon as it enters
into the world, asks the question, who am I? Ramadasa further says
that while in the womb the baby was saying ‘Soham Soham’ (I
am He, I am He), but the identity is lost as soon as the contact
with the external world begins (Dasabodha III.1.47). Consequently
man is troubled by questions like ‘Is the world real?’, ‘Who am
1? or ‘What is the ultimate Reality?’ It is these questions which
are the fundamental problems of metaphysics.

       We shall begin by noting three approaches to Reality — the
psychological, the cosmological and the theological, the last being
our main concern. We shall then tur to the proofs of the existence
of God. Coming to the epistemology of God-consciousness, we
shall discuss the view that it is impossible to know God with the
help    of   reason.   Then    follows   the   survey    of   metaphysical
characteristics of God where we shall see         how roots of later day
characterisation of God as Sat, Cit and Ananda are present here.
Spatial and numerical properties have no place in the consideration
of God. In our stride we shall notice tendencies like immanence,
immano-transcendence, polytheism, monotheism and monism.

       1. Three Approaches to Reality. For our purpose,                 the
enquiries regarding the self and the world are subservient to the
enquiry into the problem of God. Therefore, the first two enquiries
can be treated as preliminaries to the central theme, viz. God.
       (a)   The   Psychological   Approach.    The     Vedic   Rsis   were
conscious that an inner vital principle is the motive force of all
actions of man. This, we may call, the psychological approach to
the problem of reality. The Rsi prays that this motive force be pure
                                                         The Problem of God



and auspicious. However, the enigma surrounding this principle still
persists as it is referred to as a mysterious being (Yaksa). “May
that inner mysterious being, by which the wise, well-versed in the
science of sacrifice execute all their tasks, resolve on what is
auspicious,” says the poem (S.1). It was realised that the e/an vital
is distinct from the body; yet it resides in the body and functions
in consonance with it. “That principle which is living and moving
and which breathes, is interred in the body. The body, though inert
(Mrta), moves the living principle and both of them stay together”
(S.2). It sounds contradictory to read that the inert moves the living.
Contrasted with the living, throbbing vital principle (Jiva), the body
is referred to as inert. Now it is well known that the Jiva moves
the body and not vice versa. But in the first place the Jiva is not
visible from outside and its function of moving remains hidden.
Secondly, it can not disregard the laws of the inert body that is
to be moved. As such the body seems to move according to its
own laws and appears to be the moving force of the inner spiritual
principle which is the real mover.
     (b) The Cosmological Approach. Parallel to the enquiry into
the mainspring of action in the microcosm, is the enquiry into the
similar   source   of   impulse   in    the   macrocosm.        This   is   016
cosmological approach to the problem of reality. “Tell me what the
support of the universe is. In it austerities reach their consumma-
tion; in it are balanced the moral law and faith, the waters and
Brahman” (S.3). Obviously that which balances and stabilises the
physical entities like water, the psychological forces like faith, the
moral principle namely Rta and the vast cosmic reality or Brahman
is the fundamental reality. “Nothing can outdo the great Indra in
whom are concentrated all powers. He sustains Soma in                       his
stomach, prowess in his body, thunder-bolt in his hand and divine
knowledge in his head” (S.4). “Who is the support of the universe
to whom gods offer worship with their hands and feet, word, ear
and eyes?” (S.5). The thirst to know the ultimate reality is
unquenchable. “Who has been a witness to the modus operandi in
which the boneless soul, the first born end life urge of the earth,
came to inhabit this bony tenement? Who is he who, not finding
answers    himself,     has   approached      a   wise    man    to    receive

                                   73
Pathway to God in the Vedas



instruction?”, asks Rsi Dirghatamas Aucathya (S.6). The verse
epitomises the fundamental problem of all philosophy, namely, to
know how the one Reality finds expression in the multiplicity of
the world. The answer suggested is that this can be known only
with che help of a spiritual teacher.

     (c) The Theological Approach. The psychological and the
cosmological approaches gradually lead to the theological approach.
Anaximander, the ancient Greek philosopher, had already ascribed
to the highest cosmic principle all the qualities that are necessary
to the world-ground. The Infinite, he said, was imperishable and
 inexhaustible. Windelband points out that still another dimension to
 this concept was added by Anaximander when he called this Infinite
 or Apeiron To Theion or the God. Anaximander’s conception was
 deemed to be the first philosophic conception of God. But it must
 be pointed out that much earlier than Anaximander, the Vedic seer
 had identified this infinite power with God and had exhibited a
 remarkable maturity of thought. In the eighteenth century Kant
 stressed the tendency of the human mind to consider that highest
 Being as God. He said further that in order to justify this tendency,
 reason devises arguments for the existence of God.

      2. Arguments for the Existence of God. In Western thought
 the arguments for the existence of God, given by Anselm and
 Descartes, occupy a significant position, though not as significant
 as the devastating criticism which Kant levelled against them. They
 are roughly summarised into three ways of arriving at God’s
 existence. The physico-theological proof argues from the order or
 design in the world to the author of that order. The cosmological or
 causal argument starts from the fact of the world and then takes us
 back to its cause which is God. The third — which Kant takes as lying
 at the back of the first (४०-15 the ontological argument which
 analyses the idea of God and concludes that existence must be
 included in the idea of such a perfect being. Kant dubbed all of them
 as too intellectualistic and substituted his own argument which later
 on came to be known as the moral argument. The Vedas have spelt
 out at least three of the arguments; only the ontological argument
 can not be specifically pointed out.


                                  74
                                                       The Problem of God



       (a) The Physico-theological Argument. Vedic people are fond
of describing the rule of the supreme law (Rta) in the universe. This
law works under gods, who are the guardians of the cosmic order
(Gopa Rtasya). The order is exhibited in the regularity of natural
occurrences like day and night. As commanders of the natural forces
gods   become      sovereign rulers    in   the   cosmic sphere.   Such   a
description of gods comes under the purview of the physico-
theological argument. “The universe in the beginning was thoroughly
nurtured with divine waters by VisSvakarman. He then went on
moulding infinite forms. It is known that He first bestowed divinity
on the    mortal   man” (S.7). The words used           in   the hymn are
ViSvakarman and Tvasta. They suggest an architect or designer and
not the creator. This verse gives the physico-theological argument
in its pure form. But the human mind is not satisfied by calling God
a designer who is just a moulder of some material provided to him
by an external agency. He must be powerful enough to create the
material also. And thus the cosmological argument is ushered in.
       (b) The Cosmological Argument. It is noteworthy that some-
times Time or some such agency is mentioned as a cause of the
universe. “Time is the creator of heaven and earth. The past, the
present and the future are supported by him. Time has created the
world and things are visible only because he has created the sun.
The mind and the vital breath are his creations, so too are the infinite
names and forms. People revel in the idea that Time will bring them
good tidings. Time gave rise to Tapas and Brahman as well as the
Vedas. He is God incarnate and father even of Prajapati. The world
owes its existence and stability to the will of Time. By becoming
Brahman he sustains the highest. Time created all the creatures,
Prajapati, as also sun and light” (5.8). Another poem describes how
time runs the world-machine. Time is the mover but the wise who
are not carried along with the stream, control it. “The horse (time)
is running with its seven reins (SaptaraSmi). He is ever youthful. But
the wise ride him. All things in the universe are its wheels”, exclaims
the sage Bhrgu (S.9). We may observe that Time as described here,
is a counterpart of flux in the philosophy of Heracleitus. Change,
movement or transformation constitute reality for Heracleitus.
Gaudapada has used a very apt expression, Kalacintakah for those

                                      75
Pathway to God in the Vedas



who hold Time to be supreme (Mandtkya Karika, 1.8). One more
view-point can be considered here. Some of the Vedic hymns are
worded as if they preach naturalism; we may call this pseudo-
naturalism. In this connection we may consider two sets of psalms,
the import of which is of great metaphysical significance.

      The first set consists of the two Mantras from the Rgveda. “The
 Law and Truth were born of arduous penance. Then was born the
 night and then the sea. From the sea was afterwards produced the
 year which controlled every thing and which regulated day and
 night” (S.10). If we go by the apparent meaning of these Mantras,
 we may conclude that out of penance was born one element after
 another without any one, in particular, creating the world. Such a
 view is naturalism which says that the world was born of itself. But
 Tapas, night, sea and so on from which the world is supposed to be
 born, should rightly be construed as only different names given to
  the one Reality. Vedic cosmogony (like Greek cosmogony at a later
  period) finds the origin of the world in some single element. But they
  are all meant to indicate the primal reality. In the very next stanza
  we read : “Dhata in the beginning created the sun and the moon, the
  heaven, the earth, the space in between as well as the happy sky”
  (S.11). Here the shift from naturalistic creation to personalistic
  creation is clear. The word Tapas (austerities) in the preceding
  Mantra is significant. We may argue in the Cartesian fashion that
  austerities imply some one who practises austerities. Now, Sayana
  has guided us as to who this practitioner can be. He says that
   austerities are a plan of creation and that they were observed in order
   to create the world in olden times by Brahman. ब्रह्मणा पुरा सृष्ट्यर्थं
  कृतात्‌ तपसः तपश्च स्रष्टव्य पर्या लोचनलक्षणम्|‌ Further a Mantra says: “The
   mighty waters carried a germ which produced fire. Then was born
   the one breath of the 2०५५..." (5.12). This also needs to be
   understood in a personalistic fashion.

        The other set of poems at once confronts us with an idea of
   creation ex nihilo. “In the early age of gods, existence came from
   non-existence. Brahmanaspati worked on it like a smith. In the early
   age of gods, non-existence gave birth to existence. Then came the
   quarters and then the up-going trees. From the upward-moving trees

                                       16
                                                       The Problem of God



came out the earth, and from the earth, the quarters. From Aditi was
born Daksa and from Daksa, Aditi” (S.13). The description, in
addition to its naturalistic overtones, states clearly that existence
came out of non-existence. This appears to be a contradiction till
we understand the real import of the Rk. The words सत्‌ and असत्‌
are not used in the Vedas or in the Upanisads with a well-defined
connotation. Sometimes Sat is understood as the tangible, manifest,
sensible world whereas Asat means the entity which is intangible,
unmanifest and ungraspable by the senses. At other times, Sat means
that which exists eternally or the permanent, unchanging reality and
Asat means the transitory or changing world of names and forms.
In the Mantra in question, the first interpretation is obviously
relevant and the verse implies that from the unmanifest Brahman
(Asat), has all this manifest world (Sat) sprung. Sayana in his
commentary says: तेषां उपादानकारणातू असत: नामरूपवर्जि तत्वेन aA|
समानात्‌ब्रह्मणः सकाशात्‌सत्‌नामरूपवि शि ष्टं देवादि कं अजायत (The unmanifest
which was devoid of names and forms and was, therefore, regarded
as non-existence, served as the material cause; from it came out the
world qualified by names and forms). Along with the idea of ex nihilo
creation, disappears the idea of naturalism. We may only remark that
these Mantras represent early stages before the idea of personalistic
creation came to be mooted.

      Coming to the cosmological argument proper, we find that all
grandeur is traced back to God. “Man is struck with awe and wonder
by seeing only two footsteps of this God living in the heavens. No
one can summon up courage to behold his third step. Even those who
have an access to heavens, can not know Him” (S.14). “Ucchista
is the great grand father, the creator of all. He is the ruler of the
greatest one on the earth” (S.15). “Great are those gods who are born
of nature (Asaf). This nature is but one aspect of Skambha whom
the wise call the unmanifest (Asat)” (S.16). The two meanings of
Asat referred to earlier, become clear here. Nature or Prakrti is Asat;
it is not ultimately real. But the wise knowers do not call it Asat;
for them Asat means the supersensible, subtle essence of which
nature forms but a part. There are also verses which speak of a
personal God or creator. “Oh Soma, it is from your divine seed that

                                     77
Pathway to God in the Vedas


all the worlds are born. You rule over all the regions and control
them. You are the first one who sustains creation” (S.17). This
Mantra attributes to God the function of sustaining the world. This
is again seen in another passage. To a query as to what the material
cause of the universe is, the answer given is that God is both the
creator and sustainer. “Which is that forest where grew the tree from
which were created the heaven and the earth? People praise the God
who created day and night. God’s power is not limited to the creation
of heaven and earth. They are also sustained by him. He was born
even before the sun” (S.18). This is verily the view of Descartes who
said that God does not only create the universe but also sustains it
at every moment of its existence. Now Taittiriya Brahmana 11.8.9
says that Brahman is the forest, Brahman the tree from which were
formed heaven and earth. Here lies the origin of Ramanuja’s doctrine
 which combines the material cause (Upadana karana) and the
 efficient cause (Nimitta karana) into one. This view holds that God
 is both these causes.' Apart from the different specifications of the
 cause like material or efficient, which are a later-day development,
 Brahman or the One reality, is thought to be the only creator
 whatever name one pleases to attribute to it. “The first cause of the
 universe, Brahman, is itself fire, itself sun, itself air, itself moon,
 itself the bright one, itself water and Prajapati too” (S.19). Or again,
 “Vratya (the highest God) was in a state of motion and gave an
 impulse to Prajapati who saw gold in himself and further created the
 world. It became one; it became ornamental; it became great; it
 became the elder; it became the Brahman; it became the austerities;
 it became the truth; all came out of him. He began to grow, he became
 the great one, he became the great God” (S.20).
       Talking of motion or the first impulse, we are reminded of the
 famous Aristotelian concept of the mover and the moved. It leads
 us to the unmoved Mover or the highest being. “The ultimate cause
 of this motion can only lie in an unmoved; for if all motion is due
 to the action of a mover on the moved and presupposes a mover
 different from the moved, the latter presupposes in its tarn a mover

 ] २.7. Ranade, Vedanta the Culmination of Indian Thought, Bharatiya Vidya
    Bhavan, Bombay 400 007, 1970, P.97

                                    78
                                                       The Problem of God



different from itself...   Only the incorporeal       is   changeless and
unmoved; and as form is perfect being and matter imperfect, the first
mover must be absolutely perfect, that in which the scale of being
reaches its highest point... the first mover, can only be one, the final
cause itself. But pure incorporeal being can only be mind or thought.
The ultimate basis of all motion lies, therefore, in the deity as the
pure, perfect and inexhaustible mind.”' Thus does Aristotle argue
from the world to the deity; and in this, we come across a            clear,
logical and systematic cosmological argument for the existence of
God. The Vedic sage had anticipated this unmoved Mover.

     (c) The Ontological Argument. The ontological argument has
a place when thought (चि त्‌) and existence (सत्‌) are taken as two
independent entities. But when one starts with their unity, the
ontological argument is just superfluous. “The ontological argu-
ment
  — the argument from thought to being - when relieved of its
imperfect syllogistic and, therefore, analytic form is simply the
expression of that highest unity of thought and being which all
knowledge presupposes as its beginning and seeks as its end.
Idealism, in the sense that all things and beings constitute a system
of relations which finds its unity in mind, that every intelligence
contains in it the form of the universe, and that, therefore, all
knowledge is but the discovery of that which is already our own —
the awakening of a self-consciousness, which involves at the same
time a consciousness of God — this Idealism is the real meaning of
the ontological argument, and the only meaning in which it is
defensible.”? We find in the Vedas many Mantras where we find
such identification of thought and existence. “That God who was
born in the beginning, pervades the universe both from outside and
from inside. He who is born in the past will be born in the future.
He stands face to face with every thing” (S.21). The monistic
spiritualism in this utterance conceals an ontological argument —
nay, it is not an argument at all; it is but a plain way of giving


 1E. Zeller, Outlines of the History of Greek Philosophy, Kegan Paul, Trench,
  Trubner and Co. Ltd., London, 1931, P. 177
2 E. Caird, The Critical Philosophy of Kant, Vol. II, P. 119


                                     79
Pathway to God in the Vedas



expression to his conviction that the highest spiritual principle exists
in every place and at all times. The phrase Wa जनास्ति            ष्ठति   सर्वतोमुखः
(stands face to face with everything) is particularly noteworthy as
it re-doubles the faith in the unity of thought and being. Similarly,
“Having swiftly traversed the heaven and earth, as well as all the
worlds and quarters, and thereafter piercing through the screen
formed by the widespread threads of the eternal order (Rta), he saw
That and became That as he was verily That already” (S.22). We
have another important couplet where we get definite identification
of thought and being; we are told that whatever possess a soul
(Atmanvat) exists in that great support, Skambha. “The heavens and
the earth as created by the Skambha exist. Whatever possesses life,
whatever winks, all that possesses a soul and exists in the Skambha”
(S.23). The most important verse in this connection is found in the
Nasadiya Sukta where a living, throbbing being is described as all
that was existing. “There was no death nor was there anything that
was indestructible. There was no night and no day. All that existed
was something that was living and throbbing by itself” (S.24). Thus
this kind of ontologism is taught in the Vedas.

        (d) The Moral Argument. The Vedic seers wanted to construct
no rational theology and offer no speculative                ‘proofs’      for the
existence of God. They had a deep-seated faith in Him which needed
no proof. However as morality flows from God, it strengthens — does
 not create
          — the faith in the ultimate reality of God. The Rsi starts
from the fact that God is the source of morality. In that case, arguing
 from the incidence of morality to the existence of God would be only
petitio principii. Still the invariable connection between morality and
divinity fortifies the belief in the deity, wherever moral power is seen
working. “An honest theologian... will realise that the existence of
moral Deity can never be inferred with certainty from nature’s
structure and behaviour and that morality, not science, must be
counted on to justify his faith in God and a cosmic moral order.’”!
The moral proof for God is, therefore, not a logical proof, but only
an expression of the certitude of faith. There is ample evidence in

 1   T.M. Greene, (Ed), Kant, Selections, Charles Scrubner’s Sons, New York, 1957,
     P. LXIX



                                        80
                                                   The Problem of God



the Vedas that the seers had a recourse to a line of thinking which
amounts to a moral proof prevalent in the West. “Varuna looks on
all impartially; but he also looks on people differentially. He stays
in the same region as others; yet he stays in a special region. Varuna
has contact with gods as also with men” (S.25). The purport is clear.
Though Varuna treats all men without taking sides, it does not mean
that he will be equally gracious to a saint and to a sinner. He will
help the former, but punish the latter and will thus discriminate
between them. Though he enjoys no special status as a deity, he
certainly commands it as a moral overseer. He deals with gods who
are exemplars of moral laws as well as with men whom he has to
correct when they go wrong. Varuna seems to be the sovereign king
and as such, he has to take care that no discord disturbs the running
of the universe. Hence he has to see, like an administrator of law
and order, that the divine laws are properly observed by men and
proper arrangements are maintained in the society. Thus keeping
contact with both gods and men, means bringing divine laws to earth.
      Rewards and punishments are important ingredients of a neat
and tidy administration and God as moral governor has to dispense
them equitably. “Oh Adityas, you know best how to wipe away our
faults. Cover us with your benedictions as birds cover their young
ones. Your favours are incomparable and indestructible (अनेहसः व
ऊतयः), your aids are wonderful indeed (सुऊतयः व ऊतय:),” says the
sage Trita Aptya (S.26). “When all the five groups in the society
concentrate on Indra’s worship and sing in his honour, Indra instantly
makes short work oftheir adversaries and saves their honour” (S.27).
      A peculiar characteristic of Vedic philosophising is a combi-
nation of physico-theological and moral proofs. We shall grossly
underestimate the nature of the principle of Rta, if we call it merely
a cosmic order; it is at the same time a moral one. No law can attain
such a high status if it is only a cosmic law, and not a moral law
too. Consequently he who controls Rta is both a cosmic and moral
governor. We find in the Vedas that some deities are often rhymed
together. Mitra and Varuna is one pair. Indra and Varuna is another.
Here is the division of functions that are allotted to Indra and Varuna
to uphold physical and moral well-being in the world. “Indra destroys

                                  81
Pathway to God in the Vedas



all enemies in battles and Varuna is ever engaged in implementing
the holy rules and in maintaining discipline in society. We offer our
prayers to you, Oh Indra and Varuna. Kindly give us your protection”
(S.28). The Adityas are assigned another function which is more
important than maintaining regularity in the external world. “Those
mighty Adityas, profound and invincible, perceive through many
eyes. They penetrate into the hearts of men (अन्तः पश्यन्ति   ) and find
out their good or evil propensities” (S.29). Now Varuna is the moral
imperantis and is responsible for both physical and moral standard.
If, at his command, rivers flow into the ocean without overfilling it
the least sin can not escape him (S.30). He is one of the Adityas,
the guardians of justice, and is described as having thousand eyes
(5.31). Rsi Brahma reiterates the same thing: “He who crosses the
region beyond the heaven, does not yet escape the secret servicemen
of Varuna who inspect the region with their thousand eyes” (S.32).
He not only judges proper and improper acts, but also executes
justice. That is why his weapon is a noose (PaSa), and he is called
28520121. We read elsewhere: “The mighty ruler of the world
beholds, as though from close at hand, the man who thinks that he
 acts by stealth. All this is seen by the lord. If a man moves in secret,
or if two men whisper when they sit together, king Varuna knows
 it for he is present there as the third” (S.33).              4

      Incidentally, it is a fact worth noting that even in Vedic times,
 a necessity of organising an Intelligence Bureau was felt. There are
 hymns which definitely indicate the existence of this agency.
 “Varuna wears a golden armour and covers his body with strong
garments. Round about him are seated his spies or secret service-
men” (5.34). “All the gods gladly and of their own accord, offered
 their might and help to you, Oh Mitra and Varuna, while exercising
sovereignty over heaven and earth. Your spies are unobstructed and
unbewildered” (S.35). There was a battalion of spies working under
officers. “The vigilant.spies of Varuna go on their inspection duty
throughout heaven and earth, which are so excellently fashioned.
Men performing religious rites, those with great wisdom and those
engaged in sacrificial duties, all offer homage to Varuna” (S.36). The
Vedic word for a spy is स्पश. It comes from the root स्पश्‌ which

                                   82
                                                   The Problem of God



means: to obstruct, to undertake, to perform, to string together or to
touch. This sums up the (mis) philosophy of the science of espionage.
A spy obstructs or frustrates counter intelligence activities, i.e. he
prevents the foreigners    from discovering his     own   secrets;   he
undertakes or performs the duty of collecting intelligence; he collates
or strings together or evaluates the collected information and, last
but not the least, he touches i.e. he acts as a contact-man. The
vigilance of the spies is proverbial. “These spies of gods who move
here do not loiter or do not wink; O wanton, go quickly with some
one other than me; with him go forward like the two wheels of a
chariot” (S.37). This command to follow a person wherever he goes,
is termed “shadowing” in intelligence parlance. We read a beautiful
description of an officer who is very serious about his duties. The
sage Ajigarti Sunahgepa observes: “Varuna the mighty emperor,
vowed to the implementation of the holy regulation (धृतव्रतः), takes
his seat as a sovereign with a view to governing his subjects.
Perceiving from that place, he surveys all the wonderful deeds that
happen now or may happen in future” (S.38). This clearly indicates
that he even anticipates the future moves of the enemy. Most efficient
espionage! To realise that an intelligence wing is necessary to
maintain social solidarity is a mark of practical wisdom of our
forefathers.

     If from cosmic order, we go to the cosmological argument, and
from moral order to the moral argument, a combination in God of
both cosmic and moral functions will give us a physico-ethico-
theological argument. This is indeed a significant contribution of the
Vedas to speculative theology at the dawn of thinking. All this does
not mean that we get here intellectual acrobatics to prove the
existence of God. For them God is a fact, a simple, experienced
actuality and one need not present proofs for what one directly sees.
Their vision of God was so vivid that there was not an iota of
uncertainty about God’s existence. And their motive in describing
their God-visions was so pure that we can never express any doubt
regarding the veracity of these experiences. On the contrary, what
they have stated about the spiritual or religious experiences, about
different modes of divine visions, has become the standard of truth

                                  83
Pathway to God in the Vedas



for the philosophers of futurity. The classical Indian philosophy,
while discussing the criteria of truth has enumerated Sabda Pramana
or testimony as one of the criteria. It is recognised on all hands that
the testimony to be acceptable must come from a source which is
honest and unvitiated. Now, the supreme testimony           for Indian
thought always remains the Vedas and their off-shoot, the Upanisads.
And this speaks volumes for the unsullied, immaculate and pure
character of the utterances of the Rsis of dateless antiquity. Their
authenticity lies in the direct contact which the seers established with
divinity. They saw God in their ecstatic state and this intuitive
experience or Atindriya Pratyaksa set the standard of truth. Thus we
may remark that what was pratyaksa Pramana (direct vision) for the
Rsis became the Sabda Pramana (testimony) for later thinkers.

     3. Epistemology of God-consciousness. The most natural
attitude to understand a datum of experience is displayed by the
Rsis when they try to apprehend God. Every time, however, they
have drawn a blank and come to the inevitable conclusion that God
is unknowable. God is beyond the jurisdiction of the familiar, the
knowable or the commonplace and the intellect can not reach even
the outer limits, not to speak of fathoming the inner depths of the
nature of God. Our knowledge has incurable limits and it can not
jump out of itself to grasp what is essentially ungraspable. Our
intellect is a mute witness to its own impotent character and gives
in by confessing that the task of knowing the ultimate reality, is
something which strikes it dumb. “No one can know you, Oh Visnu,
whose body grows without limit. We can scan only the earth and
the mid-region. But you know the regions that spread beyond them.
Neither the ancient ones nor those of today, know you.             You
established the heaven in the mid-region and supported the eastern
horizon of the earth” observes the sage (S.39). “Who has, among
the elders, known your might which is without any bounds? You
have, from your body created the heaven and the earth” (S.40). The
same attitude of owning ignorance is found in Rsi Rebha Kasyapa
who says: “When the immortal gods can not carry their knowing
faculty anywhere near you, how can the mere mortals aspire to
collect you? You have, by your mighty power, covered the whole

                                   84              रर
                                                  The Problem of God


universe. That is why gods remain ignorant of your nature” (S.41).
Man’s reason comes nowhere near God and it is to exemplify this
same inability that the Saint of Umadi said: ““Who has ever reached
the end?” We have innumerable Mantras in the Vedas which bear
witness to the agnostic tendency in the seers. God is so mighty and
man so weak, He is such a great lord and man so poor a creature
that    it would be sheer audacity on the part of the latter to
comprehend the former. Agnosticism, therefore, is the only correct
attitude a man can adopt. “He, whom the gods, deities, men and
waters could not know, has enveloped the earth by his might. May
He, along with the Maruts, guard us” (S.42). “Some say that he
was born of the sun. I think he was produced by the highest power.
He came from anger, contend some. But only Indra knows from
where he came” (S.43).


       One very famous Sikta from the Rgveda to illustrate the
agnostic approach is the Nasadiya Sikta, “Who can elaborate on
the details of creation? Who knows it for certain? Gods came after
the creation; who can understand the nature of creation? That from
which the world originated, whether it was created or not created,
is known only to the presiding principle which resides in the highest
heavens; or perhaps not known even to him” (S.44). Lokamanya
Tilak’s comments are very illuminating. “The Rsi of this Sukta has
intuitively grasped that behind all the multiplicity of names and
forms there stands the one eternal being. The Rsi does not want to
argue with those who say that in the beginning there was Asat or
Sat or death or deathlessness and so on, because these are
relativistic and dualistic concepts; and discursive language was
evolved only after creation. When there was no differentiating
principle whatsoever, no name could name the one pulsating being,
nor could any thing cover it. What a bold assertion! Because it was
vibrating, the primeval reality was not dead matter but conscious
spirit. It is further pointed out that Brahman and Maya, darkness
and waters, are all descriptions of Brahman which later manifested
itself through austerities. The original being is greater than
everything and different from all created things. However, to
describe it and to describe creation, we have to use the language


                                 85
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of duality and use the anthropomorphic concepts like mind, desire
and so on. We can only say that Brahman desired, ‘Let there be
creation’ and there was creation. The monistic rigour of this Sikta
is remarkable. Though it is not possible to grasp with the help of
intellect the nature of creation, the Rsi does not bring in another
principle like Prakrti but sticks to his guns that Brahman alone was
there at the beginning. Even gods who are a later creation could not
know it; only Hiranyagarbha may perhaps know it, admits the seer.
But the very next moment he says that one could not be sure that
even Hiranyagarbha knew the nature of creation. After all, he is also
one of the created beings and may not know the nature of the highest
Brahman which is all-transcending. The Rsi expresses his inability
to spell out the nature of Brahman and the nature of creation, but
does not stir from his original monistic stand. Philosophy has not
yet gone beyond the Rsi’s intuitive grasp of Reality.”’ Thus the
ultimate reality from the epistemological view-point remains the
 “Wholly Other’.

       4. The Metaphysical Characteristics of God. Metaphysics is
 a science which tries to determine the general nature of Reality.
 Characterisation of Reality as Sat-Cit-Ananda, as Holy, as Absolute,
 as Nothingness or as Individual, are different ways of describing it.
 However, the view that Reality is Sat-Cit-Ananda has crystallised
 into the lore of Indian philosophy. Protocol, therefore, requires that
 we touch this concept in dealing with the Vedic view of Reality, at
 the same time bearing in mind that roots of other views may also
 be found in these time-honoured Texts. The Vedic Mantras refuse
 to be bound by a rigorous logical frame-work, as they are expressions
 of direct vision of Truth. They may appear to be logically loose at
 times. To insist on the tying of loose ends or chiselling a monolithic
 systematic whole ‘without any handles’, is to arrest the living current
 of God-experience which flows freely and is fed freshly. However
 neatly designed the rules of dialectics may be, the flood of spiritual
 experience is sure to wash them away. The Vedic Rsis have recorded
 their experience and have not philosophised in the strict sense of the

 1   Gitd-rahasya (7 edition), Published by J.S. Tilak, 568 Narayan Peth, Pune,
     411030, 1956, Pp. 241-245


                                       86
                                                  The Problem of God



term. We may well try to organise and portray them methodically for
the sake of understanding them, but we must always bear in mind
that a closed system is alien to the original Mantras and is only our
way of presenting them.

     (a) Existence, Consciousness, Bliss. God is said to be existing
before anything came into being. “Prior to the creation of the world,
Hiranyagarbha existed. He is the controller of the created world. He
holds the earth and the heavens. We offer oblation to that one (000!
(S.45). “People respect Hiranyagarbha as the Highest. The supreme
being, at the beginning, poured lustre into Hiranyagarbha” (S.46).
The implication that the omnipresent is the source of lustre of
Hiranyagarbha is clear. “Salutations to that great Brahman which is
the glory of heaven and which sustains all — past, present and future”
(S.47). The same ubiquitous existence is referred to in the famous
Mantra since taken by the Isa Upanisad. “Whatever resides in the
universe is pervaded through and through by God” (S.48). He who
realises the highest principle to be living in all beings, rises above
his propensities. “A sage visualises all things in the supreme. For
him who grasps the unity of all, how can grief or infatuation exist?”
(S.49). The eternal principle is often referred to as existence. “In
nature is lodged the sovereign principle which supports all beings
that are created” (S.50). Or, “That which abides in nature, people
regard as the Highest, and worship it” (5.51). By far the most
important poem in this connection, is where Indra is said to be Satya;
this can be interpreted either as the highest Truth or ultimate Being.
His all-comprehensiveness, power, sovereignty or glory are inde-
scribable. “Enhanced thousand times in valour by the power of
intense devotion of the seers and saints, he (Indra) has become as
vast as ocean. He is Truth incarnate. Hence I sing his glory and
lordship in the assembly of saints and devotees, on occasions of
worship” (S.52).

      There is no dearth of verses which stress the aspect of
consciousness in the highest reality. There is incessant activity;
because, such is the very nature of the immortal principle. By its own
authority it also makes the mortal to move. “The immortal one,
associated with the mortal, moves back and forth bythe force of its

                                  87
Pathway to God in the Vedas



inherent might. The two, however, move in opposite directions and
therefore people can visualise the one while the other remains hidden
from them” (S.53). Regarding the association of the mortal with the
immortal, we read: “I myself have visualised the embodied soul,
coming and going in its own unerring style. By its competence of
conjunction and disjunction it keeps coming again and again to the
mundane region” (S.54). Conjunction and disjunction obviously
indicate the combination of the deathless soul with the earthly body
and its separation from it. Of these two, the immortal, moving, active
part is the real controller and is thus the ruler of the body. “Our
humble salutations to Prana, the life-spirit which controls the whole
of the universe. Prana is the lord of all beings; in Prana everything
is situated” (S.55). A mystic talks of the undifferentiated state of the
Atman at the beginning which, by and by, assumed articulated forms.
“In the beginning the Atman stood without a foot, engrossed in its
own suzerainty. He then developed four feet and began to enjoy every
thing” (S.56). Other qualities which are ascribed to the highest being,
speak of its life-giving nature. “May that mind which is knowledge,
 and consciousness and which is the enlivening flame in the created
beings, that without which nothing takes place, resolve on what is
 good” (S.57). Mind here refers to the life-force in man. The one ever
 present conscious Being is domiciled in all its manifestations and is
 their steady foundation. “Oh Indra, your joy is for other peoples’
 profit; so are your energies. You are the bestower of power by which
 you sustain the gods” (5.58). “That which moves, falls or remains
 stable, that which controls the life-breath by releasing and holding
 it, that which winks or that which balances the earth, is One and only
 One” (5.59).


      Coming to Ananda, we find in the Vedas innumerable metrical
 hymns where God, referred to variously as Atman, Visnu, Agni,
 Soma or Indra, is declared to be blissful. “I offer my prayer to Visnu
 whose all three imperishable abodes are flooded with bliss. Alone
 he upholds the three-fold universe including heaven and earth”
 (S.60). “May 1 attain to that region so dear to him, where the ardent
 devotees dwell and experience the ecstatic joy. In that supreme
 realm of Visnu, the great strider, we have an ever bubbling fountain

                                   88
                                                           The Problem of God



of contentment. This is why Visnu is the nearest of kin to us” (S.61).
In another verse the self figures as partaking of the satisfying
immortal morsel of serene life. “Whereon (on the cosmic tree) those
fair-winged birds are ceaselessly hymning and struggling for a
portion of the everlasting life, the mighty guardian of the universe
is there above. He, the wise entered into myself ” (5.62). “He, Agni,
is like the divine sun. He discerns the truth and covers his devotees
with his patronage throughout their struggles. He is the truth in its
real form and is blissful like the self which is the sole object of
contemplation and realisation” (S.63). Two points may be noted
regarding this Rk: (i) Agni is said to be blissful like the Atman.
Sayana     has    given    an   explanation.       आत्मा   इव   परमप्रेमास्पदतया
नि रति शय-आनंदस्वरूपः आत्मा यथा सर्वा न्‌ सुखयति    1.6. Atman being endowed
with supreme love is by nature blissful.              Sayana    also refers to
Taittiriya Upanisad II.7.1       where the happy and happiness-giving
nature of Atman is discussed. (11) The Yoga-sitras have talked of
Anuttama-sukha or superlative happiness (11.42), which comes to a
man of contentment. Contentment (Santosa) as we know, is only a
landmark on the way of achieving the Yogic ideal. Even this stage
is full of well-being. Thus we can say that cheerfulness becomes the
prerogative of the aspirant who sincerely walks on the pathway to
God. A prayer addressed to Soma also enumerates this quality. “Oh
Soma, you are unconquerable and full of happiness. Keep flowing,
oh Soma; you form the main part of the offering to Indra. Those who
control their minds praise you and pay homage to you” (S.64). All
sorts of satisfying states are traced back to Ucchista, the term
meaning ‘that which remains in the uppermost region’ and this is
surely the Paramatman. “The bliss, delight, exhileration, joy or
happiness, as well as the gods who live in the heavens have come
from Ucchista” (S.65). When man is confronted with something
great, mysterious and powerful, his natural reaction is to bow before
it. “Obeisance to him who is a carrier of delight and felicity, who
blesses and bestows happiness, who is benign and yet more benign”
(S.66).
      (b) Some Pseudo-spatial Considerations. The next question
generally asked is : Where do we find God? Immanence and


                                       89
Pathway to God in the Vedas



transcendence are the properties that demand our attention. These
may appear to refer to the space-filling nature of God. But such talk
is irrelevant here. ‘Outside’ and ‘inside’ are not spatial stamps, they
are artifacts of an intellect drenched in spatial ideas. They are to
be taken as just working symbols. The Rsi may say that God makes
everything His resting place but he does          not cling   to   mere
immanence. God is outside too. In fact he is immano-transcendent.
Let us enumerate some verses, keeping in mind that immanence and
transcendence are only ideograms into which we try to fit the
description of God. (i) Immanence: we shall here consider passages
where we notice higher and higher involvement of God in the world.
We shall first note a couple of Mantras where God is said to be
present inside everything. We shall then pass on to the notion of
Him as holding objects like an inner thread, then as a haven of all,
and finally as the source of the world. We shall then see how He
 is identified with the living force in mortals. We shall next pick up
 a couple of Mantras in which God is pantheistically depicted as non-
 different from the world. A veritable climax to the series! “This earth
 belongs to king Varuna. So also the heaven which is vast and far
 away belongs to him; both the seas are contained in his belly and
 he is as well hidden in this small drop of water,” asserts the sage
 Brahma (S.67). “Agni is an indweller of water, indweller of trees
 and creepers, indweller of movable and immovable existences. Man
 prostrates before Agni wheresoever situated be He, in the sacrificial
 hall or inside a rock” (S.68). A very popular notion to indicate an
 inner existence is to call ita thread. “He who knows the thread round
 which are woven the created things, and who knows the thread of
 all threads knows Brahman. I know the thread round which are
 woven the created things, as well as the thread of threads. 1,
 therefore, know Brahman” (S.69). One verse declares that the lord
 is present in fire, in waters as in men; he creates and holds the world
 (S.70). “He who runs the chariot of the universe holds the earth,
 the heaven and the mid-region. He holds the six principal quarters.
 He enters into every dwelling” (S.71). Then we come across a
 celebrated passage which has been picked up by the poet, Rabindra
 Nath Tagore as the motto for Vigva-Bh — यत्र वि श्वं भवति एकनीडम्‌
                                      arati
 (where the universe makes its home in a single nest). “Vena beheld

                                   90
                                                    The Problem of God



that supreme principle hidden inside, where the entire existence is
stored up in one resting place. In it every thing unites and from it
every thing flows. He is the warp and the woof of the universe”
(S.72). In one Rk, God is solemnly declared to be the life-force in
the mortals. “Agni is the first sacrificer. He is the life-principle in
those who are subject to death (अमृतं मर्त्ये षु). Though immortal, he
as if grows along with the mortal body, and remains stable in the
sacrifice of life’ (S.73). Lastly we shall see two Mantras, in one
of which God is described, Spinozistically, as identical with the
world and in the second, as himself being all gods. “He was born
and knew his own mother and father. A great giver, he brings under
his sway the celestial sphere as also the mid-region. He becomes
the world and is omnipresent” (S.74). The pantheistic tinge is worth
noting. “Oh Agni, you are Indra the leader of sages; you yourself
are the revered Visnu of unfading fame. You are Brahma the
bestower of divine prowess. All intellectual abilities take resort to
your feet. You are none other than Varuna whose dictates are
inviolable and the brave Mitra too. You are not different from
Aryama the sovereign of all men as well as AmSa who confers
wealth in the sacrificial hall. You are Tvasta and so give valour to
worshippers. You shine like Mitra with regal splendour which is
also present in the universe. Like the flash of lightning, you endow
men with reason and represent the indomitable spirit in them”
(S.75). The concept of immanence has here crossed its own limits
and has brought us very near monism. (ii) Immano-transcendence.
The classification of hymns as per these qualities is more or less
of an academic nature. It is never rigid. The emphasis may shift
from one quality to another. Still it is justifiable to treat the verses
under the single heading namely immano-transcendence. “ Oh
friend of gods,” says one couplet, “I shall ask something of you only
if you would give thought to it. Was or was not the whole world
covered by three places where Visnu was worshipped?” (S.76).
“What is that wherein Adityas, Rudras and Vasus take their abode?
Wherein are the present, the past and the future established? What
is that in which all the worlds are anchored?” (S.77). Then we have
one verse where the immeasurability of Godhead is expressed both
in its signal radiance and in its superlative extensiveness. “Even

                                   91
Pathway to God in the Vedas



though hundreds of heavens and hundreds of earths come together,
they will fall short of your magnitude. Even though thousands of
suns shine simultaneously, their light will be miserably paltry”
(S.78). God is also referred to as the overplus, that which transcends
all possible estimates. He is the Divine MORE to use William
James’ phraseology. “He has encircled the whole earth and over-
shadowed the blazing heavens. No one is yet born nor will any one
be born who can be compared with you, Oh Indra. You have
outgrown the universe” (S.79). Over-stepping is a characteristic
which is a category apart, indicating some thing more, some thing
different, some thing beyond our common measures. It does not
speak only of quantitative vastness; it is also a special quality or
peculiarity of God which makes Him stand apart from the world as
 a unique something. In this very sense, the Brahma-sttra अधि क तु
भेदनि र्देशात्‌(1.1.22) uses the word Adhika. As Samkara comments:
 God is ‘additional to’ (Adhika), i.e. different from the individual self.
 In   the   Vedas   we   have   a   couple   of verses   where   immano-
 transcendence is clearly seen. “May you attain to that one who is
 the Lord of the world of light. He is present every where and being
 the earliest one, he is present in all newly created things. There are
 many paths which lead to him” (S.80). The mention of pathways
 indicates the over-stretching nature. “There is a wheel with one
 single navel in the middle and with thousands of spokes. It swings
 back and forth. Only a half of it goes into the creation of the
 universe; where can the other half be?” (S.81). Transcendence is
 present on the face of this psalm as well as in the oft-quoted Mantra
 in the Purusa—sikta where God is described as enveloping the whole
 creation without being exhausted. “Purusa is thousand-headed and
 thousand-eyed. Thousand-footed is He. Having covered the entire
 earth, He stands apart by ten fingers” (S.82).

       (c) Some Pseudo-numerical Considerations. If spatial attributes
 are inapplicable to God, so are the numerical ones. Therefore, any
 discussion pertaining to many gods or one God, is only a method of
 arranging Vedic anthology. (i) Polytheism. It is common knowledge
 that there are a number of deities to whom prayers are addressed in
 the Vedas. In some poems, gods are treated as equals of each other,

                                       92
                                                                The Problem of God



in some, they are said to be subordinate to one chief God. “None of
you, Oh gods, is a child and no one a mere youth. You are, all of you,
equally great” (5.83). “Eleven of you are in heavens, eleven on earth
and eleven in the mid-region. Be pleased to accept our offerings”
(S.84). The seer Atharvan sings the praises of many gods who inhabit
the body of some great God. “Tell me who He is, in whose body
thirty-three gods have settled. In His body reside the thirty three gods
and those who knew him know also the thirty three gods” (5.85). The
sages proclaimed that inspite of many manifestations, the basic
reality underlying all of them is always the same. This definitely is
a departure from the many-gods-theory and an approach to one-God-
concept. “Agni who is kindled in different places, is verily only one.
Surya who lights up the entire universe, is but one. Usas who
illumines all this, is only one. That which is one, has assumed a
multitude of forms” (S.86). “To Him belongs sovereign power. He
is   the   One,   the   one    alone”      (S.87).   The   gods    are   said   to    be
innumerable, but Agni has been given the place ofprimus inter pares.
“Three thousand three hundred and thirty nine gods served Agni.
They sprinkled him with ghee, they offered him a KuSa seat and
enthroned     him”      (S.88).   And      thus   we are   automatically        led   to
monotheism.       We     are   told   by    Vedic    scholars     that   in   between
polytheism and monotheism, there was a stage, which they term
henotheism. The last of the above mentioned Mantras may be said
to imply that idea. Henotheism represents a halting position where
one particular deity, is given sovereign importance. But the present
work is not a historical survey of the development of Vedic thought,
nor is it a study of the Vedic pantheon. From a metaphysical point
of view, it only concerns us to see whether there are supposed to be
many realities or only one Reality. (11) Monotheism. The existence
of one God is expressed in a number of hymns. “Here is Lord Varuna.
He is called Agni in the evening and Mitra in the morning. He
becomes Savitr and roams in the mid-region and shines in the
heavens by becoming Indra” (S.89). Similar is the import of the
following lyric. “He himself is Agni and Aditya, Vayu and Candrama,
Sukra, Brahman and the waters and Prajapati, the creator” (S.90).
We get an explanation of the manifold appearances of the same God
in another ode (5.91). This poem attributes manifoldness to the

                                            93
Pathway to God in the Vedas



inscrutable power (Maya) which Indra possesses. Here comes the
word Maya or illusion which became the veritable battle-field for
philosophers to come. The Vedic utterance is innocent of that
technical aspect of the concept, as the word is used here only as
God’s mysterious power. The same bewildering aura surrounds the
word Yaksa, which is at times used to indicate the sovereign being.
Yaksa is, to use Otto’s terminology, an ‘un-thing’; no one can say
what it is or where it comes from; it is simply an overwhelming
presence. “In the centre of the universe, there is a Yaksa, a great
venerable God, moving on the surface ofthe waters supplying energ
to all. All gods have their abode in him as if they are branches of
 a tree” (S.92). Those who study the Vedas, can not ever miss the
 famous Purusa-sikta, the hymn of Man which is found in all the four
 Vedas. Herein Rsi Narayana describes the cosmic form of Man, a
 timeless all-that-there-is, three quarters of whom created heavens
 and only one quarter remained here. From there he spread himself
 everywhere (S.93). The wording is through and through pantheistic.
 From the Purusa are born the elements, the creatures, the sun and
 moon, even the Vedic chants and meters. Like the ViSvariipa (cosmic
 form) later broadcast by the eleventh canto of the Bhagavad-gita, the
 Virat Purusa in the Vedas represents the Primeval Being, holding the
 seeds of the world in its belly, and stretching beyond directions by
 its enormity. In both the Vedic and the Gita descriptions, the
 Numinous or the Mysterium Tremendum is experienced; in the Vedas
 more in its character as mysterium and in the Bhagavad-gita more
 in its character as tremendum. The Vedic conception of God is not
 confined only to His cosmic aspect. It touches other aspects also.
 “Let the gods inhabiting all the seven places conquered by Visnu
 shelter us. Visnu encircled the entire universe, placed his feet at three
 places whereby the whole world was covered by the dust of his feet.
 He established Dharma at the three places. He observes your conduct
 from those places and in turn his deeds are open to you. Visnu is
 a great friend of Indra” (S.94). In the first place Visnu is here
 described as a cosmic creator. Madhvacarya mentions in his
 commentary that Vedas, time, qualities, worlds, gods and men, ina
 word, the living, the non-living and that which is the mixture of the
 two, is all enveloped by Visnu. Secondly, Visnu is a creator of social

                                    94
                                                          The Problem of God



rules and regulations. Thirdly, he is an ideal ruler who watches the
activities of men and whose own activities are open for inspection
by men. Here the theistic ideal of a valiant, just and kind ruler stands
in bold relief. “Oh Indra and Varuna, one of you is a sovereign
(Samrat) and the other a self-ruler (Svarat). Both of you are mighty”
(S.95).    A Samrat rules over other kings and therefore that concept
can be said to be a relative one. When there are no subordinates, no
one can be called a Samrat; similarly no God is a Samrat in the
absence of others. Swarat, however, implies self-rule — he is
almighty or a ruler of himself, or ruler of an inner kingdom and has
nothing to do with others. As Spinoza’s Substance exists in itself and
is conceived through itself, so is the Svarat who shines in the majesty
of passionless serenity. This is the archetype of the concept ofa wise
man ofthe Stoics and the Epicureans. He is characterised by apathia
(emotionlessness) and ataraxia (imperturbability) respectively, 15 not
disturbed by evil in the outer world. No one can control natural
calamities like earthquakes or volcanos. But the wise man conquers
the emotions arising out of the natural calamities; and hence he can
be said to have conquered nature. Controlling excessive heat means
controlling the pain caused by it. Victory over death is victory over
sad emotions caused by death of dear and near ones. The wise man
is “so independent of externals, his happiness so perfect and his
wisdom so inalienable that... he walks as a God among men and that
even on bread and water he need not envy Zeus.”' In his demeanour
the wise man of the Greeks is a replica of the Svarat of the Vedas
and the Upanisads. Later on, the Svarat became the Buddha or the
enlightened soul in Buddhistic thought as he became the Jina or the
conqueror in Jaina ethics. Unless one has absolute control over
oneself (Svarat), one is not entitled to rule over others (Samrat). A
real Samrat is first a Svarat and then a Samrat, if at all. Thus from
a conception of a cosmic being, we pass on to the concept of the self
as the highest Being through an intermediary stage of different
deities having their sway over different domains of life. (11) Monism.
The line between monotheism and monism is very thin in Vedic
metaphysics. Often the seers refer to a particular deity by name; but

 1   E. Zeller, Outlines of the History of Greek Philosophy, P. 239

                                       95
Pathway to God in the Vedas



the attributes connoted speak not so much of a specific God, as of
the omniscient or omnipresent Being; not so much of one God, as
of one Principle. Monotheism would require acceptance of one single
God with attributes (Saguna ISvara), while monism would do away
with the name and form of such a deity and speak only of quality-
less Brahman (Nirguna Brahman). Theoretically the bifurcation may
be rigid, but from the point of view of spiritual experience, there can
not be    any   flawless   sorting.   The   hymns   describing   spiritual
experience, can fit in both the Saguna and the Nirguna categories.
We may cite an example. “I respectfully proclaim the wonderful and
mighty power of the renowned immortal God Varuna who, standing
high above in the mid-region, has scanned the earth with the measure
of the sun. None verily could outweigh or challenge the tremendous
power of the omnipotent and omniscient God, just as the water of
all the lucid rivers watering the earth put together, can not make the
sea overflow” (S.96). Here the name of Varuna is mentioned; and
 hence the passage suggests monotheism; but the implication of the
 Mantra is the immeasurable and incomprehensible potency of the
 highest Being that be, or an undecipherable authority before which
 the creatures are almost crippled. It cannot be spelt out to be either
 physical, mental or rational power; it just suggests the lordship of
 the world-ruler. That is why we would better class this Mantra as
 monistic in import. While commenting on this Mantra, Sayana
 remarks that it should be understood to refer to the highest Reality
 rather than to Varuna, the God of waters - कर्म परमेश्वरस्यैव उचि तं न
 वरुणस्य इति न वाच्यं तस्य वरुणादि रूपावस्थानातू (it is the supreme Brahman
 which can discharge the function of serving as the world-ground and
 not Varuna. The highest Being only manifests itself in forms like
 Varuna).

      We shall consider in the sequel a couple of Mantras the monistic
 interpretation of which would be natural, though the Mantras can be
 understood monotheistically as well. Therein are mentioned various
 predicates of the highest reality, namely, the greatest, omnipresent,
 omniscient, imperishable, going beyond time and space, perfect first
 cause, immortal and inscrutable in-dweller, or self-existent.
 “Visvakarman is powerful in mind. He is the creator, sustainer and

                                      96
                                                      The Problem of God



eternal presence” (S.97). The seer Atharvan speaks of one omni-
scient Being (Ekarsi) (S.98). Then comes the concept of the Absolute
as inclusive of both time and space. “The endless is stretched
everywhere. That which has no end is intermingled with that which
has an end. It encompasses the past, the present and the future and
moves about sustaining every thing” (S.99). The idea of an all-
inclusive reality has run through philosophical thought both in the
east and the west. It is the Brahman of the Vedas and the Upanisads,
the Siinya of Buddhists and ViraSaiva thinkers, the Substance of
Spinoza, the concrete Universal of Hegel or the Absolute of Bradley.
There is parity between the infinite in mathematics and the infinite
in metaphysics. In both, infinity added to infinity results in infinity;
and infinity subtracted from infinity remains infinity. “From the
whole emerges the whole. By the whole is the whole sprinkled with
life. Hence, we shall know the source from which the sprinkling is
carried out” (S.100). This is surely a reference to the first cause of
everything. One need not sojourn far away to collect the highest; for
It has moorings in the inner depths of beings. “In the nine petalled
lotus of the human body encased by three qualities, Sattva, Rajas and
Tamas, resides a mysterious Being, the immortal Self whom the wise
know. Desireless, serene, immortal, self-existent, content with the
inner core, perfect is He. He who knows this magnanimous, ageless
principle, has no fear of death” (S.101). The theistic-absolutistic
description leans towards absolutism or even illusionism when Rsi
Brhaduktha Vamadevya labels the famed exploits of Indra as just
make-belief. “Statements like ‘Indra the strong, roamed around
exhibiting his might’ or the narrations of his battles are all fallacious.
Oh Indra, you have no enemy today and you have had none in the
past”    (S.102). The process of the elimination of the theistic
substantive is complete with this utterance.
        It is remarkable that the Vedic thinker was aware that Reality
was predicateless existence. No predicate predicates it, no name
names it, no quality qualifies it, no character characterises it. It
defies all description. To illustrate the point we may consider a
cluster of four Mantras. (i) “One may speak of Indra, Mitra, Varuna
Agni or the beautiful-winged Garutman. But there is only a single
Reality, though named also as Yama or MatariSvan” (S.103). Here

                                   97
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Reality is termed Sat by the sage Dirghatamas Aucathya. (ii) In
another Mantra       the   emphasis         is       shifted   to Asat.     Rsi    Laukya
Brhaspati asserts: “In the early age of Gods, came existence from
non existence” (S.104). (iii) The Muni Trita Aptya characterises
Reality as both Sat and Asat. “Agni is both non-existent and
existent” (S.105). (iv) Lastly Rsi Prajapati Paramesthi completes the
circle by calling reality neither Sat nor Asat (S.106). The four
Mantras taken together give us a four-fold scheme.

      Reality is;

      Reality is not;

      Reality both is an is not;

      Reality neither is nor is not.

      These are at best speculative assertions, and would not satisfy
 the Vedic Rsi. Is not reality beyond the purview of the four-fold
 model? “Yes”, says the humble mystic, “it is not given to me to know
 even this much; only He himself may know what it is; or He also
 may not know: सो अंग वेद यदि              वा न वेद” (S.107).
