                                                  107
                            CHAPTER IV




The Relation between God and Devotee


     Rigorous theoretical considerations are often accompanied by
compromises on the practical level. Gaudapada was an out-and-out
non-dualist. He asserted the existence of Brahman and Brahman
alone and refused to concede to the proposition that the world was
ever created. Still he had to admit the existence of the world for all
practical purposes. Similarly though the agnostic attitude shows the
humility of the Vedic Rsi, it can not satisfy an aspirant who wants
a God with whom he can have personal relationship. Psychology of
religion has never hesitated in giving an outright consent to a
personal deity. A man of religion will not only posit such a deity,
but will also attribute to it the best of everything. If there are beautiful
things in the world, the real beauty will be found only in God. And
as there are, in this world, persons who have imperfect knowledge,
God is there to represent the stage of perfection. In this fashion, all
superlatives come to be lodged in God. This idea of God resembles
Plato’s Idea of the Good, which is the source of all reality and power,
and which serves as the Summun Genus to all ideas whatsoever. But
there is an important difference. Plato’s ideas, by their very nature
form a hierarchy, and thus the lower ideas necessarily participate
in the higher ones. The Vedic Rsi, however, does not assert that there
is such a continuous participation of men in God’s being or in His
knowledge or power or bliss. We shall consider Mantras which
maintain that God represents the stage of perfection while man
stands far away from knowledge, power, bliss and glory. They are
concentrated in God; He is the source of all these. Therefore, the
devotee implores God to grant him these gifts as a favour. He
unhesitatingly approaches God and prays for the gifts which he is
sure to get from the deity, who possesses all auspicious qualities
in their fullness. Next, we shall note how the relation between God
and the aspirant is that between a giver and a taker. God pours mercy
                                                       God and Devotee



on man as it is His very nature to be kind. Both the devotee and the
deity have longing for each other. We shall then study lyrics which
tell that God has overflowing love for the worshipper and He
removes difficulties in his way and grants pardon even when the
devotee goes wrong. The devotee fondly looks on him as a parent
or as a friend and expects boons from Him. But worldly goods do
not satisfy him completely. In conclusion we shall see how the
devotee asks for bliss and goes in submission to God.
     1. God as a Source of Knowledge. God thus comes to be a
source of knowledge, source of power or source of bliss. Knowledge
makes all attainments easy. An ignorant man, therefore, feels at a
loss and invokes the gentle care of the ASwins in whose kindness
he believes. Rsi Kaksivat Dairghatamasa AuSija asks: “Oh ASwins,
what is it that can please you? How will both of you be gratified?
And how can an ignorant man worship you?... He can only approach
the ASwins for knowledge and his request will not go unheeded. We
therefore evoke the all-knowing ASwins. Be pleased to teach us the
proper prayer. I am offering you oblations” (S.1). Then there is a
prayer to the Lord of speech. It is speech through which knowledge
often finds expression. Thus speech becomes the revealer of
knowledge and hence its importance. “Let us pray today to the Lord
of speech, the creator, who is as swift as, if not swifter than, thought.
May he be pleased by our offerings and grant us bliss” (S.2). God
is looked upon as the fountain-head from which wisdom flows to
human minds. Man therefore wants the Almighty to make him a
possessor of that intellectual power. He appeals to a host of gods
for sharp intellect. “May Varuna, Agni and Prajapati send down to
me rational powers. May Indra, Vayu and Dhata also join hands in
doing so. Let them accept my libations” (S.3). We come to the next
characteristic of God, that of omniscience. He is the all-knower. “By
means of prayer, I harness to the chariot your pet horses that are
very swift. Be seated in the comfortable chariot, Oh Indra, who are
all-knowing and come to accept our offerings of Soma” (S.4). “You
are the first born and omniscient; you possess the highest power.
You guard our wealth” (S.5). This idea, it may be remarked, was
later reflected in the Yoga—sitra (1.26), where the Lord is said to be
the teacher of the ancients (Purvesam Guruh). An important aspect

                                   109
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of this omniscience is what Prof. R.D. Ranade calls the forefulfilment
of a saint’s desires, by God.' “When you please God by means of
the Soma juice, he who knows the minds of all, fulfils their desires”
(S.6). The Lord will not even let the devotee ask for any thing, or
suffer any longing. Every thing goes a devotee’s way before he
dreams of it. His wishes are horses which he rides. There is,
moreover, one point to be noted in this concept of forefulfilment. God
might be fulfilling these desires because the saint has, in the first
place, no bad or selfish desires, and in the second place his only
desire is to attain to God. One more point is to be noted. A corollary,
so to say, of omniscience is the unknowability of the omniscient
being. Who can know the knower, the Brhadaranyaka Upanisad
 (11.4.14) exclaimed later. “This Soma is active, is not caught by
 anyone’s intellect, is conquerer of the worlds and creator of saplings.
 He is a २51 (5.7).                                               न

        Now, such omniscience would only be qualified omniscience
 or incomplete omniscience, if God is not conceived as creator as
 well, contends James Ward. “Our partial knowledge of a thing is
 knowledge of its utterances, attitudes and behaviour as they are for
 us: hence we call this relative knowledge. But a knowledge of all
 such characteristics of all things in all their interactions would still
 only    be   absolute   as   knowledge...   it   would   leave   the   things
 themselves still independent as regards their existence...”? And
 therefore, “Such ‘omniscience’ ina word presupposes creation; thus
 it is only for creative intuition that the knowing and the being of
 objects could be said to be in any sense the same.”? In other words,
 omniscience is omniscience only if it belongs to a creator God.
 Though the Vedic sages did not argue in this way, and though they
 did not unequivocally spell out all the characteristics of God, the idea
 of creation along with the idea of omniscience is found therein. “He
 bows to Brhaspati the patriarch, and knows him to be the creator,


 1   Pathway to God in Hindi Literature, Adhyatma Vidya Mandir, Sangli, 1954,
     Pp 324-325                                    ह

 2 The Realm of Ends, Cambridge, At the University Press, 1912, P. 236
 3   Ibid, P. 237


                                      110
                                                   God and Devotee



the all-knowing powerful God who can not be bound down by any
one” (S.8). “With truth he shines in the upper direction, with
knowledge he looks on the lower one, with vital airs he controls
tangentially the place wherein the Brahman resides. He who knows
the two friction-sticks (Aranis) from whence the Vasu is created,
alone knows the great Brahman” (S.9). In another ode we read a
description of Agni who is ever alert among gods and is a creator
of bodies (S.10). In other words, to know God is to know Him as
a creator too. Then we find one very important verse counting a
number of marks of deities. We are told that they are all-knowing
and firm in moral laws; they are protectors from injury, leaders on
the path and saviours from sin. “Whether it is Varuna, Mitra or
Aryama, they are all omniscient. The laws set by them are rigorously
observed and they protect their devotees. They always follow the
time-honoured truthful laws and are lovers of righteousness. They
are leaders of the straight-forward way, are generous and are
saviours of devotees from sin” (S.11). The happy expression Rta-
sprsah makes it clear that they have the moral law in their grip and
will not forsake it. One should not desert the principles which are
acknowledged to be just, argued Socrates in the Dialogue, Crito. Of
moral integrity the Rsis have enough and to spare and that is why
they become leaders. Another Mantra speaks of the same love for
rectitude. “Oh delightful Soma, you are really divine. As a friend
to friend, bestow righteousness on us” (S.12). “Ward off all fear of
danger from me, Oh Varuna, the holy law-abiding sovereign, and
bestow thy grace upon me. Release me from the meshes of the sin
as a calf is released from the bonds of the tether. Without thee, I
have absolutely no freedom even to open my own eyelids” (S.13).
The twin qualities of possessing power, and abiding by principles,
are mentioned here. For, persons in high places can not afford to
preach a principle which they themselves do not follow. And who
will abide by the laws if not the makers of laws or setters of
standards? In fact, they have to take care of the laws all the more,
because they are leaders whom the people follow. Rules are
strengthened by pressing them into service. This is exemplified by
the wise Socrates to whom we referred awhile ago, who, when
entreated to leave his country and escape the death sentence,

                                 111
Pathway to God in the Vedas



pleaded: ‘When rules are trampled under foot the day is not far off
when states meet their doom’. So much for law-abidingness.

     2. God as a Source of Power. The Mantra under consideration
speaks of another quality, viz., the absolute power of God, and thus
in the wake of omniscience comes omnipotence. If God is all-
knowing, he is also all-powerful. “Worship Indra and sing his
glorious deeds. Drops of Soma nectar have gladdened him. Prostrate
before that supreme power with all humility”, tells the sage Gotama
Rahugana (S.14). This power of the deity has the potency           of
bestowing immortality on the devotee. “Which deity should we
worship? We should surrender to him who grants us vital force
(Atmada), and vigour (Balada), whose commands are observed by
men and gods, resort to whose shadow brings on immortality and
 its deprivation, death” (S.15). By the twin methods of Anvaya
 (positive way), and Vyatireka (negative way), the same truth is
 stressed. If you take shelter under the shadow of blessings of the
 deity, you become immortal, whereas, if you cannot get there, you
 are subject to death. God’s supremacy is sung in yet another poem.
 “Tt is God Indra without whose support man can never succeed,
 whom all contesting persons pray for help, who is the model for
 everything in the universe and who is the prime mover of all,
 including the unmoved also” (S.16). We do not find any subtle
 distinction between knowledge and power and many times they are
 found together. “Enhanced thousand times in strength by the power
 of intense devotion of the seers and saints, Indra has become as vast
 as the ocean. He is truth incarnate” (S.17). Indra is elsewhere
 depicted as dispelling illusion by slaying the mighty guileful being
 (मायि नं मृगं अवधीः) with his mysterious power (Maya). His power is
 unsurpassable and on him gods have bestowed strength, power and
 insight too. By all these glories he reveals his suzerainty (S.18).

      Psychology has long discovered that all types of eminence —
 physical, intellectual, educational or social — contribute to leader-
 ship qualities in a person. These excellences enhance his image and
 he automatically controls the masses. This is pictured in a number
 of Mantras. Indra has been acclaimed as the supreme master. “You
 are the sole ruler over the mortals”, declares one poem (S.19); while

                                  112
                                                      God and Devotee



another hymn attributes to him other qualities as well. “You are the
bravest of all; your devotees sing your praises. Master of manifold
wondrous power, Indra comes to reveal his prowess. Salutations to
him. Since bygone days, he alone has reigned over the creation”
(S.20). The eminence of the Lord is supreme and what he wills shall
certainly come to pass. None can obstruct its course (S.21). The
thought finds an echo in what the saint of Nimbargi used to say:
“Everything rests in God’s hands; nothing can come to pass unless
He wills it”. This brings us on the brink of determinism. There’s a
destiny, said Shakespeare, in the fall of a sparrow. In such a world
of cast-iron-necessity, man becomes just a puppet. The conductor
of the puppet-show is hidden from us, but he controls every thing
and the puppets dance because He makes them to. Here man’s
freedom comes to naught. Students of moral philosophy know the
anomaly of such a situation. There cannot be moral responsibility
unless man is free. But this freedom does not seem to have been
granted to man by these Mantras. In many a verse the lordly power
is said to be a result of penance. “Oh Indra,” goes one utterance,
“this Soma is meant for you, you are its enjoyer.. Bestow progeny
on us. You have won heavens on the strength of your penance”
(S.22). The Kausitaki Upanisad adds an explanatory note: “Till such
time as Indra did not have mystical knowledge, he was defeated by
the Asuras. But when he attained to mystical knowledge, he killed
them and became a Lord of the kingdom of the gods” (IV.20).
      We again read that only the wise can attain to the highest; those
that are incapable of cultivating knowledge, fall down (S.23). Then
we come across a hymn which combines omniscience and
omnipotence. “Indra can not be defeated by any one. No power can
envelop him. He can listen to the prayer of the world and he sees
the whole of it” (S.24). Thus power is traced back to knowledge or
penance. Not only’are omniscience and omnipotence found together,
but the latter can also be said to be a derivative of the former.
Knowledge, they say, is power, and rightly so. But William James,
the apostle of human freedom, will have none of this. For him,
denying freedom to man is sacrilege, and therefore in championing
human freedom he has not hesitated to deny to God the distinction
of omniscience. His oft-quoted example of the game of chess where

                                  113
Pathway to God in the Vedas



an expert is playing with a novice, makes this clear. The expert does
not have any knowledge of the move the novice may make; but he
has the power to counteract any possible move of the novice and
consequently he is always the winner. So too       God does not have
knowledge of actions of human beings. By saying this, James
preserves man’s freedom to act on his own. For, if God foreknows
man’s actions, the actions can not be said to be a result of man’s
free choice. Of course, this freedom does not help a man, for God
has the power to nullify all his actions and win ultimate victory.
Whatever the result, man is free to make his choice. To safeguard
man’s freedom, James is prepared to surrender God’s omniscience
and make Him only omnipotent.
      3. God as a Source of Bliss. We next come to a group of
verses which point to God as the source of bliss. We find three types
of approaches in these Mantras. In the first place, the sage offers
oblations and asks bliss in return. Secondly, he offers prayers and
 invokes His grace. Thirdly the Rsi confidently exclaims that it is the
 nature of God to shower bliss, and man need not move him to do
 so. These may be said to constitute three stages. At the first stage,
 we come across an attitude of some sort of barter, men offering
 libations and asking for felicity in return. “As a warrior runs his
 chariot with great speed, so are we driving ourselves hard for your
 sake. Pour your grace on us. We sing your praises and meditate on
 you for the sake of spiritual pleasure” (S.25). And Rsi Grtsamada
 says:   “he who with intense faith in his heart, offers        gifts to
 Brahmanaspati, the father of gods and worships him, enjoys strength
 and wealth along with his kith and kin, friends, sons and warriors”
 (S.26). We find some Mantras which exemplify the second type of
 attitude, that of expecting grace just for asking. This marks an
 advance over the first stage in that the devotee is conscious that there
 can not be an exchange between his offerings and God’s endow-
 ments. After all, when every thing belongs to God, where does man
 have any authority to offer Him even a blade of grass? So he just
 prays for delight. “Quelling the irreligious enemies and leaving
 behind miseries we meditate upon Soma who is the bund (Setu)
 leading to auspiciousness” (S.27). Soma on whem the devotee is
 contemplating, is called Setu or bridge. The word Setu is very

                                   114
                                                       God and Devotee



important, for it initiates an idea used many times over. In Mundaka
11.2.5, we are asked to know the self and leave all other talk because
that alone constitutes the bridge to immortality (तमेवैकं         जानथ
आत्मानमन्या वाचो वि मुंचथ अमृतस्यैष सेतुः). The self is called a bridge,
for it is used as a means to cross over from miseries to everlasting
glory. We shall deal with the idea of Setu towards the end of this
«chapter where it is used in a more comprehensive sense. Joy is
sought from all quarters by the Vedic sage. “Pray to one, pray to all’
is his method. Another Rk supports this. “Your path is easy (Suga),
thornless (Anrksara), and perfect (Sadhu). Oh Aryaman, Mitra and
Varuna, lead us along the way, so that with your guidance we shall
attain to ever-fresh happiness” (S.28). “Oh Soma, we humbly pray
for indestructible wealth and illuminating knowledge. Such a state
is desired by all, for it possessess protective power” (S.29). There
is a prayer for blessedness from the highest God who is looked upon
as the father and mother. He is full of bliss, the source of bliss and
therefore can confer bliss. “Oh Almighty, you are our father and
mother; we pray for beatitude” (S.30). There is the third and highest
way of approach to the Lord, who is considered as bliss overflowing.
It is superfluous to ask for happiness because it is the essence of
God to give. He is a spring of joy and one need not pray to him for
a drop. “Indra is by disposition magnanimous and full of energy and
cheer. He is lustrous, laudable and cherishes a liking for Soma”
(S.31). “You are wise and illuminator of our intellects. You are the
bestower of bliss. Those who reflect on you, crave for you; you are
the highest among those who endow perfection” (S.32).
     4. God as a Source of Glory. God, the source of knowledge,
source of power and source of bliss, is now portrayed as source of
glory. A popular verse from वि ष्णुपुराण (VI.5.74), enumerates the
different excellences of God, thus:

ऐश्वर्यस्य समग्रस्य वीर्यस्य यशसः श्रि यः।
ज्ञानवैराग्ययोश्वैव षण्णां भग इतीरणा ॥
(Splendour, power, fame, royalty, knowledge and dispassion
constitute the six-fold glory of God). Knowledge (Jnana), power
(Virya), have already been noticed. We come across other Mantras

                                       115
Pathway to God in the Vedas



where royalty (Sn), wealth (Vasu) and success (YaSas) are said to
be His properties. With Ananda or the highest bliss added, the Lord
becomes most glorious, Bhagavan or illustrious. The Almighty,
however, does not merely possess excellence, He radiates it. “Let
Indra impart to us that instruction by which we shall attain to a high
rank. He loves his devotees and nourishes them; let him distribute
his wealth amongst us” (S.33). “For spiritual status (Sri) is He
(Soma) engendered; from majesty he comes forth; He bestows
nobility and material prosperity on His devotees; clothed with that
dignity, His devotees attained immortality and sheerly on account
of His assistance they are crowned with success in all conflicts of
 life”, asserts Rsi Kanva Ghaura (S.34). These verses are addressed
 to Soma, Agni and Indra alike. Here is one song which mentions
 super-human qualities in Indra. “For glory (Sri) the devotee takes
 recourse to thy feet. You, bold and thunder-armed one, are the
 mighty gracious donor. Clad in vesture, fragrant and beautiful to look
 on, you appear like a rolling sun with graceful movements” (S.35).
 In another place Prajapati is invoked to shower all sorts of grandeur
 on the devotee. “Let Prajapati, the sovereign, enrich me with ever-
 growing vigour (Varcas), success (४४४३७) and ambrosial juice
 (Yajfasya Payas) like heavenly splendour” (5.36). By far the
 clearest mention of riches (both this-worldly happiness and other-
 worldly felicity) occurs in Rgveda. “Oh Indra, may your great
 powers help us in achieving wealth. You are a ruler of both types
 of glories (Ubhaya). Make us the recipients of the great and
 matchless Ratna or the jewel of spiritual experience” (S.37).
      5. God as Merciful. It is interesting to note that in the aforesaid
 verse describing God’s illustrious qualities, we find a parallel in
 Jnaneswara who substitutes Virya (power) by Audarya (generosity).
 We shall presently note a few hymns which depict a compassionate
 and generous nature of God. “Let us live in the merciful mind of that
 praise-worthy Lord. Let us carry ourselves in the manner which will
 delight him. He is powerful and protects all. Let him finish our
 enemies who hate us,” prays the sage Garga Bharadvaja (S.38). “We
 come to you by the highway of meditation, Oh mighty Indra, make
 us successful along with our horses” (S.39). It is Indra and Indra
 alone who is approached by the devotee. Such one-pointed devotion
                                   116
                                                      God and Devotee



should yield its fruit in the form of God’s mercy. “Oh you performer
of hundred sacrifices, I never asked any one else’s favour. I am
coming to you alone; please therefore be tender to me” (S.40). “With
your sheltering power, you become an auspicious king of your
devotees. Oh Indra, please, therefore be very soft-hearted” (S.41).
For the Rsis, the real nourishment comes not from the mother, but
from the mother-goddess, Saraswati. They want her to nurse them
and they also want other gods to take them under the wings and
watch over them. “Oh Saraswati, you have given feed to all great
men. Your strength is unfathomable; open your breast for us to
partake of the freely provided health-bestowing and wealth-giving
treasures” (S.42). “As birds spread their wings, spread your merciful
cover and provide us with every sort of security. Once we get your
love, we get every thing” (S.43). God’s mercy takes various forms
— removing obstructions from the path of the devotee, healing the
 sick, covering the naked and taking the whole responsibility of an
 aspirant’s well-being. “Shorten our ways, Oh Pusan, remove
 obstruction on the path; go close before us, cloud-born God. Drive
from our road, the wolf, the wicked inauspicious wolf, who lies in
wait to pounce on us. Wise Pusan, wonder-worker, we now claim
of thee the aid, wherewith you helped our sires of old” (S.44). Every
traveller wants to reach his destination by a shortcut which is at
the same time free from all dangers. And hence such a request.
Further we read: “He covers that which is bare; he gives medicines
to those who are sick. Through his grace the blind can see and the
lame can move about” (S.45). Such a One bears the burden of his
devotees on his lone shoulders. “He boosts up the morale of those
that are engaged in war. Man looks upon Him alone as benefactor.
He is the sole commander of the service squad. Let that Indra,
surrounded by Maruts, be our refuge” (S.46). God in his extreme
kindness not only benefits the devotees but also sanctifies the
sinners. “Let us remain under the safe-keeping of Varuna, who
confers His grace even upon sinners and makes them happy. Let us
observe His rules and regulations (Vratani) strictly, and be taintless.
Oh God, guard us for ever with your benign ways. Your grace
confers supersensuous experiences and ecstatic joy on the devo-
tees” (S.47). Happiness consists in the experience of God. There is

                                  117
Pathway to God in the Vedas



mention of the experience of the ambrosial juice. That oozing of
spiritual nectar blesses the man and keeps his mind pinned down
to it. “Oh Soma, you trickle down from all sides for the sake of Indra,
in order to impart exhileration and delight to him. You are the heart
of the devotee and controller of his mind as well” (S.48).

     6. God’s Longing for the Devotee. If the devotee has an
intense longing for God, God has an equally intense love for the
devotee. “What new and new eclogues can be sung by a mortal like
me? Have not the ancient devotees attained to enlightenment by
singing the time-honoured praise-songs?” Rsi Medhyatithi Kanva
asks (5.49). “May the God Savitr, the lord of creative energy, highly
 adored by sages be charitable to us who are desirous of divine glory,
 prayerful devotion and versatile intellect. In the midst of the good
 and plenty, we pray that we may be worthy recipients of the benign
 affection of God Savitr’ (5.50). The devotee asks for the grace of
 God, no doubt; for that he, on his part, remains glued to His name
 and surrenders to Him with all that he has. “Prompted by their
 intellects, the wise surrender to God meditating on Him and lauding
 His exploits” (S.51). “Only those can enter your territory, Oh Mitra
 and Varuna, who go in submission to you with all their intellect.
 Api’s sons who gave up meditation remained outside” (S.52). The
 Rk gives an impression that there is a reciprocal relation between
 surrender and grace, and seeks to establish it by means of positive
 and negative instances. Those who submit to God, reach His abode,
 and those who do not, are barred an entry!

       Narada says that a devotee feels extreme pain when separated
 from God; yes, but God more than reciprocates the feeling. He
 becomes restless. He tries to be in the vicinity of his devotees and
 attracts their attention. “The brave Indra is for ever near you. He
 tries to induce you to perform a sacrifice. He is in the midst of you
 and surrounds you” (S.53). How wonderful must be the experience
 of feeling God as surrounding the aspirant. King Pariksit, still
 unborn, is said to have had such an experience of God. Shri
 Amburao Maharaj, a saint from Karnatak, used to say “God is
 present every where. He is ever near you. But it is your greatest
 misfortune that you do not find time to see Him”. Another Mantra

                                   118
                                                     God and Devotee



goes one step ahead and says that He is always eager to open his
coffers for you. “Praise that immaculate Indra whose gifts are
purifying. He who follows Him always gets riches from Him. He is
ever eager to satisfy the desires of aspirants” (S.54). As God is
present every where, it is no wonder that He manifests himself then
and there. “Any man from any of the five tribes whose ardent prayers
are passionately addressed to Indra, receives instant attention. The
evil forces are warded off. God thus becomes a worthy object of
devotion” (S.55). “Lord Indra reveals Himself to his devotee who
worships Him with great application of mind and presents to him
Soma libations profusely, in the manner of a rich man offering his
abundant wealth. The gracious Lord extends his helping hand and
saves His devotee by lifting him up, but slays without intimation
those who hate the Divine” (S.56). It was exactly in this sense that
the Saint of Umadi uttered, “Holding them by the neck I uplift them
whom 1 have initiated”. The great God is immeasurable, but the
devotee can still measure him by his one-pointed devotion. By that
intense feeling, He can be made to appear before the devotee (S.57).
A lyric of Muni Pirana Vaisvamitra says that God is bound to the
devotee by strong cords and therefore the aspirant can always count
on His unfailing support. “When a seeker after divinity has his mind
firmly fixed on God with whole-hearted devotion and offers Soma,
God does not leave him or his cattle exposed, but favours him with
all auspiciousness” (S.58).
     The give-and-take activity which we find in worldly life finds
the receiver enthusiastic, while the giver is rather chary. In the
relationship between God and devotee it is different. The devotee
is eager to receive no doubt, but God is equally eager to give. Ina
bunch of songs we get different aspects of God’s love for the saint.
“The merciful God rushes through the region like a valiant bull in
order to help His devotees. The bountiful Lord shows the divine light
to the man who gives Soma oblations with intense devotion” (S.59).
Another important point is made by the Vedic seers, when they say
that God has all willingness to give, but He will not favour a man
who is idle. So alertness is a condition by which gods are pleased.
“Gods love the man who toils to offer Soma juice to them. They do
not like one who sleeps in idleness; themselves being always active,

                                 119
Pathway to God in the Vedas



they punish those who commit blunders” (S.60). There             is   an
assurance from the sage KaSyapa Avatsara: “The Rks will be
indulgent to him who is awake. Samasiktas will go to him who is
alert. Soma says to the wide awake, ‘I am yours, I have established
myself in your friendship’. Rks love Agni (or whoever performs his
duty of meditation on God), because he is awake. Samasiktas go
to him for
         he is alert. Soma says to him ‘I shall enjoy your friendship.
I have made your place my home’” (5.61). Similar is the import of
another Mantra. “The devotee has always got to be vigilant; the wise
 and ever-diligent worshippers are blessed with the vision of the
 supreme form of the Lord (Parama-pada), i.e. they attain to that
 blissful state on which they meditate unrestingly” (S.62). We are
 given to know that God never forgets the devotion of the aspirants.
 “Meditate on Indra, Oh Adhvaryus, till He is pleased; He is full of
 grace just as the udders of the cow are filled with milk for her calf.
 I know, nay, I am convinced that Indra will never forget him who
 has contemplated on him” (S.63). We find two verses which assert
 for certain that God, being fond of a        worshipper, hears       the
 worshipper’s call even from afar. “Revealing his divine power and
 manifesting different ways of helping a devotee, Lord Indra will rush
 to us trailing on our call, when He hears it” (5.64). “Let us get ready
 for meditation and utter the Mantra of Agni. It will reach his ear
 though he may be far away” (S.65). There is a very re-assuring
 Mantra which encourages the aspirant to continue his meditation, by
 informing him, that all those who have gone by this path, have
 attained to the goal. “I shall ever sing the praises of that great God
 who is sitting on the sacred grass, who has turned his gracious face
 towards me, who is valiant and all-comprehensive, and thus gets my
 desires fulfilled. This is the path trodden by all the ancient ones
 (Pratnatha), our forefathers of olden times (Pirvatha), worshippers
 of today (Imatha), in fact by devotees every where (४15९०१)
 (S.66).
     7. God removes an Aspirant’s Difficulties. There is a set of
 Vedic utterances laden with a hopeful message that God makes
 smooth the path of the devotee and sweeps it clean of dangerous
 spots. This gives tremendous confidence and security to the traveller
 beginning his spiritual journey. It redoubles his energy and enthu-
                                   120
                                                     God and Devotee



siasm. “Indra, you never hold back your gifts, never do you forsake
your devotees. And now your grace is being poured on us more and
more liberally” (S.67). “All kinds of powers are concentrated in you,
Oh Indra. You are ever eager to partake of Soma. Every one is aware
of the potency of Vajra which you are wearing on your arm. Do kill
our enemies. Vamra’s enemy had achieved tremendous strength, so
that he was on the outskirts of heaven. But as Vamra meditated on
you, you enabled him to conquer his enemy” (S.68). Indra who
destroys the enemies of his devotees verily becomes their refuge.
“Indra who is the leader of the brave (Satpati) and who fulfils their
desires (Carsaniprah) fought a great war to provide a shelter to the
gods. His exploits are sung by the wise in the house of the
illuminating sun i.e. they continue meditation while yet enjoying the
experience of God’s lustrous form” (5.69). No wonder that man
wishes to have more and more of His grace. “Oh Mitra, son of Aditi,
let him acquire riches who abides by your laws. He, who is your ward,
is not conquered or killed. Sorrow ventures not to touch him or even
enter into his vicinity. We are free from disease. Absorbed in your
praises, we kneel before you and worship you. Let us be recipients
of your benediction. Here comes Mitra, honourable, strong and wise.
Let us be covered by his tenderness and protective power” (S.70).
This saving armour of God is especially sought by the devotee when
he is facing hardships. It is a universal truth that man prays to God
only when in distress. That is why Kunti, the mother of Pandavas,
wished to be visited by perplexities so that, at least then, she would
remember God. The soft-hearted Lord can not stand his devotee’s
suffering and runs to him. “Sing the glories of Indra and Varuna with
all your heart, with enthusiasm, with mirth and salute them. One of
them the brave Indra, kills Vrtra (removes your obstacles) and the
other, the wise Varuna, comes to you when you are passing through
hard times” (S.71). Whatever may be the circumstances one is
placed in, one should always remember Him. So much is the
potential power of God’s words, that when he utters anything, it
must come true. His words are expressions of truth; how can they
go wrong? His desires come true (Satyakama), His resolutions come
true (Satya-samkalpa), says the Upanisad (Chandogya VIII.7.1). On
the same analogy we may say that He is Satyavak, his words come


                                 121
Pathway to God in the Vedas



true. “It is sufficient for me if you merely say that I, your devotee,
will not be destroyed. For, your words express nothing but truth” is
the observation of the sage Sukaksa Angirasa (S.72). After all, truth
is an aspect ofdivinity and rigorous practice oftruth contains a divine
element. It is in this strain that the Saint of Nimbargi used to say,
that one’s words will never be falsified if one unfailingly utters truth
and nothing but truth.

     The idea of man’s gaining freedom from enemies or vices as
a result of God’s grace is set forth in a couple of Mantras. The first
speaks of the experience of form and the other two of the experience
of flavour. “In the vicinity of the heavens appear the birds with
beautiful wings. They drive away the wolves which try to swim
across   the   expanse   of waters.   Oh   Lord,   let   my   prayers   be
remembered” (S.73). “Indra slew Vrtra by the power he gained from
Soma. He will repeat the same in future” (S.74). “Under the ecstatic
 influence of Soma draught, Indra completely destroys the evil forces
 encircling his devotees and bestows upon them the satisfaction of
 plenitude” (S.75). Needless to say the birds with beautiful wings
 stand for the experience of the form of God and Soma for that of
 the ambrosial juice. It is these that confer a sense of fulfilment or
 beatific joy on a devotee.

      8. God as Benefactor. Plato contrasted the non-rational part
 of the soul with the rational and stated that the non-rational part with
 its two divisions, appetite and spirited element, always tries to
 pull man downwards. If the rational element is not sufficiently
 powerful, man will commit errors. When the better part of man
 becomes aware of such mistakes, it wants to make atonements. It
 repents, wishes to be forgiven and resolves to bend its way towards
 good. Such requests for forgiveness are contained in many Vedic
 Mantras. “Your devotee who abides by your eternal moral law, Rta,
 sings Sama songs for you. You are pure, purifier and accomplish
great tasks” (S.76). It is presupposed that God being himself an
 exemplar of purity or purity in excess, can burn off the sins of
devotees. Thus Rsi TiraSci Angirasa prays: “Let us glorify the
immaculate Indra by sacred Sama hymns. His lustre and glory are
enhanced by the faultless repetition of his praise-songs. Let the

                                   122
                                                                God and Devotee



spotless Indra, who always blesses his devotees, be pleased. Verily,
oh Indra, you are clean and guileless and hence naturally you confer
upon your devotees felicity and spiritual wealth. Being unsullied you
are able to completely exterminate the surrounding forces of evil.
Perfect that you are, you equip your devotees with strength” (S.77).
There is an idea which talks of god as a bridge — which stems the
flow of sins or passions. “You two are holders of several nooses,
oh Mitra and Varuna, you are the bridge against irreligious nature
and invincible to hostile mortals. Pray, may we cross over all
dangers by following your path of eternal truth, Rta, as those
crossing over water by having resort to a boat” (S.78). There are
several prayers for support from God. They are usually prefaced by
a   laudatory   invocation.    They      first   praise   the   gods   and   then
surreptitiously untie the bundle of their demands. “Mitra guides even
those who are full of sins and wishes them well who commit faults.
Let us be under his shelter. Like children of Varuna, we take resort
to your benign nature” (S.79). As already seen, wrong doings just
flow from a man who has not learnt to channelise his energies
towards moral ends. Nevertheless he wishes to be forgiven for his
derelictions. Rsi Brahma makes an ardent appeal: “As a creature is
freed from the bonds, as the body is wiped clean of dirt as a result
of a bath, as butter becomes clarified and sacred after heating it, let
us be absolved of our failings and their evil effects” (S.80). “You
pardon the foibles committed against gods, the indiscretions against
men, the obliquities against fathers, the lapses against oneself, as
also   misconducts    of all    sorts.    You forgive the        wrong-doings
performed knowingly or unknowingly” (S.81). Cleansing off imper-
fections is but a negative fruit; a devotee also reaps a positive one.
Thus along with the loosening the shackles of misdemeanour the
devotee gains supersensuous experiences. “Oh God, along with
emancipation from sins we gain a taintless spiritual jewel. May the
flawless Adityas be kind to us” (S.82). This generates tremendous
self-confidence and a healthy feeling of happiness in an aspirant.
“After extracting Soma juice we befriend Indra. We no longer look
on ourselves as Sinners, as poverty-ridden or as unbelievers” (S.83).
       We shall now consider a group of verses where the worshipper
looks upon himself as a child and upon God as a mother or father.

                                      123
    Pathway to God in the Vedas



    “You are a treasure-house of glories or glory itself. Oh Agni, your
    greatness lies in your splendour. Let us participate in your fond love”
~
    (S.84). “To me, Oh Indra, you are more precious than my father or
    my brother... You, giver of shelter, are equal to my mother. Oh good
    Lord, cover me with lavish bounties” (S.85). The aspirant’s one
    desire is always to get the vision of God. Let us see a couple of
    Mantras which contain prayers for God’s vision, for a proper
    direction to the intellect, for happiness, for knowledge and for
    spiritual wealth. “Reveal thyself to me, Oh Indra. You are our kin;
    you are our saviour. Give us happiness. Be a friend, protector and
    kindest father. Give us vitality” (5.86). “You have always been
    glorified here. You have heard the praise of ancient Rsis. Pray, direct
     my intellect in the proper way” (S.87). A child has no inhibition in
    asking any thing of a parent. With this attitude the aspirant sings:
     “Oh you Lord of riches, you are our father. You are our mother too.
    That is why I approach you for granting me bliss” (S.88). “Pray, give
     us wisdom or enlightenment (Kratu), Oh Indra, as a father, to his
     sons. Guide us, Oh much-invoked on the divine path, so that we, the
     struggling souls, may secure the divine light during this very life”
     (S.89). Undoubtedly the desire is to gain the highest knowledge
     while still living. The Vedic seers will not postpone anything to
     hereafter. ‘Here and now’ is the watchword of their philosophy of
     life. That explains their desire for getting the requests granted not
     at a later date but at the earliest moment. “ I ask of you only this
     much. Enrich your devotee, wherever he is, with spiritual wealth
     every day (दि वे दि वे). Who else is our champion, Oh Indra? Who is
     our friend or refuge? Who is our guardian and father?”(S.90).

          Of all the nine kinds of Bhakti, the Sakhya-bhakti has its own
     charm, because the devotee treats God as on a par with himself. For
     once, the devotee loves to dream that God is his friend, infinitely
     superior though He may be. Such intimacy exudes an aroma of
     nearness and dearness to God. “You destroyed Vala who had a greed
     for power, at the hands of Angirasas who were chanting ‘May the
     link of our ancient brotherhood be preserved’. You then opened the
     gates of Vala’s capital city for all” (S.91). “Offer prayers to God
     Indra who is crowned as the sovereign ruler, who is all-seeing and

                                       124
                                                        God and Devotee



all-powerful. Indra is a friend, philosopher and guide to devotees”’
(S.92). Arya Canakya says that it is advantageous to have a friend
who is wealthy and powerful. At the same time he must have a
genuine love for his peer. The Rsis have found an ideal friend in God.
“I Sasa, am singing your praises. Your powers are so fabulous that
no one who befriends you can be killed or even conquered” (S.93).
The intensity of your affection is beyond measure. Sweet is your
friendship and sweeter your guidance. We need only meditate on
you”   (S.94).    Prayers addressed to    Almighty are an      everyday
phenomenon. Not so common is the assurance which comes from
God. But the Vedic Rsi on the strength of earnestness and sincerity
of his devotion, gets surety from God. This is again an indication
of his tireless efforts to win what he wants. Here is an example:
“May your praise-songs spread among all men in all quarters. Pray,
favour me with a gift which has been denied to me up till now (देहि
नु मे यन्मे अदत्तः). You are my intimate friend who walked seven
solemn steps with me        (मे सप्तपदः सखासि )” (5.95). And pat comes
the reply: “We are indeed friends and our births have taken place
at the same time. I shall give you what has been denied to you up
till now (ददामि   तत्‌ यत्ते अदत्त), I am your intimate friend who walked
seven solemn steps with you (ते सप्तपदः सखास्मि )” (5.96).

     9. God as the Ultimate Refuge. To such an all-powerful and
beneficent deity, what can a poor creature like man offer in return?
Nor does God exchange gifts for a pittance. The only thing man can
volunteer to invest is his loyalty. He can sing in honour of the deity
with heart-felt gratitude “Those who have locked you up in their
hearts are the real sages”, declares one psalm (S.97). For an ardent
aspirant there is none who is wiser than God and none who is a real
patron like Him. “You are indeed matchless. Who can be your equal
in knowledge? There never was and there never will be a compeer
for you. Take up then, the tasks which you said you would” (S.98).
“We have come to your door, for you are a liberal donor. Oh bountiful
Indra, do not withhold your charities. Besides you we have no ally
amongst the defenders of mortals to whom we may look for support.
Have you not been described as a fulfiller of men’s desires?” (S.99).


                                    125
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Being sure of God’s generosities, devotees feel free to ask for boons.
The sage Kali Pragatha observes: “Truly we are yours, Oh Lord. We
worshippers depend solely on you. There is none, Oh much invoked
one, except you who can show us grace. Pray, deliver us from this
miserable, famished and accursed state of life and extend your help
and guidance with your wonderfully versatile intelligence, Oh
mighty leader” (S.100). A steadfast admirer always looks upon God
as the only being who combines every thing that is best. “I revere
you as the holiest of the holy; I regard you as the shaker of the
unshakable; I deem you as the banner of the heroes, Oh Indra, I
 respect you as the foremost of all men” (S.101). God is again the
 bestower of fortune and of peace. “May our lot prosper by your love,
 lead us to the pinnacle of success. You alone are the protector, Oh
 God. You have hundreds and thousands of medicines. May your
 grace be on us. Remove the goddess of ill-luck (Nirrti) from near
 us and cleanse our sins. With this fond hope in my mind I am
 appealing to you and meditating on you. Be pleased with us and let
 not our lives be cut short” (S.102). Madhva, in his Rgbhasya,
 understands Bhisaja as instruments for the preservation of the world.
 God has not only innumerable aids but also aids of different types
 (जगद्रक्षा साधनानि   अपि   अनन्तानि   अनन्तधा सन्ति ). Here the protection
 sought for is from man’s own evil tendencies (नि र्क्रत्याख्या दुर्मति ) and
 the prayer is for a mind which will rest in God (मति र्मम त्वद्वि षये सदा
 अस्तु). Thus Madhva’s interpretation points out a psycho-ethical truth
 that our real disease is keeping away from God and the cure lies
 in harnessing our mind to the feet of the highest Lord who is the
 possessor of all fortunes (ऐश्वर्या दि गुणाः यस्य). From another Mantra
 we learn that from God alone does peace flow. “You are verily the
 greatest, but you have made the mortals also great. I give my word
 that none other than you can grant peace” (S.103).

     As the source of knowledge, power, glory or bliss, as full of
 mercy for a devotee, as a friend of the good and destroyer of the
 wicked or as sanctifier or father, God remains the only refuge for
 man. “My wishes and my thoughts long to be absorbed in God. My
 feelings run to him. There is absolutely no other comforter than He.

                                        126
                                                        God and Devotee



My aspirations are fixed on Him” (S.104). “I will win the favour of
God Rudra by means of invocations and offerings of oblations. He
is mellow-hearted and readily responds to the call of his devotees.
I believe that he will not allow me to fall a prey to luring temptations.
I invoke the powerful god Rudra along with the Maruts for His
geniality and I pray for the refuge at His feet just as a man who is
tortured by blazing heat of the sun craves for the relieving shelter
of a shady tree” (S.105). When a man’s mind thirsts for God, He
comes to soothe him as cool water. “As one offers water to the
thirsty, you comforted the needy who sang your gracious ways. We
are now praying for energy as well as long life” (S.106). Men with
a devout mind perform sacrifices by which Indra is propitiated; but
those with a crooked mind, seem to wander far away from Indra.
To the devout, Indra is like a lake to a thirsty man; but to the
insincere men, he happens to be like a journey on a long tiresome
road (S.107). “May Brhaspati, the teacher of gods, remove dullness
in my vision, take away sorrow and wipe out suffering from my mind
and heart. May he be propitious to me” (S.108). “Pray, grant us an
impregnable refuge (अच्छि   द्रं शर्म), Oh guileless and merciful gods, so
that none can smite off that defending armour from afar or from
near” prays Manu Vaivasvata (S.109).
     The devotee puts utmost confidence in God and rests assured
that He and He alone will come to his succour. Afflicted with various
miseries and unable to help himself, man rushes to God for help.
Tradition speaks of three kinds of distresses — physical, cosmical
and mental; but there are innumerable varieties of each kind of
affliction, so that an individual’s sorrows are countless. God cures
them all. He becomes water to the thirsty, food to the hungry, warm
sunshine to the shivering and cool wind to him who is oppressed
by heat. In fact He is a panacea for all worldly woes. The whole
world may fail a man but never the Almighty. Friends may prove
unkind but there always remains for man one ultimate refuge, God.
All kinds of powers can be deflated but God’s support to the devotee
stands firm and unshakable. Without that backing, the world is a
nasty, fearsome place of trial. With God as an ally, it becomes a
blissful existence.

Pathway to God in the Vedas
