                                            136
                               CHAPTER V




                  The Spiritual Teacher


      1. Necessity of a Spiritual Teacher. Life on this earth has
been described as a river or as a sea by many a seer. Samkara in
his commentary on Aitareya Upanisad, I.2.1, exquisitely describes
this ocean. It is filled with the water of sorrow which is caused by
ignorance and desires; it is infested with terrible crocodiles in the
form of deadly diseases and old age; people who are caught there
are lamenting their lot.      A man can be saved only if, by his good
fortune, he meets with a teacher full of compassion for the beings
that are drowning. In JnaneSvari we have a marvellous description
of Mayanadi or the flood of illusion (VII.68-102). In more or less
the same terms Kabir gives us a continued metaphor of a river of
sorrow. “Who can direct the ship of a man’s life,” he asks, “except
a spiritual guide, who knows the danger spots and situations, and
possesses the power to carry the ship successfully to the other shore
of existence?”' The subject has occupied the mind of our ancestors.
We shall see how they express their inability to proceed without
help. We will them turn to a very important topic of characteristics
of a spiritual teacher. Only he who has realised God can be a Guru.
After noting moral and mystical marks of a teacher we shall take
a survey of the functions of the teacher of which the Vedas speak.
Side by side it is important to understand the qualifications of a
student. An aspirant feels grateful to the master for his guidance.
A fortunate person is he who gets initiation from a teacher who
belongs to a rich spiritual tradition. He tries his best to be worthy
of that tradition and always prays to his preceptor for grace.

      The Vedic seers may not have put the subject of the necessity
of a Guru in a specific and precise way. But they have described
the plight of a man who is confused and is unable to find his way

1   R.D. Ranade, Pathway to God in Hindi Literature, P. 137
Pathway to God in the Vedas



 out. The sage Kurma Gartsamada exclaims: “I cannot see what
 stands on my right and what stands on my left. Oh Adityas, I cannot
 make out what is in front of me and what is behind me. Oh Vasus,
 I am infirm and ignorant. Still with the assistance of your guidance,
 I can attain the fear-dispelling light” (S.1). But it is not so easy to
 find such guides. God does not reveal Himself in a liberal way. To
 meet a spiritual teacher itself requires a good deal of merit. “Who
 is there who has known the direction that leads unto God? Is there
 any one who has declared it here? Only the lower earthly abodes are
 ordinarily seen; the higher ones exist truly in the secret supreme
 region” (S.2).   A new-comer can take only a well-trodden path. “We
 have now arrived at the road and shall follow it as best as we can.
 Whatever task we can accomplish, we shall take up and complete.
 The all-knowing Agni is the priest and he will prescribe the rituals
 and the seasons of sacrifice to be offered to the gods” (S.3). One
 who wishes to reach Brahman must learn at the hands of a realised
 soul. “He will give instruction    regarding Brahman that enlivens
 vegetation. The instruction is contained neither in the earth nor in
 the heavens” (S.4).

       He who has not experienced God cannot guide others. In fact,
* “a Rsi is one who is endowed with the intuitive vision of God. The
_ Aupamanyavas Say that the Rsis actually visualised the Mantras and
* hence they were called Rsis. It is so, because in the course of
६. meditation, the form of unborn God Himself descended before their
_ eyes. This is the essential feature of Rsihood” (S.5). The Taittiriya
, Aranyaka offers the same explanation of the word: “The form of self-
; existent Brahman descended before the eyes of the ancient holy
  saints; and they thus attained the status of Rsis. This is the most
  essential characteristic of a seer’ (S.6). A Rsi is also described in
  Satapatha Brahmana as having reached that high pedestal with
  strenuous penance (S.7). The knowledge thus gained was imparted
  to others by the sages. “The Rsis attained the intuitive experience
  of God and saw the way to Him. Afterwards they communicated by
  initiation the same to their disciples who had no such experience”
  (S.8). “Oh Soma, flow on with a clean current for the worship of
 gods and enter the heart of Indra which is your treasure-house. Carry

                                   138
                                                   The Spiritual Teacher



us along without any harm. One who is himself familiar with the
landscape can provide correct landmarks to a layman” (5.9).
     A spiritual teacher has to discharge a two-fold function. Firstly,
when a disciple strays away from the proper avenue, the spiritual
teacher makes him realise his mistake and, secondly, he brings the
disciple on to the proper path. “Please forgive us, Oh Agni, we admit
our derelictions. I was drifting far away into the wilderness, but now
I have recourse to this path. You are a friend, protector and supporter
of your devotees and you can raise the mortal men to the position
of a divine seer” pleads Hiranyastipa Angirasa (S.10). Or again, “Do
not go away from me in anger, Oh Agni. Indra, who is the custodian
of God’s laws, revealed this to me. He knew the way and he saw
you; I have received instruction from him and have therefore come
to you” (S.11). Thus does the spiritual teacher tend the erring sheep.
The same Mantra is repeated elsewhere in the Rgveda with a slight
variation in words. Coupled with it is another stanza visualised by
Rsi Kavasa Ailiisa which speaks in unmistakable terms about the
function of the spiritual teacher to offer proper guidance to a disciple.
“Indra, the custodian of God’s laws made it known to me that you
are lying hidden, Oh Agni, in waters. I have received instruction
from Him and have therefore come to you. A stranger on the way
will naturally ask for guidance from one who knows the path and
proceeds further accordingly. This is the most effective method; it
enables a man to take a straight and quick road” (S.12). The word
used is Anusista i.e., instructed or taught, a clear reference to the
teacher-disciple relation. It is also specifically mentioned that Indra
was a far advanced person as he had ‘seen’ Agni (Cacaksa). Well,
this experience of Indra also did not arise all of a sudden. Shri
Aurobindo translates the phrase अनु हि त्व चचक्ष as ‘sought after and
saw thee’. It shows that Indra must have himself practised penance,
before he could feast his eyes on the form of God. The teacher
perfects himself by his own spiritual practice and then becomes
qualified to instruct others. We read that, “one is required to
approach a Brahmana (one who has realised Brahman) for the
mystical apprehension of the ultimate reality” (S.13). The function
of giving apt directions is many times assigned to Agni who is the
intermediary between men and gods. Yaska explains the word Agni
                                   139
Pathway to God in the Vedas



thus: He is called Agni because he is Agrani, a leader, one who leads
or guides man; अग्रं नीयते - अग्रं+नी=अग्रणी (5.14). Agni leads a man
upto the supreme goal (अग्र) of human life. Thus he performs the
function of a spiritual teacher, who leads his disciples. “Oh Agni,
proceed ahead of those who wish to reach God; you are the eye of
men and gods. Those who have befriended the learned men from the
family of Bhrgus, want to sacrifice to gods. Let their journey towards
God be free of troubles” (S.15). In fact Agni is said to have been
assigned this task by gods themselves. “You are the one who extends
invitation to gods to attend sacrifices.    Gods   have   themselves
appointed you to this post for the benefit of humanity” (5.16). “Pray,
may divine Agni kindly lead me to that region where abide the sages
who by initiation and penance have realised the highest” (5.17). We
have to note that it is the celestial Agni who undertakes this
responsibility and not the Agni on the earth which is harnessed by
men. “This (terrestrial Agni) is led by men (नीयते नृभि :), while that
 (celestial Agni) leads man from this material world to the spiritual
 world, (नयति   असौ); therefore, the two Agnis, even though they bear
 the same name, are engaged in distinct types of activities” (S.18).

      There is no end to poems where Agni is mentioned as a
 spiritual guide. Thus we read: “For felicity and bliss men resort to
 the guidance of divine Agni, who has realised the truth, who is
 endowed with the vision of all things and who is ever ready to
 respond to the call of devotees” (S.19). We may refer to two verses
 addressed to Agni. One says that Agni is sitting at the seat of
 worship and acts as a giver of riches (S.20). The other stresses the
 point that Agni shows the way to    gain wealth, to those who know
 his secret place and sing his praises (S.21). The purport of another
 hymn is that Agni is bliss incarnate and radiates bliss (S.22). It is
 not possible to be free from the debt of such a teacher. The most
 one can do is to follow him with utmost faith, bearing in mind that
 the path shown by him is the surest and the best. One should do
 obeisance to him and leave the rest to him.
     2. Characteristics of a Spiritual Teacher. We find in the
 Vedas a good account of the marks of a spiritual teacher. A glance
 at these will show that some of them are moral and some mystical.

                                  140
                                                   The Spiritual Teacher



     (a) Moral Characteristics. Agni who is often deemed to be a
teacher, displays utter humility. “Inspite of His high status, He has
no inkling of pride in Him; He is lovable in manners. Though hot
himself, He radiates not heat but cool comfort. He graces the
sacrificial hall by His presence” (S.23). Pride is said to be a great
enemy of man. A man’s achievements come to nothing because of
his pride. If he is vain and proud of his abilities he is likely to hurt
others’ feelings. Agni is also described as having devotion to truth.
In one psalm it is stated: “those ancient seers who are visualisers
of real Mantras (Satya Mantra) lived happily in the company of gods.
They created Usas, 1.6. they were endowed with illumination as well
as with the experience of hidden divine light (Sun). They had one
common ideal and therefore, they saw eye to eye with one another
and never quarrelled among themselves and never transgressed the
laws of gods” (S.24). We learn about truthfulness of Agni from Rsi,
Dirghatamas Aucathya. “Agni is the first one to teach observance
of holy laws. In the clouds and in the forests He is fearsome; but
He is now established at the place of sacrifice. He is all-knowing,
is an exponent ofthe eternal laws and is truth incarnate” (S.25). Then
there is the virtue of sincerity. Sincere in their praises of Rta and
sincere in their meditation, Angirasas, the sons of Agni, exhibit this
virtue (S.26).

     (b) Mystical Characteristics. Meditation on the divine name is
the second nature of all those who want to enter the divine precincts.
“They inhabit the same exalted region and protect the source of the
eternal moral law. And they meditate on the secret name” (S.27). “To
you, who are wise and omniscient, are hereby tendered the secret
words of guidance. I, a devout seeker, adore you with ardent prayers
and eulogical songs” (S.28). Here also concentration on the divine
name is stressed. They worship the name with all their minds and
with all their hearts. By their direct experience of reality, they get
the power to instruct others. In a remarkable passage are depicted
all these characteristics of a spiritual teacher. Agni is here described
as the first to be worshipped during sacrifices (Medhesu prathama).
He is all bliss and spreads bliss; he excels in moral perfection
(Sadhu). Besides, he has the cause and means and power to enable
his disciplies to have a vision of God. This, in fact, is the first and

                                  141
Pathway to God in the Vedas



foremost function of a spiritual teacher. “How should we give
offering to Agni who is immortal and is a propagator of righteous-
ness? He surely has the capacity to fulfil the task of bringing the
immortals in the midst of the mortals. What kind of prayer should
we sing, so that it will be accepted by the great Agni?                 His
understanding is beyond measure; he is the effector of perfection. He
rides a wonderful chariot just like Mitra. Those who want to go in
submission to the gods, initially invoke Agni himself. Let that Agni
who is the greatest among men and who is a terror to his enemies
take us under his wings and accept our prayers. O Agni, be you
pleased to appreciate the supplications of the devotees who are
courageous and full of purity. The learned Gautama sang the praises
of this righteous and omniscient Agni. He was pleased with the
worshipper, and in turn blessed him with riches, purity and plenty”
(S.29).   Agni   is   also described   as   “possessing   brilliance,   all-
enveloping power and beatitude, and as being free from ignorance.
He inspires men in just the correct way and leads them to the highest
bliss” (S.30). “Those who really know, visualise the smoke coming
out of the mouth of Agni”, says the sage Kabandha (S.31). Smoke
is mentioned as one of the harbingers of spiritual experience by the
Svetaswatara Upanisad (II.11), at a later date. Perhaps the present
Vedic lyric contains a seed thereof. Agni, who is generally taken as
a minister of gods, knows both gods and men. In fact it is because
he has such knowledge that he can lead man to God. “Agni knows
the origin of gods and he knows the secrets of men. He is the
bestower of wealth. Pray to him, for he illumines the path which leads
to God” (S.32). It is noteworthy that Agni is said to be the knower
of secrets in the hearts of men. The saint of Umadi used to say that
however secret        your thoughts and actions, the saint has an easy
access to them. It is the knowledge of Reality that confers leadership
on a person. He becomes a        teacher; his age, caste or religion are
absolutely irrelevant to the spiritual height he has reached. A story
goes that Angirasa addressed the elders as ‘sons’, on which they
became angry. But he justified his action by saying that he was a
composer of Mantras (Mantrakrt) and, as such, a person in authority.
The gods later on corroborated his view (S.33). The same story is
referred to by Manu also (Manusmrti II. 151-153). He alone who

                                    142
                                                  The Spiritual Teacher



possesses spiritual knowledge can disseminate it. “The Brahmacarin
was born before knowledge; in fact it was through him that the sacred
knowledge of Brahman became manifest” (S.34). Or, “Let that pious
one who nourishes speech (Gandharva) reveal to us the immortal
treasure situated in the heart. Its three forms are hidden in the depth
of the heart and he who knows this, becomes the father of the father”
(5.35). In another verse given by the sage Nabhaka Kanva, the
learned man is said to be knowing the mysterious names (S.36). It
tells us how a spiritual teacher receives the divine names for being
imparted to the disciples. The names do not spring from his
imagination; nor are they arbitrarily chosen from among a host of
names of deities just to be passed on to the aspirants. He actually
visualises them during meditation.

     3. Functions of a Spiritual Teacher. We shall now discuss
how a spiritual teacher discharges three types of functions in relation
to his disciples — psychological, moral and mystical. In the first
place he frees the disciples from sorrow and fear; secondly, he
forgives their moral lapses and wipes out their sins; in the third
place, he guides them to attain to Godhead.

     (a)   Psychological Function. Coming to the psychological
aspect, we read that the Guru wards off an aspirant’s calamities,
becomes his friend and assures his welfare. In a beautiful orison,
which heaps virtue upon virtue on Agni, we read: “Oh you great
knower, Agni, your rays are ever alert, speedy, pleasant, devoid of
idleness, givers of shelter to devotees, untiring and protecting. Let
them come together and protect us” (S.37). “We praise that shining
and powerful God, annihilator of enemies. We invite him from his
grand abode with these eulogical prayer-songs. Let him put an end
to our sorrows and sufferings and carry us across our difficulties”
(S.38). Agni is looked upon as a solid support on whom one can ever
rely, so that once his friendship is assured, a man needs no other
confidant, not even a son. “Oh prolifically kindled Agni, devotees
are pleased to call you to the place of worship. Your form is beautiful
and soothing to the eye. If you, Oh truthful one, are a constant
companion, I do not wish to have even a son; let us not be forsaken
and forgotten in our old age” (S.39). Incidentally, it seems that right

                                  143
Pathway to God in the Vedas



from the Vedic times, man was very much worried about his security
and well-being in his old age. This kind of worry is not entirely a
result of modern industrialised world! “Oh Agni, on whom do you
look as your friend? Who offers you sacrifice? What is your real
nature and what is your abode? Indeed, you are a friend of one and
all, you are our great ally. Those who love you, get your love in sweet
reciprocation” (S.40). The spiritual teacher does not merely perform
the function of eliminating pain; he positively confers happiness on
the disciple. “I hand over this worshipper, Oh gods in heaven, to your
custody along with the treasure bestowed upon him by the great seer
Agni. Let him follow you to that highest heaven” (S.41).
      (b) Moral Function. Along with psychological ills, moral evils
are also whisked away by the master. The aspirant pleads his
 innocence and exclaims that if any evil act is committed by him
 inadvertantly, he should     be   excused   for   that.   “We   have   not
 committed any great sin secretly against you, Oh Vasus, nor have
 we openly perpetrated any provocation on gods. Let us not be
 understood as wearing the body of falsehood. For freedom and
 felicity, we offer our homage to Aditi, who is endowed with the all-
 creative power’ (5.42). “Oh Indra, you know how to cleanse the
 mind of foul propensities. You resemble the sun who remains
 uncontaminated ever and on” (S.43). The preceptor has the power
 to weed out the evil tendencies of his disciples only because he is
 purity incarnate. Is there any one who is more immaculate than the
 all-clarifying Agni? Hence he has been elevated as the holy one in
 every way. “Agni is the holiest among the holy men (Suci-vrata-
 tama). He is a virtuous sage and illumined seer. He becomes more
 radiant when He is invoked with ardour” sings Viripa Angirasa
 (S.44). The spiritual guide after flushing out the dirt from those who
 repent for having done wrong, leads them to greater prosperity. Here
 is a Mantra where the devotee prays not merely for excellence but
 for right intellect which is the basis of morality. “When your
 worshippers are over-powered by foes, external and internal, you
 tush to rescue them at the critical moment and uproot all the
 enemies. Oh omnipresent God, forgive us for going astray and
 neglecting our duty. We had to make a long journey to reach upto
 you. You are our torch-bearer and father; you yourself are our

                                    144
                                                       The Spiritual Teacher



benefactor. You can raise even an ignorant man to the position of
an illumined seer (Rsikrt martyanam). Accept our songs which we
are singing from the depth of our hearts. Lead us on the righteous
way and endow us with spotless mind” (S.45). It is known that a
man who feels real remorse for his derelictions, confesses them and
asks for forgiveness. The history of religious literature is full of
‘confessions’. He alone hides his wrong doings, who does not have
any qualms of conscience. Real penitence is not so simple a matter.
If one repents and still retains whatever was acquired by wrong
means, it is not real repentance. As Hamlet’s uncle realises, crime
is not scrubbed clean so long as one retains the effects. Could he
be pardoned for the foul murder of his brother? His sorrow-striken
words are:

             “That cannot be; since I am still possessed

             Of those effects for which I did the murder—
             My crown, mine own ambition, and my queen.”

                                           (Act III, scene 3)

Self-reproach    begins   with   giving   up   all   that   is   acquired   by
unrighteous means. The next step is a determination never to repeat
the ugly crime and sticking doggedly to that resolution in the face
of all temptations. Here is a petition by a regretful person. “Oh Agni,
be pleased with our libations and recommend us to Mitra, Varuna
and Indra. Forgive us for our faults. Let Aryama and Aditi grant us
amnesty for our misconduct” (S.46). We find utterances which depict
typical human ways. There are aspirants who are full of self-reproof
for having taken resort to rotten ways. They now ask for remission.
But there are yet others who want to escape the responsibility (and
consequent punishment) for their misbehaviour. “Whatever filthy act
we may have committed by offending the gods through words, mind
and body, it is only the result of seductive inducement of the corrupt
bystanders. Oh Vasus, place the burden of that sin on that evil-doer”
(S.47). Here is a very important suggestion that faults are committed
not merely by hands but also by mind and speech. They are subtler
ones, but they are equally, if not more, dangerous. In fact, the sin
committed by the mind is a real cancer, because it remains
undetected and eats into the vitals of an aspirant.


                                   145
Pathway to God in the Vedas



     (c) Mystical Function. The most important function of a
Spiritual teacher is to guide the devout in their spiritual pilgrimage.
Agni is usually installed as a helper and is said to inspire the
devotees. “He enlivens the minds of even those of dull intellect. He
is established on this earth as a watch and ward officer. Let him not
forsake us. Oh Agni, let our minds be ever fixed on you” (S.48). “I
chant the glories of Agni, the possessor of revealed divine words
(Rgmiya); in turn, let him reveal the heavenly beings to me. Let him,
who 30075 the sacrificial halls both of mortals and immortals, be
our constant companion and drive off the evil-minded ones” (S.49).
The function of being an intermediary is again brought into bold
relief in another ode. “&5 among men, so among gods, you are the
protector of the treasures. You are verily a natural bund. Streams
of water flow round about you” (S.50). The spiritual guide is the
source of auspiciousness. “Oh lustrous Agni, we are your devotees,
bless us; for all your blessings come true. We are just kids, be
indulgent    to   us   and    remain   with    us.    Your very       presence   is
reassuring” (S.51). Agni covers a wide range of functions. “He is
a real knower, excellent administrator and seer’ (5.52). “He is the
priest who recites the prayers of the Rgveda at a sacrifice (Hotr),
as well as one of the sixteen officiating priests (Potr). He is one of
 the chief officiating priests at a Soma sacrifice (Nestr) as well as
 fire itself; He is also an advisor (Prasastr). He-is                   again the
officiating priest who recites Yajurveda (Adhvaryu). He is one of
the four priests at a Soma sacrifice (Brahma) as well as the
sacrificer (Grhapati). Offer fuel to this Fire-god and he will run your
errands and bring gods to your sacrifice” (S.53). Agni is this, that
and what not at the time of a sacrifice; he alone runs the whole show.
So   too    the   spiritual    teacher    takes      upon   himself    the   whole
responsibility of his disciple. To him the student prays, “Oh you all-
knowing Agni, lead us to the holy treasures by the straight path. We
slip and stumble. Be by our side so that we maintain balance. We
bow to     you” says Agastya Maitravaruni (S.54).


      4. Qualifications of a Disciple. Granted that one can attain to
the status of a Guru only after arduous penance, but to become a
disciple also is not a simple matter. A pupil has to lead a very


                                         146
                                                           The Spiritual Teacher



disciplined        life.   The   relation between   them   is   reciprocal.   The
disciple receives with pious and grateful reverence what the teacher
gives with fond and loving care. Let us have a look at the sketch
of this relation. The Vedas spell out the figure of an humble student
and also of a celebrated teacher. The learner has to keep in mind
that a long life of mighty efforts and self-denials has made the
teacher what he is. “Hark, approach with humility these fervent
devotees of God. They have undergone trying austerities and intense
penance” (5.55). And what does he do? “Day after day this Agni,
who glows on the background of the dawns, who leads a contingent
of the learned, awakens all. Kindled by the devotees and deities, he
shines by his immence lustre and after staving in the door of
darkness, opens the way for light” (5.56). Vijayadasa,               a Kannada
mystic-poet, expressing this feeling, uses the same expression.
“Today, the door of the palace of spiritual experience has opened
out to me’. His Guru had given him the key to open the door.' How
does one draw near such a teacher? We are familiar with the famous
Upanisadic description:

तदि ज्ञानार्थं   स गुरुमेवाभि गच्छेत|्‌
समि त्पाणि ः श्रोत्रि यं ब्रह्मनि ष्ठम्
                                     ।।‌ (Mundakopanisad, 1.2.12)
(For knowing that Reality, he should approach, with sacrificial sticks
in hand, a teacher, who is well-versed in Vedic knowledge and is
absorbed in Brahman). The idea is already present in the Vedas.
“The sparkling Maruts paid respect to you by offering ghee for three
years. After that they received befitting names which conferred on
them authority to perform sacrifices” (S.57). Surely these are the
names on which they meditated. A real pupil is he who has an
earnestness to be free from the bonds of Samsara. The cow has a
mind to suckle the calf and the calf has always a desire to be
nourished. While the teacher has a will to teach, the student, a
determination to learn. “The cow lows gently and licks the head of
her calf who has his eyes only half open. Then she directs him to
her udder which is full with milk” (S.58). The disciple should also

1   R.D. Ranade, Pathway to God in Kannada Literature, Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan,
    Chowpatty, Bombay 400 007, 1960, P. 152


                                          147
Pathway to God in the Vedas



be prepared to lead an arduous life, and not conduct himself in a
humdrum style. He has to accept the restrictions imposed by the
teacher. “Do not rely entirely on your powers which are limited; cast
off your egoistic efforts, be intent on possessing the word carrying
spiritual power. Along with your friends stick to the moral path”
(S.59). Or, “I wish to have a heart-to-heart alliance with you.           Set
your hearts in tune with mine. I shall establish a rapport with you.
Let your behaviour be conciliatory with mine” (S.60). The student
has to bear in mind that many great persons have laboured in this
way. “The heaven and earth have come into my                fold;     so have
Saraswati, Indra and Agni. Oh Saraswati let us be prosperous”
(S.61). It clearly indicates that a student has to be very cautious so
that he remains worthy of the teacher. He has to give up vagrancies
and be prepared to accept the role of a follower. This is what is
implied in the popular Kannada song: “If you desire to come to us
you have to accomodate to our way of life. Moreover, if you want
to invite us to your place, even then you have to accomodate to our
way of life”. Thus in both the cases, it is the disciple who has to
be in conformity with the teacher’s instructions.

      We have to remember that trodding the spiritual                 path is
hazardous and the discipline expected of the            traveller is     very
exacting at times. Kabir for example, has             thrown 9      stubborn
challenge to those who wished to follow him:

कबि रा खड़ा बाजार में लि ये लुकाठी हाथ।
जो घर फूँके आपना चले हमारे साथ ।।
(Kabir, on one occasion took his stand in the market place with a
fire-brand in his hand and said: He alone need follow me who will
Set fire to his house, property and possessions).' Once tested, the
student receives the teacher’s attention in abundance.

       5. A Disciple’s Indebtedness to the Guru. Among the basic
needs of man may be counted the need for security. Not mere
physical security would do; man wants an assurance that his
minimum basic needs will always be met. If shortage of food tells


1   See R.D. Ranade, Pathway to God in Hindi Literature, Pp.295-296


                                     148
                                                 The Spiritual Teacher



on the health, the fear of shortage does no less so. Elephants in the
deep jungles of Africa have been reported to have suffered from
heart ailments when they witnessed that the forests from which they
were getting food-supply, were being cut down. We do not know
whether they had really interpreted the disappearance of trees as
portentous of shortage of food in future; but it is clear that there
definitely was some stress and strain which affected the big animals.
The phrase ‘in search of new pastures’ literally suggests a tendency
to seek an assured supply. Psychologists have found out that little
babies have shown less intense fear on seeing unkown and fearful
objects, when they are in the arms of the mother. The first thing a
young child would do, when terrified, is to clutch at the mother’s
garment. What is true of children, is true of the grown-ups. Man
seeks something to fall back upon, a nest where his anxieties are
completely taken over or at least shared. He wants     security of a
house, a father, a teacher. “Keep ascending and do not revert back.
I shall give you vitality and strength. Mount this chariot which
bestows bliss and immortality. You yourself will occupy the position
of a teacher when ripe. Do not weep for the departed; for that will
distract you. Come hither and advance to light from darkness. I shall
hold your hands” (S.62). We need not be stunned at the self-
confidence of the Guru, for the cardinal principle of teacherhood is
the Guru’s conviction of the potency of his own spiritual experi-
ences. The height he has reached raises him above the common run
of men. “Firmly establish herein your life-breath, your vital airs,
your very life and mind. I shall save you from the eye of the evil
spirit and elevate you by means of the divine word” (S.63). The
disciple for ever remembers that the teacher has straightened his
mind and brought about his spiritual regeneration. The teacher
Prajapatya YaksmanaSana exclaims, “Oh you ailing one, I have
freed you from the chains of death and reclaimed you. You are as
if reborn. Come here, you have gained clear vision. A whole life of
bliss is ahead. There is no place for anxiety whatsoever” (S.64).
      The preceptor who is more than a mother to his disciple, sees
that the latter’s vulnerable points are not exposed to the world and
that he is fully protected. “I cover your tender parts with a coat of
arms. Let Soma spread the cloth of immortality on you. Let Varuna

                                 149
Pathway to God in the Vedas



make that garment impregnable. Let the God rejoice in your victory”
(S.65). “Whosoever intends to harm me—whether an enemy outside
or inside — may God discomfit him. Prayer to the divine is my inner
coat of 11911, ब्रह्म वर्म ममांतरम्‌ (5.66). It is only the Samaveda which
makes a significant addition: Divine grace is my inner coat of mail
शर्म वर्म ममांतरम्‌ (5.67). With such words the teacher launches his
disciple on the religious course and wishes him godspeed. Wherein
lies the secret of this supremacy of the teacher? He reveals it by
pointing out that it is from divinity itself that he gets this authority.
“Varuna disclosed to me, who am possessed of a rich intellect, that
the cow has twenty-one names. He gave me an insight into reality
so that I should initiate my disciples” (S.68). It is no wonder that
pupils, who have come into contact with teachers having such a high
spiritual status and wielding a transfiguring influence, have them-
selves grown into sovereign seers themselves. The highest aim
would be to have the assurance from the Lord himself. “To you shall
I offer my invocation and prayers as you readily appear in response
to our call. To your devotees you are like a fountain in the desert,
Oh ancient sovereign Agni” (S.69). “Like men troubled by the
blazing heat of the sun, taking refuge under the shade of a tree we
 have approached you, Oh Agni. You look lustrous like gold” (S.70).
 Another sage addresses Agni with the same confidence but with a
 touch of agnostic feeling. “That omniscient and adorable Agni brings
 to us treasures that are open as well as those that are hidden. As
 waves in a powerful water-fall are difficult to be crossed, so it is
 difficult to understand this holy Agni’, says Sobhari Kanva” (5.71).
The devotee looks upon his teacher as one who is bound to lead him
 homeward and unerringly show the treasures. What is meant by the
 word ‘treasure’ or the word, ‘home’? Treasure means spiritual bliss
 or Atmananda. The master initiates the disciple into a life that takes
him to bliss, and to his real home—which is God. Jnanesvara has
 characterised the highest station to be aimed at, as Yat or the
 original home. The teacher does not merely direct the way, but
 actually takes him there. “Oh Pusan, conduct us to that learned one
 who will initiate and reveal to us the celestial wealth. Let us have
 the company of Pian who will be our pilot and take us to our homes”

                                    150
                                                    The Spiritual Teacher



(S.72). Will not the darkness wear away when we reach home, sweet
home? Or, “Oh Agni, provide us with a resting place which will be
our real refuge. You are the deity who will steer our way” (S.73).
The sense of indebtedness weighs down a pupil to surrender. “Pay
your respects to that great Indra, whose grace enabled our forefa-
thers, the Angirasas, to find the sure way to the Godhead” (S.74).
“Agni is my father, brother and my kin. He is my steadfast friend.
As people worship the radiant orb of the sun, so do we broadcast our
prayer-notes” (S.75). No true student will ever want to be separated
from his teacher. ‘‘Like a horse yoked to a chariot, deliberately have
I joined myself to the pole from which I never seek disentanglement.
He who knows the path is guiding me and he will carry me straight
to my destiny” (S.76). “I shall never exchange you Oh Indra, the
wielder of thunderbolt; I shall never exchange you for a price, not
even for thousands of silver pieces. You yourself are an invaluable
treasure and I cannot sell you even if I get a rich fund in return”, vows
Pragatha Kanva (5.77). Incidentally, this reminds us of a similar
expression by that famous pilgrim, Bunyan. “A voice would exclaim
within him “ Sell Him, Sell Him, Sell Him, but Bunyan replied “I
will not, I will not, for ten thousands of worlds.”! It is astonishing
to note how a man’s baser emotions refuse to be rinsed clean even
when he is blessed with some spiritual experiences. His passions
entice him at moments and try to charm him away from a life in God.
The very fact that the alluring voice came from within Bunyan’s mind
signifies that it is nothing but the solicitation of flesh, the bait of
desires, witchery of man’s own lower self. But the mystic who has
a firm grip over God will not part with Him or with his spiritual
teacher even at the cost of his life.

     6. Desire to serve the Master. The next point which will
engage our attention is the earnest desire of the disciple to serve his
master. We may note here how roots of ideas developed after a lapse
of centuries are found in the Vedas, how a simple suggestion thrown
by means of a word has been exfoliated by later thinkers or how
disregarding the words and stray ideas, the implication of a Mantra

1   Quoted in ‘Mysticism in Maharashtra’ by R.D. Ranade, Aryabhushan Press,
    Pune, 1933, P. 487


                                    151
Pathway to God in the Vedas



is grasped and shaped into a doctrine in                       ages to come. We shall
consider here a group of verses which together provide a basis for
the Gita verse:

तद्वि द्धि      प्रणि पातेन परि प्रश्नेन सेवया।
उपदेक्ष्यन्ति       ते ज्ञानं ज्ञानि नः तत्त्वदर्शि नः i) (IV. 34)

(Learn this by humble obeisance, by enquiry and service. The
learned, who have visualised the Truth, will impart this divine
 knowledge to you). It is no wonder that the Vedic seers salute Agni
 and other gods oft and on. They had to take a submissive attitude
 before the forces of nature. Moreover, Agni was of tremendous use
 both for cooking food and for getting warmth. From a great number
 of psalms which speak of bowing before the deities, and especially
 Agni, we gather an impression that the Vedic people were always
 on their knees! Let us see how this attitude of Pranipata or saluting
is exemplified in many a verse. “He it is who is dear and who
 eminently deserves to be approached and who has become the priest
 at the sacrifices. Oh Agni, you are shining in your habitation, we
 salute you. We go on our knees with reverence and go near you”
 (S.78). “The devotees along with their spouses bended their knees
 as a mark of surrender to that adorable Agni. They worshipped Agni
 with their bodies emaciated by penance and won his friendship”
 (S.79). The sages are in a pliant mood, no doubt; but it is                  equally
 important that Agni by his own superiority commands that frame of
 mind. Agni, the teacher, has an overwhelming personality which
 naturally results in the disciple becoming very humble «fore him.
 He who stands on a high pedestal is looked up to as a leader; and
 he that stands on a low ground, becomes a follower. The mode of
 relationship between them is called the ascendance-submission
 relationship            and    is typical     of the teacher-disciple     bond.   The
 disciple’s eyes are as if dazzled by the splendour of the teacher. “The
 seers are awe-inspiring (घोर). We salute them. Their eyes and mind
 are full of truth. To you, who are an architect of the universe, we
 pay homage; protect us” (S.80). The greatness of Agni is said to lie
 not in his external appearance, but in his truthfulness. After all, it
 is the quality of the mind and not the outward impressiveness that

                                                  152
                                                         The Spiritual Teacher



determines one’s excellence. As there is Pranipata (salutation), so
there is Pariprasna (a spirit of enquiry) too! “Ask Agni himself, for
He is coming along.         He knows all, He is omniscient. Encomiums
are addressed to Him. He is the overlord of all laws, all sacrifices,
all powers and all valours. He is a sovereign of the brave” (S.81).
Consequently he alone will be in a position to satisfy the curiosity
of the enquirer. Asking questions and giving answers is not a simple
affair in spiritual matters. As the teacher must be fortified by
spiritual knowledge, the student must have cultivated virtues. An
unbreakable bond will be established only between such minds. “If
any query is to be made, one has to go to Agni. But this*task must
be undertaken only by a proper person. Only a sage can reveal his
own mind to the Fire-god. He listens to each and every word of Agni
with   modesty and seeks        protection     from Agni’s righteousness”
(5.82). In the wake of Pariprasna follows Seva (service). “Serve that
Agni before whom the sinful tremeble. He bestows thousands of
gifts. Look upon that service as an aid to your worshipful meditation
and    serve    him   whole-heartedly”     (S.83).   “Your glory knows no
bounds, you are an embodiment of ancient wisdom. To you, who are
ablaze,   the    devotees   with   illumined    intellects,   render service”
(S.84). “We are ignorant, no doubt, but we serve the wise Agni. He
has a delightful form. Let him talk to the gods about our oblations”’
(S.85). The aspirant is always eager to get initiated at the hands of
a great spiritual teacher; for, by that he turns a corner on his Godward
journey. Perhaps he had been treading a wrong path or taking a
detour. Per chance he was stranded at cross-roads. At such times
the teacher holds his hand and brings him on to the royal road to
divinity. Hence the eagerness of an aspirant to be under the tutelege
of a magnanimous soul. “ I beg of you to bestow on me valour and
a word of wisdom on which I would meditate; with its strength you
blessed Yati. Bhrgu and Praskanva had gathered that fortune” prays
Medhyatithi Kanva (S.86). “By your grace all the four corners of the
mind get illumined. Pray, teach me. This docile child craves for
enlightenment. We will fill the air with praises and will inhabit a
dwelling which is rich with bounties received from you” (S.87). We
thus see that the spiritual teacher is looked upon as helping his
disciple in matters worldly, and in matters, spiritual. If a disciple


                                     153
Pathway to God in the Vedas



is Sincere in his devotional pursuits the teacher guarantees him
material welfare also. That is why Agni is requested to grant both
these wealths. The Vedic people cared for fullness of life; but
spiritual progress was their prime objective. Thus we read: “Hasten
to us, Oh Agni, and be a priest at the sacrifice. May the whole of
heaven and earth serve you. Carry our worship to gods so that our
minds become pure” (S.88). Or, “You are omniscient, O Lord of all
wealth. You know the right direction. Pray guide me. You are the
presiding deity of all ancient learning. Kindly instruct me” (S.89).

       7. Spiritual Heritage. We now pass on to the next point, that
of the importance of spiritual heritage. What we inherit—physiologi-
cally, psychologically, economically, socially, morally or spiritually
is the runway of our flight. Those with a rich heritage get a smooth
take off. Some aspirants may make boundless progress. Some may
remain at almost the starting-point, others may deteriorate; that all
depends on their own determination and effort. If one belongs to a
tradition, a part of preliminary discipline is cultivated without any
hardship. A carpenter’s son knows how to handle a saw without
being taught. The function which habit performs in an individual’s
life is performed by tradition in an institution. We may refer to the
wonderful assistance we receive from our habits in our daily life.
It is impossible to improve upon William James in this regard and
we may summarise his account for our purpose.’ Habit, he says,
simplifies the movements required to achieve a given result, makes
them more accurate and diminishes fatigue. We need not think over
the performance for it becomes automatic. We just follow the habit.
Our conscious attention is not demanded as routine is followed
almost mechanically. The brain need not strain itself every time, as
many actions are relegated to the lower centres. Without much effort
the movements go on in a regular manner. A fully fashioned
character where habits are ingrained will be very efficient. James’
observations are applicable to an institution where traditions and
customs are well-built. There is no new thinking on every occasion;
tracks are laid, actions roll on, and duties are carried out

1   The Principles of Psychology, Vol.I, Dover Publication, Inc. 920 Broadway,
    New York 10, 1950, Pp. 112 ff.


                                     154
                                                 The Spiritual Teacher



unknowingly. If an aspirant is born in such a society or is initiated
into such a spiritual lineage, he has innumerable advantages. In
Vedic times, for example, such a person would be familiar with the
materials and methods of performing various sacrifices, the Mantras
to be uttered, the amount of time to be given to different rituals and
so on. In a spiritual line the modes of prayer or worship, the method
of meditation, the devotional songs to be sung, are all handed over
ready-made to the disciple. What is most important, however, is the
strength and the backing of the power with which the atmosphere
is surcharged. The disciple follows the footsteps of the teacher. Such
a bond is a result of meditation of all the great realised souls
belonging to the school. The merit earned by them is the sturdy
psychological, moral and spiritual backbone of the whole heirarchy.
“Rsi Atharvan was the pioneer path-finder, he prepared the way by
means of a sacrifice. After him the law-abiding sun-god was born.
Then Usana Kavya brought out the cows of light. We worship that
immortal governor of the universe” (S.90). We read here how the
tradition was built by great seers coming in succession. We may note
the significance of the three names mentioned in this Rk. Atharvan
is said to have first brought down fire from the heaven, offered Soma
and recited prayers. He is also the one who learnt from Brahma and
then taught the Brahmavidya. So he is the beginner of a mode of
worship as well as an early teacher of mankind. Now it is clear that
the rules set out by him will have to be practised. Who but the sun-
god, the mighty strider, can do it rigorously? So he is mentioned
next. He, as if, strengthens the rules by practising them. Rules and
regulations, laws and injunctions atrophy if they are not put into
practice. What is remarkable about the sun is his unhampered
performance of duty. Next the name of USana Kavya is found. USana
or Sukra is a very wise person who brought together theory and
practice, for he is known as a writer on civil and religious law. Thus
is a system perfected—a rule is set, it is practised and is
theoretically justified. The importance of belonging to such a well-
knit group can not be over-estimated. “Meditating intensely on your
laws and strictly adhering to your injunctions, sages come to realise
the seven heavenly streams and discover the way to unlock the doors
leading unto divine treasures. The wise Sarama found out the infinite

                                 155
Pathway to God in the Vedas



expanse of light, which made it possible for humanity to bathe in
the soft raindrops of God’s mercy” (5.91). Rsi Yajiia Prajapatya
talks of seven god-men who had an intuitive apprehension of the way
of the ancient seers (S.92). It is imperative that rule of law is kept
unhampered. “Do not hesitate, run up, partake in the libation mixed
with milk. Keep unbroken the thread of sacrifice which joins us to
the times long, long past” (5.93). Requests to presiding deities not
to snap the link are numerous.

     8. Prayer for Grace. When a person takes a submissive role,
the attitude culminates    in surrender. This    mental   make-up    is
reflected in a number of verses, an assorted bunch of which may
be considered. First of all the disciple wishes to welcome the deities
at his place. “You have been kindled, Oh Agni, please escort gods
to the house of the patron who is waiting in all eagerness to offer
gifts” (S.94). Grace is expected to come from all quarters. As such
a galaxy of divine beings is invited. Agni is extolled as the first
functionary so far as the institution of Yajna is concerned. “You first
stood before Matarisvan and Vivasvan for the sake of a sacrifice.
When the heavens and the earth trembled, you bore that calamity
and saw the worship through” (5.95). “Oh ASwins, come to us just
as you have been going to Vivasvan for partaking Soma juice, or
just as you have been visiting Manu” (S.96). In order to prove
himself to be an honourable host, he talks highly of his own
connections. “The sages Dadhyan, Angiras, Priyamedha, Kanva,
Atri and Manu know my family. My ancesters belong to the
fraternity of holy men; they in turn are associated with a divine
lineage. I praise Agni and Indra in a manner observed by them and
bow to them” (S.97). Looking on himself as a dutiful and deserving
disciple, he addresses a league of gods. “Destroyers of adversaries,
Oh three and thirty deities, your patriarch is Manu himself. I pay
my respects to you. Be pleased to guard us closely. Keep us in touch
with Manu” (S.98).

     So intense is the desire of the seeker to be blessed, that instead
of waiting for the multitude of the celestial personages to be pleased,
he turns to the good old friend, Agni. “Protect us from all the hostile
demons and fortify us in our struggle for plenitude. You are the

                                  156
                                                    The Spiritual Teacher



nearest among gods and a trusted friend. I pray to you alone”, runs
a poem by Bharga Pragatha (S.99). Another Mantra reads: “Oh Agni,
you are one with Varuna and Mitra. You love the devotee who abides
by the ancient law with deep faith. In the end he receives your grace”
(S.100). The disciple knows that it is impossible to reach the final
goal by himself. Well might he display rectitude in his behaviour
in sticking to the moral path, but the denouement of the whole
performance depends on grace. “Let our path be easy to traverse and
let it be full of bounties. With you by our side, our fortune will flower,
do not forsake us, Oh Agni, take us under your wings” (S.101).

       The spiritual journey is not all that smooth. The outside world
is filled with attractive objects and enticing temptations. The inside
world is fraught with lustful passions and seductive vices. The lone
walker on this road — and every one has to walk alone — is
frightened and    longs for a safe conduct.        He therefore needs
invulnerability and begs a boon from Agni. “Oh Agni, you can
oversee the universe. Be kind to me. Guard me against dangers from
all the four quarters with the power of your wisdom. You are
immortal, I am your friend; bestow a long life on me” (S.102). “Oh
Lord of prowess, we entirely depend upon you for support just as
old and infirm men always depend upon a staff. We pray you to be
with us” (S.103). The sages know that what they have undertaken,
is an uphill task and hence they have to requisition a good deal of
energy and enthusiasm. They therefore prefer an appeal to the
deities for vigour. “This is our petition to you; cover us by your grace
and save us from sins. Burn all evil in the world. Grant us vitality
and make us self-sufficient” (S.104). Thus an aspirant wants support
from his teacher, but he equally wants to put in effort himself. What
is really wonderful is his assurance to the teacher that he will not
transgress the moral code laid down for him. “Oh Indra, remove all
dangers from our way; for we are firm in our resolve to follow this
course of Soma sacrifice” (S.105). What does a spiritual teacher
expect from his disciples if not such steadfastness?

Pathway to God in the Vedas

                                            166
                           CHAPTER VI




                   The Name of God


     1. The Name as a Royal Road to God. Strife is the essence
of the world and war is the father of all things; whatever is at war
with itself, is bound to unite and that which breaks into fragments
will come together, declares Herakleitos, the Greek philosopher.
This gives the greatest assurance to all aspirants whose constant
difficulty is that the mind runs in all directions and defies efforts
at concentration. There are forces of our cupidities that keep the
mind under their influence and make it pursue external objects. Be
it as it may. There is also an opposite force of the divine element
in us which tries to wean the mind from passions and lead it God-
ward. Thus there goes on a relentless war between these elements
in us. But there need not be any cause for anxiety. What are passions
after all? They are expressions of some energy. And while yet the
energies are under the sway of the centrifugal force of sense and
are turning away from God, a centripetal force of uttering the name
of God, can be applied to bring them together and focus them on God.
Thus out of the opposite tensions, as Herakleitos would say, there
can be brought about a harmony.

     The Vedic Rsis have much to give us regarding the place of
the name of God in spiritual life. We shall see how they deem the
name to be the most important way to reach God and note the
significance they have attached to speech. After taking a brief survey
of characteristics of the name we shall look into the power of the
name and its function of revealing God to man. The syllable Aum
has a unique place in the process of meditation though other Mantras
are not less efficacious. We shall then study the observation of the
sages regarding time and place of meditation. Though the ideal is
meditation purely for the love of God, man does expect some reward
thereof. In the end we shall state how the name acts as a supreme
joining force having its sway throughout the universe.
Pathway to God in the Vedas



      Once a devotee assiduously takes to the path of remembering
the name of God, he develops a liking, nay, a craving for it. This
gives rise to further devotion; and a deep attachment to the path
results. The devotee’s attention is drawn constantly to God and his
mind is filled with love towards Him. If the devotee’s resolve is firm
he will not swerve from the path. His meditation will lead him
directly to God-vision, for the name has a compulsive power to
attract God. Sages and saints have moreover assured us that the
name is not just an instrument leading to God; it enjoys the status
of divinity itself. A significant tenet of the way of meditation is the
identification of the name with God. Thus the name is enthroned by
the seers all over the world; and it shines in its majestic repose.
Constant utterance ofthe name brings peace to souls in anguish. Evil
passions flee as the name takes root in the mind. If God is the sole
object of attainment, uttering the name of God is the royal road
thereof. The aspirant should consider the remembrance of the name
of God as his bounden duty from which he can take no holiday. This
helps him to re-channelise the energies of the senses towards God,
because the name provides a link between his           empirical   self
engrossed in the senses, and his spiritual self looking up to the
Almighty.

      2. Meditation by Means of Speech. “There are four types of
speech. Only the learned know these. The first three are always kept
hidden. It is with the fourth that man speaks” (S.1). The four kinds
of speech are: Para, PaSyanti, Madhyama and Vaikhari. Mallinatha
in his commentary on Kumara-sambhava 11.17 says:

वैखरी शब्दनि ष्पत्ति ः म्या श्रुति गोचरा|
द्योति तार्था च पश्यन्ती सूक्ष्मा वागनपायि ।
                                           नी।

Vaikhari is articulated speech with all its stylistic characterisations
like accent or meaning; Madhyama is that stage where there is no
production of sounds and which has no phonetic quality. It is subtle
movement of the larynx or throat-throbbing; words are internalised.
As it joins the gross and the subtle, it is termed Madhyama. PaSyanti
and Para are not audible: PaSyanti is only suggestive while Para is
subtle and imperishable. The Paniniya Siksa (1.6-8) gives an

                                       168
                                                        The Name of God



excellent account of the articulation of words as under:

आत्मा बुद्धया समेतार्था न्‌ मनो युंक्ते वि वक्षया।
मनः कायाग्रि माहन्ति         स प्रेरयति   मारुतम्‌ ।।
मारुतस्तूरसि         चरन्‌ मंद्रं जनयति   स्वरम्‌।
सोदीर्णो मूर्ध्न्य   भि हतो वक्त्रमापद्य मारुतः।
वर्णा न्‌ जनयते तेषां वि भागः पंचधा स्मृतः ।।

(The individual soul (Atman) wishing to express something, sends
orders through intellect (Buddhi) to the mind (Manas). The mind
strikes at the body-fire (Kayagni) and the body-fire urges the vital
wind (Maruta) and the vital wind, passing through the chest (Uras)
generates sound-vibrations (Swaram). The sound vibrations rise up
to the top of the head from inside (Murdhan) and strike on various
spots in the mouth (Vaktr) thereby creating letters (Varnan) which
are divided into five categories). The parallels of four types of
speech with four states of consciousness and with four types of self
are also given in the technical treatment of the topic. Para is said
to be on a par with Turyavastha, or superconsciousness and is linked
with the Atman. PaSyanti has its parallel in Susuptavastha or deep
sleep and is related to the Karana-deha. Madhyama is on a par with
Svapnavastha or dream-state with its seat in the Suksma-deha while
Vaikhari has its correspondence with Jagradavastha or waking-state
and belongs to Sthila-deha. As regards meditation with the help of
speech, we are told in the Rk quoted earlier, that a common man
utters the name of God with Vaikhari or articulated speech; but only
the wise know the first three types. As we go from Vaikhari to
Madhyama, from Madhyama to Pasyanti and from Pasyanti to Para,
meditation becomes more and more internal. The name goes deeper
and deeper into our very being — till at the level of Para it becomes
inseparable from the self. It is then de-linked from the gross body
and its ‘adjuncts, because the name is not transient like the gross
body. The devotee who has realised God, may cast off his mortal
coil, but the name which is imparted to him by his spiritual teacher
and which enabled him to realise God, remains; so said the saint of
Umadi. The undying quality of the highest form of speech is brought
out in a couple of more Mantras. “Oh Viriipa, praise with

                                                169
Pathway to God in the Vedas



indestructible hymns the powerful Agni who protects devotees and
who fulfils their desires” (S.2). Brahman is said to be the ultimate
seat or the highest aim of speech when it attains to the same
imperishable status as that of Brahman as per Rsi Dirghatamas
Aucathya. “This altar is the limit of the earth, the sacrifice is the
centre of the universe, Soma is the seed of the powerful horse and
Brahman is the highest abode of speech” (S.3). The phrase Paramam
Vyoma, which is a very common Vedic phrase for the abode of gods,
indicates that it is the highest station aimed at.' Similar is the import
of one more verse which says: “he who takes recourse to meditation
on a Mantra which is the symbol of reality, reaches the highest”
(S.4). The name of God is imbued with power and, therefore, “he
who bears that sweet name of Indra, is able to kill his adversaries”
(S.5). The word used is Bibharti (bears) which implies that the name
abides with a devotee and acts as a sustaining and protective force.
 Purandaradasa,      the   great   Karnataka      saint   employs     the   term
 Namadharaka for a person who is initiated into the spiritual path and
 who fortifies himself with the power of the name of God.

        3. Characteristics of the Name.           Uttering the name of God
 is the most popular advice of saints. The name is as simple as it
 is effective. The name is sweet and is widely known to be so. Names
 are innumerable though what is symbolized by these names, stands
 unique and peerless. “Oh Agni, you are omniscient, possessor of
 food, and immortal; you have many names. Oh inspirer ofthe world,
 fulfil the desires of your devotees and counter all crooked moves
 of the wicked” (S.6). “Agni who knows all the religious rites, has
 immortalised, in the form of Rbhus, the name which is beautiful by
 nature and which is praised by all. He protects the wealth in the
 world”, observes Gathina Viswamitra (S.7). The proliferation of the
 name has been described in a number of Mantras. “The master who
 showed the right path manifested himself at the dawns and made
 famous the names of Adityas. The water deities remained silent
before him, but ultimately, though going their separate ways, they
came and joined him” (S.8). “Oh Indra, you were born in a yonder

 1   R.E. Hume, The Thirteen Principal Upanisads, Oxford University Press, Oxford,
     1971, P.283, Note 2


                                        170
                                                        The Name of God



country. You bear a name that is heard by a devotee when he is in
all-absorbing meditation. Gods tremble before you through fear. You
conquered the water which was controlled by the Dasas” (S.9). Agni
and Indra have taken a lead among those whose names are said to
be very famous. “People adorned him while he was standing there.
Bedecked by his own lustre and clothed in his own splendour, Indra
moves throughout the universe. Mighty are the names of this valiant
god. Taking various forms, he rules over the immortals” (S.10).
What saves us, is the name of God; for it contains within itself the
divine power. That power can thwart bad ideas and evil tendencies
thus helping the mind to cleanse itself. “The glorious name of the
Maruts, occupying the length and breadth of the world as the sea,
is the only thing that is of any use like strength inherited from the
forefathers” (S.11). It is mentioned that the name can bestow bliss
on a devotee; and hence the devotee wishes to offer oblations to the
name itself (S.12). Singing the praises of the Lord or His name is
the first weapen in the armoury of seers. Rsi Maitravaruni Vasistha
speaks    about   the   superabundant    power of the    name.   “Visnu
conquered this vast universe thrice. Victory to the name of this
primordial being” (S.13). “Oh you gods, you have been born from:
the earth, heavens and the mundane regions. All your names bearing
your stamp and halo are praiseworthy. They command a worshipful
attitude. Pray, listen to my call” (S.14).

      We may enumerate a few more marks of the name. The name
of the Lord is sweet, it is glorified, it is illuminating, it is sacred;
and last but not the least, it is secret. Who has not heard about the
sweetness of the name of God? “The experience of the beautiful
form and the honey-like name of Apam Napat grows without being
known. Intense faith nourishes it. Again, it feeds on the illumination
from above” (S.15). It may be mentioned that as born of clouds Agni
is called Apam Napat. This same spirit is reflected in the Bible.
“After this manner, therefore, pray ye: Our Father which art in
heaven, Hallowed be thy name”.! And “Father, glorify thy name.
Then came there a voice from heaven, saying, I have both glorified


1   St. Matthew VI. 9


                                   17]
Pathway to God in the Vedas



it, and will glorify it again”.' The charm of the name has been praised
throughout the universe. “At the time of meditation, internal energy
is released and the nectar gushes forth like a spring when the
delicious name of the Lord is heard” (S.16). The lustrous name of
Reality imparted by the spiritual teacher leads to the simultaneous
experience of flavour and sound. The sonorous lines run: “Soma who
bestows the gift of oratory, who initiates bravery, who cannot be
attacked, who is lovable and sweet and who is a spokesman of the
moral law, is being strained. He who strains Soma, gets a third
name” (S.17). This third name is the secret name or hidden name.
The first name is the popular name and the second is that which is
given on the basis of the constellation at the time of birth. Now, the
third name is no ordinary name. It radiates the brilliant heaven. It
is a mystical name — known only to the teacher and the disciple.
With this Indra illumines heaven and earth. Obtaining the divine
name implies attainment of a great spiritual status. Further, we are
told that the name of God is sacred and this sacredness adorns
 whatever it touches. “Your name dispels our doubts and clarifies our
 intellects. You yourself are the vital air which keeps us stable. Oh
 Indra, you are a protector, and will do us no harm. You are beyond
 the purview of sins. ४0ए name is eternal truth... Armed with the
 psalms in which your name occurs, the devotee worships the sun.
 Caparisoned with your name and striving relentlessly he achieves
 whatever he desires” (S.18). “As the unborn, he has held the vast
 earth and has pillared the heaven on the strength of the pious words
 (Mantras), which are the very core of reality. Pray, guardedly follow
 the foot-prints of the cow of vision (vision of god). Oh Agni, you
 are the universal life, enter now into the mystery of mysteries”
 (S.19). Those who know it, keep it concealed, guard it in the heart
of hearts and ardently meditate on it. “The enigmatic name to which
the fear-stricken worlds take resort for evoking God’s help is far far
away. You held earth and heaven near each other and bestowed light
on all human beings. Great is that unrevealed name which is
earnestly sought after, and by the power of which you created all
that existed and all that would come into existence. By its power,

1   St. John XIL 28


                                  172
                                                         The Name of God



you also generated that ancient blissful light, wherein, Oh bounteous
God, the five tribes whom you love, ultimately found refuge” (S.20).

     Let us take another Mantra from Satapatha Brahmana (S.21).
In this अर्जुन is said to be the “secret name” (गृह्यं नाम). It is a hidden
name in the sense that it is manifested to and heard by only seekers
who have realised God. It is not revealed to common people. There
are other names in the Vedas which are said to be नि ण्य (hidden),
अपीच्य (secret) etc. in the sense of God-given Names.” Unknown
though the name is, its dignity is recognised; hence the desire to
adore it. “As you know the secret names of gods, Oh Agni, kindly
convey our prayerful offerings to them”, sings Rsi Vasusruta Atreya
(S.22). The true nature of the name that fructifies, is known only to
the Guru and to God. “Oh gods, you gifted a cow to the Angirasas
and it was milked by Aryama. It is only he and [ that know what
that cow is” (S.23). Another stanza reiterates the real strength of the
name. “Oh brave soul, the Maruts practise penance in order to attain
to thy radiance. Your revelation is really reassuring, for you have
won for yourself a permanent abode near Visnu. That is why you
are entitled to guard that secret royal name” (S.24).

     4. The Import of Name. What is the real import of the name?
What is it that makes it valuable? What is its significance due to?
The answer to all the questions is one and the same: the uniqueness
of the name lies in its potency or power to make God stand before
the vision of the devotee. To reach the Godhead, one must utter His
name, the ingredients of which show a positive polarity towards
Him. The name imparted by the spiritual teacher, has a magnetic
influence; because it shares in the spiritual achievements of the
teacher. Even the remembrance of the spiritual teacher forms part
of meditation; ऋषि     अनुस्मरणमपि     उपासनांगमू says Samkara, while
commenting on Taittiriya Upanisad 1.5.1. Lingappa Savalgi, an
ardent disciple of the saint of Umadi used to say: would not a
repetition of the name of our spiritual teacher lead to the blissful
spiritual experience? It is the name laden with authority to which the
Vedic sage sticks with old-world sincerity. “The lord of prayer,
Brahmanaspati, verily reveals the words of the sacred Mantra which


                                     173
Pathway to God in the Vedas



remains surcharged with the spiritual reign of gods — Indra, Varuna,
Mitra, Aryama and others. We will repeat the same blissful and
immortal word in the assembly of saintly worshippers. If you too,
Oh men, resort to this word, you will be able to achieve all priceless
objects,” confidently asserts the sage Kanva Ghaura (S.25). The
adjective used for the name is Anehasam, incomparable or not liable
to be injured. The name of God is peerless and immortal. There
cannot be a parallelism between the name which has the potency to
lead us to God (Sabija Nama) and the one which does not have it
(Nirbija Nama). A valuable Mantra confers bliss (Sambhuvam).
Obviously, there is no greater bliss than the vision of God. Madhva
in his commentary on the Mantra, says that the name is uttered for
the sake of knowledge or illumination and bliss (ज्ञानार्थ सुखकारणम्‌).
The stress on knowledge and happiness is seen in another verse. “In
your delightful habitation, may we abide by your side and acquire
spiritual wealth and remain absorbed in meditation on your divine
self, Oh Indra” (S.26).

       It is not without hard labour that one receives such a divine
name. As the Taittiriya Brahmana puts it: it is through hardships and
penance that one comes across such a goddess of speech to whom
libations are offered (S.27). This divine lady वाक्‌ or name knits
together God and the devotee. It is because of this power that the
name becomes valuable. The Vedic Rsis have not stopped at saying
that the name of God is always turned to God as the magnetic needle
is turned to the north pole; they have added that the really fruitful
name is co-extensive with God Himself. It is non-different from God.
“Fifteen types of the sacred reverential prayers (Uktha) will ever be
chanted in thousands of places so long as the heaven and the earth
exist. They all extol the lordship and suzerainty of the Lord. The
word (Vak) is co-extensive with the Lord (Brahman)” (S.28). In this
the Vedas anticipate the Biblical idea: “In the beginning was the
Word, and the Word was with God, and the Word was God”’.' How
can Such a great name come to our hands unless we set our shoulder
to the wheel? “By resorting to worship, the wise seekers arrived at


1   St. John 1.1


                                 174
                                                                              The Name of God


the path of the Word. They found it harboured in the intuition of the
sages. After realisation of that word they distributed it in many ways.
It is the same word which is extolled in seven metres’ in the Vedas
(S.29). Weaving the name in music increases the duration of
meditation. It also keeps the mind pinned down to the name.

        5. Meditation, First, Foremost, Always. Fully conscious that
the remembrance of God’s name is the most efficacious way to God-
realization, the Vedic seers remain absorbed in it. “Oh Maruts, you
are a terror to the enemies, I call you with your lovable names. Be
pleased with us” (S.30). “Those who are full of knowledge, whose
minds are ever set on God, who speak truth, and who meditate on
the name of God, a veritable wish-cow, can reach God” (5.31). We
find from a Mantra of Rsi Vatsa Kanva that knowledge, truthfulness
and meditation are absolutely necessary for the attainment of God.
