Pathway to God in the Vedas



Likewise we read how the mortal man is engaged in prolonged
meditation on the name of immortal God (S.32). Man is devoted to
Agni and is engrossed in the contemplation on him (S.33). The
devotee is like a child of God and loves to be fondled by Him. The
seeker says: “Oh God, as a cow who gives plenty of milk, never
leaves her calf, so too let not gods forsake us. We know that you
love us as a father and therefore we request you to bestow happiness
on us” (S.34). Then we come across a Mantra later made famous
(with a little variation) by the ISopanisad. The hymn is addressed
to the mind of a person who is about to leave the body. “Let my vital
force now attain the immortal air, let the body be reduced to ashes.
Oh my mind, remember; remember the created world and remember
all that has been done till now” (S.35). These are the memorable
words of a great Rsi. The dying man converses with his mind in a
very touching manner. The word used for the mind is Kratu — that
which desires. Samkara in his commentary on his Mantra as
occuring in the ISopanisad explains Kratu as Samkalpatmaka, i.e.
the mind which always plays with different ideas. It is now asked
to do three things: (a) In the first place, the mind is asked to
remember something important. What can it be that the desire-ridden
mind should remember at the time of death? Samkara Says: स्मर यन्मम
स्मर्तव्यं तस्य कालोष्यं प्रत्युपस्थि   तः   (Remember what it is of utmost
 importance      to   remember;         for, the   appointed   time   has   already
arrived). The mind should first of all leave aside its nature of
entertaining ideas, it should become Unmana i.e. it should transcend
itself. This is implied by using the word, Kratu for the mind. “Oh
mind”, says the dying man, “I know your usual nature; but be good
enough to leave it now, and remember the name of God”. What else
can be of more significance at the last moment? Even if the mind
has avoided remembering God till death, it cannot afford to do so
any more. At least at the last moment, it should rise to the occasion.
(b) In the second place, the mind is asked to remember the created
world (Klibe smara). “Remember, Oh mind, the world which you are
now leaving. Has it offered to you anything worthwhile? Were you
ever completely satisfied here? Had it been so, you would have long
left your nature of craving for things. But you still jump from one
object to another in order to gain permanent bliss. You never found

                                             176
                                                                The Name of God


it, because you did not care to search the ground of this created
world. You always mistook the ephemeral for the ultimate truth.
Now     that   you     are   bidding   adieu   to   this    tantalising   illusion,
understand that it is illusory; hence, Oh mind, remember the world.
(c) In the third place, the mind is asked to remember all that has
been done so far (Krtam smara). Perhaps, up till now the mind has
wandered aimlessly and shown only peripheral interest, if at all, in
the practice of meditation. Understood in this sense the phrase,
Krtam    smara    is    a dying declaration which points an accusing
finger towards the mind. “Oh mind, you are guilty upto this moment;
you have no trace of devotion in you. But awaken at least at this final
occasion and take the only chance of salvation by remembering Him.
Hungry Time has taken a revenge on the body; the body does not
help you, it is a hindrance. If your penitence is sincere, if you are
prepared to dissolve yourself, you may still be saved. Remember,
that you have always been way-ward till now, but you are offered
one more opportunity. Make utmost efforts and break yourself free
from the yoke of allurements. Do it, my mind, please do it’. All this
advice is for the mind which is truant. However, there is another
possibility. The request to remember past deeds may be addressed
to the mind of a pious man. It is never caught in a frenzy and as
it has developed the habit of always remembering God, it is sure to
remember Him at the hour of departure. Understood in this way the
phrase Krtam smara is a morale booster. It means: “Remember as
you   have     always    remembered,      meditate     as    you   have always
meditated, Oh mind. Do not falter at this critical juncture. Be firm.
Let the habit of meditation which you have built up by long toil
continue. Be tied to the name of God which has been your life-long
routine and victory is yours. You have not given up meditation even
in the midst of miseries of the world, so never retreat at this crucial
time. Be courageous; remember what you have always remem-
bered”. Thus the Mantra contains three important ideas, namely, one
should remember God at the time of death, one must realise the
momentary nature of the world, and one should be penitent for errors
of commission and omission. It is incumbent on an aspirant to
remember God throughout his life if he is anxious to remember Him
at the moment of death. This is the definitive principle of the


                                       177
Pathway to God in the Vedas


philosophy of meditation. The tenet is illustrated in Jnaneswanri: “If
you doubt, Oh Arjuna, as to how my devotee can sit for meditation
when all his senses have half left him, when he is surrounded by
miseries and when death stands face to face, remember that if the
devotee has constantly remembered me, I become his servant at the
time of his death” (VIII. 120-123). God sees that the devotee leaves
this world with His name on the lips. This is the grand finale to the
holy process of life-long meditation.

     6. The Name as Bond between God and Man. By pious
repetition of the Gayatri Mantra sins are expiated. “Savitr inspires
the whole universe. We meditate on his indescribable lustre. Let that
great one direct our intellects” (S.36). We have to note that the verse
contains a prayer for God’s grace, but it lays no less stress on the
necessity of self-effort on the part of the devotee. In fact, God is
to be appropriated by much tilling of the ground. This is the purport
of yet another Vedic hymn. “Oh unique God, the lord of the universe,
the most praise-worthy and respectable divine Being, you are
 approached through prayerful meditation. Here I offer my humble
 adoration to you” (S.37). “A wise Ksatriya who resorts to the
 repetition of the name of Agni can not suffer any harm from
 encircling enemies, nor does he appear within the ken of their
 knowledge” (S.38). More important still, the name forms a link
 between man and God. In fact, the message of many a Mantra is
 that the name is a guide on the spiritual path and that it is a sutra
 or the connecting bond which brings together the individual soul and
 the universal soul. “The thief who steals the cattle (PaSvah), and who
 keeps food with himself and feeds the cattle, is located in his hide-
 out with the help of his foot-prints (Padaih anugman) by intelligent
 men. He is hence surrounded by those who are very active. Likewise,
 the learned reach God through His name and the worshippers
 surround him”, 1२51 ParaSara Saktya asserts (5.39). The word Pasvah
 (animal) is also interpreted to imply the sense-organs.' The Mantra
 thus means: “God goes into hiding into the hearts of man by taking
 with him the energies of the senses (Pasvah). The learned trace Him

 1 For both these interpretations given by S.D. Satawalekar, see Purusartha, 1960,
    P. 402


                                       178
                                                         The Name of God



out with the help of the name (Padaih anugman). The worshippers
surround him, offer him food and pray to him.” Whatever may be
the meaning of the word PaSvah, it is clear that God is to be attained
with the help of meditation on His name. Using almost the same idea
and the same phraseology another verse tells us that “the God-man
adored him and sought him out from amidst the waters as a             lost
animal is traced by its foot-prints. The wise Bhrgus who were
yearning for God found Him in their very hearts” (S.40). Similarly
we read: “Let us pray at the time of sunrise, because Oh Mitra,
Varuna and Aryaman, you are famous as guides on the spiritual
path” (5.41).

      It is, however not that easy to get hold of the Sitra or the joining
thread. The aspirant is wondering as to which name will really prove
to be of any consequence. “From among the gods that listen to our
prayers whose hallowed name should we utter? Who will protect us,
bless us and shower grace on us’’? (5.42). Then we are reminded
of an important truth that it is not a child’s play to keep firm our
grip on the name. “It is difficult to stick to the name of the immortal
as he goes on manifesting various forms. He who has perseverance,
controls the law. Oh Fire-god, watch over him assiduously” (S.43).
Sayana takes the name of God (Nama) as nature of God (Swaripa).
The Mantra,       therefore, means that it is difficult to understand
(Durmantu) the nature of the immortal being. We further understand
from the poem that God yields His secrets to him who grasps
perfectly (Sumantu) the inner bond that connects him to divinity.
Obviously, meditation on the name constitutes such a bond. It is
difficult to catch hold of a thread in the first place. Secondly, by its
lethargy, ignorance and mania for varied objects, the mind lets go
its hold on the link and the connection is snapped. William James
warns us: Never suffer an exception to occur till the new habit is
securely rooted in your life. Each lapse is like the letting fall of a
ball of string which one is carefully winding up. A single slip undoes
more than a great many turns will wind again.' One has again to pick
up the threads and begin the journey afresh. Did not the spider which
Robert Bruce was watching, fall again and again, because its hold

1   The Principles of Psychology, Vol.I, Page 123



                                      179
Pathway to God in the Vedas



on the thread was loosened trial after trial? But ultimately its tenacity
paid and the thread took the spider to its goal.

    7. The Importance of Aum. It is an accepted concept in the Vedic
and post-Vedic literature that the holy syllable Aum is a bond that
connects man with God. “ I climb all the five steps of sacrificial
performance, i.e. I take care of all the five instruments of sacrifice
namely, fried barley (Dhana), juice of Soma plant (Soma-rasa),
sacrificial animal (2851), oblations of ground rice (PurodaSa) and
Ghee (Ghrta). I carry out the performance by uttering Aum. I purify
the offerings on the altar of the sacrifice” (S.44). In the ancient
philosophico-mystical literature of India, Aum occupies a unique
position. This sacred syllable includes in its sway concepts of
benediction, peace and the power to see God face to face. The
significance of Aum does not end here; it is identified with Brahman
or the ultimate Reality itself. Aum may occur in the beginning of a
Mantra when it implies benediction, at the end it stands for peace. At
other places, it symbolises Brahman as indicated, for example, in the
Yoga-sitra तस्य वाचकः प्रणवः (1.27), or it is identified with the ultimate
Reality or Brahman as in the Pragna Upanisad (५.2): परं च अपरं च ब्रह्म
यद्‌ॐकार एव (Aum is both the acosmic and the cosmic Brahman). In
other words, it is both the means and the end of meditation.

     A couple of instances may be cited where Aum is taken to be
the supreme instrument of contemplation or where it is prefixed to
many a sacred Mantra both in orthodox and heterodox theology.

     (a) Not only does the individual approach Brahman through
Aum, even the Sun is said to be singing the syllable Aum while
traversing the sky: ॐ इति   ह्येषः स्वरन्नेति   (Chandogya Upanisad I.5.1).

     (5) ॐ खं ब्रह्म (8.45). This epigrammatic statement comes at the
end of यजुर्वेद as the concluding portion of the 17th Mantra of Chapter
XL, which happens to be the Isavasyopanisad, with a slight variation.
ॐ खं ब्रह्म means: ॐ is the symbol of Brahman (परमात्मन्‌) who, like
Akasa, is all-pervading. In तैत्ति रीय आरण्यक (X.63) we come across a
similar statement: 3० इति आत्मानं युंजीत. By the Taittiriya Aranyaka
expression चत्वारि शृंगाः (24.1.17), 9599183 understands four quarters

                                      180
                                                                    The Name of God



of Aum (शुंगाः प्रणवस्य यानि             अकारादीनि   शृंगाणि ). The statement from
Yajurveda seems to be the basis of statements of similar import in
the Upanisads. For example, (1) ॐ इति                एतदक्षरं इदं सर्वम्‌ in मांडूक्य (1),
or तत्ते पदं संग्रहेण ब्रवीमि     ॐ इत्येतद्‌ in कठ (1.2.15). Aum as the symbol
of the mystic name of Brahman was used for meditation. In ईशोपनि षद्‌
also we have a similar idea in ॐ क्रतो स्मर which has been discussed
above. Thus ॐ खं ब्रह्म implies that Aum is the means of meditation
as well as the supreme goal to be achieved, namely, realisation of
Brahman.

      (०) ॐ नमः शि वाय. The words नमः शि वाय occur in what is called
(रुद्राध्याय) (5.46) and also in तैत्ति रीय संहि ता of कृष्ण यजुर्वेद (1V.5.8). The
expression has been treated and employed as a sacred Mantra for
meditation later by the devotees of God Siva, and was termed
शि वपंचाक्षरमंत्र. To 1115 पंचाक्षरमंत्र 182 mystic syllable ॐ was pre-fixed
and thus the Mantra became ॐ नमः शि वाय. There was a further
change, and it was transformed to ऊँ नमो शि वा at the time of medieval
Saints. It is interesting to note that Jhanesvara, the greatest saint of
Maharashtra uses the above stanza in his Abhanga: ॐ नमो शि वा आदि |
कावडी घेतली खांदी 11 (The saints carry on their shoulders the name
of God).

     (त) ॐ मणि पद्ये हुम्‌. This is said to be the Mantra for meditation
used by the Lamas of Tibet. We shall come to the significance of
this a   little later.

     (€) The passage वागेवार्थ पश्यति वाग्‌ ब्रवीति वागेवार्थ संनि हि तं संतनाति ।
वाचैव वि श्वं बहुरूपं नि वद्धं तदेतदेकं प्रवि भज्योपभुंक्ते| वागेव वि श्वा भुवनानि जज्ञे
वाच इत्‌ सर्व अमृतं मर्त्यं     च declares that वाक्‌ (शब्द) or Aum is the cause
of the universe. Aum is identified with Brahman. This means that
the universe is evolved out of Aum which is the original source.

     (f) ॐ इत्येतत्‌ब्रह्मणो नेदि ष्टं नाम, यस्मात्‌उच्चार्यमाण एव संसारभयात्‌तारयति ,
तस्मात्‌ उच्यते तार इति       ॥ (भक्ति   संद्भ). This passage states: Aum is the
name nearest to God. It is called तार (the redeemer or the saviour)
because it delivers one from the cycle of births and deaths.

      8. Mantras of Different Syllables. Aspirants have attained to
their goals by using names consisting of a divergent number of
syllables. Whatever this number, they are all fruitful. “Agni acquired
the five-fold vital airs by using a single-lettered name; I too shall
do it. ASvins conquered the two-legged man by using a two-lettered
name; I shall do the same. Visnu conquered the three worlds by using
a three-lettered name; so will I. Soma conquered the four-legged
 animals by uttering a four-lettered name; I shall do likewise. Pusan
conquered the five directions by uttering a five-lettered name; I shall
follow him. Savitr, the’all inspirer conquered six seasons by using
 a six-lettered Mantra; I shall emulate his example. The Maruts
 conquered the seven domestic animals by using a seven-lettered
 name; 1 shall do the same. Brhaspati conquered Gayatri by making
 use of a ten-lettered name; I shall follow suit. Indra conquered
Tristup by uttering an eleven-lettered name; I shall imitate him.
 /15\/606 ४25 conquered Jagati by resorting                  to     a     twelve-lettered
Mantra; I shall follow the example. The eight Vasus conquered the
TrayodaSa-stoma by uttering a Mantra of thirteen letters; I shall also
conquer it in the same fashion. The eleven Rudras conquered the
CaturdaSa-stoma by meditating upon a fourteen-lettered name; I
shall follow them. The twelve Adityas conquered the Pancada§a-
stoma with the help of fifteen-lettered Mantra; so would I. Aditi
conquered the Sodaga-stoma by using the sixteen-lettered name; I
shall do the same. Prajapati conquered the Saptadasa-stoma with the
assistance of seventeen-lettered name; I am also sure to do it”
(S.47). The whole universe comes under the sway of the Mantras.
      In Aitareya Brahmana IX.7.31 we find a reference to तूष्णींशंस, a
                                           182
                                                              The Name of God



method of silent prayer or meditation on a nine-syllabled (Navaksara)
Mantra and a ten-syllabled (Dasaksara) Mantra. There is one verse
in Samaveda which identified three gods with light: “Agni is light,
light is Agni, Indra is light, light is Indra, Stirya is light, light is
Surya” (S.48). This hymn consists of three parts each, every part
containing eight syllables. Now in Aitareya Brahmana IX.7.31, we
find that one more syllable भू: is prefixed to the first part thus making

the syllables nine: भू: अग्नि र्ज्यो ति ः ज्योति रग्नि : | Similarly in the second
part two more syllables भुवः are inserted after the first four syllables
thus making it a ten-syllabled Mantra: इंद्रो ज्योति ः Yat ज्योति रि ंद्र: | Again
inserting the syllable स्व:        before the last two syllables in the third

part, another nine-syllabled Mantra is formed: सूर्यो ज्योति ः ज्योति ः स्वः
सूर्यः । It may be noted that the three syllables भूः, भुवः, स्वः are called
व्याहति ऽ or the mystic syllables. These are prefixed to the Gayatri
Mantra when it is recited as part of the morning and evening liturgy

(संध्यावंदन).   It may also be seen that Agni, Indra and Strya are
identified with light (Jyoti), as they are obviously the three aspects
of the supreme light viz., Atman or Paramatman. Aitareya Brahmana
1.5.9.4 makes a reference to उपांशु              method of जप or meditation.
Sayana describes the उपांशु method as ओष्ठस्पंदनमेव परैः दृश्यते, न तु
शब्दः श्रूयते तादृशं उपांशुत्वम्‌ (In उपांशु जप , only the movements of the
lips are visible to others, but the sound is not heard by them). In this
connection, मनुस्मृति      says,
वि धि यज्ञात्‌ जपयज्ञो वि शि ष्टो दशभि र्गुणैः
उपांशुः स्यात्‌ शतगुणः साहस्रो मानसः स्मृतः ।। (11.85)
(Uttering the name of god is ten times superior, gently murmuring
the name is hundred times superior and silent prayer is thousand
times superior to ritualistic sacrifice). The Vedic Rsis exhibit a
remarkable insight when they state that the divine name constitutes
a link between God and His devotee and has, therefore, a redeeming
power. This tradition has come down from the Vedas and has entered
into prayers of different religions. Special meaning came to be
attached to every letter of a Mantra. To take an example: “The
commonest mystic formula in Lamaism in Tibet, Om-ma-ni-pad-me-


                                          183
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Hum which literally means ‘Om ! the jewel in the lotus ! Hum!’ is
the essence of all happiness, prosperity and knowledge and the great
means of deliverance; for the Om closes re-birth amongst the gods,
ma among the Titans, ni as a man, pad as a beast, me as a Tantalus,
the Hum as an inhabitant of hell. And in keeping with this view, each
of these six syllables is given the distinctive colour of these six
states of rebirth, namely, Om the godly white; ma the Titanic blue;
ni the human yellow, pad the animal green; me the Tantalic red and
Hum the hellish black”’.!

     We may take one more example where the spiritual significance
of the name इंद्र 15 considered. “This is the great God who directs and
controls movements of the entire universe according to the require-
ments of time. He is well known by the name Indra. Hence 1 offer
my prayers to Him” (S.49). Aitareya Aranyaka towards the end of
 1.4. gives the spiritual significance of the name Indra as follows: स
                                                                                           >.


एतमेव पुरुषं ब्रह्म ततममपश्यत्|‌ इदं अदर्शं इती। तस्मात्‌ इदंद्रो नाम इदंद्रो ह वे
नाम। तं इदंद्रं सन्तं इंद्र इति   आचक्षते परोक्षेण। परोक्षप्रि या इव हि   देवाः परोक्षप्रि या
इव हि देवाः (He saw this very person as the veriest ब्रह्मन्‌. 1 have seen
 it’, said he. Therefore his name is Idam-dra, Idam-dra verily is his
 name. Him who is Idam-dra, they call Indra cryptically, for the gods
 are fond of the cryptic as it were — for the gods are fond of the cryptic
 as it were). This portion is included in Aitareya Upanisad (1.3.13-14).

      9. Time and Place for Meditation. This                         topic    has been
 discussed in many places in the Vedas. In one sense the question
 is irrelevant because all times and all places are holy for meditation.
 In Prof. R.D. Ranade’s words, what really matters is concentration
 and when that is achieved, time and place have no significance.” If
 we have to look to favourableness or otherwise of time and place,
 there would not be any meaning in the injunction which asks us to
 utter the name of God with every incoming and every outgoing

 1   L. Austine Waddell, The Buddhism of Tibet or Lamaism, published by W. Heffer
     & Sons Ltd, Cambridge, First published in 1894. Reprinted in 1959, Pp 148 ff

 2 See The Bhagavad-gitd as a Philosophy of God-realisation, Nagpur University,
     Nagpur, 1959, Pp 249-251

                                             184
                                                      The Name of God



breath. We may remark that even if a particular time or a particular
place is felt to be inauspicious, devout meditation by a God-realiser
would render it auspicious. But in another sense, we do have to pay
attention to time and place. After all, how many of the aspirants can
vouchsafe for the top-most quality of meditation which has a power
to sanctify inauspicious moments or unholy places? Hence so far
as common aspirants are concerned, we can talk of a proper time
and a proper place for meditation. By the term proper we understand
that particular time or that particular place which helps concentra-
tion. The time when a person is likely to be less alert, or the place
which will distract his attention are less useful. Hence the stipulation
regarding time and place. Rsi Atharvan advises, “He who meditates
in the early morning before sun-rise and who by such meditation
reaches God, achieves the imperishable Atmanic bliss, Swarajya.
There is no other thing greater than this” (5.50). “Perform a sacrifice
in honour of the ASwins and offer oblations to them in the morning,
because an oblation offered in the evening does not reach gods; it
does not remain propitious. Moreover, there are others who perform
a sacrifice very early. Gods favour those who sacrifice before any
one else” (S.51). The statement that the oblations offered in the
evening do not reach gods or they become stale, is to be taken with
a pinch of salt. It just reminds us to begin our meditation early in
the day and not go on postponing it. Work while it is day for one
does not know when night comes.
    Similar is the import of the latter part of the Mantra; it warns
us not to be outdone by others. It also offers an incentive that the
early worshippers are favoured by gods. Let not meditation,
therefore, be deferred. There are other hymns which ask the devotees
to meditate whenever they can. “Oh, you follower of Atharvan, pray
to Savitr, pray at night, pray abundantly, pray to whatever is
lustrous” (S.52). Asking an aspirant to pray at night is to be
understood in an inclusive sense. One should pray not only at night
but also during day. This is an instance of Ajahallaksana where the
import of the sentence is not restricted to the primary meaning of
the term. Thus the term night does not mean night exclusively but
includes day also. Leaving no room for any rhetorical interpretation,
another Mantra says, “Oh Agni, we approach you by intently praying

                                  185
Pathway to God in the Vedas



to you day and night” (5.53). Or again, “I milk the cow (i.e. I
meditate) in the morning and in the evening. I milk the cow also in
the afternoon. We enjoy the bliss and we know what is blissful”
(S.54). “I invoke the goddess Aditi, in the morning and at noon. Oh
Mitra and Varuna, I offer praises in order to gain happiness and
progeny” (S.55). As prayers are offered three times a day, so fruit
thereof    is    also     expected      thrice      daily.   “Oh   Savitr,   whatever
auspicious things you have, bring them to us thrice daily. Lord, bring
to us from the heavens three types of fortunes. There are three
radiant and invulnerable stations. The brave sons of the omniscient
lord are also three in number. Gods who embody truth, power and
victory should visit our places of worship three times a day” (S.56).
Of course, praying or meditating three times a day does not mean
that we should pray only thrice a day. Pray at least thrice, and the
more the better. “Oh brave and lustrous Agni, you are omniscient
and slayer of enemies. We meditate on you everyday”                            (S.57).
“Those who know your prowess meditate on your name continuously
(Animisam), and those who increase your strength by pouring
oblations, enter a city, well fortified against enemies” says Rsi Vavri
Atreya (S.58). The word used                        for continuous      meditation   is
Animisam i.e. without winking or sleeping. The meaning is that
 Sages are ever alert and vigilant and meditate incessantly. This
 brings into bold relief the truth that remembrance of God is itself
 wakefulness while forgetting Him is itself sleep. “I shall never fail
 in meditating on you” says another Rk, “You, Oh Indra, are a slayer
of enemies, and I know you to be so. I shall forever chant your name
because its glory is unparallelled” (5.59). Such meditation may go
on for months and years on end. “With a desire in the heart to gain
cows, they sat awhile in their search of immortality by way of
prayers.      This      itself   is   their   vast    resting   place    where,   with
righteousness they wished to live for months together” (S.60). What
really we are urged to be engaged in, is meditation, more meditation
and   still     more meditation. This link can not be                     snapped    as
generations after generations continue the utterance of God’s name.
“Oh devotee, this is the word. Future generations will continue to
sing its praises. In your orisons let us be remembered. Let us not
be cast away. Adieu, salutations to you” (S.61).

                                              186
                                                     The Name of God



    As with time so with place. “Oh generous gods, we may be
staying indoors or may be traversing a path; we pray to you for well-
being”   (S.62).   “By performing   meditation   in the caves of the
mountains and at the confluence of rivers, he rises as a man of
enlightenment” (S.63). “Oh Indra, of hundred valiant deeds, the
singers of Gayatri present ballads in your praise, the singer of Arka
(song of praise) also do the same. The worshippers do their best to
win your grace and raise you to the skies as the players lift a pole
high up to demonstrate their dexterity. When a devotee goes on
ascending from pinnacle to pinnacle, Indra understands his wish.
Look, he at once rushes to him along with the entourage” (S.64). We
find a very beautiful passage which prescribes the best time, place
and mode of meditation to gain Olympian peace. It is not necessary
that an aspirant should leave the world. He can lead a house-holder’s
life. He should observe rules and regulations prescribed by Dharma
and be pure and virtuous. He should concentrate his mind on the
immaculate Narayana, and be pure and virtuous. He should get up
from his bed past midnight. Holy of speech and self-controlled, he
should engage himself in meditation while the mundane world is
drowned in deep sleep. Sitting in an upright position on a level
ground which is sheltered from wind and sound, he should adopt the
Padma posture. He should fold his hands in respectful salutation, be
serene. and calm and close his eyes. Having uttered the sacred
syllable Aum, he should clear the mind of all doubts and meditate
on his own self. Then, having composed his senses and mind he
should stop thinking of other things and should not see or hear
anything. When he stops entertaining ideas, it is said that he has
attained Yoga. The devotee should not disturb his steady posture.
He should keep his mind ever set on Narayana. By controlled
movements ofhis vital breath, he should make the mind equanimous.
Gradually he should try to raise his consciousness to divine
consciousness. The functions of the different parts of body like the
vertebral column, face, nose, eyes or eye-brows should be in an
equilibrium. He should fix his attention on the Bhrimadhya and
slowly rise upto the Sahasraracakra. The inhalation and the
exhalation should go on in a regulated manner. Thus he should reach
the pinnacle which is declared to be the seat of the supreme Atman.


                                 187
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Such is the way to collect the God-head.

(गृहस्थधर्मे वर्तेत न्यायक्लप्तः शुचि त्रतः|

सव्यं पाद्मं दक्षि णस्य जानुनि       श्लेषयेत्‌ ततः।।

 न मनस्येद्यदा योगं तदा प्राप्तः स उच्यते।।
 हदि   एतं ऋषि ं अभ्यसेत्‌ पश्यन्‌ इव यथाश्रुति ः।
 प्राणान्‌ आयम्य चासीनो यावत्‌ तं चि ंतयेत्‌ ऋषि ।म्।‌

 एवं हि   युंजन्‌ सामान्यं न पश्येत्‌ श्रृणुयात्‌ न च।
 तदा शनैः नयेत्‌ चेतो हृदयात्‌ ऊर्ध्व मेव तु।।

                                                 । ।‌
 ललाटदेशे धार्या थ मधन गमयेत्‌ ततः।
 उच्छ्वसन्‌ च यथाकालं नाभि ं गत्वोच्छवसेत्‌ पुनः । ।
 एतत्‌ परं स्थानमुक्तम्‌ ब्रह्मणः परमात्मनः।
 एवं युक्तो महात्मानम्‌ आत्मानं प्रति पद्यते| |)
 (Extract from the introductory portion to Purusa-sukta commentary
 by Saunaka in Rgvidhana).
       A point or two of psychological significance can be picked up
 from the passage. We cannot sunder the body from the mind. We
 have a body-mind complex and it is no wonder that there is inter~
 influence between them. Change in one may not be the whole

                                                 188
                                                                The Name of God


cause of the change              in the other. There are many other factors
responsible for a modification in either. A stomach-ache may not be
caused by a gloomy mood but by indigestion; nonetheless a weary
mind    contributes        its   share   to     make   the   stomach-ache   more
unbearable. Similarly the body postures affect the mental conditions.
Here is what a great psychologist says : “.... sit all day in a moping
posture, sigh and reply to everything with a dismal voice, and your
melancholy lingers.”' On the other hand a perfectly sound physique
may not be the whole cause of euphoria, happy tidings might have
induced it; still a healthy body sends pulsations to make the euphoria
more expressive, “There             is   no   more valuable precept in moral
education than this, as all who have experience know: if we wish
to conquer undesirable emotional tendencies in ourselves, we must
assiduously, and in the first instance cold-bloodedly, go through the
outward movements of those contrary dispositions which we prefer
to cultivate. The reward of persistency will infallibly come, in the
fading out of the sullenness or depression, and the advent of real
cheerfulness       and    kindliness     in   their stead.   Smooth   the   brow,
brighten the eye, contract the dorsal rather than the ventral aspect
of the frame, and speak in a major key, pass the genial compliment,
and your heart must be frigid indeed if it do not gradually thaw!”
In like manner, though we cannot just put the body under rigorous
drill and automatically gather a rich harvest of mental concentration,
we must not be oblivious to the fact that bodily discipline has much
to offer to the          mental    make-up than it is normally realised.
Concentration on the tip of the nose (नासाग्रदृष्टि , posture (आसन),
breath-control (प्राणायाम)         are heavy-weights in the psychology of
meditation.       We have to exploit them for a proper mood for
contemplation. In the next chapter we shall see that the Vedic
thinkers were aware of the role the body plays in meditation.

     10. Fruits of Meditation. To meditate on the name of God purely
for the love of it, indicates a very high stage in the spiritual career


1   William James, The Principles of Psychology, Vol II, Dover Publications, Inc.
    1950 Edition, P. 463

2   Ibid P. 463


                                          189
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of an aspirant. Though such attitude is displayed in many a      lyric,
there are others where demand of fruit comes trailing on the mention
of sacrifice. The common man meditates for some kind of fruit. The
Vedas have made a mention of different fruits. In the first place, we
are told that one who meditates on the name of God, gains strength.
“Oh Indra, you fulfil the desires of those who invoke your grace; your
chariot is sturdy, your horses too are stubborn. Beyond doubt, Oh
valorous one, not only you are yourself full of energy, but the singing
of your name also is so. I, your devotee, full of virility am invoking
you along with all your graces. You are fond of praises and the
singing of your name makes one dignified” (S.65). “This is the secret
fruit obtained by milking the divine cow by means of meditation on
the name of the sovereign God. Those who offer this invigorating
drink to Indra are endowed with the vision of various fortunes”
(S.66). They include the capacity to overthrow one’s enemies. “The
aspirant who is lucky enough to partake of heavenly nectar (Soma)
becomes free from all kinds of afflictions. His movements become
unhindered. He enjoys prosperity” (S.67). Rsi Barhaspatya Bharadvaja
says, “Almighty Indra does not deceive; nor can he be deceived
Meditation on his name is always auspicious. He annihilates the five
demons Cumuri, Dhuni, Pipru, Sambara and Susna (the five evil
forces harassing human beings) and crushes their fortresses to ruin
(S.68). Again, “he is eager to see the cow in the form of lustre.
Fortified by righteousness and faith, Indra broke open the crest of
the mountain of passions, and by the authority of the name of God,
conquered greed” (S.69). The name is again and again mentioned as
a cover against injuries. In the last chapter we have noted how God’s
name and God’s grace are considered to be a protective armour. He
who envelopes himself with the name of God uproots the enemy.
Meditation does not merely free a devotee from the danger of a foe,
it also showers positive wealth of him. “I shall send for the cow
which gives a lot of milk. He who has skill can alone milk her. Let
the all-inspirer Savitr fill our coffers. He possesses great victory, I
must sing his praises. Look, the mother of bounteous riches runs with
eagerness to meet her calf. She should not be hurt by anyone. Let
us give milk for the ASwins and let her grow, so that our fortune
grows with her” (S.70). Almost every god is approached with a

                                 190
                                                             The Name of God



similar request. “Pour enthusiasm on our devotion which rises from
the depth of our hearts as the clouds pour waters on the earth. Oh
Soma it is you who augments the material and the spiritual affluence”
(S.71). “The devotees who were on the right path drank Soma which
energises a person. The gods were all intent on getting divine glory.
The God whose fame is spread wide and who displays a charming
form    manifested      Himself before     the devotees”      (S.72).   Having
feasted their eyes on the pleasing form of God, the devotees naturally
long to be close to Him and share in His celebrity. “When you
revealed    yourself out    of a   dried   piece   of wood,    aspirants,    on
witnessing this wonder, began to rejoice in your exploits. Having
taken to a systematic course of austerities and having practised
meditation on your name, they attain to divinity” (S.73).

      The deities that were worshipped by Vedic Rsis were legion.
That is why the names by which they invoke gods are multiple in
number. We find in one song that different deities are called upon
to protect the sacrificial altar from different sides. “Let Indra, the
Lord of gods, along with Vasus protect the eastern side of the
sacrificial altar. Let the wise Varuna leading the eleven Rudras
protect it from the west. Yama, who travels as fast as the mind,
should along with the manes, protect the altar from the south. Let
the    highest   Lord    who   controls    the   creation,   sustenance     and
destruction of the world lead the twelve Adityas and protect it from
the north. The remnants of the holy water which was used for
sprinkling will be thrown by me outside the region reserved for the
sacrifice” (S.74). Elsewhere we read: “God Soma, chanter of the
name is ever alert and invincible” (S.75).

      11. The Name as a Master Unifier. Thus while extolling gods
by means of the name, the Rsis have come to present laurels to the
name itself. They have enthroned it, saying that it has come down
to the earth for the benefit of the mortals. “Indra, the wielder of the
thunderbolt attacked the impious and got the name Maghavan
because of his generosity. The munificent God has preserved that
name even in the human era in order to bless the devotees” (S.76).
The name is a god-sent award. “We have solemnised the sacrifice.
Let there be jubilation with singing of hymns. We have worshipped


                                    191
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Agni   whole-heartedly.   In   order    to   instruct   seers   to   perform
sacrifices, gods themselves descended from heavens” (S.77). By
sacrifice we understand meditation.


    At some fabulously old time the Vedas lavishly sowed the seed
of the most wide-spread and universally appealing technique of
spiritual practice, that of meditation on the name of God, to reap a
harvest of peace and tranquillity. The sages loved the name so much
that they came to look upon it as the goal itself. The name weaves
round itself the whole of creation and acts as a mediator between
things that are totally diverse. The poet-saint makes the Goddess of
speech (वाकू) herself express the potency of the word as a unifier.
Having directly realised the non-difference between her own self,
God as well as the entire universe, Vigambhrni bursts out in ecstasy:
“TI move with Vasus and Rudras and also with Adityas and all gods.
To Mitra and Varuna I extend my backing and likewise to Indra, Agni
and ASvins too. I foster that mighty stimulus in Soma for enabling
him to deal deadly blows to the opponents. I sustain Pusan, Tvasta
and Bhaga. I recompense those who offer oblations, by giving them
abundant wealth; a similar reward is given by me to those who
worship with Soma. I am a sovereign queen and a confluence of all
the Vasus. I am omniscient and the foremost among the holy ones.
Gods found me established in many places. 1 live in various places
in diverse forms. It is only through my inspiration that one eats, sees,
breathes and hears what is spoken. People do not know me even
though they happen to be near. Hark, I am telling you what is worthy
of belief. Yes, I myself say this which is acceptable to gods and men.
I can make him whomsoever I desire, Brahman, a Rsi or a gifted
wise man. For Rudra I stretch out the string of his bow in order to
slay the deadly enemy of holy spiritual souls. In man, I infuse the
exhilerating energy and I entirely pervade both heaven and earth. I
bring forth the Father on the top of the universe. I have my home
within the unfathomable waters of the ocean. Thence I stretch out
to all the worlds and touch with my beautiful form the yonder heaven.
It is I who breathes forth like wind, setting in motion all the existing
worlds. Beyond this earth and beyond even heaven have I arisen.
I have become all this through my own grandeur” (S.78). The

                                  192
                                                                              The Name of God


goddess of speech, Vak, daughter of sage Ambhrna, is the most
exalted among the women seers of the Vedic age. She presents
herself as a joining agency. She brings together different groups of
gods. She dwells in various places but holds them all within herself.
She acts as a schematiser between heaven and earth by entering
both; between sacrifice and its unseen merit by dispensing fruit to
the sacrificer; between men and gods by declaring what both of them
cherish; between created worlds and that which is beyond them by
extending herself over them. Does it not prove that Vak, word or
naime is a connecting link between everything and every other thing?
Verily it is a band-master, or in the phrase of Leibnitz                         — a vinculum
substantiale.

                                                 200
                                CHAPTER VII




                  Methods of Meditation


       In the Vedas, the deities are regarded as intimately connected
with the world. They are, in a way, in the world, of the world and
for the world. They are in the world, because they are immanent and
not transcendent. They are of the world, in that they take part in the
activities of the mortals. They are for the world, because the Vedic
man approaches them for getting solved each and every difficulty.
Our ancestors do not worship those deities that transcend imagina-
tion, but those that are sovereigns of the natural elements. The
deistic conception of an absentee God is foreign to them. God is
वि श्वात्मक (all-enveloping). We know Him through his manifestations.
“The    nature of ultimate Reality is to be read, therefore, in its
manifestation and may be read there truly. We may be sure the
revelation is not exhaustive, for all revelation must be ad modum
recipientis; it must be proportionate to the capacity of the receiving
mind. Every advance in knowledge, or in goodness, or in the
intuitions of beauty and grandeur offered us in nature or in art, is
a further revelation of the heights and depths of the divine nature.”!
This may, generally speaking, be the Vedic view of deities, though
references to the great transcendent Being are not wanting.

       We shall take a brief resume of the discussion which is to
follow. For the purposes of meditation an idea is used. It provides
a habitation      and a name to the nebulous infinitude of God and
facilitates meditation. The mind is pinned down to the idea and its
wild jumps are arrested. This is the ideological element. The body
must be made an ally and so it is to be handled with care. The Yogic
element involves disciplining of the mind and body. The Vedic Rsi
is not expected to know the structure of the body. But it is creditable
that mention is made of plexuses and of breath control. Meditation


1   8.5. Pringle - Pattison, The idea of God, P.175
Pathway to God in the Vedas



becomes effective only when accompanied by intense love for
God—this constitutes the emotional element. We shall then take a
survey of nine kinds of Bhakti. Special mention must be made of
Kirtana. Suktas or Samas to be sung on special occasions are
recorded. In the end we shall see that the seer was fully aware of
the importance of meditation in the company of the good.

       1. The Ideological Element. A concept or an idea proves to
be a support (आलंबन) for meditation. This is called the ideological
element by Prof. R.D. Ranade.' An Alambana is the mental exercise
practised by the Yogins in endeavouring to bring before their
thoughts the gross form of the eternal. A full-blown version of
meditation on an Alambana is found in the Kathopanisad where the
supreme Alambana is said to be Aum: एतदालंबनं श्रेष्ठं एतदालंबनं परं
एतदालंबनं ज्ञात्वा ब्रह्मलोके महीयते (1. 2.17). (That is the best support; that
 is the supreme support. Knowing that support, one becomes adorable
 in the Brahman-world). While commenting on the Bhagavad-gita
 verse X.20 Samkara says that God is to be meditated upon as the
 inner enlivening principle of all entities. However, if that is not
 possible, the other manifestations enumerated subsequently are to
 be meditated upon. सर्वभूताशयस्थि तः सर्वेषां भूतानामाशये अन्तर्हदि            स्थि तः
 अहमात्मा प्रत्यगात्मा नि त्यं ध्येयः। तदशक्तेन च उत्तरेषु भावेषु चि न्त्यः अहं यस्मात्‌
 अहमेव आदि ः भूतानां कारणं तथा मध्यं च स्थि ति ः अन्तः प्रलयश्च| The higher
 the stature of the idea meditated upon, the more the intensity of
 reverence felt. Hence the necessity of an idea of supreme pre-
 eminence.

        The Vedic seer cherished knowledge, admired valour and
 worshipped lustre. He meditated on God as a reservoir of these
 qualities. We shall begin with the idea of sovereignty and power. God
 is to be meditated upon as the all-powerful and full of glory. “To that
 omnipresent and omniscient Lord, whose resplendent light illumines
 all quarters, whose life-force enlivens the entire creation and whose
 sovereign authority controls every movement of objects — to that
 Lord my prayer is principally addressed” (S.1). “Almighty Lord who

 1   The Bhagavad-gita as a Philosophy of God-realisation, ए. 240

                                         202
                                                 Methods of Meditation



protects all beings, in whom all the worlds find their anchor and who
is all supreme, rules over the entire universe. To propitiate him I
accept you,” prays the Rsi (S.2). “Oh Lord, you are greater than our
enemy and you it is, that controls all power. We worship you as the
all-enveloping one and as sovereign ruler of all. We worship you as
life-giving waters, as strength, as greatness and as plenary power.
We again pray to you as waters, dazzling power and pure strength.
We lie prostrate before you, who are the greatest, all-pervading,
goodness incarnate and all-knowing. We bow low before you, the
ever-expanding one, grand, all-enveloping and omnipresent one. You
are the richest of all, you gather all bounties and keep them with you.
We salute you,” prays Rsi Brahma (S.3). “Oh Sun, wealth and beauty
are verily wedded to you; day and night are your companions; the
stars that shine in the heavens are your forms;‘and earth and heavens
constitute your blooming face. I am your devotee, which fruit of my
action should I yearn for? Should I be satisfied with heaven? Or,
should I desire the whole universe?” (S.4).

     A fundamental pre-requisite ofspiritual life is moral purity and
that is to be found in God at its height. Morality lives because God
is there to manifest it in its purest form. As one light is kindled by
another light, the aspirant wants the princely gift of purity from God.
“Give lustre to our men of holy lore. Give lustre to our kingly men.
Give lustre to commoners and working men. Give through lustre,
lustre to me” (S.5). God is next said to be the moral governor. “Oh
Agni, you are the protector of moral laws, you are the God among
these mortals, you it is, who should be acclaimed in sacrifice” (S.6).

     God can also be contemplated upon as spiritual value. The
aspirant wishes that he himself be endowed with those valuable
qualities and blessed with spiritual illumination. “Thou art the fiery
spirit, give me the fiery spirit. Thou art manly vigour; give me the
manly vigour. Thou art power; give me power. Thou art energy; give
me energy. Thou art battle fury; give me battle fury. Thou art the
conquering might; give me conquering might ” (5.7). “Let my body
be full of vigour; let my wishes come true. Let my mind firmly
resolve to do good; let the Rsi Gharma take his abode in me along
with the lustre of the sun, glory of Brahman and the radiance that


                                 203
Pathway to God in the Vedas


shines in all the three worlds” (S.8). “Oh sun, you are self-born and
are the brightest ray of light (that bear the name of Hiranyagarbha).
As you endow people with radiance, please grant me the lustre by
the name Brahmavarcas. I am imitating your circular path and am
wandering in a circle” (S.9). Another important Mantra describes
God as full of glory and as usual the singer wishes to share them
(S.10).

      2. The Yogic Element.          The physiological aspect has not
escaped the attention of our fore-fathers.

      (a) Concentration of the Mind. One of the etymological
meanings of the word Yoga, is joining together. In meditation it is
the joining of mind with God or the concentration of mind on God’s
name. The definition appears to be very simple; but the process is
inordinately difficult. By nature the mind is unsteady. It travels on
the wings of speed; there is no place which is out of bounds for it.
 In a fraction of a second, it rockets through space and time — past
                                  to an object only to get loosened the
present and future. It joins itself
 next moment. By the time we say the mind is ‘here’, it is ‘there’, and
 till we say it is ‘there’ it is gone. How to catch hold of this tremulous
 entity and how to make it steady, are the tormenting questions. One
 thing, however, is very peculiar with the mind. Once it takes interest
 in a thing, it remains glued to it. It is therefore the duty of the aspirant,
 to bring it back from its sojourns and pin it down to the name of God.
 “From the sea of clarified butter swells up a stream of sweet nectar.
 Let it reach Agni and attain immortality. 1 know the secret name of
 this clarified butter, which becomes immortal — it is ‘“God’s tongue’;
 the navel of immortality is its other epithet. We are repeating the
 name of clarified butter in this sacrifice. We continue to offer
 oblations in the sacrificial fire so that this four-horned, white ox in
 the form of sacrifice will vomit the clarified butter. Listen to me as
 I chant the name of this clarified butter with utmost devotion” (S.11).
 Sayana understands Brahman by ‘clarified butter’ and Aum by
 ‘name of clarified butter’. In his ingenius commentary on Taittiriya
 Aranyaka X.10.2 where the same stanza occurs, Sayana mentions
 that therein the efficacy of Aum is unambiguously stated. In the
 above Mantra the name is said to be ‘God’s tongue’ (जि ह्ना देवानाम्‌) and
                                     204
                                                              Methods of Meditation



‘naval of immortality’               (अमृतस्य    नाभि ः).   Why? Sayana has his
explanation ready. तच्च प्रणवाख्यं नाम देवानां जि हा देवैः ध्यानपरैः नि रन्तरं
उच्चारमाणत्वेन frat इव सदा मुखमध्ये वर्तते (By constantly uttering the
name, gods have made it a co-tenant in their mouths, along with the
tongue). Again the name is called अमृतस्य नाभि ः as it is the support
of total liberation as the naval of the wheel is the support of the
chariot (वि नाशरहि तस्य मोक्षस्य नाभि ः रथचक्रस्य नाभि ः इव आश्रयभूतं अनेन हि
मुक्ति   रूपफलं प्राप्यते... एतस्यां ऋचि   प्रणवस्य मोक्षसाधनत्वं उक्तम्।‌ In the above
hymn, the ox is referred to as vomitting the clarified butter, meaning
thereby that the sacrificer gets back the fruit of his sacrifice. Thus
does Aum possess the power of leading us Godward. The sweetness
of the name of God tempts the mind to be joined to it. “Who is that
God whose charming name we should always remember? Who will
take me to Aditi so that I can see my father and mother? We should
 meditate on the name of Agni who stands foremost among the gods.
 He will lead me to Aditi so that            can see my father and mother” hopes
Muni Ajigarti Sunahsepa (S.12). Constant remembrance ofthe name
of God must be resorted to in order to be free from the bondage of
 this world. Madhva, in his Rgbhasya, points out that Sunahgepa did
 this in order to free himself from the pillar (यूपात्‌ आत्मवि मोक्षाय स्तुवन्‌
देवान्‌ प्रथक्‌ प्रथक्‌). This helps the aspirant to have a vision of Visnu
and Laksmi—the father and mother of the universe (कर्तुः परानंदतनोरि दानीं
मनामहे चारु वि ष्णोः सुराणां नामेशः चेत्‌ इन्दि रायै पुनदद्शि यं तां मातरं तं च
देवं... स एव माता पि ता च देवः तस्मि न्‌सदा संस्थि ताः तत्‌पुनर्दा त्‌). The chanting
 of God’s name with one-pointed devotion and deep concentration is
 the basic necessity. “Oh singers of God’s praises, propitiate with
 adoration from the depth of your being, God Visnu, the primordial
 substance” (S.13). “Oh Indra, right on being born, you made your
 mind steady and firm. Though you were alone, you slayed many an
 enemy in the battle. You destroyed mountains and won an unsizable
 number of cows” (S.14). Meditation on the name of God implies that
 one is always ‘awake to God’.*““There are seven Rsis in the body.
 They are always alert and they protect the body. When a man goes
 to sleep they are absorbed in the Atman. At that time, Prana and
 Apana that are the e/an vital and that know no sleep, are perfectly

                                              205
Pathway to God in the Vedas



awake in the body” (S.15). The seven Rsis are said to be the nose,
tongue, eye, skin, ear, speech and mind. The protectors are Prana and
Apana. “I am bringing back the mind of the aspirant, which refuses
to be controlled inspite of great effort and which darts swiftly to
heaven, to earth, to all the four directions, to the great ocean, to the
rays of the sun, to waters, herbs and plants, to the sun, dawn and to
mountains, to the remote corners of the universe and to the past and
present times” (S.16). This fickle mind, once made steady, works
wonders. “The learned devotees who have complete control over
their minds have moved Agni with the help of concentration, in order
that Agni should accept their offerings’, says the sage Virupa
Angirasa (S.17). “Prajapati, the inspirer and creator, first concen-
trated his mind and with a great insight, determined that Agni is most
essential for all beings to achieve their purposes. He, therefore,
popularised the keeping of Agni on the earth. We work under the
command of Savitr who inspires all. We concentrate our minds and
 continue the practice of keeping the sacred fire burning, for that
 constitutes the way to heaven” (S.18). But keen application of mind
 is not an easy affair. It must be done with the assistance of God. “I
 invoke the supreme Lord for an all-comprehending mind; Matarisvan,
 for equalising inhalation and exhalation; Surya, for a clear vision;
 the deity of mid-region, for a clear audition and the deity of earth,
 for a sound body; for a good power of speech I invoke the goddess
 Saraswati, who is intimately connected with the mind” (S.19).

     (b) The Psycho-physiological Aspect. Concentration is a
psycho-physiological process. The dual task of controlling both
mind and body can be accomplished by means of Yoga which, as
the Yoga-sttra definition goes, is cessation of all mental modifica-
tions         योगः चि त्तवृत्ति नि रोधः (I. 2). One point, however, must be
remembered while discussing the nature of Yoga. A clear-cut
distinction must be made between the control of mind due to
discrimination (वि वेकज्ञान) or realisation of Brahman (ब्रह्मात्मैक्य) ०1 the
one hand, and the control of mind by suppression of all its activities
on the other. The former state is positive bliss while the latter is
negative in as much as there is only absence of sorrow. “No doubt,
one feels a sort of pleasure in such Samadhi on account of the

                                    206
                                                    Methods of Meditation



absence of worries consequent upon the withdrawal of the mind from
external objects, but this does not indicate that the Yogi has realised
the supreme Truth”.' In fact, “the Yogis think that misery is caused
by the activities of the mind. Hence they direct all their energy to
the suppression of the Vrttis of the mind. But the Vrttis reappear if
the attempt        is slightly relaxed. The Yogis on account of their
ignorance of the real nature of the mind, fight with their own
shadows. The Jnani, on the other hand, realises the mind as well as
all its activities as identical with the non-dual Brahman”
                                                         .’ This is not
to devalue Yogic practice which is highly useful; but one has to
remember that the goal is not mere concentration but realisation of
Brahman. Yogic practices are an aid and not the goal. We do not find
in the Vedas any elaborate or exact knowledge of Yogic physiology;
but many of the Mantras are suggestive and commentators have read
in these verses the rudiments of Yogic doctrines. “Which are the
three wheels of your triangular chariot? Where are the three planks
to be used as a seat? Oh ASwins, when will you harness the horse
by name Rasbha to your chariot by riding which you go for a
sacrifice?” asks Rsi Hiranyastipa Angirasa (S.20). A summary of
the Yogic interpretation of this Mantra by a celebrated Yogin ofthe
16th century Shrichandra Maharaj in his Yoga-pradipa goes as
follows: “The word Trivrta in the Mantra refers to the body as
composed of the three elements, fire, water and earth, and is termed
a chariot (Ratha). We do not know the exact location of the three
lower plexuses (Tricakra) situated in the body, namely Muladhara,
Svadhisthana and Manipira; neither do we know the place of the
vital air. We are denied the knowledge of the three upper plexuses
viz. Anahata, Visuddha and Ajna along with their confluence in the
thousand-petalled lotus (Sanila) which is the abode of lord Siva. We
are equally ignorant even of the time of the union (Yoga) of the great
serpent power, with the highest Lord who is omnipotent, self-
effulgent and bliss as such, and who shines by the sport (Rasbha)
of this absorption. Oh indestructible Siva-sakti, you are verily our

union with the ultimate reality.” ( त्रि वृतः) त्रि वृद्भूतकार्यस्य तेजोऽवन्नरवि कारस्य,

अपि मातापि तरौ शक्ति शि वौ, युष्मत्‌ प्रसादात्‌ अस्मि न्‌ पि ण्डे कस्य चक्रस्य कि ं स्थानं
