सहस्रदलपदूमस्थशि वे छयगमनसमयश्च क इत्येतत्‌ सर्वमवगतं भवतु, अवगत्य च
तथानुति ष्टेयं भवत्करुणयेति          भावः).' It is really wonderful how the seer of
 the hoary past could ‘look into’ the body and think of three wheels.
 Today the physiology of the Cakras (plexuses) is well grasped. But
 their mention, though indirectly, is witness to the intelligence and
 insight of the sage of unrecorded past. From the commentary of
 Shricandra Maharaj, it becomes clear that Yogic physiology was
still an enigma to him. The process of controlling the senses is
suggested in yet another poem. “I am opening with the weapon of
knowledge the knots of your seven Pranas and eight nerve centres.
Be you a messenger of Agni and approach Yama. I will keep a place
for you in Agni. Let this Agni enter the body. Let speech achieve
the Prana” (S.21). The destruction of the seven Pranas implies the
control of the senses and the opening of eight nerve-centres is the


1   Quoted in the article वेदों में योग by Swami Gangeshvarananda, in the Yoganka
    of Kalyan, (Second edition) P.86


                                                   208
                                                    Methods of Meditation



piercing of eight plexuses of the body, 1.6. making clear the way for
the Kundalini. We also come across an allegorical description of this
process. “Oh Agni, you have seven kinds of fuel, you display seven
tongues of flame like Kali, Karali etc.; seven Rsis including Marichi
know you, you are fond of seven places like Ahavaniya and others.
Seven priests like Mitra and Varuna who officiate at the sacrifice,
worship you with seven kinds of sacrifice beginning with Agnistoma.
Oh Agni, let your seven repositories get their fill of clarified butter”
(७.22). The fuel here is interpreted! as the objects of senses (or
sometimes as seven cranial nerves); the tongues of flame are the
energies of the senses; the places are the seats of the senses and
so on. The purport of the Mantra is that the objects of senses are
to be sacrificed and Brahman is to be attained. “The body is a well
having ten mechanisms, viz. two eyes, two ears, mind, speech, the
incoming breath, the outgoing breath and the two regular outlets
directed towards elimination” (S.23). This is the human body with
different orifices; the self lives in it and the knower of that self
attains to Brahman. It is remarkable that the Rsi shows a deep insight
into human physiology. There is an explicit reference to different
parts of the body — both visible and not visible — as also to sense-
organs, to different feelings and to vital breaths. Control of bodily
functions which is of fundamental importance in Yogic physiology
is also clearly stated in Kena Sikta of Atharvaveda. In this context
specific mention must be made of the two Yogic stages, namely,
posture and breath-control. “The seeker (Atharvan or he whose mind
is not fickle but is steady) in a steady posture fixes his head so as
to bring it in line with his heart and then channelises his vital breath
into the Brahmarandhra. The head of such a Yogin is a veritable
treasure-house of gods. The vital breath, food and mind protect it
quite securely. Purusa expands in all directions and pervades all the
quarters. He who knows the citadel of Brahman knows that he is
called Purusa on account of his residence in the citadel. He who
knows that citadel which is full of nectar is endowed with real
knowledge, clear vision, vital breath and good progeny. He does not
lose his eyesight and vital energy before old age. Here is the


1   cf. Samkara’s commentary on Mundakopanisad II. 1.8

                                    209
Pathway to God in the Vedas



invulnerable city of gods with eight plexuses and nine doors. It is
radiant with blissful light. Inside it there is a shining house supported
by three spokes. In that resplendent house there is a mysterious
being infused with Atman. Only the illumined souls realise that
Being. That invulnerable radiant fortress teeming with overwhelm-
ing light does away with all sorts of miseries. In that city Brahman
takes its residence” (S.24).

     There are a number of stanzas where only the senses or the
Pranas are mentioned with an implied suggestion of channelising
them Godward. For example, “Indra has two horses. The wise say
that the horses are harnessed with a clear understanding that they
are meant for sacrifice” (S.25). The two horses are Prana and Apana
and they are to be yoked to the task of meditation. This constitutes
sacrifice. We may note here that this is the essense of what the saint
of Umadi meant by entwining the name of God with the in-coming
and the out-going breath. God’s name should be uttered with Prana
and Apana, with each breath that comes in and with every breath
 that goes out. Such kind of putting them to the service of God
 constitutes real sacrifice. On the whole, we find a mention of
 different organs of the body in a symbolic fashion. There is no
 explicit account ofthe physiological processes involved in the Yogic
 exercises. Whatever is present in the Vedas is the barest minimum.
 However, it is sufficient to serve as a foundation of the great
 superstructure built later by the Yoga system. In the Vedas there is
 a suggestion here and a suggestion there; while the Upanisads have
 a much more systematic view to present.

       (c) Symbolic Description of Yogic Exercises. The practice of
 Yoga or spiritual pursuit is often symbolised by sacrifice -in the
 Vedas. Agni, the deity of sacrifice, therefore, assumes an unparal-
 leled significance. Help is sought from him. “Oh Agni, Atharvan has
 brought you from heaven to earth; he has revealed you by churning
 waters”, observes the sage Barhaspatya Bharadwaja (S.26). This is
 understood as manifestation of light in the devotee’s mind. In
 another Rk, we have a reference to the process of yoking two horses
 to the chariot. “This brilliance at the centre can travel through the
 length and breadth of the universe. Bright orbs are surrounding it

                                   210
                                                         Methods of Meditation



and are twinkling in the sky. Servicemen are yoking horses to his
chariot. The horses are so beautiful that they immediately create a
desire in the spectator’s mind. They are reddish in colour and ready
to gallop. They are strong enough to carry the chariot in which the
valiant God is sitting” (S.27). The whole process means yoking
Prana and Apana to lead the aspirant to God. Madhvacarya in his
Rg-bhasya has      identified these horses with Manas (Mind) and
Buddhi (Intellect). When they are working together, i.e. when the
mind has turned its back on the senses, it is sure to reach God.
Requests are multiplied. “Let Sadasaspati, without whose grace no
sacrifice can be successful, bestow intellect on us” (S.28). This is
an intellect which has attained to full concentration. Savitr helps man
achieve mental concentration for entering the region of light (S.29).
This Savitr must, therefore, be given oblation by means of speech,
hearing and mind (S.30). Distant is the goal and hard the path.
Victory can be won only by the two-pronged attack of rigorous
penance and bounteous grace. Thus the sage Kutsa Angirasa
assures: “Oh you sons of Sudhanvan, my ancestors, though yet
immature, you had resorted to the pathway for felicity, and after a
long and strenuous journey, you ultimately reached the home of the
divine Savitr. Inspired by devotion, you approached him and beheld
him   face   to   face;   and   the   divine   creator    (Savitr)   bestowed
immortality upon you. The sole ladle designed for testing divine
nectar was fashioned by you into four” (S.31). If this Mantra stresses
self-effort, the following one emphasises grace. “Let all the objects
that we need be gifted to us. Let the Lord stand near us in our effort
to achieve wealth. Let him give a proper direction to our intellect”
(S.32). Mere concentration of mind is not enough unless it is touched
with divinity.

      3. The Emotional Element. The trump card in the game is the
bond between man and God; man’s wish to enter into the zone of
His love. The energies of the mind flow to God. Before a devotee,
the temptations of the world are spread in vain. Such attachment to
God and detachment from world constitutes the emotional element.

     (a) Bhava.What is important in the process of meditation is
devotion or Bhava of the aspirant. Without this life-blood meditation

                                      211
Pathway to God in the Vedas



remains pale and limp. It lacks the dynamic energy to carry us
through. The lordliness and benevolence of the deity command the
feeling of reverence. This helps Bhava. “Indra alone deserves to be
approached by all. I pay homage to him. Let him be pleased by my
singing. Himself strong, he makes his devotees strong. Full of truth
and manliness his nature is unfathomable. He is the victorious
sovereign” (S.33). Mortal man should look to Indra in his struggle
for abundance” (5.34). “Your demeanour is solemn. The El Dorado
of your riches is beyond our poor imagination” (S.35). Along with
feeling of respectful love for God comes the consciousness of our
own littleness. “Our understanding falls short of grasping your
remarkable sway which has enveloped the entire creation” (S.36).
A reverence-whippped poem runs: “None like you, Oh Indra, was
ever born in the heavens or on the earth and none will ever be born.
We, who seek cows and horses, prestige and wisdom, offer prayers
to you” (S.37). The outstanding bearing of the Lord to whom the
aspirant automatically goes into submission is a topic of many
poems. “The bow and arrow in your hand enhance your superior
status. The   rainbow-coloured garlands   of flowers   add   to your
radiance. It is through your generosity that you broadcast compas-
sion on the wide world. Oh Rudra, none can compete with you in
 matters of prowess and glory” (S.38). For achieving prosperity an
 aspirant would befriend Indra and Indra alone (S.39). With bubbling
 eagerness Srutakaksa Angirasa chants: “Sing on, Oh seekers, the
 valient deeds of Indra who partakes of your Soma offerings, who
 is all-conquering (शतक्रतु), who is lauded and invited by many, who
 is a hero of epics and renowned of old. Proclaim that his name is
 Indra” (S.40). Indra is a giver of light and knowledge.


      It is not without reason that Spinoza characterised the love
 which man has for God as the same as the love which God has for
 man and ultimately which man has for man. This mutual love, love
of man for God which is full of veneration and the love of God for
man which is nothing but blessing, is expressed by many a religious
song. “I sacrifice for you and sing praises. Be a pillar of strength
to me” (S.41). We find a Rk which prescribes a somewhat novel
way. “Oh Visvakarman, let the friendly priests know all the three

                                212
                                                     Methods of Meditation



of your bodies, the superior, the middle and the inferior. Vitalise your
own body and offer worship to yourself’ (S.42). Mahidhara, a
celebrated commentator on the Vajasaneyi Samhita, remarks: “man
is incompetent to honour the creator and so the worship must be
performed by Himself ”.!' Incidentally, स्वयं यजस्व (worship yourself)
may also mean—‘offer yourself as a gift’. In Nirukta we are given
a history. ““ViSvakarman, the son of Bhuvana performed a सर्वमेध
sacrifice. He gave away everything on earth and in the end offered
himself as libation” (S.43). This sacrifice is referred to in a verse
in Rgveda (5.44).
      Love is a great binding force and it is but natural that Gods run
to the place where the devotees gather. “Oh high-souled Soma, flow
rapidly. Come to us with your cherished halo. Let the words ‘I am
there’ be on your lips” (S.45). One is impressed by the sparkle and
gaiety of these words of Brhanmati Angirasa. Sages long to have God
in their vicinity. “We mortals kneel before the learned Agni to come
and take our charge. Oh Agni, the sage Vatsa catches your attention
from the high heavens” (S.46). Once God owns a devotee, He will
not forsake him at the critical moment. The Rsis adore the valour of
Indra who sides with his devotees at the time of a pitched battle. “Oh
learned men, I have heard that it is Indra alone who can distribute
trophies in an equitable way. He is our only solace in times of
adversity. Let him grant us what we yearn for’ (S.47).
      Bhava     is after all a complex conception and no simple
definition can analyse its constitution. It is love and faith. It is turning
away from the tantalising world. It is stepping out of illusions and
straightening the crookedness of mind. It is seeing God in all that
surrounds us and thriving on an overwhelming adoration for him. In
fine, it is an intellectual conviction that God is all in all, an emotional
belongingness to Him and a voluntary surrender to Him. In Bhava,
all the three faculties — intellect, affection and volition, are engulfed
by the holy Being. With Bhava, God is not away; without it all
worship is an empty show.
       (b) Surrendering Oneself to God. The lode-star of our actions

1   See Ralph T.H.Griffith, The Hymns of the Rgveda, Vol II, Munshiram
    Manoharlal, 54 Rani Jhansi Road, New Delhi-55, P.497, Note 5


                                     213
Pathway to God in the Vedas



must be God. “Even our incoming and outgoing breath must be
directed to God” (S.48). “One who sacrifices, pours libations, bakes
cakes for offering and sings in His honour, is a favourite of Indra”
(S.49). Only men of dull intellects (दभ्रचेताः) disregard the divine cow
who is a connoisseur in poetry, who utters sacred words, who is
virtuous and who has come here only for the service of Gods” (S.50).
This shows that only the fools fail to put their speech (cow) to proper
use and instead of uttering the name of God, they waste words on
trifling things. The devotee sincerely pleads that he belongs to God
and to none else. “We are yours, Oh Indra. Give them refuge who
praise you and who offer you gifts. Let them rest happily even in
old age in their own houses” (S.51). The final resting place is at the
feet of the Almighty. An unnamable fascination drags the devotee
to God. “Our praises would reach your ears however distant you may
be. We will swiftly come into your presence (अरं 414)” (S.52). An
important corrollary from the feeling of surrender is the intense
desire to offer to God the thing which is dearest to oneself. Real
fondness for God, entails relinquishing choicest objects for His sake.
“Protect him, Oh Indra, who gives away the gifts that are most dear
to him. We pray to you, let all our friends be free from sins” (S.53).
The way to salvation lies through a feeling of resignation. This is
a perorative refrain of yet another hymn. “Oh Agni, let his life be
vanquished who is a sinner and who wants to enslave us by means
of eyes, mind and thoughts. Salvation is a fruit of self-surrender.
You are an abode of Indra. With all my speed, with all the powers
at my command, with all my mental abilities, with all that I possess,
I surrender to you and enter into you. You are a refuge of Indra, with
all my speed.... I enter into you. You are a shelter of Indra. With
all my speed.... 1 enter into you. You are a shield of Indra.... I enter
into you” (S.54). The tenderness of mind outruns egoism.

     More important than external gifts are internal gifts. When
praises are accorded to gods in words, these words must be carriers
of the deep faith of the aspirant. This is the attitude ofa person whose
moral sense is not slumbering. Rsi Nodhas Gautama’s lyric runs:
“TI am offering libations to the deity. I am offering a beautiful song
to him who kills the enemies in no time. The wise have presented

                                  214
                                                       Methods of Meditation



him with glorificatory stanzas with all their heart, with all their mind
and with all their imagination” (S.55). Sayana interprets the word
हदा as heart; मनसा as that which is inside the heart and मनीषा as
the knowledge which flows from the mind. In this way all mental
powers are channelised towards God. It is interesting to note that

the Kathopanisad later picked up the words, हदा, मनसा and मनीषा and
yet refused to grant fruitfulness to them in the matter of God-
realisation, by claiming that they are all inadequate.' Be it so. The
present couplet only stresses the importance of laying all posses-
sions at God’s feet. “Oh you creator of the universe, you are the first
promulgator of the moral law. I present to you all that I have. Even
in our old age we shall cross this life by means of the legacy of
sacrifice (fruit of meditation)”, says Rsi Bhrgu (5.56). A very
sincere devotee pleads that he does not have any material gifts to
offer and so he offers his own mind with fervid devotion. “I do not
possess a cow, nor do 1 have an axe to cut the sacrificial sticks.
However, all that I have, I bring to you” (S.57). The mind itself is
to be offered as an oblation, for this verily is the most valuable gift
of a devotee. All material things are to be given by the mind and
not merely by hand. The mind will have to detach itself from that
possession; if it hovers round the thing which is accorded away, the
act is hollow. Non-attachment sanctifies gifts. “Knower of the whole
creation, you are the knower of all actions too. I offer to you the
libation which is made sacred by mind and heart. Accept them”
(5.58). The words हदा and मनसा occur here also. The lyric describes
the deity as a knower of actions (वयुनानि वि दान्‌) 210 this suggests
that God knows the quality of our actions, which is obviously
determined by our sincere devotion. The Lord values the devotion
and not the actual consideration. Even with intense devotion some
vestige of expectation remains; some hope for a trophy still lingers.
“With your support, we shall resist our enemy. You are ours and we
are yours” (S.59). “We along with sages pray to you for help, Oh
Mitra and Varuna. You possess an all-embracing vision. By your
grace, we may successfully try for the kingdom of self, which is

1   See, R.D. Ranade, A Constructive Survey of Upanishadic Philosophy, Pp. 248-
    249


                                      215
Pathway to God in the Vedas



infinitely vast and yet the only one worthy of our steel” (S.60).

     4. Nine Kinds of Bhakti. The usual classification of Bhakti
or devotion is a nine-fold one: Sravana (listening to the name and
exploits of God), Kirtana (singing His praises), Smarana (uttering
the name of God), Padasevana (physical service of God), Arcana
(worship), Vandana (prostrating before Him), Dasya (service of
God), Sakhya (friendliness with God) and Atmanivedana (complete
surrender to Him). Often their shades mingle and no devotional
mood can be fitted exclusively into one single pigeon-hole. We find
in the Vedas rudiments of these different Bhaktis.

     (a) Sravana : We have to listen to the utterance of God’s name
whenever and wherever possible. “Oh        Indra, bearer of sacred
spiritual words (names), new devotees who are inquisitive to know
about you, intently listen to your glorious deeds. Thereafter they in
turn relate them devoutly to others as far as they know” (S.61).
Hearing of auspicious news comes as a balm of peace. “Let me be
always hearing about the brave deeds of gods even from afar. Never
should my power of hearing be impaired or lost” (S.62).

     (b) Kirtana : By far the most vigorously practised type of Bhakti
in the Vedas is Kirtana or singing the name of God. Kirtana is more
effective than other types. Singing includes hearing. Even a lone
Singer is listening to his own words. Thus two sense organs, viz.
speech and hearing, are working in collaboration. If there is group
singing coloured by a musical tinge it soothes a man’s nerves. The
charm of musical instruments enhances the sweetness of the name.
“With righteousness, meditation and laudatory songs, the devotees
loudly extol the valiant and efficient Soma” (5.63). Their wits
sharpened on the grindstone of devotion, the sages pour out a
bewildering variety of ovations. Addressed to a large number of
deities, these prayers, decked with beautiful descriptions of nature,
spotlight the majesty of the Creator. They display a high literary
quality. The Vedas can well be called the locus classicus of the
Kirtana Bhakti, though, paradoxically, the word Bhakti is not popular
in the Vedas and the performance of Kirtana as we understand today
is not specifically mentioned. “He who offers oblations to Visnu, the

                                216
                                                     Methods of Meditation



omnipresent lord, ancient and unborn, ever fresh and creator of this
universe, is endowed with plenitude in this life and attains to the
highest ideal of human existence” (5.64). ““Visnu measures out all
the terrestrial regions and supports the highest heavens. He traverses
the entire universe in three steps” (S.65). The seeker wishes that
God’s fondness for him is always on the increase (S.66). “Oh Mitra
and   Varuna,   when   others   give    expression   to   their   meditative
applause, we also sing the Uktha Mantras with reverence” (S.67).
Rsi Samyu Barhaspatya commands the devotee to utter Arka and
Sama hymns to adore Indra (S.68). Then there are requests appended
to the praises. ‘““We want our eyes to feast upon your beautiful form
by residing in your joyous region” (S.69). This expresses a longing
for God’s nearness. The naturalness of these demands is the marvel.

      (c) Smarana : We also find in the Vedic hymns, traces of
Smarana Bhakti. “Learned men move your heart by meditating on
you. Let our encomiums glorify you” (S.70). Again, “We, your
earnest devotees—mortals though we are—mostly engage ourselves
in meditating on your name. You are omniscient and immortal”
(S.71).

      (d) Padasevana :    It is very difficult to distinguish between
Padasevana and Arcana. Padasevana, again, is very near to Dasya.
No single hymn, therefore, can be cited as exclusively exemplifying
one type of Bhakti. Mantras like the following can be illustrative of
Padasevana Bhakti. “Devotees with all humility take recourse to the
holy feet of the Lord and obtain unblemished fame. Thereafter they
realised the sacred names and enjoyed your auspicious vision”
(S.72). “For the acquisition of spiritual bliss devotees offer adoration
at your feet. Wearing a beautiful vesture, as radiant as the sun, Oh
valiant Indra, you became a source of great inspiration to your
devotees” (S.73).

      (e) Arcana : Man is indeed a worshipping animal. He folds his
hands whenever he meets with excellence. Worship may or may not
have any material; intense devotion is sufficient, and of devotion the
sages had a double dose. “I worship the sun (Savitr) who is the
protector, who creates heaven and earth, who is learned and who is
a great doer. He inspires truth, supports delightful objects, is lovable


                                  217
Pathway to God in the Vedas



and deserves to be meditated upon” (S.74). God is all-pervading and
to say that he resides in a particular place is to limit him. Inspite
of different places having been assigned to God, He really resides
in man’s heart. In his Rg-vidhana, Saunaka says, “The sages have
prescribed the worship of God as immanent in six abodes : waters,
fire, heart, sun, altar or images. For those who are given to rituals,
God resides in Agni; for the intellectual, He is in the high heavens;
‘or the dull-witted, He takes lodgement in images; whereas for the
Yogins, He has His abode in their very hearts”. Again, “Oh men,
vorship lord Indra with melodious songs; sing, Oh Priyamedhas,
vith worshipful reverence. Let children also join in idolising Indra,
 vho is mighty and strong like an invulnerable fortress” (S.75).
 Indra is vast, ageless, sublime and ever youthful. Even heaven and
 arth have failed to measure the greatness of this holy lord and they
 nll ever fail to do so” asserts Muni Gathina Visvamitra (S.76).
     (f) Vandana : Lying prostrate or saluting is a universal way of
 howing respect. It takes various forms on different soils and at
 ifferent times, but the underlying humility made         much   more
  ypealing by a touch of sincerity is the same. “Salutation is itself
  2nance. I practise that penance. The heaven and earth are sustained
  / austerities” (S.77). “Salutation to the great and to the small; to
  e old and to the young. Let us be hale and hearty to perform
  .crifice” (S.78). It is sterling merit, and not age or position or
  telligence that claims a reverential attitude.   A famous anthem
   15: Veneration to the Rudra who assumes a mild form and to
   idra who assumes a terrible form. Veneration to Rudra who blows
   vay enemies from near and from afar without effort and to Rudra
   10 with shining locks of hair, looks like a wish-tree with branches
   all directions. Veneration to Rudra who carries us across mundane
   istence” (S.79). Rudra is also adored in his ugly forms and cosmic
   ‘ms (S.80). We may particularly note how the Vedic seers could
   >] the presence of God in the minutest operation of nature. There
   no sphere of life where God’s healing touch is not found, no type
   work where His active initiative is not traceable, no kind of growth
    ere His nurturing hand is absent. “I bow to the Rudra who is on
     other side of the river of existence (i.e. who has attained
   anmukti) and to the Rudra who is on this side of the river (1.6.

                                 218
                                                 Methods of Meditation



who is manifest in the worldly life).      I bow to the Rudra who
abides in renowned sacred places and to the Rudra who has his
habitat on the bank of a small well. I bow to the Rudra who looks
with a fond eye on tiny blades of grass and to the Rudra who loves
the foam on the waters of a river” (S.81). Invocations are addressed
to gods and an award is expected in return (S.82). The seekers are
confident that by the power of their intense devotion they are
approximating God. “Oh Agni, we devotees, with minds fixed on you
day and night, are with humble obeisance coming nearer and nearer
to you day by day” (S.83).

     (g) Dasya : Service is a prominent manifestation of love. The
greater the love, the more sincere and disinterested the service. The
loving God has never hesitated in serving devotees in their distress.
By driving Arjuna’s horses he has earned for Himself the epithet
Parthasarathi which is a seal-mark recognising the genuineness of
service. It is next to impossible to reach the ideal of service which
God renders to his devotees, for He serves out of pure love, serves
without expectation of fruit, serves even in the absence of a word
of gratitude from the devotees, serves in any capacity and at any odd
time and place and sometimes without letting the devotee know that
he is being served. Humanity, in its own way has produced great
servants of God. A poem says : “Oh Agni, I am your servant like
those who serve rich men” (S.84). To love God is to obey His
commands. “I am innocent. With all my abilities, 1 529९ Varuna who
fulfils our desires and who nourishes the world. The great Varuna
gives wisdom to the simple. The wise Varuna makes his servants
rich” (S.85). The utilitarian in the Vedic seer, however, does expect
that his service be recognised; and for that matter, not only
recognised but rewarded. “Offer a beautiful hymn to that great God
who is terror to his enemies, who is forceful, yet very kind. Pour
out solemn orisons to Indra. He will never fail to notice your service”
(5.86). Again, “We are engaged in your service day in and day out.
We deem it our duty. All our hopes are concentrated on you” (S.87).
Rsi Somahuti Bhargava pleads that he is eager to serve God (S.88).

     (h) Sakhya : Who but a man of high spiritual stature be a friend
of God? Relation of friendship flourishes on a basis of equality. The

                                 219
Pathway to God in the Vedas



naturalism of the Vedas fights shy of the Mysterium Tremendum —
the mysterious terrible super-entity — the idea weaved into the
texture of philosophy of religion by Rudolf Otto. The gods of Vedic
people are somewhat exalted beings, making              it easy to strike
friendship with them. “Oh Soma of pleasant form, lord of green
herbs, be an enricher of treasures unto us, as a friend to a friend”
(S.89). “We begin cultivating your friendship, Oh Indra. Destroy the
Strings on the bows of our enemies” (S.90). What touches us is the
childlike simplicity of the request. The number of hymns asking for
friendship of gods is very vast. But in almost all of them the desire
to reap fruit from the friendship is invariably present. “Oh Soma,
be you comforting to our hearts as a father to a son or a friend to
friend” (S.91). Soma is entreated to grant health, wealth and his own
friendship (S.92). The desire for affluence is present in every other
 verse. “Oh slayer of Vrtra, let us be united for the sake of gaining
 victory. Even irreligious men should accept us” (S.93). The Vedic
 aspirants are not less interested in good thoughts than in good things.
 Wretched thoughts are enemies, while holy thoughts are friends.
 “May    noble thoughts come to us         from all   sides,   undistorted,
 unhampered and overflowing, may we live in friendship with gods”
 (S.94). “Oh    Soma, let not our speech be defiled            by   ignoble
 utterances. Give us contentment; be our friend” (S.95). The sage
 does not fail to accord eulogies to gods when pleading for a helping
 hand and he is confirmed in his devotion.

        (1) Atmanivedana : Ramadiasa the outspoken Maharashtra saint
 Stated that it is vain to call oneself a devotee if one has not fully given
 oneself   over   to   God.   Offering    one’s   whole   being     to   God
 (Atmanivedana) is the finest type of Bhakti. “I have completely given
 over my mind to you; sit like a king at the celebration of sacrifice
 conducted in your honour” is a prayer of Krsna Angirasa (S.96).

        5. Company of the Good. Meditation in the company of saints
 is a social affair and one automatically reaps many advantages in
 collective meditation. Devotion becomes more intense by the two
 processes of social facilitation and circularity of response. Psy-
 chologists have experimentally found out that when we are working
 in a group, our performance becomes better. It may be because a

                                    220
                                                     Methods of Meditation


person, when in company, is ashamed to be lazy and puts im more
effort; or it may be due to a competitive spirit that may enter into
one’s mind when every body around is working. It is clear that the
sight and sound of others doing the same thing have their effects
on us. This is social facilitation. The other process is circularity of
response,    whereby     the   effects   of social   stimulation   increase
themselves by a kind of mutually exciting reverberation.'
                                                     These two
processes make meditation in the company of other aspirants more
effective and therefore, more desirable. The sages call upon like-
minded friends to come and take part in their sacrifices and singing.
“Come, Oh you generous Maruts, the presiding deities of the
sacrifice which is to be performed in the house (Grhayajna); come
with your protective strength. Do not go back. This sacrifice is being
celebrated for your sake, Oh Maruts, who have all power at your
command, who are omniscient and have the lustre of the sun” (S.97).
“You are pure, and hence pure oblations are offered to you. Oh
Maruts,     I perform a pure Adhwara sacrifice for you. On account
of your strict adherence to moral law you have realised the truth and
accordingly have acquired the power to purify others” (5.98).
     The righteous souls not only enrich the sanctity of the sacrifice
but also sustain the moral law. “When prayers are being sung for
gods, the devotees, wearing sacred symbols, gather around for the
holy function. Their ancient father is ever present there to protect
the divine assembly from any harm. When the almighty Varuna
clouded the whole of mid-region, only those who were given to
meditation were able to remain with him” (S.99). “Top-ranking
leaders possessing indomitable courage and endowed with glorious
character are alone able to protect the moral principles, with no ill-
feeling towards any body”, says the sage Matsya Sammada (S.100).
Again, “The Maruts whose horses have coloured spots, whose
mother is Prsni, and whose desire it is to do good to everyone, should
be present here. Let Manu and others whose tongue the fire is, who
meditate on the sun, come along” (S.101). Devout worshippers,
whose spiritual achievements are spreading far and wide, and who
follow the right path, are called upon to offer prayers to the heaven
1   See F.H. Allport, Social Psychology, Houghton Mifflin Company, New York,
    1924, P.301


                                     221
Pathway to God in the Vedas



and the earth (S.102). Those who follow the path where gods have
left their footprints, constitute the right company. “Enrich us, Oh
divine Vajins by gifting to us food and wealth whenever there is such
an opportunity. You are talented, immortal and you know the truth.
Partake of the sweet juice and proceed along the path of gods”
(S.103). They alone are worthy of friendship who are friends ofgods.
Their company is sought after by the wise (S.104). One should not
converse with him who is a confidant trickster and who utters unholy
words, but must exchange words with a devotee of God. One should
be submissive to him alone who has achieved all the four goals of
life and who possesses spiritual wealth. One should not speak ill of
him, is the advice of Kanva Ghaura (S.105). Selfless souls always
wish to oblige others. None can destroy them, on the contrary they
destroy the wicked (S.106). “The saintly persons sit for meditation
in gatherings of spiritual seekers and enjoy the blissful experience
of the ancient and ageless divinity. They are also endowed with
supersensuous spiritual powers. Yet while behaving with others they
are full of love and respect” (S.107). This is the importance of
    meditation in the assembly of saints.

         As the aspirant makes progress in meditation, he is imbued with
a feeling of reverence towards God. Understood in the Kantian sense,
reverence is a compound of two feelings, viz. exaltation and humility;
exaltation, because man is essentially divine and humility, because
he is at the same time a creature of sensibility. The divine nature and
the empirical nature are antagonistic in the beginning but the latter
is to be raised to the level of the former. The process of restructuring
begins when we are initiated into spiritual life; it gathers momentum
by the force of augmented meditation and culminates in complete
transmutation of our lower nature. The feeling of reverence which
gives us a peep into Reality elevates us by loosening us from the
gravitational force of desires. And this it does, not by cutting itself
asunder from passions, not by suppressing the passions, but by
sublimating them. “Reverence, in short, is the appearance of the
moral consciousness, in the region of the sensuous feeling, in which
it must appear if it is to realise itself in finite beings like us”.

                                                           231
                           CHAPTER VIII




          The Dark Night of the Soul

     1. A Period of No-Progress. It is a characteristic of spiritual
progress, as of psychological progress, that it does not proceed along
a straight line. If we draw a graph of progress, the curve “shows a
negative acceleration, it flattens out as practice advances; the rate
of improvement decreases.”' There appears a lull without any kind
of improvement. Activity is arrested, as it happens when a        cart-
wheel is stuck up in a pot-hole and does not come out. All past effort
seems to have gone down a bottom-less vessel. Intense spiritual
practice does not yield any fruit so much so that a devotee begins
to question its very efficacy. As no indication of progress is within
sight, and as a bulk of his strenuous work appears to have gone
waste, he is plunged into a state of disappointment. The length of
the period and the intensity of despair differ from person to person.
But by no means, this intervening period of no-progress, can be
called inactivity. The surface ofthe sea looks to be calm though there
is a great turmoil within. There is hectic activity inside the mind;
things collected are being soaked in and absorbed. There is a re-
arranging of lines before a quick march is ordered. The only thing
is that all this goes on silently without sending out any news
bulletins, and giving an impression that the period is fruitless. If in
psychology, this period of no apparent progress is called the plateau
period, in the psychology of religion, it is named the Dark Night of
the Soul. This interval of apparent quietude forms the link between
the two phases of sharp profits     — one which precedes it and the
other which succeeds it. The link itself contributes a great deal to
the progress without being taken any notice of. Only in terms of
concrete gains, can it be called a negative stage; but like all periods
of incubation, it is a period of consolidation and growth. In fact,
psychologists Have characterised this silent period as one “during
1 R.S. Woodworth, Experimental Psychology, Methuen and Co. Ltd., London,
  1951, P.164
                                                     The Dark Night of the Soul



which the lower-order habits were being perfected but were not
sufficiently automatic to permit the development of the higher
habits.”' In spiritual life the plateau may occur right at the start, and
consequently it may appear that there has been no genuine beginning
at all. It is perhaps a period of internal fighting with a number of
impediments on the path. “I am always warring with the world and
with the mind”, says Tukarama. Fighting with the mind is fighting
with passions which pose no less a problem. The Yoga-sutras have
enumerated such obstructions: व्याधि -स्त्यान-संशय-प्रमाद-आलस्य-अवि रति -
भ्रान्ति दर्शन-अलब्धभूमि कत्व-अनवस्थि तत्वानि चि त्तवि क्षेपाः ते अन्तराया :। (1. 30).
(Disease, mental laziness, doubt, lack of enthusiasm, lethargy,
clinging to sense-enjoyments, false perception, non-attainment of
 concentration and falling away from the state when obtained, are the
 obstructing distractions). How do they block the way of progress?
 Swami Vivekananda’s beautiful commentary on this 50198 is : “This
 body is the boat which will carry us to the other shore of the ocean
of life. It must be taken care of. Unhealthy persons cannot be Yogis.
Mental laziness makes us lose all lively interest in the subject,
without which there will neither be the will nor the energy to practise.
Doubts will arise in the mind about the truth of the science, however
strong one’s intellectual conviction may be, until certain peculiar
psychic experiences come, ३४ hearing or seeing at a distance etc.
These glimpses strengthen the mind and make the student perse-
vere... Some days or weeks when you are practising, the mind will
be calm and easily concentrated, and you will find yourself
progressing fast. All of a sudden the progress will stop one day, and
you will find yourself, as it were, stranded. Persevere. All progress
proceeds by such rise and fall.”
     Whatever may be the explanation of this period of plateau, the
point to be noted is, that in the wake of the disappointment caused
by the absence of progress, man begins to devalue himself. Books
on Psychology of Religion are full of accounts of this negative state

 1   Ibid P. 166

2 Raja Yoga, Advaita Ashram, 5 Dehi Entally Road, Calcutta - 14, 1973, Pp. 146-
     147



                                         233
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of an aspirant’s mind. It may lead to utter condemnation of oneself.
This dimunition of self, according to Rudolph Otto,        is of two
different origins. One is the creature-feeling in the presence of the
mysterium      tremendum,       i.e.   the realisation of our own little-ness
compared to the divine prowess. The other is the depreciation,
disvaluation or the feeling of profaneness as diametrically opposed
to   the   holiness    of the      numen.'       While      the   former       is    psycho-
metaphysical as the feeling originates from the infinitude of the
numen, the latter is psycho-axiological as it is caused by the holiness
of the numen.

        2. Optimism of the Vedic Seer. It is remarkable that neither
of these two feelings of littleness, is found to be dominant in Vedic
literature. In the first place, the feeling of awe before the Sublime,
stressed     by    Kant   and      Otto,    and     the     consequent         melting      of
individuality, are rarely found here. The mysticism of the Vedas,
treated     gods   more    as     friends    than      as   terror-striking          powers.
Secondly, though the Vedic thinkers have expressed anxiety as
regards their unhappy conditions, and though they have clearly
admitted their transgressions of the moral laws, they have not
expressed      any    feeling    of profaneness.            The    ‘breezy          and   easy
tempered’ Vedic thinkers would outright dismiss this uncompromis-
ing judgement of self-depreciation                  passed        by   those    who       take
themselves to be sinful creatures before the Holy. The most that
they would do, is to express remorse for an individual wrong action,
and ask for forgiveness. There is no exaggerated abasement of
oneself. No vast-scale self-condemning feelings are found seething
in their bosoms. Another factor to be noted is that their outbursts
do not testify to any acutely piercing or long-lasting despair. They
always see an optimistic picture ahead. Instead of pondering or
grieving over the sins, they would try to remove the blot by invoking
God’s grace. Theirs was a religion of healthy-mindedness. Healthy-
minded       temperament,        according        to   William         James,        is   “the
temperament which has a constitutional incapacity for prolonged
suffering, and in which the tendency to see things optimistically is


1    See, The Idea of the Holy, Pp. 52 ff

                                           234
                                                The Dark Night of the Soul


like a water of crystallisation in which the individual’s character
is set.”! The Vedic sages do not seem to belong to the type of the
sick soul which is continually vexed with the diabolical thoughts
of the weakness of the flesh. One should not lie weeping and
brooding over one’s sins. “Evil is a disease; and worry over disease
is itself an additional form of disease, which only adds to the
original complaint.”? We should be up and doing and take the course
of righteousness again, “...for he that rises again quickly and
continues his race is as if he had never शि    ला." Thus with the Vedic
Rsis, the Dark Night of the soul is, after all, not that pitchy dark;
it is a play of light and shadow and not an unending spell of
hopelessness. We shall see in this chapter how there are times when
God is nowhere to be seen; neither does he pay attention to the
prayers of the devotees. The aspirant begins to search Him. God
is requested to forgive the devotee’s sins and visit him. The Rsis
have recorded their yearning for light and need of protection.
Sometimes a quarrel is picked up but it ends in the devotee’s appeal
for mercy.

      3. A Search for God in the Universe. A number of hymns
indicate an uneasy state of mind in search of God and prayer to God
to run to the devotee. Rsi Aindra Vasukra prays: “What sort of
cheering Soma will please you most, Oh Indra? Oh lustrous one,
listen to my prayer and speed up to my sacrificial hall. When will
the chariot bearing you appear before me and fulfil my desires?
When may I be able to worship you with my offerings of food and
wealth?” (S.1).

    A query is made to find out the whereabouts of the ASwins.
“ASwins of lordly demeanour, sovereigns over auspicious bliss,
where are you now? In whose delightful company have you been held
up? Who has detained you today? Who is that sage to whose house
you have gone?” (S.2). There is no end to this search, and a number
of similar hymns are strewn over the Vedas. “Where is Indra of

1   The Varieties of Religions Experience, The new American Library of World
    Literature, 501 Madison Avenue, New York 22, >. 112
2   Ibid, P. 112
3   Ibid, P. 114

                                    235
Pathway to God in the Vedas



godly deeds? Where and with which devotee is he staying? Oh,
Indra, which kind of sacrifice will be acceptable to you? Which Arka
hymn? Which sacrificer?” (S.3). Or, “Where is Indra, lord among
men, who is of great youthfulness and prowess, who holds his head
high and radiates bravery? Who is serving him with praises? To
whose house does the valiant God retire for partaking Soma? Who
is he that is devoted to Indra? Oh slayer of Vrtra, who is the
beneficiary of your blessings? Who profited from your valour? Who
is completely absorbed in singing your praises? In my house, a mere
mortal though I am, Soma is kept ready to be served. Hasten to my
place and receive it” (S.4). “Who are those that are singing songs
 for you and are eager to walk on the path of rectitude? Which is that
 deity, Rsi or the learned devotee, who is meditating on you? Oh
 Indra, your devotee is singing your greatness and is presenting Soma
 to you. When will you respond to his ardent call, Oh, when?” (S.5).
 A popular verse has clearly spelt out:

 नाहं वसामि    वैकुण्ठे योगि नां हृदये रवौ।
 मद्भक्ता यत्र गायन्ति   तत्र ति ष्ठामि   नारद ॥
 (1 do not reside in the Vaikuntha, nor in the hearts of Yogins, nor
 in the orbit of the sun. I am invariably present where my devotees
 sing my praises). Foreshadowing this, the Vedic Rsi asks: “To
 whose laudations are these Maruts listening? Who can that devotee
 possibly be, who has taken them to his sacrificial hall? Which is that
 highest type of devotion that will help us in pleasing these Maruts
 who fly past the sky like a hawk?” (S.6). “What are the oblations
 by which the revered and ever prospering Indra will befriend us?
 (S.7). In another poem, Agni is approached with a similar prayer.
 Rsi Vamadeva Gautama asks : “You are the presiding deity of this
 sacrifice of ours, Oh Agni, please listen to our prayers. When will
 those prayers be pleasing to you? When will you be bound to us by
 a tie of friendship?” (S.8). “How can the omniscient Varuna,
 bedecked by valour, be propitiated?” (S.9). Such psalms show a
 bewildered state of mind. The sages have the resources ready. They
 have all the material of the sacrifice. They have Soma. They have
 their minds or even themselves to offer. But they do not know how,
 when and to whom to offer these things. They are also afraid that
                                           236
                                              The Dark Night of the Soul



there are rivals who would outsmart them and earn gods’ favours.
Such a state of indetision is always disagreeable. The goal is clear
but the way seems to be unlit. In fact it is not known whether there
is only one way or there are many. There is just groping in the dark.
Looking back, an aspirant remembers the hours of battles through
which he has passed with confidence and courage. Then why this
state   of suspence   and tension? Was his progress hitherto only
spurious? Was his satisfaction illusory? Was his rejoicing prema-
ture? His doubts are greeted with silence. But the sage is not choleric
by temperament, he keeps his calm and hopes for the best. His
condition of torment is short-lived. His mind is not sealed against
the bright side of things. He promises himself that fortune will
change the scene and that lights will be switched on. As he can not
find a way out all by himself, he takes a vigorous resolve and calls
on God Himself to dart to him.


        Here are some of the entreaties. “May you be far or near, or
anywhere in the empyrean, Oh Indra, slayer of Vrtra run to our
succour. Please partake of our gift of Soma and protect us” (S.10).
“Indra, our leaders are invoking your presence from wherever you
may be — whether in the eastern, western, southern or northern
regions. Yoke your speedy horses and be with us. You may be in
the midst of celebration in the heavens or in the region of divine light
or enjoying Soma; but even then, hasten to us” (S.11). God may not
be moved by a single prayer and so the aspirant keeps waiting for
Him. He is sure that the Lord will pay heed to his prayers and has
kept his libations ready. “When I had an ardent desire for seeing you,
I said that our own worshippérs must offer this Soma to you. Do
have a regard for my true devotion and come to us to receive our
sacrifice of Soma. Oh Indra and Agni, you may be in your palaces,
in the house of your devotees, or in some Sacrificial hall; leave that
place and come to us to drink our gift of Soma. You may be in the
abodes of Yadu, Turvasa, Druhya, Anu or Piru; come to partake of
our gift of Soma. You may be in heaven, earth, plants or in waters,
come to accept our tribute in the form of Soma. Even though you
may be enjoying oblations in heaven at the time of sun-rise, come
and be pleased with our present of Soma” (S.12). The aspirant is


                                  237
Pathway to God in the Vedas



not tired of praying again and again to God and asking Him to
befriend him; he is also not afraid of pointing out that God should
not stand on formalities and wait for an invitation. After all does He
not appreciate the deep reverence that the devotee is cherishing for
Him? “How is it Oh ASwins, that we are required to extend our
invitation to you repeatedly as to an old man? Be you propitious to
us” (S.13)

     4. A Request for Forgiveness. God is invoked to forgive
man’s foibles and not merely condone them. Sins are confessed
before God so that He should forgive inspite of their magnitude.
Forgiveness should come solely out of His infinite love for His
children. If He would just condone our sins or be indifferent to our
misbehaviour, thinking that, after all, nothing better was expected of
us, it would be under-valuing our worth. It would consign us to the
level of an animal which does not know what is moral and what is
immoral. It would amount to dehumanisation of man. Forgiveness
lies in recognising the enormity of the mistake and still witholding
punishment, sheerly out of love. The aspirants, therefore, fully own
thir guilt and pray for forgiveness. They are sure that guilty though
they are, they will be forgiven by the loving God. Viciousness should
be sought out and its seriousness should be realised but at the same
time it should be graciously pardoned. He who owns that he has not
taken the right path, prays for being led along the proper one. “Oh
Agni, help us cross the dangers. We, your children, offer tributes to
you who are like a father unto us. When would you turn your gracious
eye towards us? You are the controller of the cosmic laws; when
would you take us towards the just avenue?” (S.14). We have another
prayer addressed to Agni to burn all the previous failings. “Oh ever
youthful Agni, we have failed in our devotion but we have committed
this sin out of sheer ignorance. Be compassionate and free us from
the stain through Aditi. Snap all the chains of our pitfalls which are
indeed fetters. Though we might have sinned against men or gods
even to a great extent, we, who are your friends, should never suffer.
Bestow happiness on our progeny” (S.15).

     This    reminds us of the Socratic maxim that virtue is
knowledge. Man commits a wrong because he does not know what

                                 238
                                                The Dark Night of the Soul



is right, says Socrates. That sage of Athens fondly entertained the
idea that intellectual understanding that an act is wrong, prevents a
person from doing it. Alas, it is not always so. Man knows that he
is doing wrong and still persists in repeating it. Aristotle who was
more of a practical constitution expected man not only to know what
is right but also to feel emotional attachment to it. For Aristotle,
moral goodness is the product of two factors, one intellectual and
the other emotional. The two cooperate in producing moral goodness
and each factor influences the development of the other. Intellectual
ability becomes intellectual virtue as a result of development.
Emotional     disposition   (natural   virtue   or   goodness   of   nature)
becomes moral virtue as a result of development. Complete moral
goodness     is the perfect development of an emotional and an
intellectual factor.! We may observe that this analysis of Aristotle
is a very mature understanding of the attitude which is called moral.
The Vedic Rsis are innocent of all such theorising. But they, in their
wisdom, had grasped that both understanding and feeling are
necessary in what is deemed to be morally right. After all, respect
is not just outward show of courtesy, nor is prayer, a word uttered
by mouth. Devotion depends on the quality of heart which is bettered
by intellectual conviction (Niscaya) and emotional reverence
(Adara). This blend of intellect and emotion is moral goodness
according to Aristotle; and it is this that the seers were yearning for.
They were conscious of their sins and knew well that mistakes lay
on the debit side. Thus their attitude is nearer the Aristotelian
concept of moral goodness than the Socratic idea that mere intellect
drives a man to do good. The Rsis speak of having sinned against
gods and men (Devanam uta martyanam), perhaps because they
lacked the respectful feeling towards gods or did not show proper
honour to them by kneeling before them. This drawback they
considered as moral shortcoming. Aristotle would have exactly
treated it as lack of moral goodness.
     5. An Appeal for Removal of Difficulties. However, the Vedic
people no longer seem to believe that a mistake once committed

1   See प.प. Joachim, Aristotle, The Nichomachean Ethics, (Ed) D.A. Rees,
    Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1951, Pp 70 ff


                                   239
Pathway to God in the Vedas



bequeaths a guilty conscience which would be a companion of man
here and even hereafter. They would disagree with A.E. Taylor who
declares: “Nothing is more characteristic of the human sense of guilt
than its indelibility, its power of asserting itself with unabated
poignancy inspite of all lapse of time and all changes in the self,
and its environment”.' On such a view the adage, ‘saints have a past,
as sinners, a future’, would be utterly false. Sinners would be
eternally    damned     souls   and   would      continue   self-condemnation
inspite of a healthy turn to their lives. This is rather a harsh view
and borders on pessimism. But our ancient seers would never
tolerate this idea. The suggestion thrown out by Taylor towards the
end of the discussion would, however attract them. Taylor’s remark
that, “.... the task of complete transmutation cannot be achieved in
our own unaided strength,”* would readily be picked up by the robust
optimists that were the Vedic Rsis. Well be it argued that not by self-
effort alone will the stain be wiped out; yes, but why not by genuine
penitence and God’s grace? Confession of guilt with a heartfelt
 penitence and an urge to begin anew, paves the way and God’s grace
 completely whisks disgrace away. As JhaneSvara says: a dive into
 sacred waters of penitence and intense devotion to God, is all that
 is required to become a saint. In this strain Rsi Kirma Gartsamada
 pleads: “Oh Varuna, set us free from the knots of evil-doing so that
 we may attain to the necessary strength for sticking to the rules and
 regulations. I am weaving a cloth of which meditation constitutes
 the warp and the woof. Let not the threads be cut off in the middle.
 1 am performing a sacrifice. Let it not be obstructed and left
 incomplete”      (S.16).   Perhaps    they     believe   that   meditation   and
 sacrifice can adequately amend the past failings. There are again a
 number of hymns embodying entreaties for removal of obstacles on
 the way. The obliquities that are committed stand between the
devotee and God. They have to be crossed over as a horseman jumps
over the hurdles on the way, or as an oarsman rows the boat through
waters to reach the yonder shore. “Oh respected Adityas, let us go
beyond the illusions or the meshes you have spread on the way to


1   The Faith of a Moralist, Series I, P. 174
2   Ibid P. 182


                                       240
                                                      The Dark Night of the Soul


       inflict punishment on the wicked, as a horseman rides over bumpy
       terrains. Let us march with dauntless tread beyond all obstacles
       uninjured, by means of a chariot and live happily under your watchful
       eye” (S.17). “Oh valiant Indra, pray, enable us to wade through
       lapses as a man wades through water. Let Truth be my guide. Oh
       king, when will you accord your highly coveted prizes to us?” (S.18).
       Tukarama has spoken of moral slips which hide God’s form from
       a devotee’s sight:

       तुका म्हणे सरते नव्हेचि     पायांच।ें
       बळ केलें पापें नव्हेचि    भेटी ॥
       (It seems my misconduct has become dense and clouds my vision.
       I can not see Thy feet). The Vedic prayers were not different. “We
       are calling out for you, Oh brave Indra, as calves for the cows”
       (S.19). Or, “a cow waits anxiously to be milked; in a like fashion,
       come you Oh Lord of the universe, we implore you with our prayers
       and offerings” (S.20). The Vedic seers have said that our offences
       behave like thugs. And hence their request is: “Let the robber on the
       highway be chased and destroyed” (S.21). Distress calls are sent
       out, firstly, for the removal of difficulties and then, for positive
       felicity. “We have been thrown over a barren land. Though the
       territory is vast, it is bereft of all facilities. Enlighten us so that we
       can procure the cows” (S.22). A Rsi humbly requests: “Oh Agni,
       clear the atmosphere polluted with guilt and bring me on the straight
       path. I am a poor little follower of those immortal gods who lead
       a righteous life of noble deeds like sacrifices” (S.23), “Rudradeva,
       you are a resident of Kailasa and look to the welfare of all creatures;
       be gracious and reveal yourself  to me with that form of yours which
       is taintless, reasurring and auspicious. Shower happiness on me”
       (S.24). Perhaps aware of backsliding on the moral way the aspirant
       exclaims, “Oh Agni, bless me once again with that spiritual vision
       granted earlier. Bring with you a life which will not be defiled by
       untimely death. Save us from trespasses which are committed
       unknowingly. Bring us riches that will be sufficient for our people,
       and which will flow towards us like a torrential river. Protect us”
       (S.25). “If you have accepted our friendship, Indra, partake of our
       present of Soma, so that we will conquer the hostile forces. When

                                               241




“Tel
Pathway to God in the Vedas



will he, who sings your praises be supremely happy? When will you
extend to us a grant of cattle and horses?”, asks Narada Kanva
(S.26). “You had accepted Soma at the dwelling of Vivaswan Manu;
you listened to the psalms in the abode of Trita; be now an honoured
guest at my place” (S.27).

     The evidence of such passages corroborates the thesis that for
the Vedic people, sin does not imply permanent condemnation; from
a negative stage they would always try to reach a positive one. There
may be evil; it may appear in countless guises; it may try our
patience; but it is not lasting. There is always a feeling that evil will
be overcome; that good will conquer it. We may observe that here
are hidden the seeds of the four noble truths (Catvari arya satyani)
of Buddhistic philosophy, viz., (1) Life in the world is full of
suffering (Duhkha), (2) there is a cause of this suffering (Duhkha-
samudaya), (3) it is possible to stop suffering (Duhkha-nirodha) and
(4) there is a path which leads to the cessation of suffering (Duhkha-
nirodha-marga). The Vedas recognised the existence of evil; their
naturalistic philosophy found the cause in the anger of the deities;
they believed that evil could be removed; the way is to surrender

to deities and beg for their forgiveness. The celebrated prayer, असतो
मा सद्‌ गमय| तमसो मा ज्योति र्गमय । मृत्योर्मा अमृतं गमय ॥ (Brahadaranyaka
 Upanisad 1. 3.28) (from the unreal, lead me to the real; from
darkness, lead me to light; from death, lead me to immortality) aptly
depicts their whole attitude towards evil and its removal. They have
almost a clairvoyant comprehension of God’s power to bring about
an evil-less state.

      6. Prayer for Light and Protection. Afflicted by ‘an inordi-
nately long spell of darkness in the near polar region of their
habitation, the Vedic people always yearned for light of the sun.
Tormented by ignorance and sin they longed for the vision of the
Sun of Absolute Reality. “Entertaining in my mind a desire for
plenitude, I bow respectfully like Angirasa, to that age-old deity and
make him young (i.e. renew the long-forgotten tradition of his
worship). Oh Indra, destroy those wicked souls that have no element
of devotion in them and make the divine light shine before our eyes”
(5.28). “What is keeping you engaged, Oh deities? Why are you

                                   242
                                              The Dark Night of the Soul



lingering? Leave them aside, who, in their pride, do not offer
anything to God. Lessen the longevity of those irreligious souls.
Make the sun of knowledge rise before those devotees who are
singing the praises of God” (S.29). whoever possesses light, is
approached by the Muni. “God of knowledge, your devotee like the
moon, is welcomed by all. He attains to affluence also in the
heavens.   Oh   Agni,   we,   your devotees,    should   also be   made
participants in the divine bounties” (S.30). “We offer salutations to
the shining divine boar with matted hair and with a form that is
burning with dazzling splendour; we invoke his presence. May he
come with health-giving herbs and shower happiness, fearlessness
and security on us” (S.31). There are other psalms in which there
is an appeal not to hide the shining light from the sight ofthe devotee.
“Oh you all-pervading God (Visnu), you bear the epithet ‘pervaded
by the rays of light’ (Sipivista), then why do you conceal that
grandeur from us? Display that form which you take when engaged
in battles,’ says Maitravaruni Vasistha (S.32). “Only the learned
ones deserve thy wealth. Oh Indra, why not extend thy grace to us
in order to make us share in that plenty?” (S.33). What strikes a
reader immediately, is that a large chunk of prayers contains a
petition for protection’ from enemies. One does not know whether
there were tribal warfares at the time, which made it necessary for
the people to seek outside help. But the peculiarity of all these
prayers, is that an enenty is sought to be destroyed under the plea
that he is irreligious, while entreaties for protection are accompanied
by claims of one’s own devotion. It seems the Vedic people
considered this to be a justification for a demand for help. “Drink
this Soma to your heart’s content, Oh Indra and Visnu. Kindly accept
our presents and sitting by our side listen to our great song of praise”
(S.34). “We are your close friends, Oh Indra, we are tirelessly
admiring you, help us” (5.35). “Take us back to those happy times,
Indra, when we enjoyed a contented life, by shielding ourselves from
the accursed enemies. You who are sinless, make us proof against
sins. You are a possessor of inscrutable power, guard us on both
sides” (S.36). We see clearly that protection is sought both from the
enemies as well as from sins. Though the Vedic seers have never
had a sick-soul, some kind of wrongs that were committed,

                                   243
Pathway to God in the Vedas



continued to corrode their minds and they wanted to be free from
the pricks of conscience. Their minds recoiled from the idea of evil
effects. “Oh Rudra, where is your auspicious hand? Where is the
hand which held the medicines to cure the sick? Vices grafted on
us by some unseen factors, will be scrubbed out only by your hand.
Be merciful to us” (S.37). “I have strained Soma and with that
offering,   I am coming to you with a petition. Let not my words
stimulate any anger in you at the time of sacrifice. I have come to
ask you a favour, but tell me, who will not ask for boons from the
Lord?” (S.38). Sometimes the devotee seems anxious to improve the
quality of his heart so that his call reaches God. Rsi Gotama
Rahtgana says: “Which invocation will appeal to you and please
you? Which song ofhonour arising from the bottom of the heart, will
delight you? Who is he who performed a sacrifice and obtained
intellectual eminence from you? Oh Agni, with what feeling should
I come to you with my libations?” (S.39). Being sure of his devotion
and having mustered up courage, the aspirant exclaims, “those
devotees who have control over their minds, and those born in the
family of Bhrgu, might have sung hoarse your flatteries. But come
to me, Oh illustrious one, in response to my supplication” (S.40).
Again, “we are asking you for our share; your shelter is our greatest
support; and therefore your bliss should also be ours” (S.41). God
 is a rock to which the devotee clings.


      7. An Apparent Rebuke. When a great number of prayers go
fruitless, the most natural turn a man’s mind takes, is to challenge
God or level charges against Him. Friction begins, a spark is kindled,
and there is fire; but as the sage’s disappointment was never deep
the recriminatory words also are not very sharp. They never took
the extreme form of abuse which we find in Tukarama when he
quarrelled with God: माइया लेखीं देव मेला। असो त्याला असेल ॥ गोष्टी न
करी नांव नेघे . (To me, God is dead. Let Him exist for him whosoever
believes in Him. I shall no longer speak about God. I shall not
meditate on His name). In Vedic literature the whole process of
denouncing God for not showering His boons, proceeds on a
somewhat low key. Many verses which seem to embody an
indictment sound like prayers. The piercing quality of a real

                                 244
                                           The Dark Night of the Soul


accusation arising out of intense anguish is not found in the Vedas.
The impeachment is too tame and the language too soft. Here is an
example from    sage   Sobhari   Kanva: “We are saluting you and
praying to you. Why should you not make up your mind? Oh Indra
of swift horses, you know full well our desires. You are generous
and we who belong to you have our minds fixed on you” (S.42).
“How many times should I repeat my prayers? You destroy the
sinners; which sacrifice will please you?” (S.43). The deities are
made aware of having accepted the friendship of the devotee and
then having forsaken them. The Rsi who is eager to see Varuna,
thinks aloud, “should I, on my own, approach Him? When can I
discern his inner thoughts? Will he accept my offering without a
trace of anger? When shall I, with my mind composed see Varuna’...
What wrong have I committed, Oh Varuna, so that you should
destroy your friend, the singer? Oh Varuna of burning brightness,
tell me my sin so that I shall be free from it by your worship and
be absorbed in your prayers” (S.44). “You are not deaf; on the
contrary, you are kind and have sharp ears; that is why we call on
you even from a distance. We wish that you listen to our appeals;
give us strength and be our dear and near friend. Whenever we are
exasperated, we meditate on you. Should you not understand this
deplorable state of ours? As old men lean on their staff, we are
leaning on you who are very strong. We ardently desire that you
should be present at our sacrifice” (S.45). “We always crave your
indulgence Oh Agni, who are a messenger of the immortal and
powerful gods and who are praise-worthy. If we really cherish
mutual love, your benediction will come true” (S.46).
     There are also appeals not to cut off the time-honoured bond
of love. “Oh Agni, you are a gifted and illumined soul. Do not cut
asunder the bond of mutual attachment that has been existing
between us since the time of our fore-fathers. Old age likea cloud
causes beauty to fade; therefore send your benedictions before old
age sets in” pleads Rsi 24155218 Saktya (S.47). “Where are you?
Have you forgotten our kinship, Varuna? We long for it. You are
wealthy and we shall enter your abode which has thousand doors
and which offers shelter to all” (S.48). A devotee requests that he
may not be compelled to return to the earthly vesture any more

                                 245
Pathway to God in the Vedas



(S.49).   Similarly,    “with   great    ado,   you   have   shouldered    our
responsibility. Now do not abandon us in a manner in which a man
abandons the weight under which he is reeling. Bring success and
affluence to us” (S.50). Also present are lyrics which contain some
semblance of a dispute: “Are you not named as one who heeds a
devotee’s prayers? Are you not he who protects us from the wicked?
How can you keep quiet when we are subjected to humiliation?
Throw your weapon, Oh Soma, on these haters of Brahman” (5.51).
“It is futile to call you generous. Be true to thy name by bestowing
treasures on me. We learn that you are a dispenser of power. Give
a turn to my intellect so that it will be engaged in good work. Adorn
me with abundance” (S.52). Namadeva had quarrelled with God and
lay charges at His door. He declared: Though God was known to
sanctify even a sinner, Namadev had no such experience with Him;
and having realised that God was no benefactor at all, he had decided
 to go back. The same is the purport of a hymn which says: “I had
 heard Oh    Indra, that you the prime          mover, bestow     riches on
 devotees. But ultimately, what is it that the friends who pinned their
 hopes on you, gained?” (S.53).

      8. Solicitation for Mercy. In the end it is not expostulation but
 ardent appeal that melts God’s heart. So the Vedic Rsis who have
 mastered the technique of prayers and who have many a thing to pray
 for, take resort to that way. Thus we have many a marvellous poem
 in the Vedas which seek God’s grace for spiritual experience.
 However, a considerable number of prayers seem to be concerned
 with requests for food, health, wealth, cattle, progeny, happiness and
 victory over enemy. One may perhaps wonder whether the prayers
 take things that are relatively and temporarily good to be absolutely
 good.    Such   an    understanding     sometimes     may    cause a     little
 discomfort to a religious man. The things asked for prima facie,
 appear to be trifling. However, these are only symbols of higher and
 spiritually significant things. Nirukta gives various meanings to
these words. Many songs invoke the presence of God near the
devotee. “Men perform Soma sacrifice in your honour, the talented
ones offer you manifold invocations. Oh Indra, stay not away from
us any longer” (S.54). “Hasten to us, you A§wins, both of whom are

                                        246
                                             The Dark Night of the Soul



brave and handsome. We are praying to you for prowess; listen to
those chants and be by our side” (S.55). In a Mantra by Avasyu
Atreya, Aswins, the knowers of Madhuvidya, are called upon to
leave their secret abode in order to reach the devotee’s house (S.56).
“Which actions of ours will really please you, Oh Indra, so that you
will make us valiant like yourself? Guileless friend, when will you
graciously come to us? When we desire food, you yourself should
feed us” (S.57). Perhaps the difficulties the Vedic people talk about
are those on the path of spiritual realisation while the wealth
symbolises spiritual bliss. The sage Sukaksa Angirasa hums a
music-laden prayer: “Oh wealthy and laudable Indra, be determined
to guide us along the treacherous path. No one transgresses thy wish,
not even the gods. How can a poor creature like man dare to do so?
I am preparing Soma while applauding you. Should you not reward
the singers? If you wish to make us happy, give us food and wealth
and other means of comfort. Being convinced that you would gladden
us, we have kept Soma ready and are calling you” (S.58). “Let us
be recipients of strength, Oh Indra, lord of riches; listen to our
entreaties for we are your friends. You are a warrior; truth is your
weapon. Fight on and make us participants in spiritual wealth which
was hitherto inaccessible to us” (S.59). The reference to Indra as
one whose strength lies in truth (SatyaSusmat) does allow a spiritual
interpretation of this hymn. “Oh singer, address your songs in the
direction where Indra stays. Let the generous and powerful Indra
bestow on us fearlessness, so that we attain to glory. Indra whose
arms are as strong as Vajra itself, hastens to his devotees in a chariot
with hundreds and thousands of horses. On Lord of plenitude, when
you are on our side, we will enjoy the lustrous, praise-worthy and
rich bounty (i.e., we will be able to have the vision of the shining
form of God)” (S.60). Steep is the path to God-head and full of
obstacles. So, an alliance with a powerful friend, is always an asset.
“Oh Indra, protect us, protect us today, tomorrow and ever
afterwards. Protect us day and night” (S.61). The sages never raised
insurmountable barriers between men and gods. With open minds
they ask for the cooling mercy of the deities. “Let not my devotion
to Indra suffer an erosion. His kindness itselfis the cow which fulfils
my desires. His arm is my armour. With his help, may I be


                                  247
Pathway to God in the Vedas



successful in every battle” (5.62). “With the strength of your chaste
mind, you are able to checkmate your enemies in battles. Pray, make
our minds also purified and pious so that we may also gain victory
in battles against the anarchists. Oh Agni, strong though the traitors’
forces are, make short work of them. With your help we shall attain
to our goal” (S.63). God remains behind closed doors and the
devotee   ardently prays   that God should     reveal   Himself.   Sage
Brhaduktha Vamadevya is waiting for undisclosed spiritual trea-
sures. “Thou holdest in thy sole possession all the valuable things,
whether manifest, or hidden from the ken of an ordinary person. Oh
Maghavyan, pray, do not keep me away from the fulfilment of my
longing. You are both the director and donor’ (5.64). And again,
“mysterious are the ways of Agni, as difficult to fathom as are the
waves in a water-fall. That omnipotent and adorable Agni brings to
the devotees rich gifts whether manifest or concealed” (S.65). In
another place, the function of displaying treasures is attributed to the
Maruts (S.66). “I offer my humble homage to the most venerable
God Agni, who never fails to fulfil the desires of his devotees. Pray,
Oh merciful God, grant us imperishable and inexhaustible fortune”
 (S.67). “I am trying to help the mind to attain concentration. Varuna,
 I make my intellect adept and illumine my deeds, so that I shall
overcome all obstructions on the way and climb on a boat which will
Carry us across to the other shore” (5.68). Or, 100 both the sacrificer
and myself be crowned with success; let us be victorious; let us have
 right intellect and unspoilt fame” (S.69). There are requests to God
to show Himself. The sage sings his way through the dark night to
the golden morning. “Salutations to thee Oh God, let the light of your
eyes fall on me” (5.70). The vision of God is possible only through
His grace. He alone holds the master key to the treasure-house. “It
is only when you intend to shower your blessings, Oh Indra, that we
are able to experience the vision of the spiritual sun, along with the
flood of his radiant rays” (S.71). A prayer to Indra, whom no one
equals, runs: “When will you extend to us riches that grow in
thousands of ways? Let our abilities be touched with spiritual
powers” (S.72). Once the aspirant becomes the recipient of God’s
gifts and is out of the woods, his disappointment vanishes and he
basks in the sun of ecstatic joy. The devotee rests assured that when

                                  248
                                                  The Dark Night of the Soul



he goes in submission to God, He stands by his side as a guardian.
“Direct the course of your thoughts to God, just as things are
transported across a river by means of a ship. God is omnipresent
and is moved by ardent prayers of his devotees. Touch and move
that most renowned and generous God; He will certainly respond to
your call. Know fully what Indra cherishes. Sing His praises and
surrender to Him. Be in His vicinity and do not grieve. Once He
hears your heartfelt appeals, He is sure to reward you” (S.73). “Oh
Varuna and Mitra, you are a mighty support. He whom you shelter
never suffers a defeat. Your love is disinterested, Oh Adityas of
wide-reaching intellect; he, whom you choose, encounters no danger
and suffers no great pain” (S.74). We are again told that God never
keeps His devotees frustrated: “Oh Indra, he who loves you, gets
instantly whatever he asks for. Whatever the Almighty wishes to
make     strong   becomes     strong”   (S.75).   Rsi   Avatsara   Kasyapa
expresses his confidence: “You exterminate the opponents to the
very roots. That is why in the midst of a fierce battle, I do not budge
from exalting you” (S.76).

      The dark night of the soul is characterised by some of the
disappointments but many hopes gather in the mind of the aspirant.
It is not the long night of the glacial period but the one that comes
and goes. Their hopes do not reach the nadir of dejection for they
always look up to the soothing compassion of the divine benefactor.
When God has taken it on Himself to watch over his devotee, the
devotee’s misfortune is at an end; the dark night of the soul is past;
the sun of His boons begins to shine and the only vocation for the
devotee is to sing the glories of the Almighty.
