                                              255
                                CHAPTER IX




        Varieties of Spiritual Experience

        1. The Faculty of God-realisation. A very important topic in
the Philosophy of Religion which is pursued with sustained interest
by philosophers is that of the instrument for attaining to God-
experience. That intellect alone is helpless in this matter, is accepted
on all hands. ‘Immediatism’ generally gets an upper hand over
‘Intellectualism’. The very word immediatism stands for the direct
experience of God without mediation of any other faculty
                                                     — the
senses or the intellect. William James had said that, “intellect and
senses both swoon away in this highest state of ecstasy.”! The
Bhagavata, however, does not allow the intellect to swoon away; it
assigns a positive function to it. It wants the intellect to adopt a
critical   attitude.   It   should   make   itself   superfine    by   constant
meditation on God. “You should, O Lord of men, reflect in this
manner on the soul in the body, using your intelligenae, aided by
logic    and   strengthened     by   constant   meditation   on    Vasudeva”
(211.5.9).?

        In the sequel we shall note that in the matter of God-realisation,
senses are ordered to be out; reason does not hold much credit; we
have to go beyond both and take resort to intuition. The Rsis were
well aware of this and have prayed for ‘divine vision’. For intuition
they use significant words like अण्वी धी (subtle intellect) or दैवी द्वार
(celestial door) to suggest the super-intellectual faculty. They have
also stressed that for realisation we should stop nourishing the ego
with its well-defined contours and expand the self in order to
obliterate the limits of selfishness. Turning to the marks of mystical
experience we find that it is direct vision of Reality and is beyond



1   The Varieties of Religious Experience, P. 316
2   Srimad Bhagavatam, English Translation by N. Raghunathan, Vighneshwar
    Publishing House, 283, Linghi Chetty Street, Madras-1, 1976, Vol II,
    Pp. 664-665
                                             Varieties of Spiritual Experience


space and time. Here one sense-organ can take on the functions of
others. Opposites have no place in this field. These experiences
verily constitute wealth. The sages enjoyed God as light, as colour,
as sound or as nectar. They thrived on these divine gifts.
      Reasoned knowledge which is gained through syllogism and
which is palpably mediate, can not proceed a step without a flash
of intuition. “Deduction is but a series of intuitions whereby terms
not directly related are discovered to be related through their
relations to intermediaries. Thus by a simple intuition the mind may
intuit that A = B and by another intuition that B = C, and by a third
that C = D, but in order to see for itself that A = D, that mind has
to turn back and forward quickly along the whole series, and by thus
gathering them together as the content of a single more complex
intuition render the relation of A to D no less immediately evident.
The detection of this relation involves a positive increase in our
knowledge, and therefore involves that intuitive process wherein
alone knowledge can develop.”! “Intuition”, says Descartes, “is that
non-dubious apprehension of a pure and attentive mind which is born
in the sole light of reason... such intuitions are more numerous than
most people are prepared to recognise, disdaining as they do, to
occupy their minds with things so simple.”? Whatever    the knowledge
process, the step of intuitive understanding can not be dispensed
with. If a kind of intuitive insight is necessary even in ordinary
perception of objects, how much more would it be necessary in
apprehending Reality? Descartes calls this faculty /umen naturale or
natural light of reason. Spinoza follows suit. Real knowledge is
intuitive knowledge; he calls it the third kind of knowledge. This
scientia intuitiva is said ‘not to consist in being convinced by reasons
but in an immediate union with the thing itself.’ And ‘it does not
result from something else but from a direct revelation of the object
itselfto the understanding.’ “Thus Spinoza with Plato before him and
many others since, held that above and beyond the ratiocination and
general reasoning which constitutes the way of scientific thought,

1   Norman Kemp Smith, New Studies in the Philosophy of Descartes, Macmillan
    and Co. Ltd, London, 1952, P. 70
2 Rules for the Direction of the Mind, Rule III

                                       257
Pathway to God in the Vedas



there is an intuition into individual essences. He held that this kind
of knowledge made the possessor one with the object.”!

        Why this distrust of reason? Why is mediate knowledge always
looked down upon when it is the question of grasping the ultimate?
Because, argues Bergson, as intellect is a special form of life, it can
not know the whole of life. A part can not know the whole. And hence
the “insistence on a non-intellectual variety of knowledge, which is
more fundamental and more comprehensive than intellection; which
affords, as James expresses it, real “insight” as distinguished from
the superficiality and abstraction of intellection.”? This bifurcation
of the powers of the mind and attribution of specific functions to
them is by no means a modern concept. The Vedic philosophers
were aware of the fact.

         2. Necessity of Intuition. A clear-cut distinction is made
between seeing with the physical eyes and seeing with the mind’s
eyes. The Rsis are emphatic that these two visions are categorically
different from each other and that one stands head above shoulders
of the other in apprehending reality. Hence they pray for the divine
    vision. Rsi Caksus Saurya sings, “pray, grant divine vision unto our
eyes 50 that we may have clear perception. May we be able to behold
this world all through and discuss everything clearly. Oh Strya, may
we be able to behold thy beautiful and perfect form again and again,
and behold it quite clearly” (S.1). Indra who is fond of sacrifices
and who is able to have supersensuous perception has been a
recipient of the sun’s rays. The wise sages, like fair-winged birds,
approached Indra and prayed: “Oh Indra, drive away the dark
ignorance from our eyes and fill our vision fully with heavenly light.
Free us who are bound with ropes” (5.2). Sayana interprets the word
Caksu in this Mantra as Jnanalaksana drsti, i.e. illumination. It is
not ordinary perception.

         The bodily eye has its limitations and many Vedic Mantras
express a distrust of it. Brhaspati Angirasa says: “One can verily


1     Leon Roth, Spinoza, George Allen and Unwin Ltd. London, 1929, ए. 147
2     R. B. Perry, Present Philosophical Tendencies, Longmans, Green and Co.
      London, 1929, P. 224


                                     258
                                          Varieties of Spiritual Experience



see objects in the environment by means of one’s natural eyes; yet
one is not able to perceive the divine words. One can also hear the
words expressed through physical bodies yet one is not capable of
hearing the divine words. However, they are automatically revealed
to one who is attuned to supersensuous audition” (S.3). Plato was
well aware of the unstruck music which a mystic hears in ecstasy.
Towards the end of his dialogue Crito, Socrates says: “This, dear
Crito, is the voice which I seem to hear murmuring in my ears, like
the sound of the flute in the ears of the mystic...”

     Seeing without the physiological device is always extolled in
the Vedas. “Mortals see only the tangible body; but Munis possess
the intuitive power of visualising the spiritual form within that body”
(S.4). With the same supernatural eye Indra had the vision of Soma
poured in the innermost recesses of the heart (S.5). But alas ! “Every
one is not able to grasp things with the inner eye and thus one
remains merely on the level of the outer apparatus” (S.6). The
mind’s eye functions even in the absence of the visual organ. “Being
gifted with the vision of the secret celestial eye, they (Mitra and
Varuna) are able to see better and in a more perfect manner than
those who can see only with their empirical eye. They can behold
quite clearly even when their physical eyes are closed” (S.7). In fact,
the correspondence between the mystic’s vision with his eyes open,
and the vision with his eyes closed, constitutes one of the criteria
of the validity of mystical experience according to Professor R. D.
Ranade. The inner conviction of those who have had the direct vision
of God, is not to be gainsaid. We may refer the reader to the utterance
of Yajiia Prajapatya: “I verily believe, I am actually beholding by
means of my intuitive vision the very gods who in ancient times
performed the sacrifice and created the seers (Rsis), men and our
progenitors” (S.8). There is a Mantra worthy of notice which refers
to a similar instance. At the time of a sacrifice, Vasistha offered an
oblation to Indra. The latter accepted it. Vasistha had the vision of
Indra receiving the gift. But-all the while Indra remained invisible
to others (S.9).                4

     3. Different Nomenclatures. To the non-rational faculty of
God-realisation various names“like Dhi, Medha, Manisa, Ketu or

                                    259
Pathway to God in the Vedas



Daivi-dwara have been given. That the non-reflective variety of
knowledge grasps more than the logical type, has always been the
cry of anti-intellectualists. They may not agree regarding the exact
nature of the non-rational way, but they are unanimous in pointing
out that intuition is superior to the ‘A is B’ type of statements tailored
by the intellect. In ‘A Pluralistic Universe’, William James holds a
brief for this non-intellectual supplementary though at times it may
appear to be forming just an outline round the intellectual nucleus.
Indeed, James points out, if the fringe exists, however delicate and
indistinct it may be, it should have more importance for philosophy
than the bright nucleus it surrounds. For, it is the presence of the
fringe that enables us to affirm that the nucleus is a nucleus, or that
pure intellect is a contraction, a condensation, of a more extensive
power. This kind of intellect supplemented by a superior power 15
referred to in the Vedas as Anvi dhi i.e., the reason which is capable
of very minute observation and which is sought after as a prized
possession. “Those who were praising the Soma, made him sit on
the lap of Aditi and cleaned him with their discerning intellects
(Anvya dhiya)” (5.10). “We, whose intellects are capable of deep
concentration and are attuned to gods, submit to them for protection,
implore their mercy for welfare and long for the attainment of food”
(S.11). The expression Devya-dhiya in Aitareya Brahmana V.2.1 is
interpreted by Sayana as the intellect which kindles up and illumines
the form of God and fills the heart with deep devotion to Him (तद्रूप
प्रकाशि कया तद्भावनायुक्तया). We may refer to the different qualities of
Dhi that are mentioned:

सुश्रूषा श्रवणं चैव ग्रहणं धारणं तथा।
ऊहापोहोऽर्थवि ज्ञानं तत्त्वज्ञानं च धीर्गुणाः ।।
(A desire to hear, study, understand, retain in memory, consider the
pros and cons, comprehend the sense and to visualise the truth). It
is such a kind of multifaceted ability which deserves the-
name, celestial intellect. Its greatness is sung endlessly. “Kindle up
the divine understanding within and direct your words towards the
divine” (S.12). This faculty is allotted the function of bringing
forward Agni so that he may accept the offerings (S.13). Parucchepa
Daivodasi exclaims: “Oh Mitra and Varuna, when by your inherent

                                           260
                                           Varieties of Spiritual Experience



supreme power, you dissociated untruth from the Truth, we had the
vision of your form in golden light. It was possible not through the
endeavour of our imagination, mind, or physical eyes, but through
the eyes of intuition fostered by devotion to Soma” (5.14).

     Buddhi or intellect can be said to be of two types —Sthila and
Suksma. The Sthula Buddhi is the logical intellect whereas Siksma
Buddhi     or    Agra   Buddhi   denotes   intuition.   Self-realisation   is
achieved    by    one-pointed
                           and piercing faculty of intuition. Rsi
Patanga Prajapatya says: “The wise men endowed with one-pointed
devotion and intuitive mind, perceive the spiritual sun even though
veiled by the mysterious power (Maya) of the Almighty. Piercing
through the veil of his rays, they see and get at their source, namely
the sun or the Aditya Purusa” (S.15). The craving for the vision of
God is very intense and if we look to the prayers we find that all
the time it 15 Oliver Twist asking for more. The Rsis ask for more
and still more. If they strive for achieving the wonderful power, i.e.
Dhi, they also want Medha, the retentive faculty. Here is an eloquent
petition. “Oh God Agni, enrich my powers by that apprehension
which is worshipped by groups of gods as well as by fathers” (S.16).
“Oh Agni, I pray to you for that talent which is sanctified by
Brahman, which is enjoyed by the God-realisers, which is praised
by the Rsis and which is abundantly acquired and absorbed by the
Brahmacarins. With open arms I welcome that competence which
is possessed by the artists, which is comprehended by the deities
(especially Varuna) and which is held by the Rsis. Make me a master
of that authority which is wielded by the Rsis, the creators of
objects” (S.17).
     In the appendix to the tenth chapter of Taittiriya Aranyaka, a
distinction seems to have been made between human intellect
(Manusi Medha or वाप]व Medha) and divine intellect (Daivi
Medha). This chapter was later incorporated into the compilation of
one hundred and eight Upanisads published by Nirnaya Sagar Press,
Bombay, as Narayanopanisad. The expression त्वया जुष्ट क्रषि ः भवति
देवि (by virtue of your grace, Oh divine intellect, a person can become
a Rsi) clearly indicates the power of दैवी मेधा. A commentator whose
name is not mentioned anywhere in the commentary explains the

                                    261
Pathway to God in the Vedas



term Rsi as अतींद्रि य ` द्रष्टा, a person endowed with supersensuous
perception. He has got real Jnana. Incidentally, the Mantra is found
also in Taittriya Aranyaka (S.18).


     We also come across a verse on Manisa as a deity. “Pray, may
the luminous divine energy hasten to me like a swift steed of
                                                            a well-
equipped chariot” (5.19). For-the meaning of Medha we may refer

to Amara KoSa which defines Medha as धीः धारणावती मेधा (intellect
that preserves whatever is learnt). Sayana in his commentary on
Taittiriya Aranyaka IV.42.13 refers to it 85 ग्रंथ तदर्थ धारणशक्ति    ः मेधा
(Medha is the power which enables a person to retain the contents
of a treatise as well as their meaning). In his commentary on वयः

सुपर्णा उपसेदुः इंद्रम्‌ (Krsna Yajurveda Taittiriya Aranyaka IV.42.18)
Sayana understands Priyamedhas as अतीतस्य श्रुतस्य धारणशक्ति          ः मेधा
तस्यां प्रीति युक्ताः (Medhi is the faculty which enables a person to retain
all that has been heard, and Priyamedhas are those who cherish
attachment to that power). A subtle distinction between Medha and
Manisa is seen in Sayana, where Medha is said to be Dharana-Sakti
(retentive power), while Manisa is described as Grahana-Sakti
(power of apprehension).       His    Bhasya   on   a   Mantra   (Taittiriya
Aranyaka IV.42.35) runs: मेधा मनीषे माऊ वि शतां समीची भूतस्य भव्यस्य
aS: सर्वमायुरयाणि (May the favourably inclined ability of retention
(Medha) as also the power of apprehension (Manisa) abide in me
firmly so that I may grasp and retain all that has been learnt in the
past and which is being learnt now). Sometimes Agni himself is said
to be the penetrating intuition or ray oflight, Ketu, obviously because
he is full of light of knowledge. Here is a prayer to him: “You, oh
Agni, are the ray of intuition in human beings, most coveted and most
glorious and established in the centre. Awaken us for prayers and
provide spiritual food too” (S.20). “Your devotee, the possessor of
intuition, Oh Agni, is absorbed in the singing of Sama. Bring to us
speedily that skill which manifests itself diversely and which is
always a valuable asset” (S.21). We may refer to one more passage.
“Oh Agni, you stimulate life where there was none, and confer form
on the formless. Please reveal yourself with the dawn” (S.22).

                                     262
                                          Varieties of Spiritual Experience



     The idea that knowledge is kept secret in an enclosed space
can be traced back to the Mantras in the Vedas which contain prayers
for the opening of divine doors, Daivi-dvara, leading to happiness
or success or other treasures as well as to spiritual enlightenment.
The desire for illumination is the first rung of the ladder on the way
up. Indra is described as a Mumuksu, who wants to find the treasures
of knowledge. “Indra discovered the secret riches concealed in
heaven like the embryo of a bird hidden in the interior ofa boundless
rocky   enclosure.   The   thunderer, Indra,   the   most   distinguished
amongst    the   Angirasas, swung open, with a desire to obtain
illumination, the doors by his thunderbolt and unveiled the hidden
nourishing light” (S.23). A very important pre-requisite of self-
realisation is self-effort. Indra is described as Vajrin, a wielder of
thunderbolt and it is with this weapon that he flings open the doors.
There are implorations addressed to the doors themselves to open.
“May the divine doors, in response to our humble prayers, swing
open, making easy our entry inside. May these imperishable divine
doors stretch out into the vast, spreading purity, glory and power
everywhere” (5.24). And Sumitra VadhryaSva says: “Touch the
highest peak of heaven or swing wide apart to suit the wide extent
of the earth, Ye resplendent doors. Desirous of the chariot of God,
please bear in your greatness the divine car’ (S.25). The doors
themselves are assigned the duty of bringing the divine bounties
before the devotee! The doors guarding the secrets of illumination
are many times regarded as deities (Dyara devatas) and their help
is sought in all men’s undertakings. “Kindly open yourselves, you
divine doors, and give easy entrance so as to make our sacrifice
fruitful in every way” (S.26). In one hymn we have a wonderful
account of how the doors open up along with the frames and reveal
unto the vision of the devotee an endless vista of the divine region.
The doors are said to be golden, Hiranyaya. “The resplendent golden
divine doors when adored with the Pavamana Soma are lifted high
with the frames into the vast” (5.27). It is clinging to our self-centred
‘ideas that keeps us away from God and keeps us outside the locked
doors; and hence the prayers of Vedic Rsis for the infinite expansion
of self-hood which is symbolised by opening the doors. Thus
exclaims Jamadagni Bhargava: “Pray, open yourself, Oh widely


                                   263
Pathway to God in the Vedas



expanding divine doors, with all your grace; Oh divine doors, vast
and all-pervading, be easily accessible for the entrance of gods”
(S.28).
        4.   Characteristics    of    Spiritual   Experience.      Saints        are
empiricists par excellence. They believe in only what they experi-
ence. Aided by the antenna of intuition which catches divine waves,
the Rsis gather a plethora of direct experiences. A seer belonging
to the remote past vouchsafes that what he describes is actual and
not imaginary. “We portray the Paramatman as we verily see Him”
(S.29). Psychologists have characterised that type of imagery as
eidetic which stands out as most vivid. For example, a child looks
at a picture for a few seconds; it is then asked to look ata grey screen
and report what he sees there. An eidetic child replies that he sees
the picture on the screen. “He behaves as if it were there. His eyes
move from one part to the other, he leans forward and 10075 hard
in attempting to discover a detail, his face may express emotions
appropriate to the picture...        A good eidetic subject, when asked to
describe the picture as he sees it on the screen, will often give an
astonishing amount of detail.”’ Mystical experiences can be said to
be ejdetic i.e. vivid and graphic. However, before employing the term
eidetic to characterise mystical experience, we have to take care that
the word eidetic is de-psychologised by severing all its associations
 with imagery. Thus, if in psychology we have an eidetic image, in
 mystical     experience   we   have     an   eidetic   vision.   Again,    if    in
psychology we speak of perception, in mysticism, we speak of
super-sensuous experience. There is seeing without eyes, hearing
without ears, smelling, tasting and touching without nose, tongue and
skin.

        (a) Directness of Vision. A graphic picture of visualising the
Almighty as a stark reality is found in the utterance of the sage
Vairupa Sadhri. “One bird entered into the vast ocean-like mid-
region. He then had a look at the whole universe around him. I with
my mature mind beheld Him within myself (at close quarters). The
mother caresses Him and He in turn clings to her’ (5.30). Sayana
in his commentary on this Mantra (also occurring in Aitareya

1   R.S. Woodworth, Experimental Psychology, P.46

                                        264
                                            Varieties of Spiritual Experience


Aranyaka, III.6) identifies the bird with the vital breath and the sea
with Antariksa. God can be realised in one’s own heart after one
attains to the highest knowledge and cultivates mental and moral
excellence. Great is their alterness and boundless their devotion.
What is supremely important is spiritual wakefulness.

      The directness of mystical experience is brought into bold
relief by using the word Pratyan, which literally means ‘in front of’.
But it implies that God’s vision is present on all sides simulta-
neously. In fact, we cannot go away from that vision. “As we turn
ourselves away from it, says Ramadasa, it presents itself before us.
By no means whatsoever could we turn our back on 17 (Dasabodha
XX.10.2). God stands facing all directions. He permeates the past
and the present as also the future. He instals himself right in front
of people and stands face-to-face before them. Here is a record of
a direct vision of God. “Born along with the holy law, you are Oh
Agni, the very source of all beauty, light and strength. You happen
to be ever in the fore-front of a devout seer to whichever side he
may turn his face” (S.31). The. idea is reiterated in Rgveda as well
as in Atharvaveda. “You reveal yourself in front of the illumined
sages and seekers, so that they may revel in your blissful nearness”
(S.32). A single syllable is changed in the Atharvaveda verse. It
reads मानुषीः instead ०४ मानुषान्‌.
     (b) Beyond Space and Time. The spiritual object displays one
more mark. It defies all ordinary rules regarding space and time. The
distinction between today and tomorrow, far and near is foreign to
the experience. Time and space, which form the ground of such
qualities is transcended in mystical life. “Here is where / am, now
is this felt present. And similarly, every other part of the actually
presented space and time gets a unique qualitative character from
its special relation to this here and now.”! Not so in super-sensuous
experience. The sensuous world is governed by a set of rules which
do not apply to the super-sensuous region. Then why describe the
spiritual experience in spatial and temporal terms at all? It is because
they are indissolubly connected with the finite level to which a


1   A.E. Taylor, Elements of Metaphysics, P. 251

                                      265
Pathway to God in the Vedas



mystic has inevitably to descend when giving expression to his
ecstatic experience. Be it so. “The God who nourishes is present
every where, in the heavens, in the mid-region as also on the earth.
He moves in both the places, near at hand and far away; for both
places are dear to him”, says Rsi Uparibabhrava (S.33). Or again,
“Oh you Agni, you are lovely of form, alike on all sides. Though
at a distance, you shine brightly as if close at hand. Things remain
visible to you even in darkness. Please protect us who are your
friends” (S.34). In a Rk which occurs again and again in the Vedas,
we are told by a Rsi that “Sages behold the supreme spiritual form
of the omnipresent God (Visnu) always, at all times, as if an eye
is spread out in the heaven above” (S.35).
     (c) Absence of Contradictions. The Vedas give the earliest
account of reconciliation of opposites. The super-sensuous percep-
tion may sometimes appear funny. In sense-perception, there is
division of labour as well as specialisation. No sense-organ would
undertake to do the work of another sense-organ. The eye can only
see; but it can not hear. And even if the atmosphere is full of
reverberating sounds, the eye is not affected by them. Not so in
super-sensuous field. No sense-organ is involved and so the question
of the pre-adjustment of their functions to outside objects does not
crop up. Rsi Krtnu Bhargava gives expression to such an experience.
“He covers up the naked and heals all the sick. By his magnanimity,
the blind man sees and the cripple walks” (S.36). Indra accomplishes
this task in the case of forlorn persons. “You enabled the blind one
to see and the lame one to walk. Oh slayer of Vrtra, no one else can
extend such a boon” (S.37). The qualities which appear to be
opposite in nature come together in mystical area. Distinction itself
15 not operative in the arena of one homogeneous Reality. How can
there be ‘opposites’ then? “It is no wonder that the dawn though
feetless, has preceded every one having feet” (S.38). The form of
God which stands before the vision of the aspirant has no definable
size too. It is subtler than a hair’s breadth or is not visible at all
(S.39). further, no gender is applicable to it. “Thou art male and thou
art female. When in old age thou movest with support of a stick
and when thou revealest thyself, thy face becomes visible on
all sides” (S.40). Such visions constitute imperishable wealth.

                                 266
                                         Varieties of Spiritual Experience



Mammon, the goddess of wealth is proverbially fickle and deserts a
person any moment. Not so with spiritual treasures which only grow

and never diminish. “These riches are not destructible, nor are they
liable to be snatched away by thieves. No enemy can ever injure
them” (S.41). Gifts that are subject to destruction are as good as not
given; and no worldly present qualifies to be called a gift. “Nobody
can diminish the glory and loving nature of Savitr” (S.42).

      5.   Varieties   of Mystical Experience. God may manifest
himself in infinite ways. Let us see some of them as recorded by
seers of very very ancient times.

      (a) Visual. (i) Light. Light seems to be one of the important
types of mystical experience. The brilliance of this light is beyond
all measure and therefore it cannot be compared with any earthly
light. “The wise men verily behold the shimmering show of the
primeval seed of the universe enkindled beyond heaven” (S.43).
Where is the place where such light has not entered or where its rule
is not observed? “Oh universal God, Fire ! (VaiSvanara Agni), earth,
firmament and heaven together submit to your rule. Refulgent with
your perpetual light, you have over-spread heaven and earth” (5.44).
‘Lead kindly light’, is the perorative refrain of many a Vedic verse.
“The great flashy phenomenon has become manifest for the sake of
these worshippers. All life has been liberated from the influence of
darkness. The ‘great light’ handed over to us by our fore-fathers has
made its appearance, displaying the spacious path of Daksina”
(S.45). Light has the potentiality to convert an ordinary human being
into a holy man. “Oh A§gwins”, says Rsi Gotama Rahtgana, “you
brought down from heaven that fluorescence to divinise the life of
man; pray, favour us by granting that blissful energy” (S.46). The
expressions used in the Mantra are Sloka and Jyoti. While Griffith
translates the word Sloka as hymn, his revered Master, H.H. Wilson,
takes the expressions Sloka and Jyoti to mean adorable light. The
word Sloka means an object of praise, and this Jyoti or light is
praise-worthy because it has come to sanctify man. We may not
bother whether Sloka and Jyoti indicate sound and light or luminous
sound. Suffice it to say, that it is a divine seed which fructifies and
sanctifies man. It is a heavenly gift which comes down to uplift the

                                  267
Pathway to God in the Vedas



creatures below. Elsewhere it is the Pavamana Soma or Savitr who
is credited with having generated the light. “The purifying Soma
generated the brilliant flash of light of the Truth and thus destroyed
the darkness of evil and ignorance” (S.47). “That immortal light
(Jyotiramrtam) is generated by the creator Savitr, for the benefit of
mankind. Through God’s gracious rule, that Eye has presented itself
before our vision” (S.48). (ii) Colour. The light seen at the time of
deep concentration on the name of God, is made more charming by
the display of colours and the devotee remains glued to it. “Here
appears the beautiful light (Vena) draped with variegated colours,
with refulgent halo all around, situated in a lustrous chariot. The wise
sages caress Him like a child with their hymns at a conclave of the
sun and the waters” (S.49). Or, “his chariot displays black, white
and crimson colours. It is large, straight and glorious; at times it
appears in dark red colour. Prajapati, the original creator created this
form of gold” (S.50). The desire of an aspirant to have a           rich
experience, is expressed as: “Oh Maruts, be you the honoured guests
at my sacrifices; you are known by a multiplicity of names like
Sukrajyoti (dazzling like the morning star), Citrajyoti (adored by the
rainbow colours), Satyajyoti (who reflects the essence of truth),
Jyotisman (radiant), Sukra (white), Rtapa (who protects a sacrifice)
or Atyamha (emancipated from sin or evil)” (S.51). (iii) Form. As
with light, so with forms. There is a bewildering variety of forms
that are visualised by mystics all over the world. Vision of that
sovereign of the sky, the sun, deserves to be taken first. Plato gives
an inimitable account of the vision of the Sun who occupies the
highest point in the scale of knowledge and who, as identified with
the Idea of the Good, also occupies the highest point in the scale
of reality. Thus a Mantra which is found in all the four Vedas as
also   in the Upanisads, says:     “Looking   at the   ever-increasing
transcendent Light beyond darkness, we have attained to the vision
of the spiritual Sun (5098), the God among the gods, the Light that
is supernal” (S.52). And then the all-enveloping character of the
spiritual sun: “Here arises the wonderful shining orb of the spiritual
sun —the eye of Mitra, Varuna and Agni. He enlivens and
encompasses the whole static and the dynamic world” (S.53). As
the sun is identified with knowledge and moral excellence, darkness '

                                  268
                                                  Varieties of Spiritual Experience



naturally implies ignorance or illusion and sin. “When you, Oh Indra,
were   engaged     in   dispelling   the dark      illusions spread out by the
demon Svarbhanu, the sage Atri, by means of the fourth sacred
prayer (Brahman),        spotted     out the spiritual     Sun,   who   had   been
shrouded all round by the dark veil of ignorance. None else was able
to feast his eyes on the Sun-god” says the sage Bhauma Atri (S.54).
The fourth prayer may mean the fourth state of consciousness,
Turiya,    which   goes    beyond     waking, dreaming and deep sleep.
Understood in this way Turlyena Brahmana would mean a sacred
prayer uttered in Turlyavastha.


       We may next take a couple of hymns where the experience of
a wheel (Cakra) is described. “Delighted by the dedicated worship
of his devotee, Trita, the mighty Indra smote off his enemy Arbuda
(serpent-like demon) and killed Vala by hurling at him his royal
wheel-like weapon (Cakra) sparkling like the orb of the sun” (S.55).
We are here reminded of an incident in the life of the saint of Umadi,
as narrated by Prof. R.D. Ranade. In                 1910 many devotees had
gathered    at   Inchageri,   a    village   in    Karnatak,   to celebrate   the
Sravana-Saptaha. On the Ekadasi day (30.8.1910), there was a heavy
downpour. A bund in the vicinity of the Math prevented water from
draining away. The swollen waters began to creep in the premises
where devotees were meditating. The water level began to rise
rapidly rendering the situation extremely vulnerable. All at once,
Nandyappa, a senior disciple of the saint of Umadi, rushed to the
saint’s meditation room and apprised him of the serious situation.
The saint came out, looked at the sky, bowed to Almighty with folded
hands and re-entered the meditation room. A few seconds ticked
away and suddenly a huge crack developed in the bund. In a fury
the pent up waters danced down averting the impending danger. At
the time of Pothi (spiritual discourse) the saint asked Amburao
Maharaj, another senior disciple, to sing the following verse:

पडता जडभारी| दासीं आठवावा हरी।
मग तो होऊं नेदी शीण। आड घाली सुदर्शन
                                   ।।

(In the face of a calamity, one should meditate on God, who sees
that a devotee does not suffer. He wards off the peril with his discus).

                                       269
Pathway to God in the Vedas



During the discussion the saint said, “नोड्रेपा! परमात्मा सुदर्शन ओगदु बांध
ओडदु वि द्धा" (See how God hurled his Sudargana on the bund which
instantly burst open).

     Before the vision of the nature-loving Rsis, the form of God
descended like a plant, a tree, an animal or a bird. God might
manifest Himself in the form of a Kalpa-vrksa (wish-tree), or a
Parusamani (philosopher’s stone), says the saint of Nimbargi. “He
who perceives the golden reed (Veta) flashing in the firmament, is
verily the mysterious lord of creation” (S.56). We also have a
beautiful account of the vision of ASvattha. “The immortal Asvattha,
the abode of the Gods, is located in the supreme heaven. There the
gods enjoy the close vicinity of the immortal Being and partake of
the heavenly nectar, Amrta” (S.57).

     One more form is that of a bird. “The celestial bird of reddish
colour, ageless, omnipotent and omnipresent, direct visualiser of
Truth, is seen approaching.    He gains victory over the enemies and
distributes their wealth among his devotees” (S.58). “I apprehend
from afar, by means of my intuitive mind, yourself ascending like
a bird high up in the sky. I beheld your head soaring aloft comfortably
through paths unsullied by dust” (S.59). Regarding a saint’s direct
 vision of Atman flying in the sky, Prof. R. D. Ranade used to narrate
 a couple of incidents in the life of his Master, the saint of Umadi,
 who was himself a disciple of the saint of Nimbargi. (1) Malakappa
 Mashal, a disciple of the saint of Nimbargi, used to go to the house
 of the saint of Umadi (reverentially called Maharaj) for meditation.
 He would often go to the outskirts of Umadi and Inchageri along with
 Maharaj and also accompany him to nearby villages for meditation
 programmes. One day Shri Maharaj, Malakappa and Amburao
 Maharaj started for Jamkhandi. On arriving at Bijapur, Maharaj said
to Malakappa, “1001 here, you need not accompany us to Jamkhandi.
Instead, collect the Abadagiri (whiskers made of brocade) from here
and reach it to Nimbargi. Then straightway proceed to Umadi.”
Malakappa complied with the request and returned to Umadi. On
their way ० Jamakhandi, Maharaj and Amburao crossed river Krsna
at Jambagi. Meditation over, Maharaj lighted camphor. All at once,
he looked up at the sky and said, “Behold, Amburaya, Malakappa

                                    270
                                                Varieties of Spiritual Experience


has left the body”. At that very moment Malakappa had yielded up
his ghost at Umadi. (2) There is another instance of Maharaj’s
similar vision. His grandson Anand, born on 22.9.1899, was a bright
boy. Shri Amburao used to say that the child would recite the entire
Bhajan quite well even at a very tender age. The boy was about five
or six, when he passed away. It was at Inchageri, that one day Shri
Maharaj suddenly dashed out of his meditation room and said,
“Amburaya, heat some water for my bath”. “Why a bath at such an
odd hour?” asked Amburao. ‘“‘Anand passed away,” were the words
which Maharaj uttered. He had witnessed the departure of the boy
who had breathed his last at Umadi at that very moment. A few hours
later a messenger brought the sad tidings from Umadi. (3) There is
a third instance which is very significant. Maharaj was present in
Nimbargi when Nimbargi Maharaj, his spiritual teacher, left his
mortal coil. Maharaj visualised the divine light leaving the saint’s
body and merging itself in the Infinite.

      The vision of Atman or Purusa face-to-face is liberation. A Rsi
boldly says: “I have personally realised this supreme Being (वेदाहमेतं
पुरुषं महान्तम्‌) who is bright as the sun. It is only by Yealising Him
that one transcends death. There is no other way for final
emancipation” (S.60). Sayana while commenting on वेदाहमेतं पुरुषम्‌
occuring also in 07198 Aranyaka III.12.16, says that the supreme
Being is realised by one-pointed meditation (ध्यानेन सर्वदा अनुभवामि );
and hastens to add that Purusa cannot be realised by those who are
not initiated by a worthy spiritual teacher (गुरुशास्त्रोपदेशरहि तेः मूढैः
अनुभवि तुं अशक्यः). Further, the expression तमेव वि दि त्वा अति मृत्युमेति     in
the above-quoted Yajurveda Mantra undergoes a slight variation and
reads as तमेव वि द्धान्‌ अमृतः इह भवति       in Taittiriya Aranyaka 17.12.16.
The wise man, says Sayana, who has a direct vision of God becomes
deathless in this very life though the body may still cling to him.
The realisation that he himself is the Virat Purusa or God and not
the tenement of clay in which he has taken lodgement, implies that
the death of the body does not mean the death of the soul (तं वि राट्पुरुषं
एवं उक्तप्रकारेण वि दान्‌ साक्षात्कुर्वन्‌ इह अस्मि न्‌ एवं जन्मनि अमृतः मरणरहि तः
भवति । यदा वि राट्पुरुषः अहं इति        साक्षात्‌ करोति   तदानीं वर्तमानदेहस्य तत्‌

                                       271
Pathway to God in the Vedas



स्वरूपत्वाभावात्‌तन्मरणेन अयं उपासकः न प्रि यते). This clearly indicates that
the body is not an impediment in the way of realisation. Once a
person is free from the illusion that he is the body, he is emancipated.
Here we see the doctrine of Jivanmukti clearly fore-shadowed. The
vision of the self by the self is regarded as the highest experience.
Rsi Dirghatamas Aucathya says: “I have seen the Atman who is the
imperishable overlord of the senses, moving far and near along
various   paths.   Clothed   in   the halo of gathered       and   diffusive
splendour, he migrates from one place to another throughout the
universe” (S.61). Another account of the vision of Atman is: “The
well-fortified city of gods has eight plexuses (Cakras) and nine
doors. Within the city there is a golden light-emitting sheath. The
dark shadow of pain and misery has no place here. Inside the sheath,
 which has three spokes and which is firmly supported at three
 places, there is the great venerable Being, the God Atman, whom
 the wise alone can realise. This supreme Being, Brahman is the
 sovereign of the golden city” (S.62).

       Yet other forms are referred to in the Vedas. “That Eye,
 dazzling and god-ordained has appeared before our vision. Pray, may
 we visualise it for hundred years and live in that state for hundred
 years” (S.63). This does not mean that God’s form is a heteros. The
 seer himself is God. The Rsis are emphatic about this truth. “Oh
 Vasistha, when Mitra and Varuna saw you taking the form of
 lustrous lightning and when Agastya krought you out from the
 multitude of humanity, it was your real birth” (5.64). ‘Vasistha’
 stands for the individual soul and ‘multitude of humanity’, for the
 body. Yet one more form: “To Medhyatithi, son of Kanva, you verily
 revealed yourself in the form of a ram (Mesa),” runs a verse (S.65).

       (b) Auditory. (i) Musical Instruments. We now turn to the
 experience of sound. Sounds of different musical instruments, heard
 during meditation constitute a very common phenomenon in
 mystical literature of the world. In the Vedas there is a reference
to Vana (S.66). Elsewhere Sayana understands Vana as शतसंख्याभि ः
तन्त्रि भि ः युक्त वीणावि शेषम्‌ (a harp with hundred strings). Other
instruments mentioned are Nali (S.67) and Sasarpari (S.68). The
variety of unstruck sounds includes Simhanada or roaring of a lion
                                     272
                                               Varieties of Spiritual Experience


(३.69) or Hastinada i.e. trumpeting of an elephant (5.70). (ii) Words.
The sounds of various instruments sometimes take the form of
articulated words. Of course, one must have an ear to hear the words;
one must be a sincere devotee in order to catch the messages radioed
from above. “When the Maruts travel in groups they are engaged
in conversation. Rare is he who can hear those words” (S.71). “A
fool cannot understand the significance of the sounds which arise
when Soma is being squeezed” asserts Rsi Andhiguh Syavasvi
(S.72). The words are full of meaning. But the meaning is not
apparent on the surface. “The gods living in the heavens speak a
language by which they understand every thing, but which is not
followed by others” (S.73). And then there is Amrta Vak or divine
speech. “May the auspicious ASvins infuse their delightful sweet-
ness in me so that I may,be able to address words of glorious light
to all the people” (S.74). Those that are not dull of hearing can well
take this divine sound as their guide-line. “O Maruts, you are a
constant threat to sinners. Your speech guides the holy gods as well
as devout mortals engaged in performing sacrifices for attainment
of their desired objects. May that speech of yours lead us to
realisation of our highest ideal” (S.75). “Let that truth-bearing
speech, from which arose. day and night, in which the whole world
reposes, to which the universe owes its moving power, and which
makes waters flow and the sun rise, shelter me from all sides”
(S.76). In this Mantra the Speech is indirectly suggested to be the
ground of the moral order. But we come across another Mantra
where it is called Rta. “The highest self, Patanga bears the word
within his spirit. The same was expressed by the individual self
(Gandharva) from the innermost recesses (the womb) and the sages
experience that luminous and blissful word as the basis of the moral
order Rta” (S.77). In this moral governance lies the supremacy of
words.


      Sometimes Sama Mantras are revealed to great souls. The Rsis
were the seers of Mantras, they actually perceived Mantras, says
Yaska (S.78). Aitareya Brahmana says, “he saw it and then said it”
(तदेतत्‌ क्रषि ः पश्यन्‌ अभ्यनूवाच नि युत्वान्‌ इंद्रसारथि ः इति ). The expression
तदेतत्‌ऋषि ः पश्यन्‌अभ्यनूवाच occurs again in Aitareya Brahmana X.1.33

                                       273
Pathway to God in the Vedas



and XII.1. Sayana interprets पश्यन्‌ in X.1.33 as दि व्यदृष्ट्या पश्यन्‌, ऋषि

as मंत्रद्रष्टा 0 दि व्यज्ञानेन पश्यन्‌. This clearly points to an intuitive
perception of the Mantras by the sages. “You have conquered the
valuable objects of the greedy man and have stood adored in your
own house, absorbed in meditation on truth, along with the deities.
Your devotee is endowed with three kinds of lustre and strength
when he engages himself in your prayers that resemble the sound
of Sama coming from a great distance” (S.79). Two Sama Mantras
were revealed to the Rsis Vasistha and Bharadwaja.                      Vasistha
obtained the Rathantara Sama consisting of two Mantras {3.80) and
Bharadwaja received Brhat Sama, again containing two Mantras
(5.81). In Mahabharata we read: रथन्तरेण तं तात वसि ष्टः प्रत्यबोधयत्‌
(XIV.11.19). Ratha      is the body        which   is   not    permanent.   That
teaching    which   dispels    the   illusion   that    it    is   permanent,   is
Rathantara.     A commentator draws an implication that Vasistha
taught the maxim Aham Brahmasmi by which the student’s wrong
notion was corrected. Vasistha obtained Rathantara Sama from the
shining Dhatr, Savitr and Visnu. This bears the names Pratha and
Sapratha, and is chanted in the Anustup metre at the time of
sacrifice. That secret Sama, Brhat, which enriches a sacrifice, was
gained by Dhatr and others. Bharadwaja won it from Dhatr, Savitr,
Visnu and Agni (S.82).

      The choicest words that remain are, of course, the names of
the Lord. It is ever difficult to describe the melodious nature of the
Name. “The wise seers always entertain a watchful look on the
vision of Reality and have within themselves the experience of the
secret supreme names” (S.83). The names are obviously revealed
to the devotees only. “85 the oarsman moves the boat, the green-
coloured Soma, when being poured out, directs one towards the
Pathway to God, consisting in devout prayers. The very same Soma
shows the hidden divine names to those who are loudly chanting the
praises of gods at the time of sacrifice” (S.84). Well may the names
be called secret; they are secret for those who have no longing for
God; they are common knowledge for the devout. The door is opened
to those who knock. “Oh Agni, we know your three forms and your
three resting places. We also understand your silvery name as well

                                     274
                                                Varieties of Spiritual Experience



    as the   source   from    which   it springs,” says the sage Vatsapri
    Bhalandana (S.85).

         (c) Gustatory. Coming to the experience of taste, we find that
    the Vedic Mantras abound in descriptions of partaking of Soma-
    rasa. The drink apparently means the juice which was squeezed from
    herbs and strained through a thick cloth for purposes of drinking.
    That the drinking of this herbal juice was prevalent in Vedic times
    is well known; but it is not always this drink that is meant in the
    poems. Unfortunately this draught immortal is many a time mistaken
    for the earthly intoxicating drink. The Vedic seers have emphasized
the less evident but more important truth that only a Brahmajia or
one who is awakened in knowledge and none else, can claim the
divine Soma. “To Soma the gods owe their energy and the earth,
its greatness. Therefore the Soma has been given the place of honour
over and above the stars. When the herbal drink is crushed, people
take it as Soma. But that which the realised souls call Soma, no one
else can partake of” (5.86). The Atharvaveda (IX.1.3) uses the word
पार्थि व in place of the Rgveda word कश्चन. It makes a better reading,
for it clearly says that no ‘earthly’ being (interested only in mundane
affairs) partakes of Soma. Dr. Sampurnanand reminds us that we
must not forget what Veda itself says about Soma: “In their desire
to drink Soma people press the herb known as Soma; but no one
can taste of the Soma which is known to the Brahmanas. This one
sentence completely refutes the theory that Soma was an intoxicant
prepared from Soma-herb and was used for the purpose for which
other intoxicants are used. Quite obviously Soma here referred to
is not something material but a subjective experience reserved only
for the select few. They are called Brahmanas.”' By long penance
and serene contemplation, they have shed off their earthliness.
Significantly, Soma is said to occupy the highest place in heaven,
and the mellifluous juice is described as oozing from the top-most
plexus, Sahasrara Cakra. The quality of unearthliness is thus
emphasised. Other Mantras repeat it. “Prepare for Indra, the wielder


1     An Introduction to the Vedic Study, Academy of Comparative Philosophy and
      Religion, Belgaum, 1969, P.52


                                        275
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of Vajra, the delectable heavenly nectar (Divah Piyiisa)” (5.87).
Hindi saints have deified it by calling it Harirasa or Ramarasa. The
juice which a mystic enjoys is touched by divinity. Hence the
sanctifying quality of the ambrosial honey. “The Soma is bright like
the sun and is flowing towards a vast lake-like reservoir. He gives
rise to seven Streams that can reach as high as the sky” (5.88). Here
too the process is understood physiologically to mean the flow of
ambrosia that is poured into the lateral ventricle of the brain.' “The
nectar which is ancient and laudable was extracted from the large
and deep cave of the heavens. When Indra appeared on the scene,
he was given a loud ovation” (S.89). The extraction of nectar from
the heavenly cave is certainly the out-flow of drops of the spiritual
fluid.    What   happens   is   that   “every   cell   begins   to   discharge
 ‘Amirasa’, or mellifluous juice at the time of meditation...”* Only
the higher ups in the spiritual line can prepare the Somarasa. “Those
whose mind is under control, spread a cloth with thousands of holes
for straining the juice. Their speech becomes pure. Their helpmates
are the Maruts who are refreshing (इपि र), who are free from jealousy
 (अट्रुह), who constitute an inspecting squad (स्पश), who move very
swiftly (स्वंच) and who have keen and beautiful eyes (सुदृश)” (S.90).
This whole process implies meditation in the company of fellow-
aspirants. It is no wonder that Soma is called the brain of the cosmic
form of God by Rsi Brahma (S.91).
         Intellectual and moral effects of Soma are recorded. “This is
the true nature of Soma. He welcomes the learned who assemble
at his place, creates the vast expanse for the sun to move, dispatches
good wishes to Manu but condemns the irreligious wicked to hell”
(S.92). This divine draught is a prerogative of. the devout mystic
whose life it enriches. No wonder, for Soma is borne by Syena or
Brahman Himself. Soma is Pavamana i.e. flowing; it is at the same
time purifying. Its cleansing nature becomes all the more eloquent
when we keep in mind that Pavamana is an attribute of both fire and
wind. Pawamana Soma is the life-force of the universe. It enlightens
and accords beatitude.

1   Vasudey Saran Agarwal, Veda Vidya, P. 107
2 २. 7. Ranade, Pathway to God in Hindi Literature, P. 222

                                       276
                                        Varieties of Spiritual Experience


     (d) Olfactory. Only a chosen few can have this experience.
Vedic sages were fortunate to have it. God is not merely beautiful
and sweet-voiced, He diffuses fragrance also. “We worship the
three-eyed Lord Siva who is fragrant and who nourishes all beings.
May he liberate us from death as a cucumber is freed from its stem”
(S.93). Soma too is described as having the sweet-smelling quality.
“Learned friends, may you and we drink the Soma which is brilliant
and fragrant and which will enhance our powers” (S.94).

     (e) Synchronous. Times are, when a devotee becomes a
recipient of more than one type of experience simultaneously. The
spiritual bliss over-flows and vitalises the organism from inside,
resulting in the experience resembling sense-experience. Ofcourse,
simultaneity depends on the powerfulness of the spiritual current as
well as on the adaptation of the internal apparatus to catch the waves.
The man becomes ‘all eyes’ or ‘all ears’ when the vital energy is
concentrated in one place and is attuned to God. When the internal
sensory apparatus is alert enough to enter into the magnetic field of
the spiritual energy, it is blessed with joy; and when more than one
sense enter the field, they are all blessed. At the time of such
‘cumulative’ experiences God’s power envelops an aspirant and he
responds to it with all his being. “As the resplendent ambrosial juice,
Soma, enters into the lateral ventricle (Hiranyaya KoSa), peals of
unstruck sound are heard” (S.95). If we have a combination of
flavour and sound in the above account, flavour, sound and light are
woven together in other Mantras. “Soma keeps a watchful eye on
men and is flowing in a clear stream. An embodiment of the eternal
moral order, he calls out to gods from the highest heavens. He glows
in silver fashion along with Brhaspati’s lion-like roaring. By his
libations, the sacrificial halls overflow like an ocean” (S.96). Again,
“the sacred flow of Soma made a rumbling sound and at the same
time exhibited the kindly light also” declares saint Amahiyu
Angirasa (S.97). On the flavour-minded Vedic Rsis engaged in
sincere prayers, God confers the experience of the ambrosial juice;
but as if their ardent invocations out-do his gifts, He fondles them
with the further bonus of sound and light.

     Many are the ways in which God Almighty unveils his
                                  277
Pathway to God in the Vedas



treasures to his devotees. A mystic, claim the Vedas, enjoys God
in a number of ways    — as light, colour, form, sound, flavour or
fragrance. The sages vouch-safe these experiences, of course adding
a proviso that they are showered on those who are given to Him in
mind, word and deed.
