      पवमानो अजीजनदि वश्चि त्रं न तन्यतुम।्‌ ज्योति र्वैधानरं वृहत्




                                          285
                           CHAPTER X




            Effects of God-realisation

     A Vedic sing-song holding up a picture of Elysium runs: “Being
solely devoted to the majestic God and humming his praises, we
have crossed over the limits of deep ignorance. The devotee is
offering his praises to the Almighty, the immortal One” (5.1). This
is an ideal place where knowledge and immortality await a devotee.
Many more such lyrics are to greet us now. We have hitherto traced
the journey of an aspirant on the spiritual path, partly hazardous,
partly dark but ultimately full of light and sweetness. It now remains
to take a survey of effects of God-realisation. Destruction of
ignorance paves the way for attainment of true knowledge. The
highest principle is recognised as the indwelling spirit of the
universe. A conviction of the triumph of piety follows immediately.
Evil may be present in the world but it has no demoralising effect
on the released soul. The jubilant sage is eloquent in expressing his
bliss which is the same as immortality. After reaching his cherished
goal of unison with the Supreme, the Rsi chants universal prayers.

     1. Dawn of Knowledge. There is a graphic account of how a
Saint’s ignorance is dispelled. “We have now jumped over the
boundaries of darkness once for all. Dawn of illumination shines
forth in all her manifestations of consciousness with her graceful and
attractive smiles” (S.2). The comparison of ignorance with darkness
and of knowledge with light has seized the imagination of men since
the nebulous antiquity of the Vedic period. The concept dark night
is frightening but not the fact. The Rsis toil far through the waning
night into the waxing day. The way to this lustrous region is along
the moral law. Rsi Nidhruvi Kasyapa tells us that “these tawny
coloured drops of Soma guide the devotee along the course of the
eternal law (Rta) beyond any deceit or error’ (S.3). This 15 the
hallowed region of the highest Brahman. Knowledge will be worth
the name only if the highest is deemed to be the object of its
attainment. If one enjoys an ode for the sake of the tune and does
                                                 Effects of God-realisation



not catch the message, it is of no use. “What has he to do with the
letters of the Veda who does not know its theme, namely, the
attainment of almighty ruler of the supreme region in which the
Devas dwell? But those who have come to know Him are perfect”
(S.4). The vision of such persons is so much absorbed in God that
they are oblivious of the distinctions found in the mundane world.
The saint of Nimbargi uttered : “If you term that Reality a boy, I
will call it a girl; you name It a girl and I say, a boy.” This is the
implication of a Vedic passage : “They tell me that these were males
though as a physiological fact they were females. One who has the
mystical eye sees the truth; the spiritually blind are not able to
discern It. The son may realise the truth while the father may not.
The son on account of his mystical wisdom is virtually the father
of the father” (S.5).

      The protector of the universe is no longer felt as ‘the other.’
So complete is His sway that the aspirant feels that He has entered
into himself. This is veritably the acme of knowledge. Knowledge,
which makes explicit the unity in multiplicity, finds its consumma-
tion here and returns or recoils upon itself. It is in this sense that
Aristotle described God as a thinking Theoria. God thinks His own
thoughts and remains absorbed in that beatitude. Assimilation with
this principle is consequent on the true knowledge of It. The unison
dispels the last shred of doubt from a man’s mind. “When one
realises that all beings exist in Atman and that Atman is in all beings,
one does not entertain any kind of doubt whatsoever’, asserts the
seer with adamantine certainty (S.6). The psychological aspect of
getting the spiritual experience and the epistemological aspect of its
validity are fused in the immediate conviction of the veracity of the
experience.

     2. God as Fons et Origo. God is realised not only as the only
Reality but also as an indwelling spirit of the world. The unborn, the
formless, the unmanifest is experienced as taking on variegated
names and forms. The knowledge of the natura naturans is followed
by the knowledge of the natura naturata. God is visualised not only
as the energising principle but also as the sum-total of all reality.
A devotee declares that as long as he was ensconsed in the body,

                                  287
Pathway to God in the Vedas



he was subject to repeated births. But in that very stage he acquired
divine knowledge and burst through the bonds like a hawk (S.7).
Here is enthusiasm, strengthened by penance; here is a spirit of
dignity nurtured by piety and sacrifice. God is seen to be the central
thread round which are woven all existences. God is made the
harmonising principle. This does not mean that the existences are
essentially separate from God. “He who is foremost in propitiating
the worshippers by means of Daksina is himself called the seer, or
Brahman, or chief worshipper or Sama-chanter; he knows the three
forms of the dazzling one” (S.8). Obviously the three forms are the
three bodies — the gross, the subtle and the causal. The vision of
oneness of microcosm and macrocosm frees an aspirant from all the
three bodies. He is absorbed in the foundational cause or Maha-
karana, the Cause of causes. “Obeisance to you who prevents us
from a fall. We salute your thunder-bolt and your splendour. We
know you as having taken lodgement in the cavity of man’s heart
as also in the navel of the ocean” (S.9). Or again, “Oh Brahman,
you asked me whether I am present in the three regions where Visnu
is worshipped. My reply is that in all the three places, both you and
I are present. I know both the earth and the heaven as well as that
 which lies beyond” (S.10). Elsewhere all things in the world are said
 to be ‘inside’ the Brahman. “I have beheld the greatness of that great
 Being, immortal amongst the mortals. The jaws of the voracious
 eater are constantly engaged in devouring, though insatiably, the
 manifold objects” (S.11). In Brahman which is eternal existence
even death finds a place; in fact, here death loses its teeth. Death
 is a necessary but an unalarming event. And if death itself is
accommodated why not deities of evil? Well, they are not excluded.
Take the case of the fifty-ninth Sikta of the tenth Mandala of
२९५९६५३. Here the subject-matter of first four Mantras is Nirruti!
Sayana regards Nirruti as a Papa-devata, seducting one to a life of
sin and suffering and ultimately leading one to death. “The devotees
worship the three Nirrutis. Thereafter they realise the fires who are
heard far and wide. The mystics know the original source of the
Nirrutis which lies in the supreme and mysterious vault” (S.12). The
three evils may refer to three types of afflictions, physical,
theological and psychical. “Oh Manyu, who are a giver of victory

                                  288
                                              Effects of God-realisation



like Indra, and are irreproachable, be our protector at this sacrifice.
Oh enduring one, we sing your exaltations. We know this to be the
source whence you have become mighty” (S.13).

     Another side of this unitive experience       is to see that the
universe is a theophany. The Yajurveda and Atharvaveda
                                                    join hands
in such a kind of description. “God Prajapati, the Lord of all creation
entered into the embryo of all creatures, and though He is an un-
born Being, yet He is born many times in various bodily forms. Only
mystics see His original real form in which all the worlds are
established” (S.14). “The God Prajapati enters into the embryo of
all creatures, then He is invisible. He reveals Himself through bodily
forms. He created this entire universe by one part only; the other
part constituting all bliss” (S.15). On the whole, God’s immanence
and His revelation through created objects is a perorative refrain of
the post-ecstatic exclamations of the ancient minstrels. Along with
the presence and power of God seen in all things, the mystic
perceives His benevolence also. Rsi Prajapati Vaisvamitra says :
“You were always looking to the spiritual welfare of the devotees
whereby grand success was guaranteed to them. Be thou our guide.
The wise discern the multifarious exploits of Indra who would
provide security in a sustained manner” (S.16). The Reality which
is experienced within is also experienced without. “Those who
realise Brahman in the human body, realise also the Paramesthin.
Those who realise Paramesthin and Prajapati and those who realise
the sovereign Brahman, come to realise thereafter the supreme pillar
supporting the entire universe” (S.17). Below such weighty and
magnificent poems the tide of hope flows strongly.

     3. Triumph over Evil. The problem of evil has always been
a ticklish one and philosophers have found evil to be an inconvenient
guest for whom no place is reserved at the table. It is only the
dualists and the pluralists who boldly admit the existence of the twin
principles of Good and Evil as real, though the relation between the
two is by no means explained by them satisfactorily. The absolutists
have branded evil to be only an appearance but it remains a veritable
reality on the empirical level. The problem of evil has always been
a hard nut to crack, because it is not consistent with either God’s
                                  289
Pathway to God in the Vedas



omnipotence or with His benevolence — the two attributes of God
none of which a devout theist would surrender. It seems that evil
is destined to be a persistent and perpetual problem.
       In Ethics, the problem appears under a different guise. Evil is
the agreed starting point of morality. In the absence of evil, moral
philosophy would be like Don Quixote’s battles — fighting without
enemies. Moral conquest is nothing but a fight against the principle
of evil and as such presupposes evil. The optimistic thinkers have
predicted that ultimately evil would fall to pieces and good would
rise victorious. “Ten irreligious kings confederated against the
devout Sudasa. But they were not able to overpower him. The
prayers of the devotees became fruitful and the truth was ultimately
vindicated. Gods appeared at their invocations” (S.18). The battle
between the forces of good and the forces of evil is described as
a formidable one and is fought with many a powerful weapon. “When
Vrtra and Indra faced each other in a personal encounter, they used
all their first-rate weapons; but ultimately Vrtra fell and was lost in
 abysmal darkness; and Indra, on account of his inherent greatness,
 was   crowned    with   success   and   became   the   foremost   to   be
 worshipped” (S.19). At another place Indra is depicted as fighting
 with yet another weapon. “Indra, the unrivalled warrior smote down
 the ninetynine Vrtras by means of the bones of the sage Dadhica
 (S.20). It is well-known that for the destruction of Vrtra, a special
 weapon had to be manufactured out of the bones of an extremely
 pious person and that the sage Dadhica who answered to the
 requirements, had to give up his ghost in order to make his bones
 available for the purpose. We see here how an individual sacrificed
himself willingly for universal welfare. No great man remains
encapsulated in his own realisation; he works for others. Indra can
also be understood as the soul within that fights against innumerable
vices with an instrument born out of penance and piety. Indra is by
no means a lone fighter; there are many others like him. They are
armed with inner discipline and self-culture. “Oh Soma, you too like
Indra are endowed with power to do great and valiant deeds. You
are the slayer of Vrtras and demolisher ofthe fortresses. Like Pedu’s
horse you extirpated not only Vrtra but also all the irreligious
people” (S.21).

                                   290
                                              Effects of God-realisation



     Close on the heels of the destruction of evil forces, arises the
feeling of triumph as well as the hope that the victory is ever-lasting.
This explains the joyous attitude of the sage Hiranyastipa Angirasa
when he exclaims : “In the battle that ensued between Indra (the
human self) and Vrtra (the encircling evil forces of Satan), the
missiles like lightning, thunder, dense mist and heavy down-pour of
rain, hurled by Vrtra did no harm to Indra who ultimately came out
victorious” (S.22). This cosmic victory is but an individual victory
writ large. The individual victory consists in the realisation of the
Atman. This realisation was the golden culmination of the endeavour
of Vedic Rsis and was the burden of their prayers. Here is a victory
song : “We have become victorious; we have gained trophies. All
our enemies have been toppled. So said Agni and so said Soma.
Pusan has gifted us a habitat in the holy heavens. We have attained
to the light of Atman and are united with the gloss of the spiritual
sun. We shall acquire more spiritual wealth and glory” (5.23).
Another couplet of trophy runs : “I have become victorious. I am
now the most exalted and sublime being on this earth. I have
uprooted every one on every side. I am the universal sovereign”
(S.24). Or again, “Indra has become the grand master. Agni and
Soma have also become glorious; of all the majestic beings in the
universe I am the foremost” (5.25). Praskanva Kanva expresses his
spiritual victory : “The spiritual sun with all his sparkling vigour has
made his appearance subjugating the enemies to my custody. Never
shall I be subdued by my foes” (S.26). We read likewise : “Like
Indra, I have demolished the ill-wishers without any kind of damage
to my person. All of them have been brought down at my feet” (5.27).
And again : “I have crushed those snakes in the grass; there is no
rival left now to oppose me. 1 have been ruling hitherto over these
beings only. Now, I have become the sovereign of all I survey”
(S.28). The Rsi’s faith was a rich soil for hope to grow and so he
could easily overcome the stresses of the long and cold Arctic night.
     Another effect of the realisation of the Atman is that all
miseries come to an end. To a Rsi all suffering is a result of the
displeasure of the elements whom he had invested with conscious-
ness and with supernatural power. That is why for all sorts of
suffering, help from celestial quarters is solicited. We thus read how


                                  291
Pathway to God in the Vedas



the grace of Soma or Siirya is described as pounding evil to pieces,
whether external or internal. “Now be ready with your Soma juice.
Fear not; this dark cloud of calamities is a passing phase and will
vanish   of itself’   (S.29).   Soma   is   described   at   one   place   as
counteracting the fatal effects of poison. “In the beginning a Brahmin
with ten heads and ten faces was born. He first drank off Soma juice
and thereby made the poison lose its injurious essence” (5.30).
Soma takes lodgement in the mind of the devotee and sweeps the
ills clean. “Away have fled all those misfortunes and ailments. All
the harmful forces have been driven aghast. Soma with his mighty
strength has risen high in us and we have now reached a state where
men can prolong their life” (5.31). The sun who ousts darkness also
disperses catastrophies. “The sun, foremost amongst the luminous
bodies, has appeared now, dispelling sin and misery and spreading
out illumination in all the quarters” (S.32). Even when a person is
blessed with a mere flash of religious experience, he rises superior
to the evil mongerings of the mean-minded. The sages were realised
souls and could dismiss the foul designs of the mass of ordinary
worshippers.
     We shall now notice three Mantras which refer to effects of
God-realisation that have been termed by Professor R.D. Ranade as
moral purification, intellectual clarification and spiritual sanctifica-
tion. The God-realiser becomes so pure morally that no evil ever
contaminates him. “Today we have become victorious; we have
obtained whatever was desired. We have become completely free
from sin. Oh dawn, your pure light drives away our evil dreams of
which we were afraid. Your aids are harmless and immaculate”
(S.33). The claim is conveyed in words decked with musical quality.
Next comes a Mantra describing intellectual clarification. Only
those who realise Atman as filling every nook and cranny of the
universe are said to possess superior intellect. “What are the things
in whom dwells the highest Atman and what are the things that are
included in that Atman? Provide us with the answers to these
questions which we ask in order to assess whose intellect is
superior. The answer is : The Paramatman fills all the five Pranas
and in the Paramatman itself are included all sense-organs like the
eye and so on. This is the proper reply to your query and it comes

                                   292
                                                  Effects of God-realisation



from     me   having    superior intellect”   (5.34). Prajapati   asks   the
questions and himself gives the answers. “From the surface of the
earth to the mid-region and from the mid-region to heaven, I
ascended; and from heaven I ascended to the blissful self-effulgent
light. The wise men who carry on worship of the divine support of
this universe, do not in the least entertain any craving for worldly
happiness” (S.35). This is spiritual sanctification. The result is a
spirit of surrendering oneself to the all-pervading Atman. It is they
alone who surrender to Him, body and soul, that can be taken as
having an immediate apprehension of Atman. “We rejoice in the
eternal region where the gods sacrifice to the immortals with their
minds which are themselves immortal. It is in that region again that
we behold the light of the sun when he rises. Is there any thing more
powerful which can be attained by means of a sacrifice than what
was achieved by gods who sacrificed themselves? The ignorant
sacrifice by offering a dog; others do it by offering cows. He who
sacrifices his mind to God is alone praise-worthy. Let him give us
knowledge and render proper advice to us” (S.36).

       To a mystic the world seems to be full of beings who shower
grace on others. “The sun is going to shower his grace on the region
where the devotees are engaged in auspicious activities, proper to
holy precincts” (S.37). Desires are fulfilled not by feeding them but
by collecting the highest Reality. “Visvakarman, the great architect
who designed the whole universe is endowed with the special faculty
of intuitive mind. His greatness is immeasurable. He is the creator,
arranger and also the chief supervisor over this universe. The sages
say that he is the supreme one beyond the seven Rsis i.e. beyond
the seven means of perception. On account of the beatific joy in
realising Him, there is fulfilment of all desires” (S.38). The longing
for worldly objects comes to be pale and dead. The petty things no
more attract the attention of the devotee.
       4. Attainment of Bliss. By far the most important effect of
mystic    vision   is   the attainment of bliss to which there are
innumerable references. In this connection Indra is many times
described as discharging the function of guiding travellers on the
spiritual path. The sage Vamadeva Gautama assures : “Verily the
mighty God (Bharvara or Indra) is ever eager to give assistance to

                                    293
Pathway to God in the Vedas



the devotees. His inspiration is secretly present in the heart of the
worshipper, bringing  clarity to his intellectual perception and
prompting him in gaining the bliss of God-realisation” (S.39). God
helps those who are actively benevolent. “God imparts knowledge
to those who offer sacrifices and who render valuable service to
others. He makes them more and even more affluent. He does not
hide himself from his admirers. In his own abode he establishes those
that desire to attain to divinity” (S.40). In this verse, a very important
statement has been made, viz. that God does not hide Himself from
his devotees. As Berkeley said : “God concerns Himself very nearly
in our affairs; but it is only those who have eyes that can see. Others,
though they are surrounded with such clear manifestations of the
deity, are yet little affected by them that they seem, as it were, blinded
with excess of light.” Secondly, we find here in its bare rudiments,
the concept of ‘Salokata’ or liberation by attaining to the abode of
God. The concept played an important role in the later development
of Vedantic thought. Those who are fortunate to have the vision of
the highest, enjoy the light and warmth thereof. “The spiritual form
of the peerless, bounteous Lord with the invincible fortifications is
glorious and auspicious, as if it were the vision of the celestial sun”
(S.41). JnaneSvara has immortalised the vision of the sun by calling
it Cit-strya — the Sun of Absolute Reality.
      In a number of places in the Vedas we read that the aspirants
gain a bonanza from Soma. “The invigorating ambrosial juice oozing
down into the lateral ventricle ofthe illumined sages, creates divine
light and spiritual delight” (S.42). “The swift and shining Maruts will
reach you to a place of sacrifice. They will drink the sweet, joy-
giving Soma juice and will create food by sending forth rains”
(S.43). The Soma is always praised for such services. In one place
we find Barhaspatya Bharadwaja challenging the beneficence of the
acts which Indra performed when exhilerated with the Soma. “What
did Indra do when he was intoxicated with Soma? What did he do
after he drank it? And after he befriended it? What good did he do
to the ancient adorers assembled in the sacrificial hall or to them
who have assembled now?” (S.44). The next Mantra provides the
answers. The same Bharadwaja after receiving from Indra what he
had hankered after, changed his stance. He who had come to scoff

                                  294
                                                      Effects of God-realisation


remained to pray. “Whatever Indra did in the exhileration of Soma
was auspicious. After partaking of Soma and after aligning with it,
he performed beneficent acts. All his fans, past and present, who
had assembled in the hall of sacrifice, were gratified” (S.45). Rsi
Kasyapa Marica is almost enamoured of the exciting land of Soma
and he portrays it in a peculiarly charming and unmatched lyric
known as Pavamana Sukta. “Oh Pavamana Soma, where there is
unbroken    lustre    and   where   there   is   delightful   presence of the
spiritual sun, in that land of immortality and fullness, may my seat
be reserved. Oh Soma, send forth your flow for the sake of Indra.
Where,    the   son   of    Vivaswat   is   ruling,   where    the   source   of
illumination is hidden, where giant rivers glide along, in that land
make me immortal. Oh Soma, send forth your flow for the sake of
Indra. Where, in the third heaven, one can move freely, where all
the four corners are studded with bright rays, in that land, make me
immortal. Oh Soma, send forth your flow for the sake of Indra.
Where, the residents perform only disinterested actions, where a
fountain of nectar gushes out, where mirth is spread out as a fare,
in that land make me immortal. Oh Soma, send forth your flow for
the sake of Indra. Where one finds four kinds of fecility — Ananda,
Moda, Muda and Pramoda — where the very desire of desires is
fulfilled, in that land make me immortal. Oh Soma, send forth your
flow for the sake of Indra” (S.46). The Siikta ends, but the sweet
melody of the words, इन्द्राय इन्दो परि स्रव, continue to ring in the ear.

     The devotee is always eager to enjoy the close presence of
God. “Make me shine like fire enkindled by friction, brighten our
vision, favour us with plenitude; while I am in your soothing
proximity, I feel I have won laurels. Pray, allow the Soma to continue
and progress here and hereafter” (S.47). One very important ethical
problem is touched here. Moral philosophers do not agree whether
morality comes to an end after all the wrongs in the world are righted
or whether it continues even thereafter. Both sides of the question
are represented by heavy-weights. The Vedic Rsi, even after he
marches through the arch of fortune does not think that he can, once
for all, rest there. He prays for the continued progress in bliss. Even
if the struggle for achieving the goal ceases, the march continues.

                                    295
Pathway to God in the Vedas



The point is very succinctly put by A.E. Taylor, “... the winning of
heaven would not leave the pilgrim arrived at the end of the journey
with nothing further to do. In heaven itself, though there would be
no longer progress towards fruition, there might well be progress in
fruition. Life “there” would be, as life “here” is not, living by vision,
as contrasted with living by faith and hope; but might not the vision
itself
     be capable of ever-increasing enrichment?” Verily the heaven
is the attainment of God. Its tranquil bliss has no limits. The ecstasy
of such attainment greets us every now and then in the Vedas. Thus
an aspirant addresses Indra: “Highly invigorating is thy boon, most
dazzling thy prowess; thereby the evil forces meet their doom and
immeasurable strength comes to one’s hands” (S.48). The same
emphasis on the raptures of God-vision is found frequently. “Oh you
Indra of incomparable intellect, your majesty is indicative of your
regal status. Be you an enjoyer of that glory. This splendour is
cherished by one and all, and it enabled you to eliminate Vrtra root
and branch. It brings good tidings to the devotee; be you ever
rejoicing    in   it”   (5.49).   Such   a gracious   fortune   automatically
expresses itself in post-ecstatic exclamations on the part of the
blessed one. “Contemplating upon the rays that emanate from the
light eternal, they actually beheld their own spiritual form and were
transported with joy and thereupon their thunderous shouts of victory
radiated warmth in heaven and earth’ (5.50). Maddened by the
hilarity of God-vision, the Rsi proclaims; “I am inspired by God
Savitr. Now with the arms of ASwins:and hands of Piisan, with
Saraswati’s power on my tongue, and under the direction of
Prajapati, 1 hand over to you the empire of Agni’ (5.51).

       5. Immortality. It is one of the contradictions of human life
that man wishes to be immortal in this mortal world; but who has
ever heard of a tale of immortality in the world where the dictates
of death are inviolable? Whatever is born is sure to die. In his
Farewell Address the Buddha exhorted: “Decay is inherent in all
component things. But the Truth will remain for ever. Work out your
salvation with diligence.” Death is true so far as the frail body is
concerned. But when we speak of immortality, it is not bodily

1   The Faith of a Moralist, Series I, P. 408

                                         296
                                              Effects of God-realisation


immortality. When Buddha announced his death, Ananda implored
him: “Vouchsafe Lord, to remain with us for the welfare ofthe great
multitude.” The enlightened one said: “Enough now Ananda...
beseach not the Tathagata... why should I preserve this body of flesh
when the body of the exalted law (Dharmakaya) will endure?”

     The concept of immortality can be understood in different
senses. It is a biological phenomenon that man inherits physical or
intellectual characteristics from his fore-fathers and passes them on
to his children. His genes are carriers of this inheritance. Thus even
after his death, a part of him actually continues to live. This is
biological immortality. Again, it is well-known that a man is known
through his achievements. Works of art and literature, institutions
founded and nurtured by him, are a monument to him and he lives
through them. This is social immortality. We are here concerned
neither with biological, nor with social immortality, but with
metaphysical immortality, mystically realised. The fact of identity
between self and God is the metaphysical expression of the life
eternal. The self is essentially of the nature of God — both being
Caitanya — and continues to, live in God. This metaphysical fact,
however, should not remain an intellectual assertion; a mere
reasoned conviction of identity is of no avail; it should be a matter
of mystical experience. Needless to say that once there is direct
experience of this identity, man knows that he is immortal.
Embedded in the frail frame of a mortal, the fire of immortality
blazes forth.

     The invocation of sage Saucika Agni is that they be endowed
with immortality as well as with brave sons, so that they would be
serving gods efficiently. They would be providing Indra with a
thunderbolt wherewith he would fight with his foes (S.52). This
Mantra perhaps contains a suggestion of biological immortality.
Immortality is to be achieved in a variety of ways. The intuitive
experience of God, the author of nature, is described as one of the
means. “The manifestation of this material form of Gayatra took
place on this basis of the unmanifest Gayatra-energy of the
immortal. The perishable form of Traistubha was fashioned out of
the unmanifest Traistubha-energy; and the manifestation of this form

                                  297
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of the universe was founded upon the unmanifest energy of the
immortal Jagati. Those who realise this, attain to ever-lasting life”
(S.53). Here we see the metaphor of evolution applied to the cosmic
processes. Evolution here is to be taken in its strictly etymological
sense which “presupposes a fundamental unity, in which all that is
eventually evolved or disclosed was involved or contained from the
first.”' He who has experienced that creation is only an unfoldment
of divinity, gains immortality. Naturally the highest deity is sought
to be pleased. There are a few on whom fortune smiles even without
their having traversed the trodden path. We are told that the sage
Vamadeva received illumination while still in the womb of his
mother. There itself he tried to know all the births of gods, i.e., the
ways by which they attained to knowledge. To Indra’s behest that
Vamadeva also follow the same path, the latter gave an emphatic
    ‘no’. He preferred to come out of the travails with the dramatic
swiftness of a hawk. Even a hundred citadels would not hold him.
Thus in a hymn is exemplified Vamadeva’s sudden illumination
(S.54). Service no less than knowledge, is a means to life ever-
    lasting. Rbhus earned the friendship of gods by their remarkable
service. Their moral stature too was enhanced. Their works became
    acceptable to the powerful and lustrous gods. On account of the skill
and deftness in performing duties, Vaja became a favourite of gods.
Rbhuksas       was    particularly   appreciated   by   Indra   and   Vibhvan
especially      by   Varuna   (S.55).   “The   Rbhus    zealously     practised
penance in the company of venerable seekers. Mortals as they were,
these sons of Sudhanvan attained to eternal life. IIlumined with the
lustre of the sun, they became worthy of homage” (S.56). We clearly
see in this lyric a reference to the moral and spiritual effects of the
company of the saints. Meditation in the company of pious souls
enables a devotee to attain to Holy Will. Though Holy Will is the
privilege of God, it can as well, belong to ‘just men made perfect’.
Association with the great always brings good to man. A cruise in
the current oftheir friendship refreshes him. Deities like Soma, Agni
or Yajna are said to be helping an aspirant in getting the fruit of
deathlessness. “You, Oh Soma are the accomplisher of sacrifice.


1     James Ward, The Realm of Ends, P. 100

                                        298
                                               Effects of God-realisation


Through you we have attained the ever-glittering light and have
crossed over death. From earthly existence, we have risen to the
state of illumination; we have realised the gods and also the self-
effulgent light” (S.57). What can an adversary do to such souls and
what will the crafty guiles ofa mortal do to them? Along with Soma,
Agni is also described as extending his spiritual hand in gaining
immortality. “Oh Agni holding fast to your ageless wings which soar
high and which destroy the demons, we fly to that region to which
the ancient seers have repaired” (S.58). Here we are reminded of
the last words of Shri Amburao Maharaj, one of the great saints of
Nimbargi Sampradaya, “I am free from all desires as my highest
desire, namely, self-realisation is fulfilled. Like trailing clouds of
glory, I will now fly God-ward with the words ‘Narayana, Narayana
on my 115.” Morality and meditation constituted the two wings for
his flight to the Infinite.

      There are also hymns in the Vedas where the sages describe
their having gone to the divine exalted realms. They call it Swarga
or Dyuloka and speak of it as an abode of bliss and light. It is,
however, difficult to understand literally many a phrase, such as
travelling in a golden chariot to Indra’s abode or going to heaven
by holding on to Agni’s wings. It is better to understand the concept
of places bathed in light as indicating an elevated moral and spiritual
state of an aspirant. The light may be symbolic of moral exaltation
or of mystical lustre. Brahaspati’s or Indra’s sorrowless and sublime
vaults may be indicative of the blissful peace of the seers. “Oh you
Indra of beautiful armour, ride your golden chariot. We shall also
ride that air-borne chariot which is auspicious, immaculate and
majestic and which emits light-rays. We shall then stand by your
side. The devotees salute the gods and sit in their vicinity. They
persuade the gods to attend their sacrifices. They expect a precious
gift from the divinities. Thus Priyamedhas have followed the
traditional route and reached the destination of ancient seers” (5.59).
The same reference to the higher regions is found in a celebrated
passage which is repeated in later literature. We are here told that
as dictated by primeval ordinances, devotees worshipped God in a
spirit of complete self-surrender. These great men attained to the


                                  299
Pathway to God in the Vedas



highest spiritual state which was reached by the by-gone saints
(S.60). We read how an aspirant passes from a profane state to a
sacred state. “I shed my earthly nature and became divine. At the
time when I refused to participate in your auspicious sacrifice, I was
still under the influence of binding self-love” (5.61). What after all
is immortality? It is not going from one region to another region; it
consists   in   visualising God everywhere and      at   all   times.   The
Kingdom of God, it is said, lies within us. Immortality, therefore,
is realising god here and now, which is the same thing as abiding
in one’s own self. It is also rejoicing in the Spirit, says Rsi Bhrgu
(S.62). Finding spiritual treasure in his own self, the           aspirant
becomes confirmed in immortality.

      6. Union with the God-head. For one who has the vision of
God everywhere, all duality comes to an end.             He becomes a
Samadarsi, one who has equality of vision everywhere. There is a
 feeling of identity with everything. This unitive vision is clearly seen
 in many a Vedic stanza. “Hail to thee Oh heaven, hail to thee, Oh
 earth, hail to thee Oh intermediate space. The sun is my eye, the
 Vayu is my vital force, the intermediate space is my self and the earth
 is my body. 1 am he who is known as Amara or the deathless. I am
 offering my heart and soul in order to be protected by heaven and
 earth.” This is a 5011049 of the sage Brahma (S.63). Such a seer
 is credited with the power of creating the world, for he asserts that
 he has given rise to seasons and seven seas. Another important point
 which he makes is that the distinction between truth and falsehood
 no longer holds. He himself utters propositions which a common
 man dubs true or false; but he stands beyond them (S.64). As with
 nature so with the various deities he feels to be identical. Thus Rsi
 Vamadeva says : “It was I who lived as Manu and also as Sirya.
 I am the wise sage, Kaksivan. It is I who made Kutsa, the son of
 Arjuni, very powerful. And I am that wise poet USana. Behold me
 here. I have bestowed this earth upon Aryas. I have bestowed
showers of rain upon the mortal who offers oblations. I have guided
the course of the loudly roaring waters. Gods move as per my
directions. Exhilerated by the influence of Soma, it is I who razed
to the ground ninety-nine fortresses of Sambara altogether. The

                                   300
                                              Effects of God-realisation


hundredth fortress I gave to Divodasa for his habitation and protected
him at his sacrifice” (S.65). Similarly Rsi Vaikuntha Indra says, “I
was the first possessor of all this precious wealth of creation; I am
its over-lord. All men approach me alone like a son unto his father.
I favour my devotees with the enjoyment of bliss. I am Indra, the
invincible self. Never was I a prey to death. All devotees approach
me for the delicious mellifluous juice. Those who are attached to me
in sweet reciprocity of friendship shall never perish” (S.66). This
identification with Manu, Sirya and Indra mentioned above is
extended to many other deities; and the aspirant feels that he has
achieved great powers also. There are a number of Mantras which
describe such powers. “I am the ruler of all the mortal beings as well
as the immortal gods. The gods act only as per the dictates of Varuna.
I am the sovereign of all men and their possessions. [ am that
sovereign Varuna. All the first heavenly powers were gifted only to
me. I am Indra, I am Varuna, I am this wide earth, this unfathomable
firmament and this grand canopy of heaven. I know all beings. I am
the impelling force behind all the movementsof all creatures of this
vast universe. I direct the showers of rain to descend on the earth.
It is at my behest that the heavenly bodies move according to the
laws of Rta and it is I who governs the threefold creation of this
universe following the laws of Rta,” proclaims Rsi Trasadasyu
Paurukutsya (S.67). It is worth while noting that the aspirant
gradually becomes endowed with the divine attributes of omnipres-
ence and omnipotence.

     The mystic now boldly declares that he verily enters into
Brahman and becomes all-that-there-is. He merges in the supreme
Self and does not remain outside. He also realises that he is the
immanent bond in the universe. “Performing various good deeds, he
gets some light and then follows the Pathway to God with great
vigour. Thereafter he tries to rise higher up and reach the state of
bliss; and ultimately he merges his self in the supreme Self” (S.68).
The released soul expresses its identity with the sub-human world
as well as with the human world” (S.69). And finally, the mystic
realises that he is the innermost principle not only of the beings on
the earth but also of the gods in the heaven. Thus is confirmed, by
first-hand experience, the doctrine of all-pervasiveness of Brahman.

                                  301
Pathway to God in the Vedas



The dictum that all is Brahman can be established by intellectual
argument, no doubt; but it becomes significant only when corrobo-
rated experientially.

      God-realisation irons out whatever imperfections an aspirant
may have. He becomes a whole and integrated personality. Once
there is realisation of the oneness ofexistence, altruism follows suit.
Being interested in the welfare of all, a saint necessarily engages
himself in activities which are beneficial to humanity. “Brahman is
the sacrifice; Brahman is the sacrificer; Adhvaryu is born from
Brahman; and the oblation is contained in Brahman. Brahman is the
sacrificial ladle full of ghee. Brahman is the first principle of
sacrifice” (S.70). As Brahman is perfect, so is the saint. “My powers
of seeing and hearing are inseparable; my breathing, viz. inhalation
and exhalation and my diffusive breath are also inseparable. I am
inseparable, an individual whole” (S.71). He possesses things that
bestow happiness on mankind. “Agni am I; I have full knowledge
of all things ever since my birth. I have illumination in my eyes and
immortal ambrosia in my mouth. I am the three-fold vital air and
measurer of the mid-region. I am the eternal light and heat and I am
also the offered oblations” (S.72).

      The God-intoxicated Rsi, Nabhanedistha Manava declares to
the world the supreme bliss of identification with the highest Reality.
“This is my navel centre, here is my home; these are my gods. I
myself am all. 1 am the twice-born; I am the first-born of the eternal
order” (5.73). “Iam the first-born of the Rta.         1am more ancient than
gods; I am the immortal name; he that gives me, keeps me; him, who
eats all food, I eat as food” (S.74). The same is repeated in another
verse. “I have not consumed the food nor has the food consumed
me. The food itself has consumed the food” (S.75). Thus there
comes to be one’s identity (1) with the supreme spiritual Principle,
(ii) with the entire universe and consequently with all entities —
 living or non-living, conscious or unconscious, moving or non-
moving; (iii) there also comes to be identity between the subject and
the   object   as   well   as   the   subject-object   relation.   These   are
respectively, the psychological, the cosmologico-metaphysical and
the epistemological expressions of the self-same identity.

                                       302
                                               Effects of God-realisation


     7. Universal Prayer. The two moral ends, according to Kant,
are perfection of oneself and happiness of others. We can not Strive
for others’   perfection, contends Kant, for that will be under-
estimating their autonomy. The individual himself must strive for his
perfection, while others can only promote his happiness. But in the
first place, it is a superfluous luxury in moral theory to tell a
perfected man to strive for others’ happiness. Benevolence is no
longer an ‘ought’ with him; it has become an ever-lasting ‘is’. No
one ever orders water to find its own level; it is its nature to do so.
Secondly, trying to promote others’ happiness is to treat them as
phenomena in the world of phenomena, i.e. to regard them as wholly
and solely under the thumb of sensibility. This certainly is not Kant’s
idea of man. According to him man is essentially rational and his
rationality consists in constantly overcoming sensibility. Thus man
is viewed as a noumenon in the world of phenomena. It would
therefore be wrong to strive for others’ happiness, for in doing so
we forget that they belong to a higher plane. When a person is trying
to eliminate sensibility and become perfect, why should others instill
sensibility in him by making him happy? Therefore there seems to
be some theoretical inconsistency in the duality of moral ends
prescribed by Kant. Lokamanya Tilak has corrected Kant by saying
that perfection of oneself includes perfection of others. On the
Advaitic metaphysics, with its concept of the all-pervasiveness of
one Brahman, only a unitary moral end can be consistent. Thus
according to Tilak, man has to strive for perfection, both of himself
and of others. Thirdly, we have to remember that though Kant as a
moral purist stopped at the Bonum Supremum which excluded
happiness, Kant as a great humanist had to include happiness in the
Bonum Consummatum. In his concept of complete good, he allowed
happiness, proportional to the amount of virtue. However one has
to remember that virtue is not to be practised for the sake of
happiness; happiness may follow as a result of virtue. This becomes
explicit in Plato’s idea of justice. Plato knew that left to themselves,
the sensibilities will indulge in pleasure and not direct people to the
good. Passions, therefore, must always be under the guiding
principle of reason; they are not to be eliminated, but controlled and
directed. Plato wanted a harmony which is “the beauty and health

                                   303
Pathway to God in the Vedas



of the soul, the supremacy of the divine part in man over the lower
impulses.”

       This is a re-echo of the Vedic ideal of perfection, which allows
neither a licentious freedom to the sensibilities nor an ascetic
suppression of them. The ancient seers never approved of a bi-
section of life into secular and religious. This explains the presence
of a good number of petitional prayers in Vedic literature. Behind
such a kind of prayer lurks the feeling that the prayer will get for
them what they need. Also present are the two feelings that are a
counterpart of each other, viz., consciousness of one’s creatureli-
ness and God’s omnipotence. Our consciousness of ourselves as
finite, says Descartes, implies the consciousness of God as infinite.
Thus petitional prayers are addressed to this great Giver. Needless
to say, the propitiatory element plays its own part in these prayers.
“Pure at heart and holy in nature, we engage ourselves in singing
applauses of God and remain absorbed in the perennial vision of the
spiritual sun” (S.76). Sages of yesteryears who valued a balanced
life, wisely gave to earthly matters what belonged to earthly matters
and to spiritual matters what belonged to spiritual matters. “Oh God,
be you kind to deliver to us the choicest goods — an efficient mind,
spiritual eminence, abundant wealth, healthy bodies, sweet speech
and fair days”, requests the pious Grtsamada (S.77). There are
poems which extol corporate life. “May all beings look upon me with
the eye of a well-wisher. May I look upon all beings with the eye
of a well-wisher. May we look on one another with an eye of a well-
wisher” (5.78).

       Not that the petitional prayer is the only type found here. The
Vedic seer has transcended the stage of petty needs. Now his eyes
are set on communion with the Highest. Many are the poems which
imply this purpose. “Intense spiritual aspiration, the emotional
contemplation of an ideal, is a kind of prayer” says J.B. Pratt.'
Craving for union with the highest Ideal is expressed in the form
of a prayer. “May our parents and our fellow-men live in joy and
peace. May we attain to dignity and an everlasting mystical vision”

1   The Religious Consciousness, A Psychological Study, The Macmillan Com-
    pany, New York, 1930, P. 329


                                    304
                                                   Effects of God-realisation


(S.79). Cordial relations with higher powers are sought for. “Never
be this bond between thee and sage Vimada be loosened, Oh Indra.
We are aware of your brotherly feelings. Pray, allow this connection
to be soldered fast” (S.80). The perfected saint exudes universal love
and good will. His prayer is an expression of benign emotions which
spring from him. No distinction between individual welfare and
social welfare remains. His altruism is a natural consequence of his
equanimous vision. “Let those that are already happy continue to be
happy, Oh immortal Indra. Let there not be a shadow of sorrowful
sin. Be gracious towards us who are your admirers, and make us
sufficiently strong and vigorous” (S.81). An humble and affectionate
prayer   to   Rudra   reads   :   “May all   men    in the world    live in
contentment, absolutely free from disease and with minds bending
low with righteousness and devotion” (S.82). So powerful is the grip
of auspiciousness on the mind of the sage that he wants to sweep
the world clean of evil odours
                          — physical, in the shape of pain;
psychological, in the form of agony and moral, in the outfit of sin.
“May we hear with our ears what is good and auspicious. Oh you
adorable ones, may we see with our eyes what is good and
auspicious. May we, with firm limbs and a sturdy physique, enjoy
the divinely ordained term of life by offering supplications and
laudations to you” (S.83). “Uncontaminated by disease, distress and
dereliction of duties and stuffed with devout purpose and intuitive
power, may we, the human souls be gifted with your ever-rising
companionship, Oh Surya, who are the light creative. Let that holy
figure remain in our full view” is an exhortation from Saurya
Abhitapas (S.84). There are prayers where it is difficult to sift the
propitiatory element from the thanks-giving element. These prayers
are addressed to various gods who are credited with showering
gentle drops of mercy on the pilgrim. “Obeisance to the splendour
of the supreme Brahman that sets aglow the shining gods and has
been there, even before gods came into existence. The divinities who
first beheld the prime light of Brahman, said that a mystic who can
set his eyes on that flame, shall be able to control all the shining
heavens” (S.85). “Exaltations to Rudra who makes his devotees
flawless. Oh Rudra, keep us happy with that benign body of yours,
which is calm, which is benedictory, which cures physical pains and

                                     305
Pathway to God in the Vedas



which blunts the thorns of worldly adversities” (S.86).

      Having themselves reached the spiritual summit, the only
motive of the sage is to help those that are down below. The social
aspect of such benevolent desire is the spirit of cooperation. Those
that want to reach the other shore should strive with one mind.
“Come you, come all of you. Be harmonious in conversation, just
as the venerable gods assemble and rejoice in their respective
positions” (S.87). The prudent sage           insists on    not   merely the
sameness of outward actions but a unity of purpose. Psychological
resonance, in their opinion, would automatically result in harmony
in action. Thus in the valedictory Mantra of that great epic, Rgveda,
Rsi Samvanana Angirasa visualises an Utopian state : “Let all of
you have one prayer, let your assembly be cohesive, let your minds
and thoughts be one. Now I prescribe a common prayer for all of
you and offer a single oblation to Agni on behalf of all of you.
Common be your aim, united be your hearts, of one accord be your
minds, so that all of you may live together happily and peacefully”
(S.88). Here togetherness is piled upon togetherness, symphony
upon symphony. Such prayers are the limits of human effort for
social solidarity. But the Rsi does not stop at prayers. He is confident
that the prayer would come true. “The efficacy of certain public
prayers depended on their being offered by the right persons”, quoth
a psychologist.'! Not a medicine man, nor a priest is the right person.
The right person is he, who has a direct vision of God and who
struggles to sow and harvest goodness on the earth. Thus a gracious
promise is held out to mankind. “I bless you with a union of hearts
and minds. J drive away all feeling of hatred. May you love one
another with the same warmth as a cow loves the calf which she
has borne. Be not parted. Lead a noble and dignified life. Behave
with prudence and care. Moving under a common yoke, thrive you
together; employ friendly and sweet language. I will make you all
have one aim, and be of one mind, with one leader to follow. Like
Devas who have been preserving their immortality, may you ever
have a heart full of love at all times” (5.89). 1116: 5826 wished; he


1   W.D. Selbie, The Psychology of Religion, The Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1924,
    P. 213


                                      306
                                                            Effects of God-realisation



prayed; and he received. The assurance is wonderful indeed, and
the sage Atharvan has not forgotten to plant weighty words of
practical wisdom in it. Centuries later, many saints gave vent to the
same feeling when they declared that they have achieved the end of
their life and lived only for the welfare of mankind. In fact the Vedic
prayers are minute, yet potent seeds of the tree of love which took
roots in different faiths to provide balmy shade to the afflicted souls.
The tree is thriving yet.

     The final aim of Vedic philosophy is attainment of the highest
peace which is the apex of the bliss of Brahman. The Vedas have
given to the world a countless number of Santi-mantras or peace
hymns. They may wear a simple garb of honey-decked phrases but
therein are couched profound mystical truths. “Let there be peace
to the heavens and to the mid-regions as well as to the earth. May
waters and herbs and trees gain peace. Let all the inhabitants
                                                          of the
universe be immersed in peace. May Brahman, and may everything
partake of peace. Peace unto peace itself. May that peace find its
course   to   this   humble           devotee”    (S.90).   But   wishing   peace   to
everyone is only verbal expression of peace and not the real silent
communion with the Godhead. The saint, therefore prays that even
the utterance of the word शांति            should cease (शांति रेव शांति ः) and give
way to that living, enveloping and sanctifying पराशांति — the supernal
peace.

51.   देवस्य त्वा सवि तुः प्रसवेऽधि नो 5 बाहुभ्यां पूण्णो हस्ताभ्याम्।‌

       अन्यो ATH वल्गु वदत एत सप्रीचीनान्वः संमनसस्कृणोमि। ।

       देवा इवामृतं रक्षमाणाः सायंप्रातः सौमनसो वो अस्तु
                                                      ।। अथर्ववेद 11.30.1, 5, 7

90. ` द्यौः शान्ति रन्तरि क्षं शान्ति ः पृथि वी शान्ति रापः शान्ति रोषधयः शान्ति           ः।
       वनस्पतयः शान्ति      र्वि श्वे देवाः शान्ति र्ब्रह्म शान्ति ः ad शान्ति ः शान्ति   रेव
       शान्ति ः सा मा शान्ति          रेधि ।। यजुर्वेद XXXVI17

                                    349
      Long long ago the sages of India
kindled beacon-lights on the spiritual path
that are still shining. By meditation on the
name of God the Rsis had attained to
Almighty. The four Vedas contain Mantras
which express the mystical experiences of
the   seers.   The hymns    are   eminently’
hummable as they have wings of music.
But more important is their spiritual core.
The message of individual salvaion and
welfare of the entire human race, is rich in
instruction. The Vedic selections in this
volume depict stages of a pilgrim’s progress
towards Divinity. The Vedic Mantras have
been arranged topic-wise and treated from
the twin angles of Psychology and
Philosophy of Religion. Here we see a
Pathway to God as reflected in the immortal
psalms of the Rsis.
